Silver Nets Small Gain After Setting 4-Year Low
Commodities
Silver Nets Small Gain After Setting 4-Year Low
By Nicholas Elliott
Dow Jones Newswires
670 words
9 July 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Silver eked out a small gain Friday at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange, but the four-year low it set earlier in the session was the story of the day, analysts said.
While the dethronement of gold has been the subject of much comment, silver has taken more of a battering
this year, losing 9% of its value, while gold is little changed from where it began the year.
The near-term causes of silver's weakness are its poor technicals, or the weakness of its price trend when
analyzed with charting tools, and the strength in the dollar, a negative for most metals markets. But according
to some analysts, the fall in silver is further proof of its transformation from a precious to an industrial metal,
and its consequent sensitivity to the performance of the world economy.
July silver rose 2.7 cents to $4.235 a troy ounce after setting a low of $4.18, while the most active September
contract also rose 2.7 cents, to $4.265.
According to David Meger, analyst at Chicago brokerage firm Alaron Trading, there was a no-man's-land
between support levels at $4.30 and $4.15, a situation that explains the technical weakness and steady drop
last week.
Traders noted the net balance of long positions, or bets on higher prices, held by speculative funds, as a
reason why the market is vulnerable to long liquidation, or sales of those futures. The latest update on the
size of that speculative position won't be available until today, when the Commodity Futures Trading
Commission releases its weekly Commitments of Traders report.
Noting that silver has been trading more closely with copper than gold of late, James Steel, a metals analyst
at Refco, said, "Silver is behaving as more of an industrial than a precious metal -- I think that's one
conclusion you can draw."
Mr. Steel pointed out that between 75% and 77% of silver is mined as a byproduct of industrial metals, so that
unless production of those industrial metals slows, there will be constant new supplies of mined silver.
In copper, "we're still at [price] levels where you're going to see at least modest base metals production," he
said.
Barclays Capital, in a report released in January, said the world silver-supply deficit would be at its narrowest
this year in 10 years, and that the market could move into surplus.
A murky factor in the silver price downtrend is selling from China, which stockpiled "several million ounces" of
the metal from the 1950s onward, Mr. Steel said. China has probably been selling silver for 18 months, but
"there's no doubt that the strong dollar is a big component in their thinking," encouraging sales now, he
added.
Gold may indeed look good next to silver, analysts said. William O'Neill, senior futures strategist at Merrill
Lynch, said last week that silver isn't benefiting from several influences now supporting gold, such as less
central-bank lending and a reduced contango, or premium of future over nearby delivery months. A reduced
contango means less incentive to sell gold forward.
In other commodity markets Friday:
CRUDE OIL: Futures rallied at Nymex, as a fall in inventories and technical factors overshadowed the
imminent return of Iraqi exports to the market, analysts said. The August contract rose $1.19 to $28.21 a
barrel, adding to Thursday's gain of 78 cents.

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
ORANGE JUICE: Frozen concentrate orange-juice futures gained on the New York Cotton Exchange division
of the New York Board of Trade, extending the previous session's gains. The September contract rose 0.6
cent to 84.9 cents a pound. "It's the same story" as Thursday, a Florida-based trader said. "We saw
short-covering by funds, very light [commercial] buying, while the trade was selling more aggressively [Friday]
throughout the session."

Letters to the Editor:Go for the Gold
Letters to the Editor: Go for the Gold
135 words
2 July 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Jack Kemp's suggestion that the Fed's regulation of the money supply be anchored to the price of gold is
excellent and timely ("Our Economy Needs a Golden Anchor," editorial page, June 28). Mr. Kemp is right that
it would both assure the stability of the purchasing power of the dollar and eliminate a constant source of risk
and distraction for economic actors. But it would have another even more powerful effect: It would limit the
scope of the Fed's responsibilities to nothing more than administering the non-inflationary level of the money
supply. The Fed would have to abandon its dystopian fantasies of controlling the business cycle and
protecting us from irrational exuberance.
Donald L. Luskin
President, CEO
MetaMarkets.com Inc.
Menlo Park, Calif.

Gold Loses Its Luster as Dollar Reigns Supreme
Commodities
Gold Loses Its Luster as Dollar Reigns Supreme
By David Bogoslaw
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
584 words
29 June 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- What a difference a day makes, not to mention a meager quarter of a percentage point.
That is what bullish gold traders discovered yesterday as they watched the recent gains accrued by the
yellow metal wash away in a tide of disappointment. That followed the Federal Open Market Committee's
opting to trim interest rates by a conservative quarter of a percentage point instead of the half-point some
expected.
August gold futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange plunged more than $4
before bouncing to end the session at $270 a troy ounce, $2.80 lower than Wednesday.
To be fair, it wasn't the Fed decision alone that pulled the legs out from under gold prices. Even ahead of the
announcement Wednesday afternoon, gold had begun to weaken on a selloff in the oil sector.
Market watchers are increasingly "looking at energies as leaders for other inflation-geared commodities,"
according to George Gero, first vice president at Prudential Securities.
Gold's failure to get much upside mileage from the over-the-counter options expiration Wednesday morning
also helped convince a major investment bank it was safe to sell gold 15 minutes before the Fed announced
its decision, London-based UBS Warburg said in its daily report.
It was the U.S. dollar's reasserting itself as "king of the currencies" yesterday that was seen as the
predominant factor dragging gold prices lower.
"If we had gotten 50 [basis] points [from the Fed], a lot of players were expecting weakness in the dollar,"
said Dave Meger, senior metals analyst at Alaron Trading Corp. in Chicago. A basis point is one-hundredth of
a percentage point.
Trader Jim Pogoda at Mitsubishi International Corp. in New York agreed, noting a retreat in the euro down to
a low of 84.46 U.S. cents from more than 86 cents and weakening of the Australian dollar, back to a 50.60
U.S. cents low.
Dollar-denominated gold appears more expensive in foreign currency terms when those currencies weaken
relative to the dollar, discouraging consumer buying in places such as Europe and spurring producers, such
as those in Australia, to sell more of their production forward.
"It's strange that one day can change a mind-set, but that seems to be what has happened here," Alaron's
Mr. Meger said. "We've had such a positive tone in this gold market over the last couple weeks. A lot of
people have said it's had to do with the strong lease rate environment we've had."
In other commodity markets yesterday:
COPPER: Comex futures slid after speculative funds sold in the wake of the Federal Reserve's cut in interest
rates. The July contract fell 1.35 cents to 70.8 cents a pound. One result of the quarter-point rate cut has
been a higher dollar, which deters demand for copper. "The stronger dollar in reference to the euro zone has
been one of the big culprits in the weakness we've seen in copper of late," said Mr. Meger.
WHEAT: Futures fell to new contract lows on the Chicago Board of Trade ahead of today's U.S. Department
of Agriculture Acreage and Stocks report and on forecasts of ideal harvest weather in the Southern Plains.
The July contract lost 4.75 cents to $2.4275 a bushel.

Our Economy Needs a Golden Anchor
Our Economy Needs a Golden Anchor
By Jack Kemp
1,396 words
28 June 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
How many more dashed hopes and false recoveries must we experience before politicians and monetary
authorities accept the fact that our inability to manage fiat currencies is causing the global economic
slowdown? They keep waiting for interest-rate reductions to kick in, yet more than six months after the Fed
began lowering rates the economy continues to weaken. Waiting for the recently enacted tax cuts to provide
"stimulus" will prove futile as well. The economy does not suffer a lack of consumer demand, and more
money in people's pockets will not revive the supply side of the economy.
Ronald Reagan once said he knew of no great nation in history that went off the gold standard and remained
great. Since Aug. 15, 1971, when the U.S. ceased to redeem dollars held by foreign governments for gold,
we have put that thesis to the test. For the first time in human history, not a single major currency in the world
was linked to a commodity. Economist Milton Friedman called the situation "unprecedented" and said it is "not
a long-term viable alternative." "The world," he said, "needs a long-term anchor of some kind."
In the short term, at least, he was vindicated. In creating a world monetary system of floating fiat currencies
with the stroke of a pen, President Nixon touched off world-wide inflation that lasted through the '70s and
early '80s.
Yet America recovered to preside over the demise of world communism, and overcame the rising inflation
and unemployment of "stagflation" to enjoy an unparalleled 18-year economic expansion. Today, the U.S. is
at the pinnacle of its power and enjoying its greatest prosperity ever.
Were Messrs. Reagan and Friedman wrong? I don't think so. If the U.S. has so far come out on top in this
experiment, it is only because other countries' economies have suffered even more from floating currencies.
Once the U.S. government ceased redeeming gold at $35 an ounce, its price quadrupled on world markets to
$140 to reflect the dollar's diminished value. By breaking the gold link, the Nixon economic team forced the
unwanted liquidity pouring out of the Fed -- which had thus far built up in the Eurodollar market and the
portfolios of foreign central banks -- to remain inside the U.S. economy where it would manifest itself in price
inflation. Robert Mundell was the first to predict, in January 1972, there would soon be a dramatic rise in the
price of oil, with general inflation to follow.
Where the rest of the economics profession blamed the Arab oil-producing states for quadrupling the oil price
in 1973, Mr. Mundell and those supply-siders who followed his intellectual lead knew that gold's quadrupling
had led the way. Tax rates rose through "bracket creep," capital formation stopped in its tracks, and it soon
took two workers to produce the same income that one had brought home before the experiment. The
stagflation that had its roots in leaving the gold standard was compounded when Congress and three
different presidents tried to fight it with wage and price controls and high marginal tax rates.
But discretionary monetary policy is Janus-faced, and instead of too much liquidity in the world economy we
now have too little. Deflation began in 1996 when the Fed tightened monetary policy to combat some
inflation it had created attempting to offset the economic drag of the Clinton tax hikes. A rising dollar then
caused the dollar pegs of emerging economies to snap, set off the Asian, Brazilian and Russian economic
meltdowns, and caused the price of oil and other commodities to collapse. Oil producers took a two-year
holiday from drilling, which in turn created an oil shortage and drove energy prices sky high.
Now, the energy-price hikes are working their way through the economy and are misconstrued by the Fed as
inflation. Once again, central bank errors in the discretionary management of floating fiat currencies have
put the entire world economy at risk.
The Fed has cut interest rates 275 basis points since the start of the year, but the price of gold is still down
to about $272 from $385 in 1996, having fallen $5 yesterday alone on the Fed's announcement that it was
lowering the fed funds rate another 25 basis points. Commodity prices are near their lowest levels in 15

--- Page 11 ---
Page 11 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
years, and the foreign-exchange value of the dollar has risen against all major currencies since the Fed
began its interest-rate-easing cycle.
Without a gold standard, the Fed has no means of determining how much liquidity markets demand, and all it
does by targeting interest rates is guess how much liquidity to inject or withdraw to counteract mistakes it
made earlier. The Fed may be on its way to mimicking the mistakes the Bank of Japan made when it lowered
interest rates to zero, all the while prolonging and deepening Japan's monetary deflation.
This is no way to manage a currency. It's obvious that we have accumulated a long series of small
deflationary errors by the Fed that are dragging down the U.S. economy and helping depress world
commerce. It's time to restore a golden anchor to the dollar before our luck runs out and we suffer a real
economic calamity.
The Fed may yet get lucky with its rate cuts, although the Bank of Japan never did. The only certain way to
end this deflation is to have the Fed stop targeting interest rates and begin targeting gold directly -- not by
"fixing" the price of gold by administrative fiat as some people mistakenly characterize it, but rather by
calibrating the level of liquidity in the economy, over which the Fed has exclusive and precise control, to keep
the market price of gold stable within a narrow band closer to $325 than $275.
There is nothing mysterious about how gold could be used as a reference point or how a new monetary
standard for a new millennium would work. It would simply mean the Fed would stop guessing how much
liquidity is good for the economy and allow the market to make that decision for it. With the dollar defined in
terms of gold and with American citizens free to buy and sell gold at will, the Fed would forget about raising
or lowering interest rates and simply add liquidity (buy bonds) when the price of gold tries to fall and
subtract liquidity (sell bonds) when it tries to rise. Markets would determine interest rates.
The paper dollar would once again be as good as gold -- no more, no less. There would be no need for the
U.S. government to maintain a large stock of gold or to redeem gold and dollars on demand since people
would be free to do so on their own in the marketplace. As long as the Fed calibrated its infusions and
withdrawals of liquidity by the market price of gold, the world would be free of monetary inflations and
deflations caused by the whims and errors of central bank governors, as was the case for more than 200
years when the private Bank of England managed the pound sterling in exactly that way.
The good news is that this could all be done easily, if President Bush and Treasury Secretary Paul O'Neill
could work out an accord with Alan Greenspan. That accomplished, I believe Britain would soon follow to
make the pound as good as gold and avoid having to adopt a sinking euro.
There is nothing simpler than a gold standard, as Alexander Hamilton pointed out when he persuaded the
first Congress to adopt one. Just as President Nixon took us off with an executive order, President Bush can
put us back on with the stroke of a pen. It would be politically popular, as ordinary people benefit most. At
Camp David in 1971, as President Nixon signed the papers, he is reported to have said: "I don't know why I'm
doing this. William Jennings Bryan ran against gold three times and he lost three times."
---
Mr. Kemp, co-director of Empower America, was the Republican vice-presidential nominee in 1996.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Go for the Gold" -- WSJ July 2, 2001)

Data on GrowthIn Supply DrainEnergy Prices
Commodities
Data on Growth In Supply Drain Energy Prices
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
651 words
28 June 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Oil futures dropped at the New York Mercantile Exchange as reports showed growing stockpiles of crude oil
and gasoline and, surprisingly, imports, which may signify weakening of demand overseas.
The nearby August crude-oil futures fell 5.1%, or $1.37, to $25.61 a barrel, hitting a 14-month low during the
day before recovering a bit before the Nymex close.
The price of the August gasoline contract fell 8.2%, or 6.35 cents, to 71.44 cents a gallon, after hitting a
17-month low during the session. Natural-gas and heating-oil prices registered smaller decreases.
It was a second consecutive session of declines that began when traders geared up for a weekly inventory
report that was released after the close of the markets Tuesday by the American Petroleum Institute.
With the actual API numbers in front of them, plus statistics released yesterday morning by the Department of
Energy, analysts said the energy-supply picture is even stronger than previously believed. API reported a
buildup of 565,000 barrels in U.S. crude-oil reserves, while DOE said inventories were unchanged last week.
Analysts expected a 500,000-barrel buildup.
The gasoline-inventory numbers were even more bloated, as the API reported a 3.3 million-barrel increase in
reserves, and DOE reported a rise of one million barrels. According to both outlets, that leaves the nation's
gasoline stockpile at more than 220 million barrels -- near a five-year high.
That buildup already has translated into lower prices at the pump for drivers -- a trend the financial markets
seem to indicate won't end soon.
"It looks like a rocket ship taking off, just phenomenal," analyst John Kilduff, of Fimat USA Inc., said of the
inventory statistics. "And on the other side, gasoline demand has been a little weak so far this year.
Consumers have really responded to what they viewed as high prices before."
Retail gasoline prices have fallen 13 cents this month, to a nationwide average of $1.63 a gallon for all
grades, including taxes, according to Lundberg Survey, a newsletter that polls 8,000 service stations. That
decline included a drop of almost a dime in the past two weeks alone, and the newsletter's publisher, Trilby
Lundberg, says further declines are a "strong possibility."
The flow of foreign gasoline into the U.S. could help keep supplies high and prices low. The DOE's latest
inventory report showed gasoline imports above one million barrels a day for a third straight week, which
analysts said shows weak demand from overseas drivers.
Yesterday's interest-rate cut by the Federal Reserve also helped push energy prices lower. It fanned energy
investors' worries about a sagging U.S. economy that they say has hurt U.S. consumption.
"If we're going to turn the market around somewhat, we've got to see demand in the U.S. pick up," said
analyst Phil Flynn of Alaron Trading Corp. "It seems our economy is turning around a bit, but that hasn't
shown up in prices yet."
In other commodity trading yesterday:
GOLD: Futures fell after the Fed's quarter-point rate cut, disappointing those who had expected a half-point,
which might have raised the risk of inflation and enhanced the appeal of gold as a safe asset. The August
contract on the Comex division of Nymex dropped $4.20 to $272.80 a troy ounce. Falls in energy futures also
weakened gold, traders said.

--- Page 13 ---
Page 13 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
COFFEE: Futures fell to a new contract low on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange division of the New
York Board of Trade after selling by speculators and producers. The September contract fell 0.6 cent to 57.95
cents a pound and set a low of 57.4 cents.

Market Tests Gold-Fund Manager's Mettle
Fund Track
Market Tests Gold-Fund Manager's Mettle
By Theo Francis
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
803 words
28 June 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C25
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Jean-Marie Eveillard is a patient man. As evidence, consider that he runs a gold fund.
Founded in 1993, Mr. Eveillard's First Eagle SoGen Gold Fund has languished for much of the past eight
years. An investor putting $10,000 in the fund at inception would have a little more than $7,000 today. With
declines like that, plus investor withdrawals, the fund's assets have dwindled to $10.9 million from a high of
$70.2 million. So it was little wonder that in 1998, Mr. Eveillard publicly pondered closing the thing altogether.
He didn't, and during autumn of last year, finally, the fund began to glitter. It has gained 28.35% since the
beginning of the year and more than 37% since bottoming out in November.
Still, Mr. Eveillard, 61 years old, remains wary.
"Do I think the recent upturn and then downturn in the price of the metal are significant? I don't have the
foggiest idea," Mr. Eveillard says. "The gold market is a very opaque market."
To say the least. The portfolio Mr. Eveillard oversees is hardly the only leaden gold fund. The fund-tracking
firm Lipper Inc. ranks it in the top 5% of funds for five-year returns, but that is in a decidedly lackluster field. In
fact, for 11 out of the past 17 years, Lipper's Gold Fund Index has lost ground. Yesterday, gold futures fell
$3.95 to $272.30 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
"Any long-term measure you look at, [gold funds] are absolutely the dead-last rate of return," says analyst
Don Cassidy of Lipper, which tracks mutual-fund performance and produces the gold-fund index.
Small surprise, then, that so many financial advisers and savvy investors shun the stuff.
"I certainly wouldn't recommend gold as part of a traditional asset allocation," says Paul Merriman, a Seattle
investment adviser and market timer who sometimes trades gold for clients who insist on it. "Gold has really
become more of a speculation than an investment."
And yet some stick with it, including Mr. Eveillard, who also manages global and international value funds that
have returned 18.1% and 12.44%, respectively, during the past 12 months. "No. 1, gold is a good insurance
policy," he says. "No. 2, currently it's a cheap one."
Mr. Eveillard started his fund, which primarily invests in companies that mine and process gold, at a time
when gold was soaring. Boosters predicted that demand for gold would outstrip supply, driving prices even
higher. Then, instead of rising more, gold sank.
Still, especially at such low prices, gold continued to speak to Mr. Eveillard, a bargain-hunting value manager
by temperament. But increasingly there were signs that gold had lost some of its appeal as a haven. During
the stock-market crash of 1987, gold didn't fare particularly well; nor did the Gulf War in 1990 drive up
demand.
By 1998, even Mr. Eveillard had grown discouraged. He considered shutting the fund down. "I said to myself,
`Patience is a virtue, but stubbornness was not,'" he remembers.
Later that year, however, the Russia debt crisis struck, and the Long Term Capital Management hedge fund
teetered on the brink of failure before being rescued by a consortium led by Federal Reserve officials. "And I
thought, `Hey, if the financial system is so fragile, inherently fragile, that the Fed has to arrange the bailout of
a hedge fund, I'm not going to close my gold fund,'" Mr. Eveillard recalls.

--- Page 15 ---
Page 15 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But once again, the world made it through the crises without much lasting benefit to gold. Others say
investors preferred the safety and reassurance of cash and U.S. government securities to that of gold. Mr.
Eveillard chalks it up to another save by Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan.
"Nothing happened to gold, because Mr. Greenspan proceeded to throw a lot of money at the problem, and
that was enough to douse the fire," Mr. Eveillard says. He reasons that Mr. Greenspan and other central
bankers fear they would lose their reputations for mastery of the economy if investors turned to gold rather
than national currencies in times of trouble.
For now, Mr. Eveillard plans to stick with gold, however tarnished its image. Eventually, he reasons, some
crisis will flare that central bankers can't or won't salvage, or demand will finally outstrip production, and then
gold will shine again. But he won't altogether rule out the possibility of eventually shutting the fund and
turning his attention to something else.
"Every now and then," Mr. Eveillard says, "I say to myself, `Hey, maybe I'm missing something big.'"

Strindberg Through the Eyes of Bergman---The Acclaimed Swedish DirectorTakes on `The Ghost Sonata';A Coda From Mr. Larson
LEISURE & ARTS
Strindberg Through the Eyes of Bergman --- The Acclaimed Swedish Director Takes on `The Ghost
Sonata'; A Coda From Mr. Larson
By Amy Gamerman
1,495 words
27 June 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
New York -- EARPHONES WERE PROVIDED for the non-Swedish-speaking culturati who crowded the
Brooklyn Academy of Music's Harvey Theater last week to catch Ingmar Bergman's staging of August
Strindberg's "The Ghost Sonata," which ended a very brief engagement on Sunday. Instructions for the use of
these earphones, through which audience members could listen to Inga Stina Ewbank's English translation of
the play, were painstakingly delivered at the start of the night. "Put them in your ears," a BAM staffer loudly
announced, in a manner only slightly less surreal than Strindberg's masterpiece itself.
But as the lights dimmed and "The Ghost Sonata" began, no translation was needed for Mr. Bergman's
beautifully sinister vision to work its disturbing magic. It's the images, not the words, that make this legendary
film director's "Sonata" the haunting experience that it is. A smiling, sweet-faced old lady with a face as
powdered and painted as a china doll huddles over her knitting, an enormous red ear blossoming out of one
side of her head like a tomato. A distinguished-looking military man in a jacket trimmed with gold braid sits
raptly before a nude statue of a woman, then lovingly dusts her breasts with a cloth. Slumped in a wheelchair
in a heavy fur-trimmed coat and fur hat, an old man peers at a newspaper through a pair of impenetrable
black glasses; a fine lady in black with a giant boil on her face stands behind him, mouthing words no one can
hear. In between snatches of Bartok, a fat, slatternly maid lifts up a manhole cover in the floor of the stage
and pours in a bucket of slops the color of blood.
And that's only the beginning. Mr. Bergman himself seems haunted by this dark, defiantly anti-naturalistic
1907 play, which he has returned to throughout his life. He first directed "The Ghost Sonata" as a young man
fresh out of Stockholm University in 1941. Now 82, he has staged the play for the fourth, and possibly the last
time, for the Royal Dramatic Theatre of Sweden. At the play's core is another octogenarian, the malevolent
and mysterious Old Man, who is played by Jan Malmsjo, a veteran of several Bergman films. Also called
Hummel, he's the one sitting in the wheelchair as the play begins. Mr. Malmsjo captures the creepy essence
of this puppet master, who drains the vitality from everyone he touches: When he takes off his concealing hat
and glasses to reveal a large, bald head and glittering eyes, he looks like a well-fed vampire. By turns
wheedling and cajoling, then mercilessly commanding, the Old Man manipulates everyone onstage to carry
out his will. Is it a coincidence that he seems a bit like a movie director?
As "The Ghost Sonata" begins, the Old Man meets the Student, a virtuous but naive young man -- who in a
neat twist, is played by Jonas Malmsjo, the older actor's son. The Student asks a girlish Milkmaid (Virpi
Pahkinen) for water from a fountain. (Goran Wassberg's pared-down set transforms that fountain into a
subterranean well set beneath the floor of the stage.) The Old Man watches him, perplexed. It turns out that
the Milkmaid is a ghost from the Old Man's past and the Student is the only person who can see her. Struck
by this gift of sight, the Old Man declares that he will make the Student his heir and help him win the beautiful
and haughty Young Lady (Elin Klinga) who lives in the grand house opposite.
But "The Ghost Sonata" makes a grotesque joke of the young man's longing for the lady, the house and "all
the beautiful, elegant things there must be inside." As the Mummy (the transfixing Gunnel Lindblom, another
Bergman stalwart), a spectral, birdlike creature who lives in a closet of that elegant house declares, "We are
not who we appear to be." Mr. Bergman probes this gulf between appearance and a hopelessly corrupt reality
with startling stagecraft. As the Student sighs over the Young Lady, Ms. Klinga stares out at the audience.
Her eyes -- with the help of special contact lenses -- are terrifying white blanks. (You wonder if she might
have escaped from a remake of "Cat People.") "Not everyone recognizes her beauty," the Old Man reflects,
with newfound irony.
Even without the contacts, Ms. Klinga is deeply spooky: Her Young Lady seems at once completely virginal
and totally toxic. Although her posture is ramrod straight, her expression a haughty mask, from time to time
she pants like a dog, or twists to sniff her own armpit. Her corrupted purity is dramatically exposed in the
play's final scene, with an embellishment completely of Mr. Bergman's invention. The Student yanks down the

--- Page 17 ---
Page 17 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Young Lady's fine silvery gown, revealing ugly red slashes and bruises all over her chest and arms and
stripping her to a white shift stained with blood between the legs. Of the many unveilings that take place
throughout the play, this is the most unsettling one.
There aren't many props on the bare stage -- a grandfather clock, some chairs, a box of dying hyacinths --
and the colors are for the most part dreary monochrome with splashes of dull red and blue. The production's
vividness is supplied by the actors themselves, with help from Anna Bergman's eerie costumes and Leif
Qvistrom and Barbro Forsgardh's wigs and masks. Ms. Lindblom's Mummy -- the Young Lady's mother --
emerges from her closet prison cackling and trilling like a deranged parrot, greeting acquaintances with little
nibbling kisses on the cheek. Wrapped in a decomposing reddish smock and a shroud-like cape, with pink
sores covering her balding scalp, she's like a vivacious corpse. The actors in smaller parts are equally
eye-catching. Gerthi Kulle's Dark Lady -- she's the one with that giant boil, which she shields uselessly with
her hand -- seems positively steeped in shame. She averts her eyes from the others onstage, or furtively
approaches them, only to be rebuffed or ignored. It's a deeply expressive performance, and yet she says
almost nothing.
But then words are a decoy in "The Ghost Sonata," whose characters see language as a repository of lies
and deceit. As the Old Man says, "I prefer silence. . . . Silence conceals nothing." How pleased he would be
by Mr. Bergman's "Sonata," which finds its music between the lines.
I wonder if much attention would be paid to "Tick, Tick . . . Boom!", a musical at the Jane Street Theatre, were
it not one of the final works by the late Jonathan Larson. Mr. Larson was the prodigiously talented young
writer and composer who died at the age of 35, just before the opening of his rock musical megahit, "Rent," in
1996. "Tick, Tick . . . Boom!" arrives as nothing less than a sacred relic of musical theater -- one of the last
remnants of a gifted artist whose life ended far too soon. That's just way too much baggage for this
occasionally charming but terminally self-absorbed little musical to bear. This is the kind of show that asks
you to sympathize with its hero, a struggling creator of musicals, as he complains, "For years I've been hiking
in the foothills. Cutting demo tapes, scrounging for grants, presenting songs in countless workshops. . . ."
Originally conceived as a one-man show to be performed by Mr. Larson himself, "Tick, Tick . . . Boom!" has
been retooled as a musical for three actors by David Auburn (the author of "Proof") and Stephen Oremus, the
show's musical director. Directed by Scott Schwartz, the show is deliberately small in scale. A talented cast
plays the principal roles: Jonathan himself (Raul Esparza), his girlfriend Susan (Amy Spanger) and his best
friend Michael (Jerry Dixon). They also play a fleet of smaller characters (Jon's Dad; his fatuous agent), all
but knocking elbows on the theater's small stage. A four-piece band sits on a riser just above their shoulders.
Although many of the songs are catchy, with persuasive rock melodies and deft lyrics, this slender
autobiographical revue about the agonies of turning 30 and still not having made it on Broadway seems
painfully jejune. At other times, it just seems painful. The show's title is underscored by a clock that loudly
ticks throughout the show, as our Sondheim wannabe frets that time is passing him by. "They're singing
`Happy Birthday,'" Jon laments in the title song, "30/90." "I just wish it all were a dream/ It feels much more
like doomsday." Time was running out for Mr. Larson in a way that he couldn't have imagined. It's hard to
listen to his words now without wincing.

Home Depot, Caterpillar FallWith Economy-Linked Stocks
Abreast of the Market
Home Depot, Caterpillar Fall With Economy-Linked Stocks
By Robert O'Brien
Dow Jones Newswires
888 words
26 June 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- On a day when investors were looking ahead to an aggressive reduction in interest rates later
this week, but despairing that the economy needs one, many economy-sensitive stocks fell.
For example, shares of retailing issues, which have been up and down with spending patterns of consumers,
posted a decidedly downbeat session. Home Depot declined $1.71 to $49, while Lowe's, which had run up to
a 52-week high last week, declined 1.50 to 75.50, Sears Roebuck lost 1.01 to 40.74, and Gap fell 1.19 to
30.84.
Shares of capital-equipment makers and other manufacturers exposed to the economy also weakened, with
Caterpillar off 2 to 52.65, Minnesota Mining & Manufacturing losing 1.09 to 115.69 and Tyco International
easing 32 cents to 52.98.
The declines came ahead of the Federal Open Market Committee meeting that gets under way in
Washington today. When the outcome of the meeting is announced tomorrow, investors expect to hear that
the Fed has cut rates for a sixth time this year, and by as a half point.
"Up until a week ago, I think the consensus on the Street" was for a quarter-point only, said Philip Orlando,
chief investment officer at Value Line Asset Management who has stuck by a forecast of a half-point
reduction all along. "That's what is pressuring the market." If the Fed goes for a half-point move, he said, "that
implies the economy is weaker than we thought a few weeks ago."
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished 100.37 points weaker, off 0.95%, at 10504.22. However, the
Nasdaq Composite Index managed to finish in the black, rising 16.03 points, or 0.79%, to 2050.87.
Shares of some of the fiber-optics manufacturers that faded badly last week managed to post gains, giving
the technology sector some leadership. Juniper Networks (Nasdaq), which sank to a 52-week low June 19,
moved up 1.51 to 30.50. Avici Systems (Nasdaq), which also dropped to a 52-week low last week, gained 55
cents to 7.45. Optical Communications Products (Nasdaq) moved ahead 76 cents to 12.17.
Several chip makers also managed to prosper, despite a mixed news backdrop. Communications-chip
makers Xilinx gained 2.64 to 43.71, while Altera improved 1.52 to 26.89, both on Nasdaq, and Broadcom
(Nasdaq) advanced 1.81 to 35.49.
After the close, however, Applied Micro Circuits, a maker of high-bandwidth communications chips, reduced
its guidance for its fiscal first quarter, citing continued weakness in demand. The shares fell 8% in after-hours
trading.
Compaq Computer announced that it will use Intel's Itanium processors in all its server products, thereby
transferring key enterprise processor technology to Intel. Compaq gained 40 cents to 13.90, while Intel
(Nasdaq) moved ahead 1.07 to 28.58.
Internet service provider Yahoo (Nasdaq) gained 2.42 to 19.73. The gains came after an analyst at U.S.
Bancorp Piper Jaffray said in a research report that the online advertising market bottomed in April, and that
prices have stabilized.
Nokia fell 90 cents to 22.40, following some cautious comments from Credit Suisse First Boston about the
outlook for the Finnish mobile-phone maker's earnings.
Business Objects (Nasdaq) fell 2.69 to 22.07. CIBC World Markets reduced its rating on the French provider
of business- intelligence applications, citing concerns about the company's ability to live up to earnings
forecasts for the current quarter.

Families:Genealogy Gone Haywire
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Families: Genealogy Gone Haywire
By Elizabeth Bernstein
2,005 words
15 June 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
[Grandpa had two wives? Uncle Pierre wasn't French? America's obsession with exploring roots is airing
family secrets, sparking feuds and even raising privacy concerns. Elizabeth Bernstein reports.]
CONNIE CARRILES always wanted to know more about her ancestors. But recently, her genealogical search
has made her wish she belonged to someone else's family.
After scouring the Internet and cemetery documents, she discovered one relative had two families, found out
her great-grandparents' bodies might have been removed from their graves and was berated by a relative
who thinks she is sticking her nose where it doesn't belong. Sometimes, just thinking about it all can be
upsetting: "One problem just seems to lead to another," the Chesapeake, Va., woman says.
Aren't families complicated enough without digging skeletons out of the closet? Many Americans are learning
this the hard way, thanks to soaring interest in genealogy during the past decade. This urge to connect with
the past has spawned an estimated $200-million-a-year industry of Web sites and research outfits. It also has
amateur genealogists uncovering everything from minor embarrassments (ancestors who were horse thieves)
to long-concealed scandals (grandpa had a mistress). And the potential for genealogy to go haywire is only
growing, now that new technology, including do-it-yourself DNA screening, can fill practically every twig of the
family tree.
If the news is bad -- one Tennessee man discovered he wasn't even descended from the family he spent
decades researching -- it also can come at a high price. While a year of basic document searches costs
about $700, according to Everton's Genealogical Helper, a more detailed effort can easily run $20,000 or
more. What's more, all this has created unexpected privacy and harassment issues. A Congressional hearing
last year, for example, questioned whether genealogy sites post too much personal information. One
mounting concern: that family names, often used as bank and credit-card passwords, will get into the wrong
hands. These sites are "a gold mine for identity thieves," says Joel Reidenberg, a professor at Fordham Law
School.
Certainly, not all genealogy searches turn into disasters, and plenty of people love the search. But for Jim
Reynolds, researching the dead has caused serious strains among the living. He learned that his late parents
never actually divorced when they separated. While he was glad to know the truth, his step-siblings won't
speak to him anymore. Mr. Reynolds, a retired engineer in Santee, Calif., says he regrets hurting his
relatives, but "the facts are the facts are the facts."
Even less-dramatic findings can get in the way of cherished family lore. Katherine Cole says her late
grandfather long prided himself on family ties to a Revolutionary War colonel who fought alongside George
Washington. Then military records revealed the colonel's true claim to fame: his weight. At 320 pounds, he
couldn't find a horse that could carry him fast enough. "That's when Grampie's interest in genealogy sort of
petered out," says Ms. Cole, a writer in Portland, Ore.
The number of these searches started growing about a decade ago, as Baby Boomers, seeing their elders
pass away, began wondering about their place in history. That's accelerated now that so many once-remote
archives are on the Internet. Today, there are at least 100,000 such sites, according to Cyndi's List, a service
that catalogs genealogy links. On Ancestry.com, which offers everything from Census data to message
boards where long-lost relatives can connect, membership has doubled to 400,000 in the past year. Another
site, JaysKids.com, seeks to find "all 57 children" of the late Blues singer Screamin' Jay Hawkins. In all, a
recent poll by Maritz Marketing Research found that interest in genealogy has risen 33% since 1995.
Not to leave any stone unturned, there are now kits that enable the most compulsive researchers to compare
their genetic makeup to that of others. (You use a cotton swab to scrape cells from inside your cheek, and
send it off for a $220 lab analysis.) Justin Howery, a Denver law student who has been corresponding online

--- Page 23 ---
Page 23 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
with about 30 other Howerys, recently persuaded one of them to take a DNA test. The two men believe they
have a common ancestor from the 1400s but wanted to prove it. The result: a match. "It's wild," he says, "to
think we have the same DNA."
But this technology can also open the door to problems. Jack Stidham discovered he was actually climbing
up the wrong tree. He spent 30 years tracking his roots, including three years traveling the country in a motor
home. He traced his lineage back to a 17th-century Scandinavian doctor. But, when he and 10 other
presumed descendants took a DNA test recently, the results were quite a surprise. Almost everyone showed
a genetic match -- but not Mr. Stidham, the man who had launched the genealogy effort.
The Morristown, Tenn., retiree, says he regrets that the DNA test "opened a can of worms." His son David
believes that somewhere in the line there was an adoption or birth outside marriage: "Who knows what
happened behind bedroom doors?"
Ms. Carriles, the woman who is looking for her great-grandparents' bodies, was totally unprepared for what
her research turned up. This spring, she posted a query on FamilySearch.org, a site that offers birth,
marriage and other records. She asked visitors to the site for information about a relative and got a response
from his immediate family. Actually, two responses, she says -- from two daughters who appeared to know
nothing of each other. Ms. Carriles says she isn't about to tell. "I'm just doing this for me and my children,"
she says. "I don't want to offend anyone."
For some, the shock comes in the form of the bill. Besides the sea of genealogy tchotchkes from book bags
("Genealogists Never Die, They Just Lose Their Census") to baby bibs ("I'm the Newest Sprout in Our Family
Tree"), the Internet is loaded with fee-based services that experts say sometimes promise more than they
deliver. "There's a certain amount of fiction and fairy tales in all of these databases," says Dick Eastman,
author of a popular online genealogy newsletter. Even the DNA testing is controversial; some geneticists say
it errs about one in 100 times.
Royal Lineages to Adam, of Farmington, Utah, sells a CD-ROM for $59.99 that traces lineages all the way
back "to Adam and Eve," assuming you already have mapped your roots to a figure such as George
Washington, Charlemagne or Francus, King of the West Franks. Julia Schacht, who compiled the CD, says
others have questioned how it's possible to trace roots to the Garden of Eden but that she's done "the best I
possibly could with the records I had."
Another service, the now-defunct publisher Halbert's, sold book-style lists of surnames as well as such
memorabilia as keychains imprinted with family "coats of arms." But, according to numerous complaints to the
local Better Business Bureau, the lists really were little more than telephone directories.
There also are privacy issues, including the worry that criminals or marketers will get names or birthdates of
living people that are posted in online family trees. At the request of the House Judiciary Committee, the
Federal Trade Commission last year reviewed genealogy sites and concluded that some fail to filter such
data or warn users about the risks of posting it online. Another concern: Web sites that sell data that
members provide. One site, Genealogy.com, packages some of those details into CD-ROMs. Genealogy.com
says only one part of the site collects information for resale and that users are clearly warned about that.
"When you put stuff on a genealogy database, you're putting it in the public domain," says Fordham's Prof.
Reidenberg, who testified in the Congressional hearing last year.
Still, the genealogy business continues to grow because so many love the hobby. For many families, these
searches have led to happy reunions and turned up proud tales that can be passed on to future generations.
Some don't even mind the surprises. On RootsWeb.com, there's a Black Sheep message board, where
people boast about notorious ancestors like Jesse James and John Wilkes Booth. There are always going to
be a few people "you wouldn't necessarily have wanted to go to dinner with," says Bijan Bayne, a Cincinnati
author who has been researching the slaves in his family and the people who owned them.
David Gumpert, a marketing executive, wishes he could be so unruffled about his findings. He became
interested in his roots after an aunt died, prompting an eight year, $20,000 search during which he learned
that another now-deceased relative neglected to help his late aunt escape the Nazis in occupied Belgium.
Now, he is having trouble finishing the book he has been writing on his family, worried about how other
relatives will take the news. "Every time you turn over a rock, more bugs crawl out," says Mr. Gumpert, of
Needham, Mass.
And beware: You may not be so glad to meet every long-lost relative who appears in your e-mail "in" box.
Dennice Goudie, who has traced her family back to 17th century France, recently began getting e-mail from a
distant step-cousin she had never met. At first, the woman was polite, agreeing to share information about the
family. The exchange soon turned into a family feud, as the cousin blasted Ms. Goudie for posting information
online without her permission. Says Ms. Goudie: "Ever since then, I've been really careful about checking out
my relatives."

Regional Report:States Strive to Bridge Budget Gaps---Most Focus on Cost CutsIn Hopes They Won'tHave to Raise Taxes
Regional Report: States Strive to Bridge Budget Gaps --- Most Focus on Cost Cuts In Hopes They
Won't Have to Raise Taxes
By Wall Street Journal staff reporters Will Pinkston, Andrew Caffrey, Robert Gavin and Russel Gold
1,188 words
13 June 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
B13
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
IT'S LOOKING GRIM for state number crunchers.
For months, states have been struggling with their most difficult budget cycle in a decade. The slouching
economy has slowed growth in tax revenues while health-care and education costs are surging. The result:
yawning budget shortfalls.
So what are states doing about it?
In Arizona, Gov. Jane Hull used her line-item veto to lop $250 million -- including money for colleges and the
mentally ill -- out of the state's $14 billion budget, a precaution against potential slumping revenues. Michigan,
facing a shortfall of $450 million, is considering suspending cash payments on construction of a judiciary
complex. And in North Carolina, one controversial proposal calls for closing at least two of the state's three
schools for deaf children by 2003.
The theme emerging here is spending cuts. Not tax increases. Not yet, anyway.
In most states, budget planners are scouring the books to trim expenditures so they can put off major tax
hikes -- such as bumps in sales or income taxes -- until truly desperate times. The hope is that the economy
will rebound before states must resort to such measures. So far, "most don't have the dire situation that
justifies dramatic action," says Scott Pattison, executive director of the National Association of State Budget
Officers in Washington, D.C.
Of course some states are feeling no cost-cutting pain. Colorado, which hasn't been hit as hard by the
economic slowdown, actually is poised to hand out $600 million in tax cuts. Revenues are still exceeding
expectations. And a $5.34 billion budget set to take effect July 1 includes a 6% spending increase from
current levels.
But at least half the states do face some sort of "revenue problems," according to a May report by the Nelson
A. Rockefeller Institute of Government in Albany, N.Y. In its latest assessment, scheduled for release today,
the State University of New York research program warned: "States will have to monitor the slowing economy
and revenues carefully to avoid serious trouble in the coming year."
One such state is California, which has the largest budget at roughly $80 billion. Falling tax receipts from
capital gains and stock-options income are fueling fiscal woes in the tech-heavy state. Total revenues are
projected to fall by some $3 billion, or 4%, in the 2002 fiscal year beginning next month.
So far, state finance officials expect only a scaling back of program expansions and not cuts in services. But
California faces a bleaker picture down the road: Currently, the state is on track to spend $4 billion more than
it will collect in the following fiscal year. Deeper cuts almost certainly will have to be made, and lawmakers --
scheduled to remain in session until mid-September -- are debating whether to make them now.
A few states are pushing for across-the-board spending cuts to ensure the pain is spread evenly. For the next
two years beginning in July, Wisconsin Gov. Scott McCallum is asking all state agencies to cut 5% from
current spending levels. Mississippi Gov. Ronnie Musgrove wants departments to hold back 5% of their
planned spending for the first six months of the coming fiscal year, until the state is sure it will get all of its
budgeted revenues. Similarly, Utah Gov. Mike Leavitt is ordering agencies to identify 4% in cuts from its fiscal
2002 budget to cover possible shortfalls. Alabama attached a proviso to its $4.2 billion education budget for
fiscal 2002: If revenues weaken further, spending can be trimmed by 1% before the plan takes effect in
October.

--- Page 26 ---
Page 26 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Iowa lawmakers asked state agencies to cut spending by up to 15% from this year's levels. But even so, the
fiscal year starting July 1 "could be a very big problem" if the economy doesn't rebound, says legislative fiscal
director Dennis Prouty. "We are certainly not out of the woods."
Confounding most states is the difficulty of reining in the two largest areas of state government: health care
and education.
Medicaid, the state and federal health-insurance program for low-income people, is far and away the biggest
budget buster, with expenses growing at 7.4% a year, more than twice the rate of inflation. Critics point to
rising prescription-drug costs as a common cause. But there are other problems, too. Last week, for example,
a New York state appeals court ruled the Empire State must provide Medicaid benefits to new legal
immigrants, which a Republican legislative leader says could add as much as $200 million a year in expenses
to the $31 billion program.
As for education, the Republican South Carolina Legislature adjourned last week without closing a $500
million hole in a proposed $5.4 billion spending package for fiscal 2002. Contributing to the gap: Democratic
Gov. Jim Hodges's insistence that K-12 school funding not suffer. The governor now must recall lawmakers in
a special session this month.
What's next if spending cuts don't get states out of their budget squeeze? Arturo Perez, senior policy
specialist for the National Conference of State Legislatures in Denver, says turning to rainy-day funds, or
savings accounts, and other special sources is a logical option. Already, Indiana is tapping $375 million from
a special account funded by state lottery and gaming revenues, and may use $53 million from its rainy-day
fund over the next two years. Wisconsin, hoping to avoid more taxes and shore up its $23.4 billion budget for
the next two years, is mulling a plan to "securitize" the state's tobacco settlement. The state would sell to
investors future payments from tobacco companies in exchange for a smaller lump sum up front.
Begrudgingly, a few states are turning to tax increases, albeit mostly minor ones.
West Virginia expects to raise $130 million in the next fiscal year through expanded regulation and taxation of
video-gaming machines and by raising taxes on smokeless tobacco. In Maine, Independent Gov. Angus King
had pushed a 26-cents-a-pack cigarette-tax increase, but House Democrats are arguing for a 15-cent
increase dedicated to expanded health coverage. Nevada expects to bring in a total of $27 million annually by
raising business-filing fees and eliminating a tax exemption for rental-car companies. And the North Carolina
Senate wants to close $190 million worth of tax loopholes, in part by extending the corporate franchise tax to
limited-liability companies such as law firms.
At least one state is being forced to consider a major tax hike. Tennessee, one of nine states without a
personal income tax, has been sidestepping budget problems since 1999. Now lawmakers may have to
swallow a bitter pill: raising the state's sales tax -- already one of the nation's highest at 6%. This week, the
Legislature was mulling a 1/2-cent or 3/4-cent increase.

You Don't Often FindThis Kind of MogulIn the Arctic Snow---Russian Tycoon's Whim DragsBaffled Friends, BodyguardsInto Aiding Blighted Region...
You Don't Often Find This Kind of Mogul In the Arctic Snow --- Russian Tycoon's Whim Drags Baffled
Friends, Bodyguards Into Aiding Blighted Region
By Andrew Higgins
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,463 words
13 June 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
ANADYR, Russia -- The first time oil executive Andrei Gorodilov landed here in the capital of the Arctic's
Chukotka region, he had a sudden craving "to get back on the plane and immediately fly out."
Entombed in ice and lashed by savage storms, Chukotka is so desolate that even reindeer tend to flee this
autonomous district. The human population, blighted by alcoholism and grinding poverty, has shriveled to
around 72,000, half what it was a decade ago.
So why, two years later, has Mr. Gorodilov ditched a cushy job in Moscow and moved to Chukotka? The
short answer: His boss had a brainstorm.
Until he fell for Chukotka, Roman Abramovich, Russian oil tycoon and Kremlin insider, was the most secretive
and perhaps most powerful member of a small coterie of moguls known as oligarchs. Now, after years of
snatching up valuable state assets on the cheap, the 34-year-old billionaire says he is "fed up" with making
money all the time.
"I started business early, so maybe that's why I'm bored with it," says Mr. Abramovich, who owns nearly half
of AO Sibneft, a big oil company, and last year helped create Russian Aluminum, the world's second-biggest
aluminum combine in terms of production after Alcoa Inc.
Many in Moscow say the mogul has some money-making scheme in mind. But it is hard to see why a man
whose favorite place is the French Riviera town of Nice would move to the Arctic when he could control any
resources there from warmer latitudes.
Mr. Abramovich himself offers no motives except to say that he wants to foment a "revolution toward civilized
life" in Chukotka, a Texas-sized chunk of tundra across the Bering Sea from Alaska. And he has asked
baffled friends, bodyguards and colleagues, such as Mr. Gorodilov, to join him.
Pledging to make life in Chukotka less miserable, Mr. Abramovich ran for governor in December and won
92% of the vote. His nearest rival was an impecunious local expert in reindeer herding.
Mr. Gorodilov, a close friend of the oligarch, was one of two senior Sibneft managers recruited to serve as
deputy governors. "For me, for all of us, Roman is the boss," Mr. Gorodilov says. "If he makes a decision to
build Chukotka, then, naturally, we build Chukotka."
Many rich Russians, keen to burnish their reputations, now splurge on good causes. President Vladimir Putin,
who came to power vowing to "eliminate oligarchs as a class," has helped pry open their wallets. Last fall, he
called a group of tycoons to the Kremlin for a lecture on civic duties -- and hit them up for $52 million for a
church-run charity fund.
But Mr. Abramovich, a college dropout whose parents died when he was a small child, has outdone them all.
He has adopted an entire region and now spends up to three weeks a month in Chukotka and tens of millions
of dollars of his own money.
The region needs a lot. Anadyr has no trees, no roads to anywhere and no hotels. Its Pizza House restaurant
doesn't serve pizza. Its professional soccer team played -- and mostly lost -- in Moscow, 3,700 miles away
until it folded late last year. The airport, a decrepit former military base, offers the only way out.
These days, people keep flying in. Chartered jets from Moscow deliver executives, bankers, engineers and --
ahead of a recent ice-fishing festival -- fireworks experts, Mr. Abramovich's wife and two big-name Russian
pop bands. Some, such as the mogul's spouse, stay just a few days. Others, such as Mr. Gorodilov, are here
for years: Mr. Abramovich's term ends in 2005.

--- Page 28 ---
Page 28 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A 30-year-old bachelor, Mr. Gorodilov says he is learning to appreciate the subtleties of snow. He has a room
at the governor's "mansion," a prefabricated two-story house with aluminum siding. It was flown in
unassembled from Canada and put together on a bluff overlooking a frozen bay. Bodyguards stay there, too.
Their boss, Mr. Abramovich, hasn't completely left his business past behind. He recently got summoned by
prosecutors in Moscow to answer questions about an alleged $500,000 tax dodge by his oil company,
Sibneft. The company says it owes nothing.
As governor, Mr. Abramovich gets an annual salary of around $11,500, which includes an Arctic bonus. Not
bad for a Russian civil servant but hardly a big draw for a billionaire. His stake in Sibneft alone is worth more
than $800 million. He says a third of Chukotka's 2001 budget will come from his income-tax payments of
around $35 million.
Mr. Abramovich says he'll spend a further $60 million to $80 million out of his own pocket this year to help
keep locals warm and fed -- and entertained. Among projects on the drawing board are a heated, indoor ice
rink (it's too cold to skate outside), a bowling alley, new television and radio channels and a movie house.
The aim, he says, is to bring "civilized life" to the "the end of geography." It isn't easy. The bands he flew in
for the ice-fishing contest whined about the weather and lip-synched all their songs. Not used to dealing with
crowds, local police sat back as scores of youths battled to get into the concert hall. Mr. Abramovich himself
finally waded through the melee, bodyguards trailing behind, to rouse officers dozing in a heated jeep.
Mr. Abramovich says close friends, who include Tatyana Dyachenko, the daughter of former President Boris
Yeltsin, "can't understand and can't even guess" what he's doing.
Also puzzled, but mostly delighted, are Chukotka's residents. "I used to think oligarchs all had pistols in their
pockets," says Vladimir Etylin, a leader of the minority native population, the Chukchi, and defeated
gubernatorial rival. "I don't know what's really going on inside his head, but it's a blessing for Chukotka."
How it affects his oil company also isn't clear. Buried deep beneath the ice lie oil, natural gas and gold.
Sibneft will start drilling this summer and has plans for a pipeline to transport gas to Anadyr's power plant. But
proven reserves are modest and separated from markets by an oceanic expanse of snow.
A meat factory that Sibneft owns in Omsk, six time zones away, flies in supplies. The company also has
taken over a bankrupt Anadyr bakery. The wife of a Sibneft manager runs Pole of Hope, a charity
organization that has just brought in 100 trucks and is flying out 8,000 local children for beach holidays and
dental care. And MDM, a Moscow bank close to Sibneft, just opened its first Arctic branch.
"We all understand that, sooner or later, we'll all be working out here," says Mr. Gorodilov. Chukotka's
desperation, he says, does have an upside: "If you start to do anything here, you see the result right away."
It's hard these days to leave a mark in Moscow, he adds.
Sergei Koptsov, a consultant who used to run Sibneft-funded election campaigns in Omsk, says he initially
turned down Mr. Abramovich's latest job offer but then relented. "I decided to return to the roots of history --
namely to Chukotka." Now in charge of shaking up sleepy local media, he's busy purging staff members he
says "are frozen in another world." Chukotka's main newspaper, the Far North, has just started to use color.
"People here don't live, they just exist," says Mr. Abramovich after a helicopter trip to an impoverished
settlement where residents survive on meat chiseled from the carcasses of dead whales.
The tycoon prefers Chinese cuisine himself -- and has asked a favorite Moscow restaurant to find chefs in
China willing to decamp to the Arctic. His other enthusiasms include listening to a Russian rock group called
Spleen and watching violent movies like "Straw Dogs," "Pulp Fiction" and "Brat 2," a Russian film about a hit
man.
Most of his time in Chukotka, though, is spent listening to complaints and pleas for help. On a visit to Anadyr's
ramshackle hospital, he invites doctors and nurses to explain their problems. A torrent of woe engulfs the
room. They need medicines, bedding, books, housing and, most urgently of all, a way to get out of Chukotka.
Larissa Ponomareva, the mogul's personal assistant, scribbles down each request in a notebook. "They have
absolute faith that he can solve all their problems," she says. "They think he's God."

California May Be Hazardous to Big Tobacco's Health---Philip Morris Verdict Could BeStart of Lawyers' Gold Rush
California May Be Hazardous to Big Tobacco's Health --- Philip Morris Verdict Could Be Start of
Lawyers' Gold Rush
By Gordon Fairclough
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,303 words
8 June 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
California is giving the tobacco industry a major case of nerves. Quick to boast of its record in beating
cigarette-liability cases brought by individual smokers, the industry was brought up short this week when a
Los Angeles jury said Philip Morris Cos. should pay Richard Boeken, a longtime smoker with lung cancer, $3
billion in punitive damages.
Equally troubling for the company and the industry: The verdict marks Philip Morris's third straight loss in the
Golden State. That leaves analysts and legal strategists on both sides wondering why Philip Morris's
courtroom strategy, which works in most states most of the time, hasn't succeeded in California.
The big question: Is the health-conscious and consumer-friendly state, where juries are becoming more
hostile to industry, an anomaly -- or is it a harbinger of a shift in national attitudes against Big Tobacco?
"It's too early for me to say we've got a California problem," says William S. Ohlemeyer, Philip Morris's
associate general counsel. "But I grant you, we have a California issue."
The "issue" got its start in 1998, after the state legislature repealed a law barring suits by sick smokers
against cigarette makers. But whether the industry's three losses in the state since then will inspire a new
wave of litigation depends on whether the verdicts withstand appeal, and that's far from certain.
If Philip Morris does end up having to pay sizable judgments, it will encourage more attorneys to file such
suits, which are now relatively easy to bring. The trial lawyers who pioneered the current round of tobacco
litigation have assembled the most damning industry documents and deposed the best witnesses. That
material is all available to other plaintiffs' lawyers, online and elsewhere, for use in what is termed a
"trial-in-a-box."
For the moment, Philip Morris is playing down this week's defeat, calling it a glaring exception to its winning
record elsewhere in the country. The New York-based company pledged to appeal and expressed confidence
that the extraordinarily punitive damages would be cut down to size.
Still, the case against Philip Morris brought in Los Angeles by Mr. Boeken is ominous for the industry in part
because of its similarity to other individual claims filed against tobacco companies in recent years. It relied
heavily on reams of damaging internal industry documents that were disclosed as a result of suits brought
against cigarette makers by state governments in the mid-1990s.
Mr. Boeken, 56 years old, who testified that in the past he had been addicted to both heroin and alcohol, said
he started smoking Philip Morris's Marlboro cigarettes regularly at age 13. He has smoked pretty much ever
since and alleged in his suit that Philip Morris misled him about the dangers of smoking. He was diagnosed
with lung cancer in 1999. The disease has since spread to his brain.
The trial-in-a-box documents presented in the case didn't sway other jurors elsewhere, Mr. Ohlemeyer says:
"Juries have been looking at these documents since 1998 and mostly shrugging their shoulders."
California is unquestionably hostile to smoking. Since 1989, the state government has waged an aggressive
antitobacco campaign -- warning people of the health dangers of cigarettes and blasting the companies that
make them. It is one of the longest running state tobacco-control programs. And, coupled with strict state
laws banning indoor smoking -- even in bars and nightclubs -- the program is credited with helping push the
state's adult smoking rate down to about 17% -- lower than in any state other than Utah. Per-capita cigarette
consumption in California has dropped by about half since the late 1980s.
It is not only attitudes toward cigarettes that have changed. Attitudes toward tobacco companies have been
transformed as well. "The culture has changed," says Stanton Glantz, a professor at the University of

--- Page 30 ---
Page 30 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
California, San Francisco, and an antismoking advocate. "In much of the rest of the world, the tobacco
industry is the only voice the public hears. Here there's another voice."
Recently, the state health department has started running a series of ads aimed at rebutting Philip Morris's
flashy, $100 million-a-year corporate-image campaign. Philip Morris's television spots feature the company's
charitable giving and end with the tag line: "Working to make a difference. The people of Philip Morris."
One health-department ad features an animated crocodile with the words "Big Tobacco" stenciled on his
chest. The cartoon reptile describes a TV ad campaign, saying: "We don't say anything about cigarettes on
the tube. We talk about beer. We talk about cheese. We talk about community service." An announcer asks:
"And get that smoky old brand name out there, right?" The croc responds: "Bingo."
Another commercial features a tobacco executive talking on the phone. "Do some more feel-good charity
commercials and we'll just P.R. the hell out of it," he says. Another man asks: "The beauty is, we're selling
cigarettes without selling cigarettes, right?"
"I don't think you can ignore," the impact of the state's campaign, says Philip Morris's Mr. Ohlemeyer. "That's
an issue we've talked about a number of times in the past 24 hours." And it's an issue that is likely to grow in
importance for cigarette makers as more states move to adopt tobacco-control strategies similar to
California's -- some of them using money they are receiving from the tobacco industry under the 1998 state
tobacco settlement.
Philip Morris's defense seems to have inadvertently fed just the kind of skepticism that the California state
ads are designed to foster. The company's case reflected changes in its conduct in recent years and its
new-found willingness to admit that it made mistakes in the past. Talking about Philip Morris's refusal to
accept public-health authorities' conclusions about the health dangers of cigarettes, the company's lawyer,
Maurice A. Leiter, said: "These choices by Philip Morris and the tobacco companies were the wrong choices.
They fell out of step. They fought too long."
Juror Peter Brosnan, a 48-year-old child-abuse investigator for Los Angeles County, said he went into the trial
"leaning towards the defense." But he said in an interview yesterday that documents showing that cigarette
makers tracked smoking among kids helped change his mind. "They were selling a deadly, addictive drug to
children," he says.
That figured heavily when it was time to calculate punitive damages, Mr. Brosnan says. At first he was
hesitant to award anything in the billions, he says. "Then I thought about how many people these guys have
killed. And I thought $3 billion doesn't even begin to do justice for all the evil they've done."
Jurors in two other California cases also expressed anger at cigarette makers with large punitive-damage
awards. In 1999, a jury in San Francisco ordered Philip Morris to pay a former smoker $51.5 million in
punitive and compensatory damages, an amount that was later reduced by a judge. A second San Francisco
jury last year awarded $21.7 million to a woman who has since died of lung cancer and her husband. Both
cases are on appeal.
The industry has certainly lost cases elsewhere. The biggest is the landmark Engle class-action in Florida. In
that case, a Miami jury hit the country five largest tobacco companies with a record-setting $144.87 billion in
punitive damages. Cigarette makers are appealing.
(See related article: "Former Two-Pack-a-Day Man Satisfied His Urge to Sue" -- WSJ June 8, 2001)

Gold Stocks' SurgeOutshines MovesIn the Metal's Price
Gold Stocks' Surge Outshines Moves In the Metal's Price
By Neil Behrmann
Special to The Wall Street Journal
737 words
30 May 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13A
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
LONDON -- Commodity traders have focused in recent days on the trials and tribulations of gold, but the real
action has been in gold-company stocks.
Gold broke through a trading band of $255 to $275 an ounce on May 18, rising to $298 in Hong Kong three
days later. The price then tumbled on fears of Russian sales and closed in London on Friday ahead of the
long weekend at $278, up 9% from its April 2 low of $255.
But the gyrations of the shares of gold companies have been even greater. The stocks began to rise in the
past few months, well before the metal price did, partly because hedge-fund bargain-hunters were buying,
analysts say. After sagging to a 20-year low in November, gold stocks began rising in December and
January.
They took off in February on expectations that U.S. interest-rate cuts would spur inflation and encourage
investors to buy gold, traders say.
HSBC's gold-stock index has surged 60% from November's low of 40 points to peak at 64 on May 21. By the
end of last week, the index was down 12% from its high, but gold stocks still had outperformed the metal by a
substantial margin.
The question dogging investors is whether gold stocks have peaked or if the price of the metal could take
them even higher. That is a tough call, given that gold stocks have posted heady gains from their 52-week
lows early this year. Kinross Gold Ltd., the Ontario miner, is up 131%; Homestake Mining Co., the Walnut
Creek, Calif., producer, has risen 113%; Gold Fields Ltd. in South Africa is up 81%; Franco-Nevada Ltd. in
Toronto is up 62%; AngloGold Ltd. in South Africa is up 59%; Placer Dome Inc., Vancouver, B.C., is up 58%,
and Normandy Mining Ltd. in Australia is up 33%.
"The latest bullion price spike illustrated that gold stock investors tend to anticipate gold's moves," Roger
Chaplin, head of research at Canaccord Capital, Canadian and London brokers that specialize in mining,
said. Several gold rallies in the past were preceded by gold-stock surges, he said.
But the runup in the price of gold and its subsequent turnaround dealt a severe setback last week to some of
those highflying stocks. That damaged latecomers, Mr. Chaplin said. For gold stocks to rise from present
levels, the metal's price must consolidate above $275 and continue upward, he said. But if gold tumbles, the
stocks will suffer greatly, he said.
Gold stocks surge or slide more than the metal itself because of leverage, Mr. Chaplin said. For example, if it
costs $200 to produce an ounce of gold, the operating profit at a price of $255 is $55. At $283, the profit is
$83, an increase of 51% from the previous level. If the price declines, the leverage works in reverse.
Complicating matters further, the majority of gold mines hedge against price fluctuations by selling varying
proportions of their gold production forward at predetermined prices. These gold mines won't fully benefit
from the rise in the price of gold from its lows, analysts say. On the other hand, the hedging should cushion
investors from any slump, they add.
"We're expecting a golden calf rather than a bull market, so investors must be highly selective in choosing
gold stocks," Jon Bergtheil, head of global mining at HSBC Investment Bank, said. Gold rallies faltered
during the past two decades, but this time the rally is based on "solid fundamentals rather than wishful
thinking," he said.
If the dollar were to slide, gold would take off again, because it is inversely correlated to the currency.

--- Page 32 ---
Page 32 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
On the other hand, many gold stocks have factored in much higher metal prices, Mr. Bergtheil said. HSBC's
gold-stock picks are Gold Fields, a South African producer that is a pure gold play because it doesn't hedge,
and Placer Dome.
Mr. Chaplin is recommending Harmony Gold Mining Co., an unhedged South African producer, while Barrick
Gold Corp., Toronto, and Placer Dome are major producers and liquid stocks, he said.
Analysts caution, however, that gold stocks are only a buy if the price of gold doesn't falter, and that is a
variable that is too tough to call.

Prices of GoldAre CausingBewilderment

Prices of Gold Are Causing Bewilderment
By Michael R. Sesit
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
753 words
29 May 2001
The Wall Street Journal

English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
LONDON -- When British soldiers surrendered to American colonials following the Battle of Yorktown in 1781,
they marched to the tune of "The World Turned Upside Down."
Contrast the feeble state of the U.S. economy and not-so-healthy state of the European economy with the
recent soaring price of gold and galloping U.S. and European bond yields, and you would think maybe it is
time to dust off the old tune and play it again. That is because gold prices and bond yields usually don't rise
when economies slow.
Gold prices on the New York Mercantile Exchange's Comex division soared to 14-month highs last week,
near $290 an ounce. But on Friday, gold prices retreated as speculative funds sold some of the contracts
they bought in that rally. The June future fell $1.20 to $278.20 a troy ounce. "Open interest is still up, so the
funds are not completely out of their [long] positions," one bullion dealer said.
Many analysts say the gold and bond markets are out of whack.
Consider the following: U.S. productivity fell in this year's first three months. Growth in U.S. gross domestic
product -- the value of all goods and services produced -- for the first quarter was revised downward on
Friday. U.S. corporate investment "is dead in the water and will remain so for some time," money managers
Bridgewater Associates told clients late last week.
As for the other side of the Atlantic, Germany and France last week reported sharp declines in first-quarter
growth.
Now for the seemingly incongruous. While the global economy looks as if it is on the decline, the following
occurred: In less than two months, the price of gold catapulted 17% to the highs hit last week; gold-mining
stocks went on a tear; and commodity-based currencies -- those of Australia, New Zealand, Mexico and
South Africa -- climbed.
So what gives?
"The market is taking out insurance; it's telling [Federal Reserve Chairman Alan] Greenspan, `OK, you can
cut rates, but we're taking out insurance against a weaker dollar and higher U.S. inflation,'" says Christopher
Dunn, who owns "The Good Trader" Web site. "It's a classic hedge against worsening U.S. inflation fears."
John Butler, chief market strategist at Dresdner Kleinwort Wasserstein, says the inflation expectations are
overblown.
He says the prophets of doom are failing to understand that, given the structural changes to the global
economy in recent years, energy-price shocks tend to damp demand and slow growth rather than push prices
higher. "This isn't an environment in which markets should expect inflation to pick up," Mr. Butler says.
"Quite the opposite."
Similarly, John Reade, UBS Warburg's precious-metals analyst, is "a little cynical" about the argument that
gold's recent price rise augurs higher inflation. He notes that the Philadelphia Fed's quarterly survey has
shown no pickup in inflation fears and that his firm's leading inflation indicator peaked during the first
quarter of 2000 and has declined in almost every month since.
Still believe in gold? "It's worth remembering that the last two times it's rallied, gold gave up most or all of its
gains within four to eight weeks," Mr. Reade says.
Meanwhile, U.S. financial markets were closed yesterday for Memorial Day. In commodity trading Friday:
