Gold Rides CloseTo Week-Ago High,Then Collapses
Commodities
Gold Rides Close To Week-Ago High, Then Collapses
By David Bogoslaw
Dow Jones Newswires
697 words
25 May 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The recent wild ride in gold took another exciting turn, as an early rally up to the highs of a
week ago collapsed, sending the market careening through critical support levels.
Since surging to 14-month highs Monday on follow-through buying from Europe and Asia, the gold market
has been sagging, but nothing prepared participants for the sharp selloff yesterday.
Bank-dealer buying overnight helped gold open stronger in New York. Then a 10-cent spike in the spot silver
price in London helped trigger buy-stops in gold, or preplaced purchase orders, which sent the June contract
at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange to a high of $288.50 a troy ounce.
After absorbing some profit-taking at the start of trading, June gold plunged as trade-house selling touched
off sell-stops and rebounded from lows to finish trading $4.60 lower at $279.40 a troy ounce.
"There have been all kinds of stories and rumors. The thinness of trade has contributed to it coming right
back down," said James Steel, metals analyst at Refco. "The market has been whipsawed in very thin
conditions."
The stories he alluded to included one about Russia considering selling gold and diamonds from its central
bank reserves in order to aid flood victims in eastern Siberia.
John Tyree, a broker at Fimat in New York, noted that Russia President Vladimir Putin's stating that he would
sign a decree authorizing such sales if one was put in front of him seemed to coincide with the deepest part
of the selloff.
"Clearly, if you look at the timing of the market today, that report seems to have caused a reversal of firm
overnight trading of futures and bank buying," agreed Bill O'Neill, senior futures strategist at Merrill Lynch.
But Mr. O'Neill said he believed the market's reaction to the story was overdone.
"I don't think Russia will sell gold in any basic way that will change the outlook for the market," he said.
"If you look at open-interest patterns, this market was sitting on a tinderbox, waiting for a correction," Mr.
O'Neill explained.
The Putin announcement served as a ready match to light the flame.
Open interest, the number of outstanding contracts, has increased by nearly 30,000 contracts to 136,663
over the last 10 days. The source of the rally has been a mystery to some, but Merrill Lynch's Mr. O'Neill said
its genesis was a sharp narrowing in the price contango in Comex contracts over the last few months.
Contango is the premium of deferred months over nearer contracts.
That means that the $16-an-ounce premium producers could have expected to get for selling their production
forward a year had shrunk to roughly $6 an ounce, thanks to high gold lease rates and the Federal Reserve's
aggressive interest-rate cutting.
George Gero, first vice president at Prudential securities, said the rally has been a response to fears of
supply tightness, citing a precipitous drop in Comex warehouse stocks over the last two months.
In other commodity markets:
GASOLINE: Nymex futures slid, correcting after a sizzling rally that set a record high of $1.175 a gallon. The
June contract dropped 4.2 cents to $1.1125 a gallon. "It was overdone; the weak longs, those who got in over

--- Page 36 ---
Page 36 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
the last two days, are taking profits," said Mike Fitzpatrick, analyst at Fimat USA, Inc. Gasoline had vaulted
six cents Tuesday and Wednesday.
ORANGE JUICE: Futures recovered from Wednesday's sharp loss at the New York Cotton Exchange division
of the New York Board of Trade, with the July contract rising three cents to 82.15 cents a pound, after hitting
a three-month high of 82.6 cents. Boyd Cruel, a soft commodities analyst with Alaron Trading Corp. in
Chicago, said the market's strength came from its holding of a 50% retracement level of 78.7 cents during the
torrid selloff Wednesday.

Euro Falls Against Dollar, Yen on Index Change
Foreign Exchange
Euro Falls Against Dollar, Yen on Index Change
By Jennifer Downey
Dow Jones Newswires
815 words
22 May 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C20
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Deteriorating sentiment and a reduction in the weighting of euro-zone stocks in benchmark
global equity indexes dragged the euro lower against the dollar and yen.
The reweightings in the provisional Morgan Stanley Capital International indexes, announced over the
weekend, will shrink the representation of euro-zone stocks in funds world-wide. Shifts in stock-index flows
help markets determine the values of currencies.
Although the news reinforced existing pressures on the common currency, including heavy continuing equity
outflows from the euro zone, the euro/dollar pair held to a familiar range as traders awaited today's Ifo survey
of business sentiment from Germany.
"It [the survey] is hard to ignore because it's correlated with the broader economy," said Sean Callow,
currency strategist at IDEAglobal in New York. He expects the Ifo to show further declines, perhaps pushing
the euro to test its low for the year.
"We've had a target of $0.8705" and the Ifo "might finally be the trigger," he said.
Japan's weight on the MSCI indexes also decreased, but not as much as analysts had predicted. Some
attributed a 2.1% rise in the Nikkei and a modest yen gain against the dollar to a sense of relief that Japanese
equities wouldn't be as hard-hit when the MSCI changes go into effect in November.
But "investors have been pretty keen on the Nikkei recently anyway," Mr. Callow said.
"I don't share their optimism," he added, particularly after yesterday's Bank of Japan monthly report "read like
the script for a horror movie."
The BOJ kept its economic view unchanged in its May report, but conceded to sharp declines in industrial
production and exports and increasing inventories, placing more pressure on corporate profits and business
investment.
Undaunted, the yen continued to build on its strength against the euro and dollar throughout the day on
stop-loss-related orders, dealers said.
Late yesterday, the euro was at 87.72 cents, up from 87.49 cents in London and down from 88.08 cents in
New York late Friday. The dollar was at 122.76 yen, down from 123.21 yen in London and from 123.53 yen
late Friday in New York.
Against the dollar, sterling traded at $1.4402, up from $1.4387 late Friday. Against the Swiss franc, the dollar
traded at 1.7473 francs, up from 1.7425 francs late Friday.
Though currency analysts had been hashing out the MSCI index's implications for some time, and had
gauged rather accurately the shifts in countries' weights, "you always want something to reinforce your
position," said Paul Bednarczyk, analyst at 4Cast in London.
As expected, the United Kingdom emerged "the big winner," Mr. Bednarczyk said, posting a 4.98% weighting
gain on the Europe, Australasia and Far East Index.
Sterling indeed benefited from the news, and selling on the euro/sterling cross brought the common currency
to a five-month low against the pound, said traders.
But there are other pressures on this "generally soft pair", Mr. Callow said.

--- Page 38 ---
Page 38 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The reweighting follows a "general perception that the U.K. has suffered less and is more likely to turn
around," he said, particularly as the Bank of England is seen in a better light than the European Central Bank.
With the U.K. economy performing relatively well, Mr. Bednarczyk said, traders take "any excuse to buy
sterling."
Traders and analysts also said more gloomy news from the euro zone's largest economy was likewise a
factor on euro/sterling. Some noted that over the weekend German newspapers presented a negative outlook
on the country's economy.
In one article, one of Germany's "Five Wise Men," or prominent economic advisers, suggested the country's
annual growth could be lower than the government forecast of 2%. In another daily, a survey of economic
institutes hinted that the research groups might be lowering growth forecasts again.
Australia, which gained moderately on the MSCI provisional indexes, saw its dollar hit a three-month high of
53.17 U.S. cents in Sydney trading. But analysts said the main catalyst for the currency's jump was a surge in
gold prices.
Most Asian currencies fell late in their local sessions yesterday amid nagging political and economic
uncertainties, with the New Taiwan dollar falling to a 31-month low after an influential think tank called for a
much weaker currency, dealers said.
A spike in global oil prices, following increased Israeli-Palestinian violence over the weekend, also cast a
shadow on most Asian foreign-exchange markets, dealers said.
The Mexican peso closed stronger against the dollar despite steps taken Friday by the central bank to
loosen monetary policy, as bullishness about the prospects for increased investment flows offset the impact
of the easing.

Gold Futures Prices Rally to an 11-Month High
Commodities
Gold Futures Prices Rally to an 11-Month High
By David Bogoslaw
Dow Jones Newswires
737 words
21 May 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- What looked like a lazy day of consolidating recent gains turned with a vengeance into a bull
run for Comex gold futures.
After fending off some early profit-taking and rebounding Friday, the leading June contract on the Comex
division of the New York Mercantile Exchange broke through the key level of $275 a troy ounce in what was
seen as mostly a technical move. It finished the session at $287.80 an ounce, an 11-month high.
Gold has steadily been building strength and melting negative sentiment since holding above a contract low
of $255 in early April and patiently making its way higher. Gains earlier last week, which saw the June
contract holding above the 200-day moving average of $267.50 an ounce on technical charts, confirmed that
gold had indeed moved into a higher trading range above $270.
But nothing prepared the market for the explosion on Friday afternoon after the London market had closed.
"After 27 years in the industry, it doesn't quite smell right," said Leonard Kaplan, president of Prospector
Asset Management in Evanston, Ill. "The reason is there wasn't corroborating activity in affiliated markets,"
such as silver, platinum, the U.S. dollar, interest rates and the U.S. stock market.
While he conceded that trading houses had been seen buying, the bulk of the rally was strictly on funds
covering shorts -- the buying of futures to cancel previous sales -- and adding fresh long positions, or bets
that prices will rise.
The manager of precious-metals trading at the New York-based trading arm of a Canadian bank said he
doubted the market would have seen significant selling into the move had London still been open when prices
exploded.
He did acknowledge that the lack of liquidity created by London's closing probably exacerbated the buying
frenzy, with supply quite thin above $275.
He also saw firming in gold equities earlier in the month as a leading indicator for the rally in futures prices.
He cited an increase in the Philadelphia Stock Exchange's XAU index of gold and silver equities to 66 from
the low 50s recently. The index was at 65.27 late Friday.
"That highlighted gold and silver to the general public and the investing public and there's been some fresh
money coming in," he said.
Despite growing talk of inflation creeping back into the U.S. economy now that interest rates are so low, Bill
O'Neill, senior futures strategist at Merrill Lynch, said that last week's rally in gold couldn't be viewed as a
proxy for inflation.
But low interest rates -- the Federal Reserve cut the federal-funds rate Tuesday to a seven-year low of 4% --
have conspired with historically high short-term gold lease rates to depress forward rates. This has
discouraged producers from selling forward, now that they are getting only $6 an ounce for each year they
sell forward instead of the $16 an ounce they were reaping a few months ago, Prospector's Mr. Kaplan said.
While producers might have been inclined to sell into the first half of Friday's rally, up to about the $280 level,
they probably would back off and be more prudent after seeing the new highs, the manager of
precious-metals trading said.

--- Page 40 ---
Page 40 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"We are in a bull market and we have been in it for weeks or months," Mr. Kaplan said. "This move is just too
far, too fast." He added: "If it retraced, my two targets would be the $271 area and the extreme target would
be around $267."
He pegged resistance for June between $292 and $300 an ounce.
In other commodity markets:
GASOLINE: Futures vaulted higher at Nymex amid continued jitters over refinery outages and an energy

COPPER: Futures leaped on the Comex division of Nymex, supported by gains in London Metal Exchange
aluminum and short covering. The July contract added 1.9 cents to 78.75 cents a pound.

Cotton Prices Fall on Signs of a Big U.S. Crop
Commodities
Cotton Prices Fall on Signs of a Big U.S. Crop
By Enza Tedesco
Dow Jones Newswires
640 words
17 May 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- After haltingly accumulating gains earlier this week, cotton prices surrendered them all on the
New York Cotton Exchange.
Cotton has been gripped by a bear trend for nearly six months on ample U.S. and world supplies, huge
inventories and the lack of substantial demand.
Renewed forecasts for rain in the U.S. Southeast and Delta regions for later this week, which would raise the
potential for a large U.S. crop, triggered bearish reaction enough yesterday. Then, word of the closure of two
textile mills, news that is a reminder of declining domestic cotton demand, was more than enough to induce
bearish options strategies and encourage locals and speculators to sell contracts.
The Delta states are Arkansas, Louisiana, Mississippi, Missouri and Tennessee, while the Southeast is
composed of Alabama, Georgia, Virginia, Florida and North and South Carolina.
The leading July contract fell 1.46 cents to 44.62 cents a pound, slightly above its life-of-contract low of 44.45
cents, and not far from a 14 1/2-year low of 43 cents at the NYCE, a division of the New York Board of Trade.
Ron Lawson, senior vice president, investments, for Prudential Securities Inc. in Napa Valley, Calif., said
news that textile maker Pillowtex Corp. will close two plants by July 15 fed bearish sentiment in the market.
Mill closures in the Southeast are nothing new, as a slowing economy and huge textile imports continue to
squeeze that sector. Mr. Lawson attributed of U.S. mills' inability to sell overseas to a strong dollar.
"Until the world perceives that the investments opportunities are elsewhere, the [U.S.] dollar will stay strong
and that is the essence of why cotton is getting killed," Mr. Lawson said.
Alan Feild, a cotton broker with STA Trading Services, a brokerage firm in Memphis, Tenn., attributed
yesterday's slump mainly to forecasts of rain this week and the lack of strong buyers.
Wet weather is projected for Friday, Saturday and Sunday, but Mr. Feild warned that should the rains fail to
materialize the market is likely to shoot up to the higher end of its range.
Weather developments will continue to grab the market's attention as farmers are in the middle of their
planting season.
Recent rains across the cotton belt will enhance the soil conditions for sowing, although some participants
highlighted the dryness affecting Georgia.
However, a "drought scare may be premature at this stage of planting," Frank Weathersby, a cotton broker
with Affinity Trading LLC, wrote in his newsletter.
"The crop that survives will only be stronger as the tap root will burrow until it finds moisture -- the deeper it
goes the more likely it will survive droughts at more critical points of growth," he noted.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Prices rallied at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange due to buying from trade
houses and speculative funds, traders said. The June contract rose $3.90 to $272.40 a troy ounce. A major
trade house in New York pushed the market to buy-stops, or preplaced purchase orders, attracting buying
from a second trade house, said dealer Frank McGhee at Alliance Finance LLC in Chicago.

--- Page 42 ---
Page 42 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
GASOLINE: Nymex prices fell as traders reacted to data showing continued growth in U.S. supplies. The
June contract slipped 1.60 cents to 98.76 cents a gallon. The Department of Energy reported that inventories
swelled more than four million barrels to 204.1 million barrels in the last week due to a fall in demand.
Gasoline prices have dropped 11 cents this week, and analysts said the nearby contract looks set to hit a
floor in the low 90 cents.

AngloGold SeesIndustry EnteringConsolidation Phase
AngloGold Sees Industry Entering Consolidation Phase
By Michael Wang
Dow Jones Newswires
550 words
14 May 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
No Page Citation
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
DUBLIN -- AngloGold Ltd., the world's biggest gold producer, said last week that the industry is entering a
strong consolidation phase which would lead to between just three and five global, integrated gold
companies by the end of the decade.
Bobby Godsell, AngloGold's chairman and chief executive, vowed his company would be one of the survivors
through asset accumulation and an expansion of the current strategy of business and geographical
diversification.
The relentless decline in the gold price -- which has averaged between $256 and $273 an ounce this year,
down from more than $400 an ounce in 1996 -- was forcing the pace, he said in an interview. Without bold
consolidation, the industry faced a slide into "ignominious obscurity like the silver industry," he said.
Mr. Godsell wouldn't rule out that AngloGold could one day make a merger or takeover offer to its biggest
South African rival, Gold Fields Ltd., but he stressed that he isn't breathing down the neck of Gold Fields.
"Do we have negotiations seriously towards a [takeover] deal? The answer is no."
Nevertheless, Mr. Godsell noted that several Gold Fields mines lay adjacent or near AngloGold operations,
providing a springboard for further swapping of assets to simplify ownership and boost capacity. "Gold Fields
is important in terms of some assets that are next door to our assets; on the other hand, I don't want to be the
only gold company in South Africa," he said.
An ownership tie-up between South Africa's number one and two producers would put control of roughly 85%
of South African gold production in one pair of hands.
Mr. Godsell made his remarks on the sidelines of an international mining conference in Dublin.
Mr. Godsell's company also has been tipped as a front-runner in the bidding for the gold assets of Australia's
WMC Ltd., but he cautioned that AngloGold would resist the temptation to pay a "high premium" for the sake
of expansion.
Earlier this year, AngloGold concluded the sale of two marginal mines, Deelkraal and Elandsrand, for about
$130 million. It shrunk its output by some 500,000 ounces, moving down its cash-costs profile to just above
$200 an ounce, still short of a target of $190 an ounce by end 2001.
Of more importance is establishing a greater presence in businesses that handle gold after it has been
mined.
With a spot gold price of $265 an ounce and finished gold jewelry selling for about $900 an ounce, "there's
$600 in value added that we are looking at."
During the next two months, Mr. Godsell said AngloGold would launch an electronic-commerce site, dubbed
GoldAvenue, which would directly retail bullion to end users. AngloGold's partners are Swiss-based Produits
Artistiques de Metaux Precieux, the largest closely held gold refiner in the world, and investment bank J.P.
Morgan Chase & Co.
The gold company also is considering expanding industrial uses for gold, along the lines of a cheaper
alternative to sister metal platinum.
Mr. Godsell warned AngloGold is facing wage negotiations with its unionized work force. Typically, this is a
bruising, months-long process overshadowed by union threats of strike action unless inflation-busting
settlements are reached.

Options Investors Maintain Protective Hedges Amid Fears Fed Has Less Reason to Cut Rates
By Kopin Tan

English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Consumer confidence was up, but investor confidence was down.
Strong April retail sales data released Friday had investors fretting that the Federal Reserve now may have
reason not to cut interest rates aggressively.
So option investors kept their protective hedges in place as stocks stumbled. Fearful that a rate cut of less
than the anticipated half-percentage point would annoy investors and send stocks swooning, some investors
are contemplating defensive strategies such as put buying, call selling and the purchase of bear spreads. But
the uncertainty and the possibility that stocks might rally before that is giving pause to some investors.
Meanwhile, investors and traders continued to focus on selling near-month options and taking up positions
that expire further down the road -- a sign that they expect volatility and premium to ease in the coming
weeks.
In EMC Corp., at least one investor bought more than 3,000 contracts of the January 45 calls that expire in
2003. At the Philadelphia Stock Exchange, these calls gained 30 cents to $12.50 on volume of 2,351
contracts, while an additional 655 contracts traded at the American Stock Exchange. Open interest was
1,057.
In Wal-Mart Stores Inc., which reports quarterly earnings soon, investors bought just out-of-the-money May
55 calls, which traded more than 5,000 contracts. In Target Corp., investors bought the just out-of-the-money
May 40 calls, which traded 11,498 contracts, compared with open interest of 1,919.
Even though these contracts expire this week, investors were willing to pay a comparatively small price for
the right to benefit should the stocks pop in the next few days. With Wal-Mart closing up 68 cents to $54.10
on Friday, the May 55 calls rose 15 cents to 70 cents at the Chicago Board Options Exchange. Target closed
up 43 cents to $39.93, and the May 40 calls gained 30 cents to $1.15 at the International Securities
Exchange.
Mining and metal-processing companies continued to find favor among some investors, perhaps because
they are looking to these as inflation hedges. In Homestake Mining Co., at least one investor sold more than
8,800 contracts of the January 7.50 calls, likely as part of a covered call-writing strategy that also included the
purchase of stock. Homestake, which extracts gold in North America, Australia and Chile, fell 19 cents to
$6.81. The January 7.50 calls edged down 10 cents to $1.25 on CBOE volume of 8,851 contracts, compared
with open interest of 13,319.

NEW YORK -- They keep coming back.
Perhaps encouraged by how Cisco Systems has held up despite delivering its first quarterly loss in 11 years,
some options investors continued to make cautiously bullish, longer-term bets on the network-equipment

Cisco this week offered a limited outlook along with its first quarterly revenue decline in more than a decade

In the options market, an institutional investor looked beyond the six-month window by buying a large call

CBOE volume of 15,531 contracts, with 10,005 contracts trading at other exchanges.
In Barrick Gold Corp., at least one institution bought more than 10,000 contracts of the July 17.50 calls. It
isn't known what prompted the trade. But gold -- even though its price has been declining -- often is regarded
as a textbook hedge against inflation. Concerns have surfaced that the Federal Reserve may have to bump
up interest rates subsequently, after its recent aggressive rate cuts, in order to rein in inflation, and this
might possibly be an early play on that speculation.

States' `New' Answer to Traffic: Build Roads
After pushing in the late '90s for so-called smart-growth measures to ease sprawl and highway gridlock,
states now are turning back to a more conventional approach: build more roads. The renewed emphasis on
asphalt comes as new research illustrates how the economic price of congestion is rising.
Saying "it's time to act" on his state's traffic problems, Washington Gov. Gary Locke last week unveiled a
10-year, $17.2 billion transportation proposal that would widen existing highways and build new ones in the
Central Puget Sound region, which includes Seattle and Tacoma. Two days earlier, Georgia Gov. Roy
Barnes signed into law a plan to fast-track funding for highway projects, including a proposed 70-mile stretch
of road that would connect booming suburbs outside Atlanta. Northern Virginia lawmakers, meanwhile, are
pushing for a regional sales tax to fund improvements on traffic-clogged highways outside Washington, D.C.
While states aren't exactly turning their backs on environmentalists' calls for more smart-growth strategies like
mass transit and better planning, they're bending to political pressure as motorists grow frustrated and
trucking firms gripe about not being able to move cargo fast enough to meet manufacturers' "just-in-time"
inventory demands.
Data released this week by the Texas Transportation Institute, a state-funded research agency, show big-city
congestion cost the country $78 billion in wasted fuel and lost time in 1999.
Despite the high cost of gridlock, environmentalists remain dead-set against most new highway-building
plans, especially in urban areas, where auto emissions are leading to worsening air quality.
-- Will Pinkston
Gas Highs and Lows
Although the price of gasoline has shot up in recent weeks across the country, the cost of filling your tank is a
lot higher in some regions than others, according to a survey of pump prices by Lundberg Survey Inc., a
Camarillo, Calif., market-research firm.
The most expensive cities are in California and the Upper Midwest. Chicago, at an average of $2.02 a gallon,
is the most expensive, followed by San Francisco, Milwaukee, San Diego and Sacramento. The least
expensive were mostly in the Southeast. The gas in Atlanta -- $1.46 a gallon -- was the cheapest, followed by
Spokane, Wash.; El Paso, Texas; Charleston, S.C.; and New Orleans. The national average was $1.76.
Trilby Lundberg, owner of the research firm, says regional prices are most affected by state gas-tax rates and
local pollution-control requirements.
-- Russell Gold
Look, No Sales Tax
As most states try to find a way to capture billions of dollars in sales tax lost to e-commerce, their efforts
could also be a boon to a different group of states: those without sales taxes.
Officials in Oregon, one of five states without a sales tax, are preparing to devise strategies that would use
their state's lack of a sales tax to bring in e-tailers that want to escape the hassle, costs and pricing pressure
that may come if other states reach an agreement on Internet taxing.
The other states without broad-based sales taxes -- Alaska, Delaware, Montana and New Hampshire -- are
not so far along in their thinking, but some are intrigued by the possibilities. "If we have an advantage, we're
going to pursue it," says Vivian Manuel, spokeswoman for the Montana Department of Commerce.

--- Page 48 ---
Page 48 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
To some e-tailers the advantage is already there. Goodguys.com Inc., the Internet affiliate of electronics
retailer Good Guys Inc. of Brisbane, Calif., chose Portland, Ore., as its home last fall in large measure
because there's no sales tax, just as another Portland-based e-tailer, 800.com Inc., chose Delaware as the
spot for its East Coast distribution center.
Currently, states can only impose sales taxes on e-tailers with a physical presence within their borders. A
group of 32 sales-tax states are trying to devise a uniform system that would allow them to tax each others'
Internet companies, but they have yet to come up with one, a prerequisite for getting needed congressional
approval.
-- Robert Gavin
Odds and Ends
The price of a barrel of Alaskan North Slope crude is up this year, but that's little consolation for Alaskans
getting annual dividend checks from the Alaskan Permanent Fund, fed by royalties from oil and gas
production. The reason: The $26 billion fund has lost 3.1% in value so far for the fiscal year ending June 30
because of poor investment returns in the stock market, meaning this year's check will be about $1,900, down
from $1,963 last year. . . . About 300,000 Washington consumers last week began paying more for electricity
during peak morning and evening hours, and less during nights and weekends, following regulatory approval
of time-of-day rates by Puget Sound Energy, the state's biggest investor-owned utility.

Spooked Consumers May Pinch Pennies---Latest Unemployment DataStir Fears That FrugalityCould Trigger Recession
Economy
Spooked Consumers May Pinch Pennies --- Latest Unemployment Data Stir Fears That Frugality
Could Trigger Recession
By Wall Street Journal staff reporters Nicholas Kulish, Robert Gavin and Russell Gold
1,180 words
7 May 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
American consumers might not be able to rescue the economy from its latest slump.
The nation's hearty appetite for goods and services in recent months has helped to prevent a recession, even
as businesses have pulled back. But the latest job-market figures raise the danger that consumers will finally
get spooked and stop their spending spree.
The Labor Department announced Friday that unemployment jumped to 4.5% in April from 4.3% the month
before. Employers cut back significantly last month, slashing nonfarm payrolls by 223,000 -- the largest drop
since the last recession, a decade ago. Payrolls fell by a revised 53,000 in March, slightly better than the
initial estimate of an 86,000 drop.
Since October, some 870,000 people have lost their jobs, while the unemployment rate has risen 0.6
percentage points from 3.9%. That means hard times for the growing portion of Americans who want work but
can't find it. Beyond that, new-found frugality could spread to friends and neighbors nervous about the jobs
picture.
Friday was the last day on the job for many of the 850 workers let go at two Honeywell International Inc.
printed-circuit board plants in the Minneapolis area. "There are people I know who live week to week on their
paycheck," said Shirley Haglund, a quality-support technician at the plant in Roseville, Minn.
Even though local unemployment is still low, colleagues were "very upset and very nervous," Ms. Haglund
said. Many workers had seen this day coming since the beginning of the year, when orders started drying up
at Honeywell, based in Morristown, N.J. In response, they didn't eat out as much and put off large purchases,
she said. "People are being more careful with their money."
That carefulness could set off a vicious cycle: less spending leading to less production, triggering more
layoffs and still less spending.
"Usually it's the jobs that go first," said Maury Harris, chief economist at UBS Warburg in Stamford, Conn. He
points out that in the last recession, weakness in the job market began in the third quarter of 1990, followed
by declining consumption in the last quarter of that year and the first quarter of 1991.
In recent months, consumer confidence has plunged, a trend that is likely to continue unless the jobs picture
brightens. "Concerns about future job prospects have depressed consumer spending in the past," said
Richard Curtin, director of consumer surveys at the University of Michigan, which releases its preliminary May
consumer-confidence report on Friday. "And it is likely to further erode consumer confidence in the months
ahead." Consumers already are reporting greater reluctance to make major purchases such as cars and big
household appliances, Mr. Curtin said.
A week ago, recession fears seemed to abate when the Commerce Department announced that gross
domestic product in the first quarter grew at a 2% annual rate, far from a contraction. One of the big positive
surprises in the report was that consumer spending increased at a 3.1% pace last quarter, faster than it did in
the previous quarter.
That support may not be there in the second quarter. "As these job losses mount and income declines,
consumers could pack it in," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Economy.com. "The consumer has been the
buffer between expansion and recession, and that buffer has a few big holes in it now. It threatens to be
breached."
Jacob Miller, of Tacoma, Wash., was among those laid off in April, losing his job as a technician for Lucent
Technologies Inc., of Murray Hill, N.J. The 25-year-old Mr. Miller, who had started working at the company's

--- Page 50 ---
Page 50 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
facility in Kent, Wash., just four months ago, says telecommunications jobs are few and far between these
days. Even though he thinks he'll still find work, he expects to take a big pay cut.
He will likely have to take work at one of the local warehouses where wages are $2 to $3 an hour less than
what he earned at Lucent.
He and his wife, Alicia, who had their first child less than two months ago, have already scaled back on
purchases, even cutting out snack foods. "It's pretty stressful," Mr. Miller said.
As the job market weakened in late 2000 and early 2001, most of the losses were in the manufacturing
sector. But by April, the service sector accounted for more than half the decline in payrolls, with losses even
in the robust construction sector.
The distribution of job losses points to a broader weakness, affecting areas across the economy.
The latest jobs report raises the odds that the Federal Reserve will cut interest rates by another half
percentage point when policy makers meet next week.
Still, it may be premature to assume too much based on one bad jobs report. Consumers have confounded
the experts before.
In Portland, Ore., a center for semiconductor and other high-tech manufacturing, there has been a steady
stream of announcements of hiring freezes, job cuts and layoffs. A week ago, Epson Portland Inc., a unit of
Japanese manufacturer Seiko Epson Corp., said it would lay off 800 of the estimated 1,050 employees at its
printer factory in nearby Hillsboro. Earlier this year, Freightliner Corp., the Portland truck manufacturer,
announced layoffs of more than 1,000 workers.
But for the most part, the local economy appears to be holding up. Stuart Shleifer, president of Shleifer
Furniture Co., in downtown Portland, said this year's sales of "midrange" furniture are running slightly ahead
of 2000, a particularly strong year. Consumers are still buying readily and expressing optimism, Mr. Shleifer
said, though that could turn around quickly, he conceded, if layoffs continue. His company is hedging its bets
by keeping inventory lean and holding down costs. "They're coming in and buying and not hesitating," he
said, "but I'm not stuffing my warehouse."
---

Monthly Mutual Funds Review:Dear Shareholders: You Think This Is Bad,You Should Have Been Around in the '70s---Letters From Sequoia Fund...
Monthly Mutual Funds Review: Dear Shareholders: You Think This Is Bad, You Should Have Been
Around in the '70s --- Letters From Sequoia Fund 30 Years Ago Reverberate With Lessons for Today
By Karen Damato
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,691 words
7 May 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
A HUGE RUN-UP in selected stocks that were market darlings. Then a frightening, drawn-out decline that
seemed as if it would never end.
That is the way many mutual-fund investors will remember the past couple of years. But it is also the way an
older generation of investors and fund managers remembers a much earlier period, the first half of the 1970s.
Indeed, the late 1990s run-up in technology stocks echoed the early 1970s rise in the so-called Nifty Fifty
growth stocks, companies such as Polaroid and Avon. But the subsequent downturn was far more painful in
the 1970s, with the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index tumbling 42.6% from December 1972 through
September 1974.
"It was vicious. . . . It just took everything down," recalls William Ruane, the now-75-year-old chairman of
Sequoia Fund in New York. "You just couldn't believe the fear that crept in."
Letters sent to shareholders of Sequoia Fund back in the 1970s provide a window into that earlier period --
and also serve as a reminder that stock-market declines don't last forever. Sequoia Fund, which Mr. Ruane
started in July 1970 with Richard Cunniff, today has nearly $4 billion in assets. Over its 31 years it has
returned an average 17.2% a year, well ahead of the 13.4% return of the S&P 500, according to fund tracker
Lipper Inc.
Sequoia, which has been closed to new investors since 1982, got its start when Warren Buffett terminated an
investment partnership he had been running and referred some clients to his friend Mr. Ruane.
Sequoia's early results weren't impressive: The fund's refusal to buy Nifty Fifty stocks hurt performance in the
first few years, and then the fund got creamed along with others in the 1973-1974 bear market.
Wrote the managers in early 1974: "We have been in this business close to 25 years and this decline is one
of the most devastating we've ever seen." Even then, there was much pain still to come.
But the bear market eventually ended, and stocks whose prices had been knocked down to single-digit
multiples of earnings exploded upward. In early 1976, Messrs. Ruane and Cunniff happily declared that "1975
was a vintage year," before going on to post what turned out to be an even better year.
Here are some excerpts from Sequoia's shareholder letters during that period as Messrs. Ruane and Cunniff
explained their strategy while going through some stormy -- and then much-improved -- investment weather:
---
July 1971
Shunning the Nifty Fifty growth stocks, Sequoia failed to keep up with market averages in its first fiscal year:
It would have been easy to buy a broad representative list of high grade common stocks and have achieved a
similar performance to that of the general market. However, we do not feel that you as shareholders selected
us to be indiscriminate in our stock selections. . . .
The stance of the Sequoia Fund at the present time is still probably one of the most conservative in the
mutual fund industry. This is in large due to our reluctance to pay the prevailing price/earnings ratios which
exist for many industrial stocks.
---

--- Page 53 ---
Page 53 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
March 1972
While the Nifty Fifty were still flying high, Sequoia had more than 40% of its assets in bonds and conservative
bank and utility stocks:
In passing we would like to comment on the present tendency of managers of very large investment funds to
buy "premier" growth companies almost regardless of price. There is merit, undeniably, in sticking with good
companies, but we believe there is real danger in totally ignoring the price factor. The market currently is
awarding extremely high price/earnings ratios to both prime and secondary growth stocks despite the clear
historical evidence that every investment fashion, when carried to excess, has led to a painful correction. We
have chosen to ignore this approach to investment although we concede that it produced handsome results
for its practitioners in 1971.
July
It would seem obvious that the buyers of Polaroid, Eastman Kodak and Cheesebrough at current prices
expect not only above average growth, but growth with a great degree of certainty. This may well occur, but
our approach to managing money is based upon achieving investment results with companies whose prices
seem to offer a much more modest appraisal of what may or may not happen in the future.
---
January 1973
Sequoia licks its wounds after returning only 5.5% in 1972, while the S&P 500 rose 19%:
In many ways the approach major institutions followed in 1972 of buying growth stocks at 30, 40, 50 and even
100 times earnings (Polaroid) reminds us of the foreign practice of hoarding gold. The relationship between
intrinsic value and price is not as important as the sense that the investment will endure after all else is
worthless. . . .
Certain stockholders of Sequoia Fund were fortunate to have Mr. Buffett run their money in the past. In this
regard 1972 made us feel like Mickey Rooney filling in for Wilt Chamberlain.
April
Market averages are slumping, amid worries about rising interest rates and inflation:
In many respects the stock market today resembles a cemetery mined by the enemy; many who simply came
to pay their respects wound up as permanent residents. The average investor who ventured into stocks in the
last four or five years with anything less than a competent guide has been seriously hurt.
July
Sequoia's stocks fell an average 21% over the fiscal year through May, despite the companies' strong
earnings growth:
If one has any faith in the viability of the American economic system, and we do, the current price of
admission is one of the biggest bargains since we entered the investment business twenty five years ago.
---
January 1974
Sequoia was doing worse than the market, tumbling 23.4% in 1973 compared with a 14.7% decline for the
S&P 500. Making things worse in late 1973, several Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries cut off
their oil exports to the U.S., triggering gasoline shortages and further economic worries:
We have been in this business close to 25 years and this decline is one of the most devastating we've ever
seen. . . .
Today many stocks in our portfolios sell at four and five times earnings and in one or two instances even
three times earnings. We believe stocks today are selling at the most attractive absolute basis since the last
forties and early fifties. . . .
In general we think the stock market is almost discounting the end of the capitalistic system, but we have
more faith in the durability of our society.
October

--- Page 54 ---
Page 54 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Nifty Fifty have gotten clobbered, with Disney, Polaroid, Avon and Xerox down an average 80% from
their highs:
One characteristic of dramatically bad markets is that they often follow dramatically good markets and tend to
correct the excesses which existed previously. There is much evidence that this market has come a long way
toward correcting such previous excesses. One need only point to the American Exchange where stocks
which on average sold at 45 times earnings in 1968 now are valued at a norm closer to 3 times earnings. . . .
. . .[W]e believe our holdings represent striking values which, barring a world-wide collapse, will ultimately be
borne out in price appreciation.
---
January 1975
Sequoia weathers 1973 better than many, falling 15.6% in 1974, while the S&P fell 26.5%:
The recession which the stock market appears to have been predicting for the last year and a half has now
become a realization and appears to have real force. . . .
. . .[W]e have attempted in the Sequoia Fund to invest in companies which are in a strong position to weather
difficult times.
March
The bear market finally ended. The fund rose 30.1% in the three months through February 1975, while the
S&P 500 rose 18.7%:
In the face of some of the worst business news in decades the market has staged a remarkable rally. . . .
Such changes prove once again the futility of attempting to forecast general market action based on near
term economic trends. . . .
Today the market may be reflecting a more realistic appraisal of the situation which is that we are neither
facing disaster nor are we out of the woods.
---
March 1976
Sequoia stresses a long-term outlook, after rising 60.5% in 1975, while the S&P 500 rose 37.2%:
The experience of the securities market and that of the Sequoia Fund in the last few years has pointed up the
necessity of having only that portion of one's capital invested in common stocks which one can expect not to
need for other purposes for a considerable period of time. . . .
---
January 1977
Sequoia again cautions against exuberance after gaining 72.5% in 1976, while the S&P 500 returned 23.9%:
We are not as smart as the current results might indicate nor were we as dumb as we felt back in 1973 and
1974 when the stock market sent the prices of our companies to levels which appeared to us to predict the
end of our society as we know it.
---
Postscript
These days, unlike at the tail end of the 1973-1974 bear market, most investors haven't been terrified out of
stocks. Many seem downright "complacent," Mr. Ruane says, and most stock prices, while lower than a year
ago, still don't look like bargains to him. "We just don't see many things out there that are undervalued," Mr.
Ruane says.
While he avoids predicting market action, Mr. Ruane has modest expectations for the coming years. "I would
be delighted to do 10% [a year] over the next five years," he says. "I think it will be very, very tough to do."

Page 55 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
IMF Early Warning Should Start on the Web
IMF Early Warning Should Start on the Web
By Steve H. Hanke
773 words
1 May 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Ever since the 1995 Mexican peso devaluation, the International Monetary Fund has sworn that such messy
bailouts would not recur because it was developing an early warning system. Yet after this weekend's G-7
meeting, Horst Kohler, the fund's managing director, found it necessary to pledge that the central focus of his
tenure at the IMF will be -- what else? -- the delivery of an early warning system.
An IMF early warning system, if it worked, would be a major breakthrough for the fund. After all, prior to the
currency crises in Mexico, Asia, Russia, Brazil and Turkey, the IMF's silence was deafening. And if that isn't
bad enough, the IMF has often failed to acknowledge a crisis, even after it has erupted, the latest example
being the Turkish lira's meltdown last November.
One reason why the IMF has failed to anticipate crises is that many central banks do not produce accurate
and current information. In fact, central banks have a history of hiding information and, yes, lying. In the
1930s and '40s, both Britain and Germany misstated their gold reserves to fool the other one about its
strength. In more recent times, central banks of emerging-market nations have been the flimflam artists.
The National Bank of Ukraine, in 1996 and 1997, overstated its net foreign reserve position, allowing Ukraine
to con the IMF. In 1997, the Bank of Thailand hoodwinked the IMF by not revealing the fact that, in
off-balance sheet transactions, it had sold $23.4 billion in foreign exchange on the forward markets.
Consequently, before the collapse of the baht in July, Thailand's foreign-reserve cupboard was virtually bare.
The Bank of Indonesia illegally lent billions of dollars to commercial banks, which put up no collateral. I, not
the IMF, uncovered the problem when I was advising then-President Suharto in 1998. An official audit later
found this practice had left the bank insolvent. So much for the IMF's early warning prowess.
At present, there are 174 central banks in the world. Only 124 have Web sites. Consequently, 50, ranging
from the central banks of Afghanistan to Yugoslavia, report no useful information. These countries may be
poor, but poverty is no excuse for not setting up a minimal, inexpensive Web site. Just what do they have to
hide?
The IMF, which keeps a lot of these nations afloat, demands current and accurate disclosure of central banks'
conditions. Financial markets operate in real time; a month-old paper report is useless. So what better way is
there for a central bank to open its books than on the Web?
What should central banks be required to disclose? There is nothing more important than a consolidated
balance sheet, revealing monetary liabilities, the make-up of those liabilities, net domestic assets, and net
foreign reserves.
Yet even those central banks that operate Web sites fare poorly. Only 82 post some form of a balance sheet.
Of those, only 14 display current balance sheets in which weekly or semi-monthly data are published within
two weeks.
But this isn't the end of the story. To find the 82 balance sheets is nothing less than a nightmare. Only four
countries -- Argentina, Aruba, Costa Rica and Slovakia -- link their balance sheets directly to their home
pages. Of the remainder, the balance sheets for 26 countries can be obtained in two clicks of a mouse; the
rest are three to six clicks away -- if you know where to look.
Once you find the balance sheet, it takes serious detective work to interpret the financials because there is no
standardization there either. Central banks need the equivalent of the private sector's International
Accounting Standards Committee to set rules on disclosure.
Not surprisingly, only countries that installed currency boards in the 1990s are exceptions to this mess. They
have no monetary policies and nothing to hide. Consequently, their Web sites contain current balance sheets.

--- Page 56 ---
Page 56 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
One concludes from this that central banks with no Web sites, or those that say little, have something to
conceal. The IMF should mandate that any country without a current balance sheet displayed in English and
in an orthodox fashion on the Web site's home page, does not qualify for IMF support. Before this is
accomplished, the IMF's much-vaunted early warning system will remain chimerical.
---
Mr. Hanke, a professor of economics at Johns Hopkins University, is chairman of the Friedberg Mercantile
Group, Inc.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Don't Let IMF Plead Victim" -- WSJ May 16, 2001)

Stock Nightmares? History May Comfort
Getting Going
Stock Nightmares? History May Comfort
By Jonathan Clements
1,000 words
24 April 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WELCOME TO THE HORROR movie with the happy ending.
Despite last week's rally, stock-market bears still see more terror ahead. How bad could it get? The bears
chortle with delight and point to two of the stock market's longest nightmares, 1929-54 and 1966-82. Over
both stretches, the Dow Jones Industrial Average went nowhere. It wasn't until late 1954 that the industrial
average finally got back to 1929's peak. Meanwhile, in mid-1982, the industrial average was still 22% below
its early 1966 high.
Unnerved? You shouldn't be. Yes, these were rough spells for stock investors. But they weren't nearly as
rough as the industrial average's stagnant performance suggests.
For starters, when the bears dwell on the industrials, they neglect to account for dividends. Yet dividends
used to be a much bigger component of stock returns. Consider the results for Standard & Poor's 500-stock
index, which -- like the industrial average -- measures the performance of larger U.S. companies.
According to Chicago's Ibbotson Associates, over the 1929-54 stretch, the S&P 500 gained an average of
6.2% a year, once you include dividends. Meanwhile, adding dividends boosts the S&P 500's return to 5.1%
a year for the 1966-82 period.
To be sure, both stretches included some gut-wrenching market declines. No doubt some panicked investors
sold at the worst possible time, while others bought at dirt-cheap prices and made a killing. But as a group,
the results for large-company stock investors would have looked like the S&P 500. These investors did
indeed see their portfolios grow.
Moreover, those who diversified beyond blue-chip stocks may have fared even better. Smaller U.S. stocks
outpaced larger companies during both stretches.
What about diversifying into foreign stocks? There, the record is more mixed, according to data from
"Millennium Book II: 101 Years of Investment Returns," a study by Elroy Dimson, Paul Marsh and Mike
Staunton that is published by London Business School and ABN Amro. Their data are available only on an
annual basis, so they don't precisely match the industrial average's two rough spells.
Still, the numbers are intriguing. Over the 25 years through year-end 1953, most other markets lagged behind
the U.S., with Italy, the Netherlands and the United Kingdom posting modest gains, while the French market
was flat, and German and Japanese stocks lost money. But in the 16 years through year-end 1981,
international diversification paid huge dividends, with Germany, Japan and the U.K. all handily outpacing the
U.S.
The bottom line? Over the 1929-54 stretch, a U.S.-only stock portfolio might have gained a tad more than 7%
a year, while a globally diversified portfolio would likely have returned around 6%. Meanwhile, inflation ran at
1.7% a year, and both bonds and Treasury bills were lackluster performers.
Indeed, if anything, the 1929-54 period provides a ringing endorsement of stock investing. "Stock returns over
that period were quite good, thanks to dividends," says Jeremy Siegel, a finance professor at the University of
Pennsylvania's Wharton School. "The dividend yield averaged 5% or 6%. That yield was much higher than
you could get from other financial assets."
By contrast, the 1966-82 stretch was nastier, because of the period's rapid inflation. A U.S.-only stock
portfolio might have returned just over 7% a year and a globally diversified portfolio might have earned 8%
annually, while inflation clocked in at 7%.

--- Page 58 ---
Page 58 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In other words, after inflation, stock investors barely treaded water during those grim 16 1/2 years. But they
wouldn't have fared any better with bonds or Treasury bills. "The only assets that did well in that period were
land and gold and other real assets," Prof. Siegel says.
Whatever the differences between 1929-54 and 1966-82, the two periods had this in common: Both stretches
richly rewarded those who had the fortitude to invest regularly in stocks. Suppose that, during both spells, you
stashed $100 every month in a portfolio consisting of 75% larger U.S. stocks and 25% smaller companies.
Ibbotson calculates you would have invested $19,800 during the 1966-82 stretch and amassed $41,944 by
the end. But the really impressive result came with the longer 1929-54 period. Over that stretch, you would
have socked away $30,300 -- and had $233,018 by late 1954.
Those impressive gains were made possible by the market's long plunge, which gave folks the chance to buy
at bargain-basement prices. Today, we may not get so lucky. Historically, the market has traded at an
average of 15 times earnings. Bears predict that stocks, which are still at well over 20 times earnings, are
headed in that direction, which means we are in for a further 30% or 40% decline.
Likely? I doubt it. But even if the market fell to 15 times earnings, we wouldn't be in for anything like 1966-82,
yet alone 1929-54.
"If you get earnings growth of 5% to 10% a year, it wouldn't take long to get the market's price-earnings ratio
down to 15," Prof. Siegel notes. "Could it go down that far? I've argued that P/E ratios should be higher [than
15] today. I think it's extremely unlikely that you'll get a flat market for five or 10 years."
---

European PC Sales
Rose 7% in Quarter,
Beating Forecasts
European personal-computer sales held up better than expected in this year's first quarter, according to
provisional figures from two market-research firms. PC sales in Western Europe grew 7% from a year earlier
to about 8.25 million units, U.K.-based researcher Context said. Meanwhile, International Data Corp. of the
U.S. said first-quarter PC sales in Europe, the Middle East and Africa grew 4.4% to a little more than nine
million units, while U.S. sales declined 9.5%. "A tentative business recovery offset a dreadful consumer
market" in Europe, said Andy Brown, a London-based analyst with IDC.
Hewlett-Packard of the U.S. and Dutch giant Philips Electronics last week warned that European consumers
are cutting back on technology spending. But H-P's first-quarter performance in Europe was "little short of
astonishing," according to Context. H-P's drive in the business market boosted its overall unit sales 39%,
lifting H-P into third place in Europe behind Compaq Computer and Dell Computer.
Berlusconi Cites Safety Threats in Italy
Citing fears for his safety, Italian opposition leader Silvio Berlusconi canceled campaign rallies and limited
public appearances before general elections May 13. He called a press conference Friday to denounce a
"hatred campaign" allegedly orchestrated against him by Italy's ruling center-left coalition. The parliamentary
leader of his Forza Italia party also warned of a "foreign plot" to assassinate the media magnate and former
prime minister. The statements drew attention away from the government's presentation of its election
agenda. Mr. Berlusconi spoke with anger about a series of intimidation incidents; the party also said it has
received threats from groups linked with the Red Brigade, a far-left terrorist group that murdered former
Premier Aldo Moro in 1978. Prime Minister Giuliano Amato said law-enforcement agencies are looking into
the alleged threats, and initial efforts produced a "negative outcome."
EU Officials Assert Europe's Leadership
Ahead of a meeting of officials from the world's major economies that begins tomorrow in Washington,
European Union finance ministers and central bankers sought to get across a single message: The euro zone
is assuming the driver's seat for the global economy as growth slows in the U.S. and Japan. EU officials
countered comments by U.S. Treasury Secretary Paul O'Neill, who said last week that he was "mystified" by
Europe's belief it can escape the global slowdown. EU finance officials, meeting in Malmo, Sweden, over the
weekend, tried to present a united front, recently undermined by some EU ministers who publicly doubted the
European Central Bank's policies. ECB President Wim Duisenberg said Europe is "not immune to
developments in the U.S. and the world at large," but the impact "is limited" and the central bank is
"confident that we are weathering this storm." The ECB's council meets Thursday.
Congo Revokes Israeli Firm's Monopoly
Congo said it revoked an Israeli-based company's monopoly over the country's diamond exports, undoing
one of the most controversial deals struck under the country's late president, Laurent Kabila. The company,
International Diamond Industries, is welcome to join other bidders for diamond-export contracts that will now
be offered on the free market, Congo's vice minister of mining said. Congo is the world's third-largest exporter
of diamonds.

Canada's Consumer Inflation Declines
Canadian consumer prices rose 2.5% in March from a year earlier, down from 2.9% the month before,

Petrobras Revises Oil-Output Targets
State-owned Petroleo Brasileiro, or Petrobras, said it revised its domestic oil-production targets for this year
and 2002, after analyzing losses resulting from the sinking of its P-36 offshore oil rig last month. Petrobras
said Friday it set a target of 1.39 million barrels of oil a day for 2001 and 1.52 million barrels a day in 2002.
The P-36 was producing more than 80,000 barrels a day, representing 6% of the company's total output.
Petrobras said it decided to replace the P-36 rig with two production systems, one slated to begin operations
in the second half of 2002 and the other expected to begin pumping oil at the beginning of 2004. Last year,
Petrobras produced 1.27 million barrels a day and was originally hoping to bring in 1.42 million barrels a day
this year. Meanwhile, Brazil's environmental agency Ibama said Friday it fined Petrobras 20 million reals ($9.1
million) for environmental damage caused by the P-36 rig and its P-7 rig, which last week leaked 6,500
gallons of oil.

it the largest shareholder of the merged bank, which will be called Kookmin Bank. The government will hold a

Get a Move On, Greenspan
Get a Move On, Greenspan
By Alan Reynolds
891 words
20 April 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Since the Federal Reserve took the blame for suffocating the economy and stock market, are we now
obligated to praise the Fed for providing an emergency oxygen tank? Not necessarily.
In fairness, the Fed does appear far more alert than a decade ago, when it waited until many months after the
end of the 1991 recession to finally cut the federal-funds rate in half. This time around, it was three months
late to get started, but compensated to some extent with larger and more frequent rate cuts. Still, the latest
fed funds target of 4.5% is only 25 basis points below the level of June 1999 -- before the Fed started putting
it up. To simply unwind previous rate increases is not enough, and they know it.
A 4.5% interest rate on bank reserves is now uncomfortably high in real terms. The broadest measure of
inflation, the gross domestic product deflator, was already below 2% in the last half of 2000. The recent drop
in producer prices and nearly flat consumer price index suggest that gross domestic product inflation may
now be closer to 1%. That means the real fed-funds rate is at least 3%. Such a high real rate on cash would
be viable only if real growth was remotely near 3%.
Nearly everyone expects the Fed to do more, of course. But if so, why wait? Lowering rates in a series of
gradual little steps just makes things worse. Fed gradualism invites procrastination, by providing a perverse
incentive to postpone both real and financial investments.
Fed officials would surely answer that they want to wait and see the impact of the latest rate cuts before
moving further. Unfortunately, corporations, consumers and investors are also waiting -- for the Fed to do
more. Estimates that the funds rate will gradually drop by another 100-150 basis points are common on Wall
Street. So the Fed waits for us and we wait for them. And the result of this waiting game is prolonged agony.
The economy can't really get out of the woods until the Fed gets around to doing what it is going to do
anyway. Sooner is better.
Does it even matter what the Fed does or when it does it? That would be a silly question except that
economists themselves tend to become silly (agenda-driven) whenever Washington policy is in flux. Several
conservative economists have recently gone out of their way to downplay the importance of the Fed, perhaps
because they hope to emphasize myopic arguments for random tax rebates (as contrasted with supply-side
arguments for lower tax rates).
A few conservative economists have argued that interest rates on Treasury bills or fed funds are somehow
unaffected by Fed purchases of T-bills, and all that matters is growth of the monetary base (bank reserves
and currency).
That theme involves a distinction with little difference. When the Fed announces a lower target for fed funds,
they buy T-bills until that rate is achieved. The Fed pays for bills by writing a check that ends up being
deposited as new bank reserves -- part of the monetary base.
Other economists who claim the Fed can't get us out of this slump say lower interest rates won't induce
consumers to buy cars or homes on credit, because they already have too much debt. They also claim lower
interest rates won't induce businesses to invest in more equipment because firms have "excess capacity"
(relative to depressed sales). These arguments miss the point.
The point of lower interest rates is not to encourage companies or consumers to borrow more, but to make it
much easier for them to cope with the debts they already have. Heavily leveraged firms will face much less
pressure on profit margins once they can roll over their old debts at a prime rate of 7.5% or less.
Besides, monetary policy doesn't work only through its impact on borrowing. "Cash is king" whenever the
Fed keeps the real interest rate on cash far above the real growth rate of the economy. Cash then offers a
better and safer real return than other investments, so everything tends to be marked down in terms of cash.

--- Page 63 ---
Page 63 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
To build bigger hoards of cash, people have liquidated equities, inventories, commodities and foreign
currencies at distress sale prices.
The dollar has been rising, industrial commodity prices have been falling, investors have shunned gold and
other hedges against inflation, and bond yields are quite low in absolute terms and relative to short rates.
These are classic warning signs of deflationary overcooling, and they give the Fed a lot of room to maneuver.
But how promptly the Fed moves matters almost as much as how deep the interest-rate cuts ultimately turn
out to be.
The fact is that increases in the fed-funds rate preceded every postwar recession and near-recession, and no
slump has ended without a substantial cut in the funds rate -- not a mere reversal of previous increases. This
time is no exception. A bright light is dimly visible at the end of this tunnel. The tunnel could be much shorter,
however, if the Fed put a rapid end to the waiting game.
---
Mr. Reynolds is a senior fellow at the Hudson Institute.

Corn Prices Plunge as Planting Outlook Improves
Commodities
Corn Prices Plunge as Planting Outlook Improves
By Dyanna DeCola
Dow Jones Newswires
573 words
20 April 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
CHICAGO -- Corn futures plummeted to contract lows at the Chicago Board of Trade, pressured by weak
export data and U.S. forecasts showing better weather for the spring planting season.
Earlier in the week there was a perception that U.S. farmers would plant less corn and more soybeans
because weather forecasters said the Midwest would experience an extended wet and cold period. That
helped send new-crop corn prices tumbling to new contract lows.
November corn futures collapsed to a new low of $2.26 a bushel, ending the session 5.25 cents lower at
$2.265.
"The market overacted to a potential change in the weather forecast in the Corn Belt," said Steve Bruce, an
on-floor grain trader with ED&F Man in Chicago. "The market broke on the updated eight-to-14 day weather
outlook, which shows drier weather."
Dan Basse, executive vice president with consulting firm AgResource & Co. in Chicago, said, "The market is
anticipating producers will be able to make excellent seeding progress during the next week's four-to-five-day
cool/dry interlude."
Cool and dry weather would allow farmers to make significant planting progress.
Traders said another bearish factor in the background was the U.S. Department of Agriculture's weekly
export-sales report, which showed corn sales in the past week of 550,800 metric tons, well below market
expectations of between 700,000 and one million tons.
"Our exports just can't seem to pick up steam," said Victor Lespinasse, a grain trader with A.G. Edwards in
Chicago.
There also was bearish news for the soybean market in the USDA's weekly report, which showed net
soybean sales of 72,400 metric tons, the lowest of the current marketing year, and well below the
250,000-450,000 tons expected.
"The sales were horrible for soybeans," Mr. Lespinasse said.
July soybeans sank to a new contract low of $4.29 a bushel, ending down 6.75 cents at $4.30 a bushel.
Soybean futures have seen more than 8% of their value vanish since early March amid continued bearish
export news and talk of a larger 2001 soybean crop in the U.S. and South America.
In other commodity markets:
COPPER: Futures fell at Comex, giving back more than half of Wednesday's rally as participants revised their
initial bullish interpretation of the Federal Reserve's rate cut. The May contract fell 1.20 cents to 77.3 cents a
pound. "The rate cut was taken positively initially, but the more people look at the situation, the more they
think this is another indication of how bad things are and the worries the Fed has about economic growth
over the next couple months," said Adam Rowley, metals analyst at Macquarie Equities.
GOLD: Futures rose at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange due to short covering, or
the buying of futures to balance out previous sales, in response to a weaker dollar. The June contract rose $4
to $266 a troy ounce. "There was no fresh buying," said Tim Gardiner, head of precious-metals trading at
Mitsui USA in New York. "I would expect to see open interest down tomorrow." Open interest, the total
number of contracts outstanding in a futures market, is reduced by short covering.

Can Investors Smile Again?
Can Investors Smile Again?
By Bruce Bartlett
1,092 words
19 April 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
A few weeks ago, a reporter called and asked me if I thought the current economic slowdown was Bill
Clinton's fault.
Knowing my opinion of the former president, he clearly expected an affirmative answer. Reluctantly, I replied
that as much as I would love to blame anything bad that happens on Mr. Clinton, I was forced to say that the
slowdown is 100% a monetary phenomenon. If anyone is to blame, I said, it is Alan Greenspan.
There is simply no question that the roots of the slowdown can be dated to June 30, 1999, when the Federal
Reserve's Open Market Committee voted to raise the federal funds rate -- the interest rate on overnight bank
loans -- to 5% from 4.75%. This was the first of six rounds of monetary tightening, the last of which occurred
on May 16, 2000, when the fed funds rate reached 6.5%, its highest level since 1990.
At the time, there were a number of analysts saying that the Fed had already tightened too much and that the
economy was on a downward trend. The problem, they said, was not inflation but deflation, falling rather
than rising prices. The Fed was looking at the wrong indicators in their view -- at low unemployment, instead
of declining prices for sensitive commodities, such as gold. The inevitable result was that once these
deflationary forces worked their way through the economy, a falloff in economic growth was bound to occur.
These forecasters emphasized that Fed policy affects the economy only with a lag, and that the ultimate
impact of its actions wouldn't hit for several more months. This was a minority viewpoint. For example, in
May, the Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia's consensus economic forecast was marked up. Real gross
domestic product growth was raised to 3.6% from 3.3% in the third quarter, and to 3.1% from 2.9% in the
fourth. Data now show actual growth of just 2.2 % in the third quarter and 1% in the fourth.
Shortly after the election, George W. Bush's advisers began to give warning of an imminent economic
slowdown due to an over-tight Fed policy, for which they were roundly criticized by the outgoing Clinton
administration. But on Jan. 3, the Fed validated the critics when it unexpectedly cut the fed funds rate by 50
basis points, after having voted to keep it at 6.5% just days earlier, on Dec. 19. In its Jan. 3 statement, the
Fed supported its action in unusually strong language. "The risks," it said, "are weighted mainly toward
conditions that may generate economic weakness in the foreseeable future."
That is about as close to predicting a recession as the Fed ever gets. Nevertheless, the Fed chose to ease
monetary policy slowly and deliberately. Fed funds were cut again on Jan. 31, March 20, and yesterday,
bringing the rate down to a current level of 4.5%, its lowest level since 1994. Yet, apparently, this 200 basis
point cut in the Fed's primary policy instrument has done little to improve its own outlook for the future. In its
April 18 statement, the Fed repeated precisely the same language about economic risks as it used on Jan. 3.
Importantly, the Fed has finally taken notice of the steep falloff in capital investment and sharp reduction in
"equity wealth." This last is an oblique reference to the collapse of the stock market in recent months.
Between March of last year and February of this year, the total market fell by $3.5 trillion, virtually all of it
accounted for by the nosediving Nasdaq, which fell by $3.1 trillion -- 50% of its peak value.
Many investors, I am sure, are saying that it's about time the Fed took notice of their plight. After all, the Fed
justified its tightening policy to a large extent on concerns about the so-called wealth effect. This says that
consumption rises about four cents for every $1 rise in household wealth. Thus the sharp rise of stocks in
1999, which saw the Nasdaq go up by $2.6 trillion, caused the Fed to fear a rise of $160 billion in consumer
spending the following year on top of an already high level of consumption. Without a corresponding rise in
production, the Fed feared the result would be inflation.
The reality turned out to be different from what the Fed expected. Instead of a negative wealth effect that
would cause consumer spending to slow, the brunt of the stock market selloff fell on investment. Real
personal consumption expenditures rose by a healthy 5.3% last year, the same as in 1999. And so far this

--- Page 67 ---
Page 67 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
year, they have risen at a respectable 4.2% rate. But investment has virtually disintegrated. Real gross
private domestic investment, which rose by 23.6% in the second quarter of last year, declined at a 4.1%
annual rate in the fourth. Going from plus-23.6% investment to minus-4.1% in the space of three quarters
explains almost all the slowdown in GDP growth that we have seen.
The irony, of course, is that although falling investment is the principal cause of the economic slowdown --
and not consumer spending -- Congress and the Bush administration appear convinced that it is consumers
who need stimulus. Both talk obsessively about the need to put dollars into people's pockets, so that they will
go out and spend, giving the economy a boost. It now appears likely that whatever tax cut is enacted this year
will contain some sort of tax rebate, despite the failure of the same scheme in 1975.
It stands to reason that a deflationary Fed policy would cause investment to fall more than consumption. Low
prices actually stimulate consumer spending -- indeed, spending on durable goods by consumers has been
unusually strong. But investors are not going to add to productive capacity when they cannot sell at a profit.
And although interest rates have fallen, deflation means that the real interest rate remains historically high.
The stock market's extremely positive response to yesterday's Fed action suggests that a corner may have
been turned. A good indicator will be whether Bill O'Reilly of Fox News, an outspoken Greenspan critic, backs
off from his almost daily barrage of criticism, or finds something else to talk about. In this case, silence may
well be golden.
---
Mr. Bartlett is a senior fellow at the National Center for Policy Analysis in Washington.

THE AMERICAS
Canada's Bombardier
Reaches Pact to Sell
Jets to Air Wisconsin
Canada's Bombardier signed a final contract to sell 75 CRJ200 regional jets, valued at $1.68 billion, to Air
Wisconsin Airlines. The controversial deal also gives Air Wisconsin, a closely held commuter affiliate of UAL's
United Airlines, options on an additional 75 jets. The Canadian government earlier this year agreed to supply
low-interest loans to help Bombardier win the big order over Brazilian rival Empresa Brasileira de
Aeronautica, or Embraer. Ottawa argued that the move was justified because Canada had won World Trade
Organization rulings against Brazil's export-subsidy program, but Brazil hadn't curtailed subsidies to Embraer.
Following yesterday's contract announcement, Bombardier said it will raise output of its 50-seat regional jet to
14.5 units per month starting next Feb. 1, from 12.5 currently, to handle its growing order backlog.
Bombardier said the production-rate increase will add as many as 1,000 jobs to its Montreal operations.
Canada's Hurricane Spurns Bid by Holder
Hurricane Hydrocarbons rejected a proposed bid by its major shareholder to control the company and its
Kazakstan oil operations, with its chairman blasting the offer as "ridiculously low" and "unprofessional." An
affiliate of Kazakstan banking group Kazkommertsbank surprised the Calgary, Alberta, firm with plans to offer
189 million Canadian dollars (US$121 million) to gain a majority stake. The entity, Central Asian Industrial
Holdings, said it would offer C$10.25 for each of 18.4 million Hurricane shares to raise its stake to more than
50% from 30%. Hurricane's board is slated to meet today. The energy concern said the proposed offer
translated into only three times year-2000 earnings per share, and didn't reflect improved operating
performance from lower transport costs. It is also in breach of the shareholders' agreement, Hurricane said.
Chile Plans Reforms to Attract Investors
Chile will announce foreign-exchange and tax reforms by the end of this week to attract more foreign
investment, President Ricardo Lagos told the U.S. Chamber of Commerce on a visit to Washington, touching
on a U.S. concern in free-trade negotiations with his country. Finance Minister Nicholas Eyzaguirre said "quite
significant changes" will make some foreign-exchange operations more flexible for foreign investors, and give
them a tax break. Differences in the tax treatment for Chileans and foreign portfolio investors, as well as
nonresidents buying Chilean paper abroad, will be eliminated. Separately, Chilean gross domestic product is
expected to grow by "around 5%" in 2001, held back from even faster growth by softer global economic
conditions.
Peru's Garcia Focuses on Paris Club Debt
Peru's left-wing Apra party, led by presidential hopeful Alan Garcia, says it will focus its debt-restructuring
attempts on money owed to the Paris Club of sovereign lenders. Moreover, party officials say they will
renegotiate Peru's letter of intent with the International Monetary Fund, should Mr. Garcia become president
after a second round of voting. The Finance Ministry says Peru owed $8.35 billion to the Paris Club at the end
of last year. Multilateral organizations were owed $5.83 billion, and there were $3.73 billion in Brady bonds
outstanding.

Chamber Shuts Down Web-Banking Scam
The International Chamber of Commerce said it foiled an online-banking scam that sought to defraud scores
of would-be investors of hundreds of thousands of U.S. dollars. The ICC said people behind the scam
published fake European banking guarantees valued at a total of $3.9 billion on 29 different Web sites in an
attempt to lure potential clients to invest in bogus high-yield projects and finance schemes. Online addresses
used in the scam gave investors the impression the sites were being run by international clearinghouse
Euroclear Bank or Bloomberg, the financial-information services provider. The operation has been closed
down, the Paris-based ICC said, and investigations are continuing.
