Turkish shares gained nearly 5% yesterday, as brokers said a newly announced economic program calling
for big state spending cuts, banking reform and aggressive privatization to handle the country's financial crisis
gave some investors hope of a major foreign bailout. The main ISE National-100 index climbed 4.91% to

356.6 trillion lira Friday. However, Standard & Poor's cut the nation's long-term sovereign credit ratings to

public debt stock remains highly uncertain.

DaimlerChrysler plans to scrap its alliances with South Korea's Hyundai Motor, said Rolf Eckrodt, Mitsubishi
Motor's chief operating officer, in an interview with Automotive News. He said talks will cease on joint

Thinking Things Over:The Moral-Hazard Bubble
Thinking Things Over: The Moral-Hazard Bubble
By Robert L. Bartley
1,018 words
9 April 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A29
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The current economic slowdown is not all that severe. The real sector is flat, not meeting the recession
benchmark of two down quarters. In the financial sector, technology stocks have plunged, but the Dow Jones
Industrial Average has not yet met the bear-market rule of thumb, closing down 20% from its peak.
We've survived real recessions and real bear markets before, yet somehow the current pause has been
particularly frightening to a lot of people. For one thing, it came off years of uninterrupted progress and
spectacular growth from mid-1999 to mid-2000, and it also hit rather by surprise. But the most important
reason the downturn is scary, I suspect, is that its cause remains a mystery.
Lay aside the canard that the Bush campaign and administration "talked down" the economy. In fact, the
Nasdaq started to tank in March of 2000; GDP growth fell to 2.2% in the third quarter from 5.6% in the
second; earnings warnings spread in October; and by December the slowdown was evident in
unemployment, consumer sentiment surveys and Fed warnings of "economic weakness."
Clearly, tech companies were overextended and OPEC managed to raise oil prices, but these have the feel
of merely contributing factors. So in the absence of any other good explanation, eyes have turned to
monetary policy. "Maestro" Alan Greenspan has even come in for some carping.
Some of my best friends argue he was too tight for too long. I find this view at best problematic. Yes, a strong
dollar in forex markets and a falling price of gold are signs of monetary tightness. But the consumer price
index is showing inflation around 3.5% a year, which seems to me high. (The gold price, while not an
eternal verity, has sometimes been a good advance indicator of the CPI. This isn't working just now.) In any
event, I am opposed to the Fed targeting the real economy as opposed to the price level; GDP growth does
not depend on quarter-point changes in the Fed Funds rate a quarter later or a quarter earlier.
There is another school that argues that Mr. Greenspan let the boom get out of hand and had to apply strong
medicine to check it. This can't be quickly dismissed. Monetary policy certainly can affect the real sector, so
long as changes are unanticipated, and after a lag of up to two years. The Fed surprising the markets on the
upside and then surprising them again on the downside is a plausible explanation for the roller-coaster
economy we've recently observed.
Arthur Laffer has argued on these pages, for example, that the Fed pumped out a lot of liquidity to avert a
Y2K bank run, then suddenly withdrew it when the fear proved groundless. The first caused the 1999-2000
boom and the second the 2000-2001 slowdown. Time, he suggests, will cure everything.
Mr. Laffer bases the Y2K scenario on movements in the monetary base, a statistic embracing currency and
bank deposits at the Fed. He makes a good case that the Fed mostly follows rather than leads market
interest rates. Maybe, but I stopped following such statistics in 1981, partly at the urging of a brash young
economist named Arthur B. Laffer.
So I decided to take a look at what was going on when the Fed changed its Fed Funds target, which at least
is what it says it changes when it wants to affect things. The result is shown in the accompanying graph; a
different roller-coaster explanation leaps out at you.
The Fed's big easing came in late 1998, in the context of a spreading world financial crisis. The Thai baht
collapsed in July 1997, followed by crises all over Asia, followed by the Russian default in August 1998. In
July, Mr. Greenspan had warned Congress that the Fed might need to move "promptly and forcefully" should
inflation appear. With the Russian debacle starting to spread into the U.S. markets, the Fed governors came
back from their annual Jackson Hole retreat to do the opposite, aggressively provide liquidity.
If we had a "bubble," this was its origin. This liquidity found its way into dot-com stocks and also provided the
monetary accommodation necessary to OPEC price boosts. With price increases starting to spread, the Fed

--- Page 72 ---
Page 72 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
tightened, giving us the downturn we're currently experiencing. After a lag, we'll see the effects of the more
recent easing.
Mr. Greenspan's easing in late 1998 was a classic lender-of-last-resort action, providing enough liquidity to
prevent a cascade of failing financial institutions. In my view it averted a much more serious collapse than
anything we're now experiencing. When you inject emergency liquidity, though, you're likely to pay for it in
inflation that has to be wrung out later.
The origins of the problem lie in the climate in which U.S. lenders thought they could throw money at
international risks. It started with the bailout of the Mexican tesobonos in 1994, continued through the Asian
crisis and into the notion that Russia was "too big to fail." This led to riskier and riskier loans, and ultimately to
emergency liquidity. Economists call it "moral hazard."
Mr. Greenspan, it seems to me, is relatively innocent. Yes, going back even further, he might have
recognized that he was creating liquidity that went into Thai skyscrapers. And the Fed-sponsored bailout of
Long-Term Capital Management added to moral hazard. But the emergency was created by mismanagement
of the international economy, the responsibility of the International Monetary Fund and the U.S. Treasury.
With a formula of devaluations, high taxes and investor bailouts, they built the climate of moral hazard that
has come back to haunt the economy with yesterday's boom and today's slowdown.
We are paying a price, that is, for the mistakes of Bill Clinton, Robert Rubin and Lawrence Summers.
Treasury Secretary Paul O'Neill, a professed admirer of their stewardship, please take note.

Page 73 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Letters to the Editor:What the Fed Can and Can't Do
Letters to the Editor: What the Fed Can and Can't Do
658 words
29 March 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Arthur Laffer hit the nail on the head in "So You Thought the Fed Set Interest Rates?" (Editorial page, March
22). Inflation -- a persistent rise in the average level of money prices -- is a monetary phenomenon driven by
excessive growth in high-powered money and is therefore directly under the control of the central bank. As
Mr. Laffer notes, "the rate of growth of the monetary base ultimately determines inflation, interest rates, the
price of gold, exchange rates, etc." Of course, he means nominal, not real, interest rates, etc.
There is overwhelming evidence that excessive growth of the monetary base (currency plus bank reserves)
causes inflation. If one were to substitute the "Banco de Mexico" for "the Fed" in Mr. Laffer's article, the logic
would be identical. To its credit, the Banco de Mexico has slowed the growth of the monetary base and thus
slowed nominal demand while allowing the peso to float. As a result, inflation expectations and actual
inflation have fallen.
There are limits to what monetary policy can do, but what it can do is achieve monetary stability and zero
expected inflation over the long haul. Real growth can then be more robust, especially if government is
limited and tax rates and regulatory costs are cut. The Fed, as well as other central banks, would be wise to
heed Mr. Laffer's advice for steady, not erratic, monetary policy aimed at a noninflationary growth path of
nominal demand.
James A. Dorn
Cato Institute
Washington, D.C.
---
Arthur Laffer deserves much credit for developing supply-side economics, but in "So You Thought the Fed
Set Interest Rates?" he displayed a troubling ignorance about monetary policy.
First, after correctly noting the near irrelevancy of the Fed's discount rate and how little the Fed lends, he
claims the discount rate has to track yields on three-month Treasury bills, otherwise bankers would profitably
arbitrage the yield differential by borrowing at the discount window. In fact, the Fed follows the market in
order to appear relevant in setting interest rates.
Second, he incorrectly asserts that the Fed can "move planets" through its control over the monetary base
currency in circulation, vault cash and bank deposits at the Fed. Even the Fed admits that it does not control
the monetary base. Instead, the Fed has elected to be an interest-rate signaller through its day-to-day
manipulation of the overnight Fed Funds rate. If governments could control both the price and quantity of
anything, socialism would have worked. The Fed has chosen interest-rate signalling over monetary base
control.
Third, Mr. Laffer erroneously claims that the monetary base, in conjunction with reserve requirements,
determines bank liabilities and ultimately the overall price level. In fact, over half of the U.S. monetary base
consists of currency circulating outside of the United States while 87% of deposits today are in noncheckable
accounts exempt from reserve requirements. Further, bankers' adept use of "sweep accounts" has steadily
trimmed checkable deposits. Interest rates, not changes in the monetary base, determine the price level.
The Fed, through the Federal Funds Rate Target set by the FOMC, causes great economic harm, but how
that harm is caused cannot be fully understood unless the execution of monetary policy is accurately
comprehended.
Bert Ely
Ely & Co., Inc.

--- Page 74 ---
Page 74 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Alexandria, Va.
---
If the U.S. were on a gold standard, Mr. Laffer's monetary analysis would be applicable. However, we are
not, and it isn't. The Bank of Japan has driven interest rates to zero, but the Japanese are finding out the
hard way that the old rules of monetary cause and effect do not apply with floating currencies. Before the U.S.
economy heads down that same slippery slope, you might consider a more contemporary analysis of how
monetary policy functions in a floating exchange-rate world.
Eileen M. Debold
Piscataway, N.J.

Six States Discuss Pool to Curb High Drug Prices
Six States Discuss Pool to Curb High Drug Prices
By Russell Gold
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
789 words
23 March 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Looking for ways to control rapidly escalating prescription-drug costs that threaten to swamp health-insurance
budgets, officials from a half dozen states are expected at a meeting today in Atlanta to lay the groundwork
for forming a multistate drug-purchasing pool.
The states hope to assemble more than a million state employees, retirees and their families under a single
pharmaceutical-benefits manager and use the combined leverage to demand steep rebates from drug
companies. If successful, this would be the first pool of employees from different states -- and a potential
threat to drug companies' bottom lines.
If the "Southern Coalition" can hash out an agreement, West Virginia officials say, there is no reason private
companies couldn't join and enjoy the rebates. In fact, a regional health-maintenance organization has
already expressed interest, say officials.
Ten states were invited to the meeting. In addition to West Virginia, state health officials from Georgia, North
Carolina, South Carolina, New Mexico and Washington have said they will attend. As news of the incipient
pooling effort spreads, it is drawing interest from additional states, such as Kentucky, Alabama and Maryland.
This comes just a few months after Maine, Vermont and New Hampshire began an effort to purchase
prescription drugs together. The New England pool is targeting Medicaid recipients and may ultimately
include state workers. By comparison, the West Virginia-led pool would include state workers at first and then
grow to include Medicaid.
This multistate cooperation is a signal of how serious the pricing situation has become. "On what other issue
have we seen states pool together? Usually states want to one-up each other," says Amanda McCloskey,
health-policy director for Families USA, a Washington-based consumer advocacy group. "It tells you just how
desperate they feel and how much of a crisis this issue is."
It is not hard to understand the sudden state interest in confronting rising drug costs. Escalating drug
spending are causing states to raise employee premiums and is causing Medicaid in several states to run
over budget. The cost of prescription drugs in West Virginia's employee health plan is expected to rise 28% in
fiscal 2001, and an additional 18% in fiscal 2002, according to Tom Susman, director of the West Virginia
Public Employees Insurance Agency. These costs are being passed on to state employees in the form of
hefty premium increases.
The multistate pharmaceutical purchasing pool is the idea of Bob Wise, West Virginia's new Democratic
governor. In his February State of the State address, he promised to pool the buying power of state agencies
to "drive a harder bargain with the drug companies." But it was soon apparent the state's 200,000 employees
were too small a bargaining chip. So, West Virginia officials set their sights on the politically tricky job of
joining multiple states together to assemble a large-enough pool.
If the meeting today is successful, state officials hope to steer their members toward certain drugs and use
that leverage to extract concessions from drug companies. "If you can direct people in a certain direction,
then I think companies would be inclined to give bigger discounts," says Mr. Susman.
This type of steering is already being done by some states. The West Virginia health plan, for instance,
requires patients to pay a $15 co-pay for the cholesterol-lowering drug Lipitor and a $25 co-pay for rival
Pravachol, made by Bristol-Myers Squibb Co. of New York. The result is a significant market share for Pfizer
Inc., the New York City-based manufacturer of Lipitor, which the state uses to get a rebate.
But the pharmaceutical manufacturers contend this cure, a multistate purchasing pool, doesn't fit what ails the
states. "To the extent they are concerned about price, the fact is the pharmaceutical inflation rate has

--- Page 76 ---
Page 76 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
moderated sharply in recent years," says Jeff Trewhitt, spokesman for trade group Pharmaceutical Research
and Manufacturers of America.
A competitive marketplace in 2000 meant drug expenditures rose only 3.9% due to cost inflation and 10.8%
due to increased utilization, especially of newer, more expensive drugs, according to Mr. Trewhitt. "We have
more medicines and better medicines," he says, and doctors are prescribing them more often.
One obstacle to the plan could be to get multiple states, each with their own legislative agendas, to agree on
a list of preferred drugs. "You will have to overcome the politics of it all," says William Ashmore, executive
director of the Alabama State Employees' Insurance Board. "Each of us has a legislature. Lobbying plays a
big part in the decisions we have to make."

So You Thought the Fed Set Interest Rates?
So You Thought the Fed Set Interest Rates?
By Arthur B. Laffer
1,273 words
22 March 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
On Tuesday, like Moses of old, Alan Greenspan brought the tablets down from the mountain to the people.
The word engraved in stone was a 50-basis-point drop in the discount rate. And the earth shuddered and
markets faltered. Some even went so far as to second-guess the great man. But the word is what the word is.
For the time being the decision is final.
Mr. Greenspan, in my opinion, did just what he had to do. What astounds me, however, is why anyone cares
or why anyone was surprised. The Fed is enormously important for the U.S. and world economies, but not
because it changes the discount rate or the targeted federal funds rate. All this hoopla over the Fed's rate
changes is misplaced.
First of all, the discount rate borders on being totally irrelevant because virtually no one borrows from the Fed.
The discount rate is literally the interest rate the Fed charges member banks for borrowing reserves. At
present, member banks have about $34 million (yes, million) borrowed from the Fed, and that number has
stayed below $1 billion for a long, long time. Total member bank reserves -- which consist of vault cash and
deposits at the Fed -- total $67 billion. Borrowed reserves are less than 1/2 of 1/1000th of this sum.
Second, the discount rate always follows the three-month Treasury bill rate. The Fed is never proactive when
it comes to the discount rate, save for perhaps the time it takes the Fed to follow the T-bill rate. In January,
the Fed announced a discount-rate cut of 50 basis points four weeks prior to a scheduled meeting -- only the
fourth time since 1994 that the Fed changed the discount rate between scheduled meetings -- but that
change in the discount rate still followed a greater than 50-basis-point fall in the T-bill rate. This is about as
proactive as the Fed gets.
Between the last 50-basis-point discount-rate cut to 5% on Jan. 31 and Tuesday's 50-basis-point cut to 4.5%,
the three-month T-bill rate fell to 4.38% from 4.86%. The Fed's move was right on schedule. And, although
the Fed has historically exercised some latitude in when and by how much it follows changes in the
three-month T-bill yield, it still always follows those changes.
There sure as heck is no new information in this widely anticipated Fed move. So, where's the beef? Judging
from the press and the stock market, you'd think the world was coming to an end.
The reason the Fed follows the three-month T-bill rate is simple: If the two rates got too far out of line,
borrowing would either go to zero or infinity. That's the nature of incentives. Banks can borrow reserves from
the Fed at the discount rate and lend those funds risklessly at something akin to the three-month T-bill rate. If
the three-month T-bill rate gets much higher than the discount rate, member banks will borrow all they can for
a guaranteed profit. Just imagine how much you'd borrow from the government if it would borrow back from
you those funds at much higher rates.
If, on the other hand, the discount rate far exceeds the three-month T-bill rate, only those banks in desperate
straits would ever borrow. The real cost of borrowing reserves from the Fed is the differential between the
discount rate and the three-month T-bill rate -- not the level of the discount rate. The Fed has no choice but to
move the discount rate in sync with the three-month T-bill rate.
In addition, the so-called federal-funds target rate set by the Fed couldn't be more vacuous. Just what does it
mean when the Fed says it's going to target a rate that is determined in a market -- interbank loans -- in which
the Fed is not a participant? If that isn't jawboning, I don't know what is. And a jawbone without teeth isn't
much of a threat.
All of the interest rate hoopla surrounding the Fed's Open Market Committee meetings is nothing but a
sideshow. I too wish the Fed could just wave a wand and change interest rates, but it can't. In the near term,
the Fed has very little power to do much of anything. In the long run, however, the Fed is the single most
powerful force in our economic universe. It can and does move planets and change the world. But it doesn't

--- Page 78 ---
Page 78 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
change the world by directly changing interest rates. The key to the Fed's power is its total control over the
monetary base -- the sum of currency in circulation, vault cash and member-bank deposits at the Fed.
Conceptually, there are three stages of monetary policy linking Fed actions on the monetary base to the
overall economy. First, the Fed controls the monetary base. It increases or decreases the monetary base by
buying or selling bonds in the open market. Second, the monetary base, in conjunction with reserve
requirements, determines bank liabilities. And finally, bank liabilities along with real output give us the overall
price level. In dynamic terms, the rate of growth of the monetary base ultimately determines inflation,
interest rates, the price of gold, exchange rates, etc. By controlling the monetary base, the Fed really does
control our nation's destiny and probably the economic well-being of the world.
Back in 1999, the Fed made an understandable error, but an error nonetheless, by expanding the monetary
base far too rapidly. It feared a Y2K run on the banks. To doomsayers' chagrin, the run on the banks never
materialized, but the excessive growth of the monetary base did push interest rates, the Nasdaq, and real
economic growth way too high. Fed policies have consequences.
Early last year, the Fed realized its error and withdrew all of the excess reserves in a very short period of
time, a veritable monetary liposuction, if you will. But a little nip and tuck wasn't going to make the scars of the
error magically disappear. With the monetary base now back to where it should have been, interest rates are
tumbling, real growth is at or near recession levels, and the Nasdaq, too, is back close to where it should
have been.
The Fed is now doing everything correctly. If it were to once again grow the monetary base too rapidly our
short-term euphoria would come back, at the risk of long-term inflation and economic stagnation like that of
the 1970s. By maintaining stable, modest growth in the monetary base, the Fed will help the economy start to
recover and secure our longer-term prosperity. In the meantime, Congress can do its bit by passing a
retroactive tax cut to help stimulate the economy.
The recent fall in the three-month T-bill yield is the natural consequence of the Fed's remedial actions on the
monetary base. The reduction in the discount rate is simply the "me too" response to the fall in the
three-month T-bill rate. If ever you want to know where the real economic action is taking place, you'll watch
what Moses does with the monetary base.
---
Mr. Laffer is chairman of Laffer Associates.
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: What the Fed Can and Can't Do" -- WSJ March 29, 2001)

Zimbabwe's Economic Woe Puts Its Neighbor in a Bind --- South African Officials Fear The Flow of
Immigrants Will Become a Flood
By Robert Block
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
817 words
20 March 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
JOHANNESBURG, South Africa -- As Zimbabwe's slide toward economic collapse accelerates, officials in
South Africa are increasingly afraid, and at a loss for what to do.
Already tens of thousands of Zimbabweans looking to escape the political and economic upheavals in their
country have headed to neighboring South Africa. Now, with Zimbabwe running out of fuel and a food
shortage looming, South Africa is worried that the heavy flow will soon become a flood, threatening its own
efforts to fight crime, disease and poverty at home.
A delegation of senior Zimbabwean government officials yesterday ended two days of meetings with South
African ministers to look for ways to restore confidence in Zimbabwe's battered economy. South African
Finance Minister Trevor Manuel, wrapping up the closed-door ministerial sessions, said the talks focused on
ways to shore up Zimbabwe's shortages of hard currency and fuel. He gave no details.
"We have to help our neighbor because an implosion there will affect every man, woman and child here in
South Africa," Mr. Manuel said. Zimbabwe's mining, agricultural and industrial output are plummeting as
demand falls and a hard-currency shortage limits imports of raw materials. South Africa is officially supportive
of Zimbabwe's government, but privately, officials here express mounting worries that their neighbor's
economy will soon collapse.
Zimbabwe has been in turmoil since last year, when government-backed militants began seizing land
belonging to white farmers. Then political violence in the run up to parliamentary elections scared away
investors, and foreign loans and aid slowed as unemployment and inflation soared. Zimbabwe's involvement
in a war in the Democratic Republic of Congo has also siphoned off tens of millions of dollars a month.
A succession of politicians and international delegations visiting Zimbabwe have voiced worry over violence
against government opponents, members of the judiciary and a breakdown in the rule of law.
But South Africa has pursued a controversial soft-diplomatic approach with Zimbabwe, fearing that
confrontation might merely serve to anger President Robert Mugabe and make matters worse for both the
region and South Africa, already struggling with high unemployment, one of the worst AIDS epidemics in the
world and grinding poverty.
A worsening of the situation in Zimbabwe could lead to refugees marching over the border "in their millions,"
said South African Deputy Foreign Minister Aziz Pahad, adding, "They are already marching over in their
thousands."
The U.S., Australia, Canada and Britain all say that in recent months they have noted an increase in the
number of Zimbabweans, both white and black, applying to emigrate. But most Zimbabweans prefer to
remain in the neighborhood.
Kaypascal Tshuma hails from southern Zimbabwe but has been working as a waiter in Johannesburg for five
years. Recently his brother moved here and other members of the family are looking to make the trek as well.
On his last visit home a few weeks ago, Mr. Tshuma said he saw about 50 people crossing the Limpopo
River between the two countries.
"The situation is so bad at home that people are willing to risk currents and crocodiles to escape," Mr.
Tshuma said.

--- Page 80 ---
Page 80 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
South Africa says that in the last year as many as one million Zimbabweans, out of a population of 12 million,
have come to South Africa for an extended stay, most of them illegally. Illegal immigration is a hot political
issue in South Africa, where attacks on foreigners accused of taking jobs from South Africans are increasing.
John Robertson, an economist in Zimbabwe, doubts that the numbers are as high as South Africa asserts,
but warns that those figures could be prophetic unless Mr. Mugabe stops the violent nationalization of the
white-owned farms that form the backbone of the country's economy.
There are no signs that Mr. Mugabe has any plans to halt his land-reform program, and the economy
continues to stagger. One of the country's major gold-mining companies, Luxembourg-based Falcon Gold,
said yesterday that it could no longer sustain its losses, caught between a strong, government-set rate for the
local currency and sagging world gold prices. Falcon Gold said it had "no option but to suspend mining
operations and gold production with immediate effect." Gold is Zimbabwe's second-most important
hard-currency earner after tobacco.
Helping the mining industry is one area where South Africa, a major mining power, could make a difference.
South Africa also could help its neighbor through its supplies of electricity and fuel to the country, although
Zimbabwe is deep in arrears to South African suppliers. "We have to try, but in the end what South Africa can
do may be very limited indeed," said one foreign ministry official.

Credit-Card OffersGet Record LowIn Response Rate
Marketing & Media
Credit-Card Offers Get Record Low In Response Rate
By Calmetta Coleman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
418 words
19 March 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
B10
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Mailboxes are being stuffed with more credit-card come-ons than ever, and more people are tossing them
out.
The typical U.S. household received more than three card offers a month last year, as card issuers mailed a
record 3.54 billion solicitations, up from 2.87 billion in 1999, according to a survey by BAIGlobal Inc., a
market-research firm based in Tarrytown, N.Y.
The glut of mailings met with a record-low response rate of 0.6%, compared with 1% in 1999. BAIGlobal
collects mailings sent to a sampling of households and uses the data to extrapolate overall mail volume and
other facts about the credit-card industry.
Andrew Davidson, president of the firm's competitive tracking services, said consumers shrugged off so many
offers last year largely because there were so many of them. What's more, 75% of households already have
a general-purpose credit card and, of those, the average household has about 2.4 cards. Well accustomed to
the credit industry's attention-grabbing tactics, such as teaser interest rates as low as 2.9%, people simply
didn't bite.
"Going forward, issuers really need to break through the clutter and offer something compelling to get a
response," Mr. Davidson said.
In addition to the resurgence of offers for so-called Gold cards, more aggressive marketing by companies
whose primary business is credit-card issuing, such as Providian Corp. and Capital One Financial Corp.,
contributed to the jump in mailings.
Card issuers also were pushing more products with Internet-related features, such as American Express
Co.'s Blue card and Visa International's smart card, which is in embedded with a computer chip for data
storage. As more companies develop smart-card technology, such offers likely will further drive mailings.
About 25% of credit-card offers had annual percentage rates of more than 19%, a high rate that indicated
more companies were pursuing the sub-prime market in their search for new cardholders. The sub-prime
market consists of consumers with low or no credit ratings. Mr. Davidson noted that many of the offers
featured introductory rates as low as 0% that rise sharply later.
While direct mail continues to be the leading way that companies attract new cardholders, about 5% of
consumers surveyed applied for a card using the Internet in 2000, up from 2% in 1999.

Gold Futures Tumble on Bearish Auction Results
Commodities
Gold Futures Tumble on Bearish Auction Results
By David Bogoslaw
Dow Jones Newswires
808 words
15 March 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Call it a quirk of timing or concede that gold has given up the ghost as a monetary metal, but
gold prices failed to attract investors fleeing the stock-market meltdown in search of a haven.
The gold contract for April delivery on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange plummeted
$4.60 to $262.90 a troy ounce.
The timing had to do with the Bank of England's auction of 25 metric tons yesterday morning, which pointed
out a lack of physical demand in the market.
"The auction was simply horrific," declared Leonard Kaplan, president of Prospector Asset Management in
Evanston, Ill. "The market had been rallying on one hypothesis, which is that there was short-term tightness
in gold.
"The oversubscription rate of 2.2 proved it not to be the case. There was no demand for physical [metal] and
it got heavily sold by the trade or by anyone who thought that it was rallying on faulty logic." Such an
oversubscription rate means that there were slightly more than two bidders for every ounce of gold. The rate
was the third lowest seen in the central bank's 11 auctions.
The allotment price for the auctioned gold was $266.00 an ounce, $1.45 below where the spot, or cash, price
was fixed in London yesterday morning. That was the second-largest discount in the allotment price seen in
the 11 auctions.
Short-term gold lease rates, charged for lending gold, fell another percentage point to 3% for one month,
down sharply from the 7.5% peak seen last Friday, after lending came into the market during the Asian
trading session, according to Jim Pogoda, a trader at Mitsubishi International Corp. in New York.
The bearish auction results and the decline in lease rates prompted recent buyers to sell futures they had
bought in anticipation of prices' rallying further, traders said.
The loss of confidence in gold was all the more startling in light of the massive selloff in U.S. stock markets.
Typically a stock-market meltdown spurs a flight to quality as investors seek more reliable investment
pockets, such as commodities.
"But we're now in a mode where we have real value disappearing so people have to liquidate to raise money
to cover other things," such as higher margin calls, explained Frank McGhee, a dealer at Alliance Financial
LLC in Chicago.
Dave Meger, senior metals analyst at Chicago-based Alaron Trading Corp., confirmed that contributed to the
rout in gold.
"My own clients have been liquidating some physical gold holdings in order to gain capital" to buy stocks as
they fall to lower prices, he said. "This would need to be more of an extended bear market in order to see
some of that safe-haven buying."
But gold is now behaving like an expensive industrial metal, not a monetary one, argued Prospector's Mr.
Kaplan and Mitsubishi's Mr. Pogoda.
"When the stock market drops, it diminishes the wealth effect and hurts disposable income," Mr. Kaplan
explained.

--- Page 83 ---
Page 83 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
As an estimated 90% of demand for gold is from the jewelry sector, it makes sense that gold demand
should suffer with stock markets brought to their knees, he said.
But Afshin Nbavi, senior vice president of trading at MKS Finance SA in Geneva, said it was just a matter of
time before flight-to-quality buying materialized. He said there was no other place for investors to hide.
A key to where gold prices go from here will be whether short-term lease rates can be sustained at current
levels, said Mr. McGhee at Alliance Financial. If the one-month rate doesn't hold above 2.5%, he predicted
further selling.
In other commodity markets:
SOYBEAN MEAL: Futures prices tumbled to contract lows at the Chicago Board of Trade, pressured by
concerns that the outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease in Europe will significantly reduce consumption of this
animal feed. The May futures contract lost $4.50 to $149.90 a short ton. "This could kill us," said Nathan
Tross, an independent soy-meal broker on the CBOT floor. "Until there's a resolution to the matter in Europe,
the market is going to be on the defensive. In fact, we could lose another $5 in value the next few weeks."
CRUDE OIL: Futures prices fell to a low for the year at the New York Mercantile Exchange, with worries that
sinking stock markets will crimp demand, analysts said. The April contract lost $1.18 to $26.41 a barrel,
having set a low of $26.35. The weakness in stocks overshadowed news of lower inventories and plans for
production cuts by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries.

Cut Us Some Slack, Alan
Cut Us Some Slack, Alan
By Wayne Angell
1,181 words
12 March 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Last year, the rate of annual U.S. economic growth slowed dramatically to 1% from 5%. Yet despite signs of
impending deflation, the Federal Reserve didn't once lower interest rates. The Fed clearly ended the year
behind the curve.
Then on Jan. 3, in a rare move between meetings of the Federal Open Market Committee, the Fed lowered
the federal funds rate 50 basis points, to 6%. On Jan. 25, Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan explained that
move. He suggested that the priority for monetary policy was to achieve a "V"-shaped recovery from near
zero growth, which was dependent on the success of monetary policy in avoiding the further fall in
consumer confidence that was apt to accompany an increase in layoffs.
His words were buttressed by what he knew would be a further half-percentage point reduction of the federal
funds rate, to 5.5%. In just 28 days, the federal funds rate was reduced by one full percentage point and
monetary policy seemed to be focused on returning the rate of economic growth to our economy's long-run
potential.
No such luck. The Fed soon began backing away from rapid interest rate cuts. Mr. Greenspan's
congressional testimony two weeks ago didn't provide any clear explanation of what would drive the extent
and timing of further Fed easings. Instead he said that the lagging indicators of car and home sales in
January were holding up better than measures of consumer confidence suggested. If he intended to pour
cold water on the notion of another inter-meeting rate cut, he certainly succeeded.
As a result, it seems likely that the Fed will reduce interest rates by half a point at its March 20th meeting. At
other times, 150 basis points of rate cuts in one quarter might been seen as rapid and forceful. Not now. The
Treasury bond market now assumes a 10-year inflation rate of 1.6%, which puts the real forward-looking
federal funds rate slightly below 4%. For an economy facing near-zero growth, this is a very restrictive federal
funds rate, especially since the Fed seems to agree that it does not see significant inflation risks going
forward. It seems to me that backing away from rapid and forceful easings poses significant and unnecessary
risks to a V-shaped recovery later this year.
Consumer sentiment in February, as measured by the University of Michigan's consumer sentiment index,
plunged to near-recession levels. That was confirmed by the Conference Board survey that was published
the day before Mr. Greenspan's updated testimony to the House Financial Services Committee.
Yet sometime in February, it seems, as anecdotal evidence of some rebound in consumer spending was
apparent, Chairman Greenspan either decided that "rapid and forceful" easing was no longer warranted or
that the Fed could tolerate a longer period of near-zero growth.
Wall Street didn't like the confusion. The Nasdaq index fell in recognition of a longer downturn and bond
prices moved higher. The best bet seemed to be no "V" recovery.
What the Fed needs to do to help a recovery is establish transparent goals for monetary policy. What it
shouldn't do is continue with what looks like short-run activism aimed at fine-tuning demand. Short-run
activism has never proven successful. Activism led to over-restraint in 1998, contributing to some risky
world-wide deflationary forces. The Fed then eased up late that year. But the sustained easy money from
November 1998 to February 2000 brought on both an escalation of inflation and a higher level of economic
activity, likely in response to increases in wealth from higher home and equity prices.
Then one year ago Mr. Greenspan issued the "good-is-bad" proposition. He said that if labor productivity
growth remains rapid, it could lower unit labor costs, increase corporate profits, and increase equity prices in
a way that could contribute to higher household net worth and an excess of demand over supply. The 6%
four-quarter growth rate of real personal consumer spending did seem excessive. So an activist monetary
policy had a new priority -- restrain the growth of equity prices and household wealth. It worked.

--- Page 85 ---
Page 85 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But it worked not only because of a decrease in household net worth, but also due to the increase in average
personal federal tax payments as a percentage of income to nearly 16% in 2000 from 12.4% in 1995. In the
year ending prior to the second quarter of 2000, real disposable income grew three percentage points less
than the growth rate of real gross domestic product. From September 2000 to January 2001, while personal
income grew at a much-reduced 3%, personal tax payments soared at a 10% annual rate. As long as
increases in stock market wealth covered over the increased tax drag, the economy could continue to grow.
But when the stock-market plunge led to a decrease in wealth, consumer and business spending plunged.
Economists and policy makers should consider the likely possibility that short-run monetary activism was
producing growth rate swings of increasing amplitude. Why not then discard short-run stabilization activism in
favor of the Fed adopting a strategy aimed at price level stability?
Think what that would mean. By stating and acting as if the price level is its only priority, the Fed would
encourage investors with the promise of lower inflation rates and therefore lower effective capital- gains tax
rates.
The Fed should recognize and act on the deflation indicators all around us -- falling core commodity prices
confirmed by a continued fall in the price of gold. The dollar seems to be appreciating against everything.
That means the Fed should increase the money supply by choosing successively lower federal funds targets
until core commodity prices rebound.
In addition to commodity prices, another good indicator is the forward inflation expectation shown by the
spread between the 10-year nominal Treasury note and the 10-year inflation-linked Treasury note. This can
be used to monitor the real federal funds rate to determine whether or not monetary policy is getting more or
less accommodative. Augmented with an index of core commodity prices, including gold, this spread
provides an invaluable guide.
Experience during the last four years has shown that when the economy is operating close to capacity, a real
federal funds rate of 4.5% has been a decided monetary restraint. Experience has also shown that while the
economy is near capacity, a 3.5% real rate has been over-accommodative. But surely if the economy
continues to sag toward recession a drastic reduction of the real federal funds rate -- to a range between zero
and 2% -- may be needed.
Why not act rapidly and forcefully to follow price-level indicators? Why not end the increasing amplitude of
growth-rate swings in response to an activist stabilization policy? It's time for the Fed to make its priorities
clear.
---
Mr. Angell, a former governor of the Federal Reserve, is chief economist at Bear Stearns.

A Year After the Peak:For E-Business Booster Meeker, Fame Is E-Phemeral
A Year After the Peak: For E-Business Booster Meeker, Fame Is E-Phemeral
By Randall Smith and Mylene Mangalindan
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
1,877 words
8 March 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Where is Mary Meeker?
A year ago, the Morgan Stanley Dean Witter & Co. Internet analyst was basking in adulation. Dubbed the
"Queen of the Net" by Barron's magazine in 1998, subject of a glowing profile in the New Yorker in 1999, Ms.
Meeker graced the cover of Fortune as the third most powerful woman in business later that year, and
addressed the World Economic Forum in Davos, Switzerland, in early 2000.
Then the Internet bubble burst. The Nasdaq Stock Market is now down 56% since its peak last March 10 --
and Ms. Meeker has fallen off the radar screen.
She hasn't appeared on CNBC-TV since October, when she was taken to task on the business channel for
staying too bullish when the Internet stock bubble burst. She was assailed last month by "60 Minutes II" as
being overly aggressive in promoting stocks of Morgan Stanley clients, a charge she denies. And she was
dethroned as the top Internet analyst in a widely watched Institutional Investor magazine poll.
In an interview, Ms. Meeker says she never aspired to become a media star, and in fact worried publicly
about becoming a "poster child" for dot-com market mania. "I did not aspire to be a media celebrity in any
way, shape or form. I felt uncomfortable being in the limelight," she says, disputing speculation that she has
been under pressure at the firm to lay low.
Ms. Meeker is far from the only Internet analyst to have gotten it wrong last year. But she has been among
the most powerful, partly because her firm has been a top underwriter of Internet stocks.
Ms. Meeker, 41 years old, acknowleges some regret over the "especially disappointing" fate of some of the
initial public offerings which Morgan Stanley led and she recommended at the height of the frenzy. In an
interview, she cites Ask Jeeves Inc., drugstore.com Inc., Priceline.com Inc., Women.com Networks Inc. and
HomeGrocer.com. Those stocks had all fallen by 67% to 96% as of late last month, according to Morgan
Stanley's own calculations.
But she remains resolutely bullish on the online sector, saying its drubbing could represent a huge buying
opportunity for selected leaders. And she says the stock-market value that was vaporized among her picks in
the dot-com crash is far outweighed by the value created by stocks she has recommended over more than a
decade, including Microsoft Corp., America Online, Compaq Computer Corp. and Dell Computer Corp.
"I had a bad year. I would hate to be judged on one bad year," she says in a corner conference room at
Morgan Stanley's headquarters in midtown Manhattan. Although she concedes she "stayed too long on some
stocks," she did raise plenty of yellow flags amid the dot-com mania. For example, in a March 2000 Webcast
to retail clients, she said 90% of "net" stocks were overvalued and 10% were undervalued. And she says she
wasn't seduced by the fat fees she and her firm stood to earn on the deals she helped push.
Morgan Stanley took in $479.6 million in fees for Internet IPOs and follow-on offerings in 1998-2000,
according to Thomson Financial Securities Data. But Ms. Meeker argues that the fees Morgan received on
Internet deals needn't necessarily have posed a conflict to her analytical objectivity, because investment
bankers make more money by taking successful companies public. "The bigger they get," she explains, "the
more [investment-banking] services they will need."
Ms. Meeker declines to discuss published reports that she received a $15 million pay package in 1999 and
widespread speculation on Wall Street that her 2000 pay slipped.
She says the entire Internet sector was engulfed by a series of forces that couldn't have been anticipated,
including a slowing economy, uncertainty over the presidential election, the increases in interest rates

--- Page 87 ---
Page 87 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
engineered by the Federal Reserve, and cautionary notes about the economy sounded by President-elect
Bush as he campaigned for a tax cut.
What is more, she argues, Morgan Stanley was more selective in the Internet IPOs of stock that it chose to
underwrite than some competitors. Indeed, her firm's IPOs generally performed better than those led by major
competitors including Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and the Credit Suisse First Boston unit of Credit Suisse
Group.
Ms. Meeker says bankers and analysts at the firm misjudged the size of the market opportunity for
Women.com after the early success of an IPO of iVillage.com Inc., another Web site for women. They
thought Priceline's model would work longer than it did. And they and the management team misjudged the
costs at AskJeeves.
"There were unique forces at work that we will never see again in our lifetime," she says -- among them the
mushrooming of financing volume by Internet companies world-wide, which exploded from $4.1 billion in 1998
to $47.9 billion in 1999 and $35.2 billion in the first quarter of 2000. At that point, she said, "the market for
`new paper' for `new ideas' was saturated in March 2000."
Ms. Meeker says she and other bullish Internet analysts -- such as Henry Blodget of Merrill Lynch & Co., who
took her place as the top Internet analyst in the latest Institutional Investor poll -- are "all being judged on a
period that was an anomaly." In hindsight, she says, "whenever you have a gold-rush mentality that's
created, and the music stops, it's not pleasant. It's unfortunate."
However, she adds, Internet usage is still growing, and "this will be viewed as one of the most dynamic
periods of innovation in the history of the world. The fact that there is more sanity in the market is good. Just
when people think it's over is the time it starts up all over again."
It is a message she has been stressing to investors. In a report earlier this year, she said: "Given the
performance of tech stocks in general and Internet-related stocks in particular over the last year, the market
seems to be saying that `the Internet is dead,' and many prognosticators seem to be insisting that `Internet
investing was crazy, anyway.'" That view may present "some good money-making ideas in 2001."
Despite the Internet slowdown, Ms. Meeker says she hasn't cut back her workaholic schedule. On Tuesday,
she stayed at the office until 12:30 a.m. writing a new report. However, she says she doesn't get approached
daily in elevators and taxicabs, the way she used to, by strangers proffering Internet business plans.
She also suggests that one reason she hesitated to downgrade many of the stocks she covered was concern
about possible adverse market fallout. At one point in November, questions she raised about whether Yahoo!
Inc. would meet revenue targets triggered a 15% downdraft in the stock.
Yet some rivals contend that the number of Morgan Stanley clients clamoring for Ms. Meeker's name on their
research reports left her stretched thin. As of last November, she was listed as one of the analysts on 22
different companies. Among Morgan Stanley IPOs touted in reports with her name on them: Artistdirect Inc.,
AskJeeves and Tickets.com. All are down more than 85% since their IPOs. (Morgan Stanley says Ms.
Meeker covers just 14 companies as the lead analyst.)
Ms. Meeker, a former desktop-computer analyst, did pioneering research on the Internet dating back to the
IPO of Netscape Inc. in 1995 -- well ahead of her chief competitor, Mr. Blodget, who came to prominence as
an analyst in late 1998 with a super-bullish call on Amazon.com Inc.
But some money managers say the amount of Ms. Meeker's investment-banking work these days undercuts
her credibility. (At one point just over a year ago, Ms. Meeker addressed the board of Time Warner, a Morgan
Stanley client, on the advisability of buying AOL, one of her earliest Internet stock picks dating back to
December 1993.)
"She became more of a celebrity spokesperson, doing banking deals and all that. I haven't paid attention to
her or talked to her or even looked at any of her research in three to four years," says Gary Dvorchak, a
money manager at Provident Investment Counsel in Pasadena, Calif.
Questions about how much analysts in general, not Ms. Meeker in particular, are influenced by
investment-banking fees were raised just last week by a senior market strategist at her very own firm, Byron
Wien. In a Feb. 27 report titled "The Issue of Integrity," Mr. Wien said analysts shouldn't "gloss over a
negative change in the fundamentals of a company" due to concerns about investment-banking relationships.
"Investment bankers may believe they will have better relationships with their clients if analysts say nice
things about the companies they cover," he said in the report. "But they must realize opinions have little value
if the person delivering them has no credibility. Analysts must honestly and openly express their views or their

--- Page 88 ---
Page 88 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
franchises will erode and they will ultimately be of no use to investors or bankers." (Mr. Wien said Ms. Meeker
called to compliment him on the report.)
Some rival investment bankers are sympathetic to the plight of both Ms. Meeker and Mr. Blodget. "If you're
covering the cruise-ship industry and the Titanic happens, what are you going to say?" says one. "Were there
examples where her recommendations overstayed the viability of the business model? Undoubtedly. She is
no different from anybody else on that subject," says Storm Boswick, a tech-stock investor at J&W Seligman
Co.
Besides, some institutional investors say, they don't focus on analysts' ratings as much as the content of their
reports. "I can't imagine any professional fund manager picking up a research report and saying, `Oh, they
went from a hold to a buy. We should load up,'" says William Gurley, a partner at Benchmark Capital, a
venture-capital firm.
Ms. Meeker, for her part, doesn't shed many tears over the impact of dot-com carnage on the individual
investors who might have been more susceptible than professionals to following her calls. Her "constituency,"
she notes, is more the institutional clients and mutual-fund managers who invest on individuals' behalf.
She shouldn't be blamed, she says, if individuals enraptured by the average annual gains of 42% on the
Nasdaq between 1995 and 1999 put too much of their money in high-risk Internet stocks. "Every individual
has got to be accountable for how they're allocating their investments," she says.
As Ms. Meeker takes a lower profile, some say other analysts at Morgan Stanley are getting more of the
spotlight. Her colleague, Charles Phillips, who follows software companies, appears to have become more
prominent lately, says Caryn Marooney, who runs public-relations firm Outcast Communications in San
Francisco.
"The man is Chuck Phillips. His name is coming up much more as the analyst," Ms. Marooney says. As for
Ms. Meeker? "We seem to hear her name less."

ASIA/PACIFIC
Talk of Manipulation
Tied to Nikkei Bonds
Is Focus of Hearings
Japan's securities watchdog is conducting hearings at "several" foreign and domestic brokerage firms to look
into speculation about stock-price manipulation tied to Nikkei index-linked bonds, an official at the agency
said. The bonds are structured to pay principal as well as relatively high returns so long as the benchmark
Tokyo Nikkei 225 index stays above a preset "knock out" point, said the Securities and Exchange
Surveillance Commission official. If the Nikkei falls to the knock-out point, however, the principal is no longer
guaranteed, and the bond's redemption price will be determined by the closing price of the Nikkei index when
the bond matures, he said.
The SESC is investigating market talk that some of the brokers who arranged the bonds tried to push the
Nikkei index down to these trigger points, thus accelerating the market's fall. That decline is especially
threatening because the health of the nation's banks is intimately linked to the markets through their huge
stock portfolios. Falling share prices threaten the banks with stock losses and rob them of a source of capital
gains to write off bad loans. Last week, Japan's minister for financial affairs called for "severe" measures to
be taken if stock-price manipulation had taken place. The Nikkei index, which yesterday rose 60.36 to
12322.16, is down about 11% this year and 41% off its 12-month high.
TPI Loss Widens on Exchange Charges
Thailand's biggest corporate defaulter, Thai Petrochemical Industry, announced a worse-than-expected
annual loss, stemming mainly from hefty foreign-exchange charges. TPI posted a consolidated net loss of
39.28 billion baht ($905 million) in 2000 compared with an 8.55 billion-baht loss the prior year. A landmark
deal to restructure TPI's $3.8 billion in debt, involving a debt-to-equity conversion and capital injection, still
hangs in the balance. TPI's founder has appealed against the transaction to Thailand's Supreme Court.
Philippine Inflation Slowed in February
Inflation in the Philippines slowed in February because of softer food-price increases, but the decline won't
be enough to spur the central bank to resume cutting its key interest rates to invigorate the economy,
analysts said. Data released by the government show the annual inflation rate rose to 6.7% last month after
reaching 6.9% in January, a 22-month high. The government has set a target of 6% to 7% this year, which it
is expected to comfortably meet. February's inflation report was marked by a sharp slowdown in food-price
rises, up 4.3% from 5.1% the previous month.
Matsushita Electric Seeks Higher Profit
Matsushita Electric Industrial, the world's largest consumer electronics maker, said it aims to boost flagging
profit at its overseas units, in part by shifting U.S. and British output to cheaper locations. The Osaka, Japan,
giant best known for its Panasonic, Technics and Quasar brands plans to raise its consolidated operating
profit at production units abroad to 5% of sales over the next three years, said its managing director for
overseas operations. That would compare with barely 1% for the current business year to March 31.
Group Appeals for Rights Reform in China

--- Page 90 ---
Page 90 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Reforms in the way China processes accused criminals have in some areas led to greater abuses, failed to
curb widespread torture and made defense lawyers' work more difficult, said Human Rights in China,
appealing for major reforms to protect defendants' rights. Dissidents, labor organizers and others viewed as
political threats to the government, including members of the banned Falun Gong spiritual movement, are
routinely denied legal rights, the human-rights group said.

EUROPE
Pubmaster, Candover Plan Bid for Pubs
Britain's Pubmaster and private-equity firm Candover are planning a GBP 1.5 billion ($2.21 billion)-plus bid for
Whitbread's 3,000 pubs, with Pubmaster set to buy the leased pubs, people familiar with the industry said.
Private pubs group Pubmaster has put a GBP 600 million value on Whitbread's 1,700 leased pubs, with
Candover interested in the 1,300 managed pubs, as Pubmaster looks to recover from being narrowly beaten
last month to a group of Bass pubs. The Hartlepool, England, pub concern is eager to expand its own holding
of 2,000 pubs toward an optimum 4,000- to 5,000-pub estate, but faces stiff competition from Guy Hands of
Nomura International, who beat it to the Bass pubs.
France Bans Exports of Livestock
In another blow to Europe's farm industry, France banned exports of animals at risk for foot-and-mouth
disease as the Continent went on high alert to try to stop the virus from spreading from Britain. After a series
of false alarms, Germany, Belgium, Sweden, Denmark and France had said they had no confirmed cases of
the sickness, which has ravaged 70 farms in Britain and Northern Ireland since the first cases were
discovered Feb. 19. Initial examinations on nine herds in France showed the livestock, which had been
imported from Britain, had produced antibodies after being in contact with the virus.
Britain to Raise Minimum Wage by 10.8%
Britain said it will increase the national hourly minimum wage by 10.8% to GBP 4.10 ($6.03) from GBP 3.70,
effective in October. The government also aims to raise the wage to GBP 4.20 in October 2002, if economic
circumstances allow. A rise to GBP 4.10 will add just 0.07 percentage point to underlying inflation in the first
year and 0.05 point the second year. Britain introduced the minimum wage two years ago. From 1.3 million to
1.5 million adult workers are paid the minimum amount, but their wages account for only 0.1% to 0.3% of the
country's total wage bill. A spokeswoman for the Confederation of British Industry said raising the minimum
wage "significantly higher than GBP 4 would be damaging."
British Beef Consumption Rose in 2000
Britons last year downed more beer, indulged a sweet tooth and seemed to shrug off the spread of mad-cow
disease -- formally known as bovine spongiform encephalopathy, or BSE -- a government survey found.
Home beef and veal consumption jumped 12% last year from that of 1999. The gain suggests "the onset of
the Continental BSE crisis in the last quarter of 2000 had not adversely affected beef consumption in Great
Britain," said the authors of the study by National Statistics and the Ministry of Agriculture Fisheries and Food.
The data suggest that some British consumers, after years of hearing about BSE, had gotten over their fears
by the time the health scare reached the Continent.
BRIEFLY:
-- Amsterdam-based Buhrmann announced a global-equity offering to raise from 600 million euros ($561
million) to 700 million euros to finance acquisitions, including the takeover of the office-supply division of U.S.
Office Products.
-- Suez Lyonnaise des Eaux approached Air Liquide about merging, the companies said, but was rebuffed by
the French provider of industrial gases, marking yet another setback in the utility group's attempt to define its
strategy. Suez said a merger between the two companies would generate "important synergies" and create
value for shareholders. But Air Liquide disagreed, saying a merger wouldn't make "industrial" or "economic"
sense. The merger attempt underscores Suez's apparent confusion about what it wants to be, coming on the
heels of the company's abrupt withdrawal from France's auction of third-generation wireless network licenses
in January and its decision in September to pull out of merger talks with German utility group E.ON.

Wall Street Bullish on Argentine Minister
A number of Wall Street investment banks reversed their lowly opinion of Argentina, telling investors to start
buying the country's sovereign bonds now that fiscal hawk Ricardo Lopez Murphy has taken charge of the
dire economy. Merrill Lynch, Bear Stearns, Deutsche Bank, J.P. Morgan Chase and UBS's UBS Warburg all
shifted their recommendation of Argentina's external debt to, or very close to, marketweight on the
expectation that Mr. Lopez Murphy would restore confidence and kick-start growth in the economy mired in its
ninth straight quarter of recession. The benchmark Merval Index yesterday jumped 8.1% to 485.17 on his
appointment.
Mexico Insurer May Buy ING Unit Portfolio
Mexico's largest insurer, Seguros Comercial America, or Segcoam, said it may buy the life-insurance portfolio
owned by the local unit of Netherlands-based ING Group. Segcoam said in a filing with the Mexican Stock
Exchange that it is conducting talks with ING Seguros and that it already has contacted regulators. Segcoam,
a unit of financial-services and biotechnology conglomerate Savia, said the acquisition forms part of its
strategy to focus on personal lines in the insurance segment, such as individual life and health-insurance
products.
BRIEFLY:
-- Judge Juan Ramirez delayed orders by Mexico to extradite to Spain Ricardo Cavallo, who faces torture,
kidnapping and terrorism charges, to hear an appeal filed by his defense lawyers. His accusers say Mr.
Cavallo served at the Navy Mechanical School during Argentina's dictatorship.
-- Venezuela's state-owned gold-mining concern Minerven may replace Placer Dome as concession holder
at its Las Cristinas gold mine by the end of 2001, according to a person familiar with the deal.
-- Ford Motor's Mexican affiliate yesterday said it had raised workers' salaries at two plants by 13.5%,
warding off a strike.

Minnesota's Gov. Ventura Takes On Challenge of Tax Overhaul
Politics & Policy
Minnesota's Gov. Ventura Takes On Challenge of Tax Overhaul
By Will Pinkston and Russell Gold
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
1,480 words
5 March 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Corrections & Amplifications
MINNESOTA GOV. JESSE VENTURA isn't pushing immediate legislation to extend his state's sales tax to
Internet transactions, although the governor does want to tax electronic commerce in the future. A Politics &
Policy article Monday about Mr. Ventura's tax-reform efforts incorrectly implied he was seeking taxation of
Internet sales this year. (WSJ March 7, 2001)
ST. PAUL, Minn. -- Minnesota Gov. Jesse Ventura -- political rebel, former professional wrestler, part-time
football commentator -- is winning fame for yet another pursuit: trying to overhaul his state's tax system.
Fiscal-policy experts around the country credit him for tackling one of the nation's most sweeping initiatives to
revamp state taxes. In between play-by-play analysis for the upstart XFL football league, Mr. Ventura is
pressing for an all-out blitz on Minnesota's property, sales and income taxes. In addition to cutting all three
levies at once, he is asking lawmakers to extend the sales tax to Internet purchases as well as a broad range
of professional services ranging from haircuts to legal work.
He faces an uphill battle; many of the new taxes are unpopular. But if he succeeds, he would reinvent a
common structure that many say is rendering states' tax systems inadequate, as the economy increasingly
shifts from goods to services.
"Anything of importance in state finance this year is going to be touched on in Minnesota," says Dan Bucks,
executive director of the Multistate Tax Commission, a group of states pushing for more-uniform tax codes.
Mr. Ventura's plan to broaden the sales tax, adds Harley Duncan, executive director of the Federation of Tax
Administrators, a Washington, D.C., association of state tax collectors, "meets most precepts for sound tax
policy you'd find in textbooks."
Mr. Ventura, of course, isn't exactly a textbook governor. After confounding pundits by winning the governor's
office two years ago as a third-party candidate, he has charged headlong into wide-ranging causes: from
sending out sales-tax rebates the past two years to recently trying to get statehouse reporters to wear badges
labeling them "official jackals." His style, seen most dramatically in the quest for a tax makeover, is to push
his advisers to find answers regardless of political feasibility.
"What kind of nonsense is this?" he thundered one recent day in his Capitol office, ticking off what he
considers inequities in Minnesota's tax system, including a maze of sales-tax exemptions extended to some
goods and services but not others. But do his wholesale changes have a prayer of passing? "If the legislature
doesn't want meaningful tax reform," he answers, chewing on an unlit cigar, "they don't have to do it."
Indeed, lawmakers are hardly lining up to support Mr. Ventura's tax ideas. The political independent faces
substantial opposition in both the Republican-controlled House and the Senate, controlled by Minnesota's
own liberal version of the Democratic Party, called Democratic Farmer Labor. Detractors question whether
wholesale tax cutting is fiscally responsible when a slowing economy is reducing revenue growth. Others
warn that tax cuts will cause undue pressure to hold down spending in vital areas such as higher education.
Powerful groups ranging from lawyers to accountants, whose clients would be hit by a broadened sales tax,
are whipping up opposition, as some taxpayer groups argue the sales tax disproportionately hurts the poor.
"The politics of it is horrible," says Wayne Simoneau, a former Democratic legislator who has served in the
administrations of both Mr. Ventura and his Republican predecessor.
No matter. Pam Wheelock, a former economic-development chief for the city of St. Paul whom Mr. Ventura
recruited as his state finance commissioner, concedes conditions might not be perfect for advancing "so

--- Page 93 ---
Page 93 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
many controversial issues at the same time." But for Mr. Ventura, she adds, "political considerations really
aren't factors."
Mr. Ventura does enjoy considerable popular support that makes his foes uneasy. A poll in early February by
the St. Paul Pioneer Press and Minnesota Public Radio found voters evenly split on broadening the sales tax
while cutting property taxes. Among Mr. Ventura's supporters are blue-collar Minnesotans, who like the total
of $2 billion in sales-tax rebates mailed out the past two summers under one of Mr. Ventura's favorite
initiatives. "People in the VFWs, the beer joints and the coffee shops, they love him," says Mr. Simoneau.
Mr. Ventura, who as a wrestler was known as "the Body" but pledged to become "the Mind" as governor, also
has won some respect for his willingness to tackle thorny policy issues. "This guy is a lot more sophisticated
than anyone thought he was when he first started running," says Art Rolnick, research director at the Federal
Reserve Bank of Minneapolis.
And another first-term governor, George W. Bush of Texas, didn't fare too badly when he proposed massive
property-tax relief in 1997, offset in part by broadening another tax, to help the state increase its share of
local school funding. Although opposition from groups that would be newly taxed killed his idea, then-Gov.
Bush's fallback plan for a smaller property-tax cut succeeded, winning him political points for boldly attacking
a complex issue.
Mr. Ventura has been grousing about Minnesota's tax system for years, first as mayor of Brooklyn Park in
suburban Minneapolis from 1991 to 1995, then from his pulpit as a Twin Cities talk-radio host. Particularly
troubling to him: the belief that local governments can't control property-tax rates because, among other
things, constant increases are needed to cover their 30% share of rapidly escalating education costs in the
state.
After being elected governor, he vowed to study the tax system before changing it. Instructing advisers to pay
close attention to his frustrations over the property tax, Mr. Ventura charged Revenue Commissioner Matt
Smith with overhauling the state's entire tax system. "We took it and ran," says Mr. Smith, a 13-year state
employee. Revenue Department staffers divided into teams to analyze the state's tax code as if they had
been given carte blanche to rewrite it. Eschewing the "blue-ribbon panel" approach to tax reform, Mr. Smith
launched a statewide series of public hearings and focus groups, where he found widespread disdain for the
property and income taxes but less opposition to the sales tax.
Mr. Ventura, who attended some of the hearings, insisted on updates at least once a week. While the
governor generally isn't one to concern himself with details, he can focus sharply when he wants to. "If I quote
a number to him, it better be right because he will remember it," says Mr. Smith.
By Labor Day of last year, a plan was emerging: A centerpiece of tax reform would be shifting a greater share
of state-mandated public-school costs to the state. (Currently the state pays around 70%; it would carry as
much as 85% of the burden under the governor's plan.) That would allow local governments to lower property
taxes. At the same time, the state would shave income-tax rates by half a percentage point and reduce the
sales tax to 6% from 6.5%.
The price tag of all those tax cuts, along with some other minor ones: nearly $1.9 billion in 2003, according to
the Ventura administration. To help offset lost revenue, the governor has proposed extending the sales tax to
services and Internet transactions, while maintaining exemptions on food and clothing. Administration officials
estimate that will raise $860 million, and Mr. Ventura says revenue growth and a projected $1.5 billion budget
surplus in the 2002 and 2003 fiscal years will help cover the remaining amount -- provided the state holds
down spending to a level near inflation.
While tax reformers from outside Minnesota credit Mr. Ventura for such an ambitious plan, the proposal is
being scrutinized from all political sides within the state. Among the rallying cries: warnings that
professional-service providers -- and companies using their services -- will reduce their presence in
Minnesota if the sales tax is broadened. Mr. Ventura generally ignores the outcries. He recalls a "sob story"
from one estate-planning lawyer about how his elderly clients would face higher legal costs. "Maybe you
ought to cut your rates," Mr. Ventura says he suggested.
The governor's tax advisers say it won't be clear until April or May whether lawmakers will adopt any of his
proposals. But Mr. Ventura, who says he hasn't decided whether to seek re-election next year, appears
unruffled by the prospect of failure. "We're raising the level of consciousness out there," he says. "That's a
victory."
