A Year After The Peak:Bursting of the Tech Bubble Has a Familiar `Pop' to It---Lesson From Past Debacles:It Can Take a Long WhileFor Share...
A Year After The Peak: Bursting of the Tech Bubble Has a Familiar `Pop' to It --- Lesson From Past
Debacles: It Can Take a Long While For Shares to Come Back
By Stephen E. Frank and E.S. Browning
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
2,426 words
2 March 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
It was a bubble, and it did burst. About that, most people agree.
What everyone really wants to know is, what's next? How much, and how fast, can technology stocks
recover?
Investors trying to make sense of the dot-com debacle might do well to recall the story of William C. Durant.
Early in the 20th century, Mr. Durant founded General Motors Corp., one of a revolutionary group of auto
companies that in some ways were the dot-coms of their era. Investment capital poured in and GM stock
soared more than 5,500% from 1914 to 1920.
But when the overcrowded automobile industry failed to deliver on inflated expectations in the early 1920s,
auto stocks plunged. GM lost two-thirds of its stock value in six months. A panicked Mr. Durant borrowed
money and feverishly bought the shares in a futile attempt to prop them up.
GM, of course, eventually recovered and soared again -- too late for its founder, who lost his entire fortune
and wound up running a bowling alley. Many other once-great auto companies, such as Packard, Studebaker
and Hudson, fared less well than GM, disappearing one by one in the ensuing decades.
And therein lies a cautionary tale for investors who have sat in shock as Cisco Systems Inc. stock has lost
more than 70% of its value and Yahoo! Inc. shares have fallen close to 90%.
The current technology boom and bust, it turns out, is nothing new. It is simply the latest in a long history of
investment bubbles that have plagued shareholders since investing began. From the Dutch tulip bulb craze of
the 17th century, to the locomotive revolution of the 19th, to the rise, fall and resurrection of
personal-computer stocks and biotechnology stocks in the 1980s and 1990s, investors have fallen madly in
love with -- and then madly out of love with -- the hot technology of the moment.
These past debacles offer lessons for investors trying to figure out what to do after the Nasdaq Composite
Index's 57% fall from its peak last March 10 of 5048.62.
Bubble watchers say the worst may not be over. Just as the stocks tend to overshoot on the way up, bubble
stocks tend to overshoot on the way down as well. Through yesterday, the Nasdaq index has lost 3.5% this
week alone, and is below where it ended the year in 1998.
History does suggest that, eventually, many of the pieces of a burst stock bubble get patched back together.
Some of the stocks will soar again. But the recovery typically takes longer than investors hope -- usually
years, not months. Many of the stocks caught up in the bubble fail to survive, and of those that do survive, all
but the most robust wind up behaving less exuberantly than they did before.
Technology companies "are going to take a good whack, a good hard landing, and you can't expect that they
are just going to shrug it off and go up to new highs and forget it," warns George Roche, chairman of
Baltimore mutual-fund group T. Rowe Price. "You are going to require a period to repair it."
Some optimists hope the recovery this time may come faster than it has after past bubbles. They point to the
intervention of the Federal Reserve to stimulate the economy, and to today's pace of technological change,
information flow and inventory adjustment, which is far faster than anything known in the past.
But even the most bullish observers aren't predicting an immediate snapback. In fact, a ghoulish fascination is
developing among professional investors over the parallels between the current tech wreck and past stock
disasters. Many of the pros have been sending one another charts overlaying an index of Internet stocks or

--- Page 95 ---
Page 95 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
the Nasdaq Composite against charts showing the biotechnology bubble that collapsed in 1992, the Dow
Jones Industrial Average around 1929, or the 1990 collapse of Japan's Nikkei 225 Average.
The basic truth market historians draw from past bubbles is that it almost always takes longer to repair the
damage than people expect.
After the economic crash of 1837, for example, it took a handful of railroad stocks a solid decade to regain
precrash levels -- and most didn't. GM's recovery took six years, about the same period it took most
personal-computer and biotech stocks to rebound in recent times. The Japanese stock market topped out at
the end of 1989, and remains 67% below its peak.
Most recently, investors point to the 1992 biotechnology collapse. Biotech stocks, as measured by the
American Stock Exchange Biotechnology Index, didn't stop falling until March 1995. They didn't regain old
highs in a lasting way until December 1998.
There is also the fate of the electric utilities in the 1920s. Bearers of a breathtaking new technology that
transformed the world just as the Internet is doing today, it was 1964 before they returned to their 1929 peak,
and they never regained the vigor of their youth.
Radio Corp. of America was another highflier of the 1920s. America's fascination with the radio propelled
RCA's shares to a high of $500 just before the '29 crash -- an increase of 2,000% over six years. The crash
wiped out all those gains, and it took more than three decades for the stock to revisit its highs. Even then,
RCA, which had become a television company, ultimately was undone by competition, and wound up being
split up and sold.
Today's technology pessimists, such as Jeremy Grantham, co-founder and chief strategist at Boston
money-management group Grantham, Mayo, Van Otterloo & Co., argue it could be a decade before
technology stocks turn convincingly upward.
Mr. Grantham has tracked more than a dozen past bubbles involving gold, oil, nickel, the dollar, the British
pound, Japanese stocks in the 1980s and U.S. stocks in the 1960s. In every case, he says, the stock,
commodity or currency gave back all the extra, inflation-adjusted gains beyond what it would have attained if
it had simply maintained its slower, prebubble performance. He believes tech stocks have still farther to fall,
and that they will pull the broader market down into a lasting slump as well.
Not everyone's outlook is so bleak, of course. Henry Blodget, the Merrill Lynch Internet analyst whose name
became synonymous with dot-com euphoria, says the worst of the downturn will be over by the latter half of
this year. But even he believes complete recovery to last spring's highs could take as long as three to five
years, even for some Internet bellwethers like Yahoo.
"I think we still have a ways to go in terms of working through the ripple effects of the bubble," Mr. Blodget
says.
Another lesson from past bubbles is that, while some stocks such as General Motors do bounce back and
soar again, it can be maddeningly difficult to figure out which will be the winners.
It is almost always a mistake to "catch a falling knife," as they say on Wall Street, which means to buy a
plummeting stock. Even once-strong competitors can sag. Today, most investors would identify names such
as Cisco, Sun Microsystems Inc., AOL Time Warner Inc. and eBay Inc. as leaders of the technology and
Internet sectors. But those stocks all are down between 40% and 70% from their highs. Will they all recover?
Veteran money-manager Steven Leuthold, who heads Leuthold Group in Minneapolis, recalls his first
electronics bubble, in which he lost some money in 1961 and 1962. The companies to own back then often
had the syllable "tron" in their names, such as Transitron and Flowtronics. They aren't around today. "The
lesson that you learn from all of these experiences is that they don't go on forever and there aren't very many
survivors," Mr. Leuthold warns.
Consider the biotech stocks. A few, such as Amgen Inc., rebounded quickly from their 1992 collapse. But
they were the exceptions. Other early stars, such as Xoma Inc., survived the debacle, but never returned to
their peak prices.
"In biotech, there were numerous one-technology companies whose technologies didn't pan out," says Larry
Feinberg, managing partner at Oracle Partners, a hedge fund in Greenwich, Conn. (which isn't related to
software company Oracle Corp.). Something similar is true of many Internet companies. "The analogy, from a
fundamental viewpoint, is that the industry has to be rationalized and developed," he says. "You get this wave
of enthusiasm and then investors realize that they have shot too far in one direction."

--- Page 96 ---
Page 96 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Morgan Stanley software analyst Chuck Phillips has a name for this phenomenon: the "classic technology
hype cycle." The cycle, as he describes it, has three phases: a "frenzy and land grab," followed by a slow
maturation of the technology, during which the stock-market euphoria cools off, followed by "the production
phase," when the real benefits of the technology kick in, a few clear winners emerge and the market gets
excited again.
Many modern-day tech investors reassure themselves that tech companies will do better than past bubble
victims, because today's companies are leading a wave of revolutionary change that will transform society for
years to come. Sadly, one of the most painful lessons of the past is that bubble companies often are involved
in a revolutionary technology. That doesn't prevent their stocks from crumbling once they get pushed up too
far.
Visionary technologies and ideas have been coming and going for centuries, in fact, leaving stock disasters in
their wake. In the 18th century, the promise of foreign trade excited Britain. An outfit called the South Sea
Co., given a monopoly on trade with the Spanish Empire, soared on London's stock market. Trade eventually
did make fortunes for some people, but not for the South Sea Co., which became a disaster dubbed the
South Sea Bubble and helped throw the British economy into a slump.
After Charles Lindbergh piloted the Spirit of St. Louis across the Atlantic in 1927, proving trans-Atlantic flight
was possible, airline stocks took off. Few survived. One company that saw its stock soar, Seaboard Air Lines,
was in reality a poetically named railroad.
So, too, today. Though his company is expected to post sales of around $3.5 billion this year, Amazon.com
Inc. founder Jeff Bezos frequently has warned investors that it isn't yet a lasting company -- and may never
become one. "The track record of innovators is not good," he has said.
He is right. Visionaries aren't always good managers, and the much-heralded "first movers" can lose out to
companies that get the kinks out of great ideas.
Look at what happened to the creators of modern database software in the late 1980s. A host of start-ups
went public -- with names like Informix Corp., Ingres, Oracle and Sybase Inc. -- promising to change the way
information is stored. Their products allowed customers to find and manipulate data easily, using plain
English commands. Orders rolled in and the stocks soared. But the software, riddled with bugs, began
crashing, and so did the software makers' stocks.
By 1991, Oracle was losing money. Its shares plunged to single digits, shaving more than 80% off the nearly
$4 billion market value it had as of mid-1990. The company was forced to take on an $80 million loan from
Nippon Steel Corp. because no bank would lend it money. Oracle survived, in part because it brought in
additional managers to help implement founder Lawrence Ellison's vision. A decade later, Oracle's market
value is north of $105 billion.
And Oracle's rivals? Informix and Sybase today have market values of less than $2 billion each. Ingres was
acquired by Computer Associates International Inc. in 1994 for about $300 million. Since the intervening
decade, of about 2,000 other software firms that went public, just 417 exist as independent companies. The
rest either were acquired or went out of business.
There are some reasons to hope that a tech-stock recovery may happen faster than has been the case after
past bubbles. Apart from the government riding to the rescue with interest-rate cuts and a possible tax cut, a
more fundamental reason for hope is that technology's life cycle is shortening. That means equipment is
becoming outdated far faster than in years past. Like it or not, companies that want to keep up with changing
markets have no choice but to buy new gear all the time.
An example is Intel Corp., the world's leading chip maker. Though chip demand has dropped off, Intel plans
to spend $7.5 billion this year on new manufacturing equipment, an increase of about $1 billion over last year.
Intel has the equipment to produce chips with very high processing capacity, but those technologies are
rapidly becoming stale. Intel's latest-generation Pentium IV chip is built to operate best at an even higher
processing capacity, and that requires expensive new equipment. In another four or five years, on average,
that new manufacturing equipment, too, will become outdated.
Contrast that with the 1920s, which witnessed revolutions in transportation, telecommunications and
manufacturing. Unlike today's equipment, with a lifespan of just a few years in some cases, manufacturing
plants constructed during the 1920s were supposed to last for decades.
"With information technology, you can make huge investments now, but basically three years from now it's
depreciated to almost nothing," says Peter Garber, a global strategist at Deutsche Bank and a professor of
economics at Brown University. "Because of innovations in hardware and innovations in software, information
technology just evaporates."

--- Page 97 ---
Page 97 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
So tech companies will continue selling gear, perhaps at a faster rate than utilities sold electricity and PC
makers sold computers when their stocks were declining. What no one knows is whether the demand will be
strong enough to make today's tech stocks start to rebound sooner than those of yesterday.
"Things happen quickly today, so it wouldn't amaze me if it [a recovery] happened in two or three years," says
Mr. Grantham of Grantham Mayo. Still, he adds, "history says that the great bear markets take their time."

BUSH SUBMITTED a fiscal 2002 budget of $1.96 trillion and took to the road to sell it.
The president called the spending blueprint "compassionate," but it cuts funding for 10 of the 25 main

A STRONG EARTHQUAKE HIT near Seattle, but it caused relatively light damage.

Thinking Things Over:Rating Presidents, Viewing Reflected Images
Thinking Things Over: Rating Presidents, Viewing Reflected Images
By Robert L. Bartley
1,046 words
26 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A23
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Last fall The Wall Street Journal joined with the Federalist Society to sponsor a rating of the presidents by a
panel of 78 academics, carefully chosen for political balance. The rankings and impressive commentary can
be found as "Hail to the Chief" on the Web at our OpinionJournal.com. Going up just in time to be swamped
by the Florida election contest, the project has not received the attention it deserves.
Anyway, I'd like to take my own cut at the subject. The panel's results were reasonably predictable, with
Washington, Lincoln and FDR as the "great" presidents. It did vault Ronald Reagan into eighth place,
compared with 25th in an earlier ranking conducted by Arthur Schlesinger Jr. (a long-time member of our
Board of Contributors and of course one of the respondents to our survey).
As a new wrinkle, the questionnaire also asked which presidents are overrated and underrated. This is what
I'd like to address, restricting myself to the 20th century, which is to say, to presidents who cast a
contemporary shadow. I for one think that to grasp our own lives and times you have to understand that John
F. Kennedy is overrated and Calvin Coolidge underrated.
I've been a fan of "Silent Cal" for years, since poking around a used bookstore in Maine and discovering a
copy of "Puritan in Babylon: The Story of Calvin Coolidge" by storied Topeka editor William Allen White. (I list
a few other books on Coolidge, Kennedy and Vietnam at the bottom of Thinking Things Over on
OpinionJournal.) White and I were not alone in our admiration. One of Ronald Reagan's first acts as president
was to hang a portrait of Coolidge in the Cabinet room. And in putting down the air-traffic controllers' strike,
the Gipper quoted the line that put Boston Mayor Coolidge on the road to the vice presidency and presidency
in suppressing the police strike: "There is no right to strike against the public safety by anybody, anywhere, at
any time."
President Reagan also cited the Coolidge (actually Coolidge-Mellon) tax cuts, which helped fuel the boom of
the 1920s and increased rather than decreased government revenues. Succeeding with the death of Harding
in 1923, Coolidge also cleaned up the Teapot Dome scandal by appointing a special counsel (actually two, a
prominent lawyer and a former Democratic senator). In the process, he demanded the resignation of his
attorney general. Handily elected in his own right in 1924, he presided over an extraordinary period of
economic progress.
The conventional-wisdom rap on Coolidge is that the boom of the late 1920s somehow caused the
Depression of the 1930s. Even today, seers scan the economic horizon looking for a "boom" that will cause
tomorrow's "bust." Mainstream economists have perhaps not reached a consensus on the cause of the Great
Depression but few blame Coolidge; Milton Friedman cites mistakes in monetary policy, while Robert
Mundell blames a generalized shortage of world liquidity as nations resumed the gold standard. Also,
President Hoover made the downturn immeasurably worse with two blunders, signing the Smoot-Hawley
Tariff and raising taxes in 1932 to fight the deficit.
My view of Coolidge gets considerable support from the Federalist Society/Wall Street Journal panel. His 14
votes in the "underrated" category came in second to 16 for Ronald Reagan. But 23 panelists rated Reagan
"overrated"; no one suggested this of Coolidge. In an excellent OpinionJournal essay, John McGinnis of
Cardozo Law School notes that conventional wisdom maliciously truncates the famous Coolidge quote: "The
chief business of America is business, the chief idealism of America is idealism."
If the survey supports my view of Coolidge, it overwhelmingly endorses my view of John F. Kennedy. No
fewer than 43 raters, an outright majority of the panel, consider him overrated. An essay by Akhil Reed Amar
of Yale Law School faults JFK for appeasing Southern Democrats by stocking the lower federal bench with
"notorious segregationists"; the best civil rights judges were Eisenhower appointees. And with the
appointment of his brother as attorney general, he tried to found a political dynasty, abhorred by the Founding
Fathers. "The metaphor of Camelot, after all, is ultimately un-American and undemocratic, conjuring up
images of crowns and dashing young princes and noble birth."

--- Page 101 ---
Page 101 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
My own view of President Kennedy is much more direct: His callowness and amorality got us into Vietnam.
Or to be more precise, his blunders turned a limited commitment into an open-ended one. One of the few
specific pieces of advice Gen. Eisenhower gave his young successor was to put American troops in Laos,
that is, astride what later became the Ho Chi Minh Trail. Instead, Kennedy sent Averell Harriman and Roger
Hilsman out to negotiate an agreement on Laos, which of course gave Ho the access he wanted.
Infiltration and subversion increased in South Vietnam. With Buddhist bonzes committing self-immolation, the
best and brightest argued the problem was the Catholic recalcitrance of our ally, Ngo Dinh Diem. President
Kennedy authorized a coup against Diem; the generals who took over killed him on Nov. 2, 1963. On Nov.
22, Kennedy was assassinated; his martyrdom fixed his reputation.
Yet the pieces, and much of the blame, fell to Lyndon B. Johnson. Just before moving into the White House,
Ellen Hammer has reported, the new president showed Hubert Humphrey a picture of Diem. "We had a hand
in killing him," LBJ said. "Now it's happening here." Johnson of course made his own errors, but the die was
already cast. In overthrowing an ally in the name of winning the war, the U.S. had made a commitment from
which it could scarcely walk away. Many of Kennedy's admirers later became war protesters, of course,
chanting that American society was immoral.
These two presidential images are reflected in today's debates over economic policy, foreign interventions,
the role of morality in politics. Not least, they raise far broader questions about whatever other myths are
propagated today by the conventional wisdom that rates Kennedy a hero and Coolidge a goat.
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: Take Another Look At 'Silent Cal' Coolidge" -- WSJ Mar. 9, 2001)

Bush Focuses on Tax Plan, China's RoleIn Iraqi Air Defenses in News Conference
Politics & Policy
Bush Focuses on Tax Plan, China's Role In Iraqi Air Defenses in News Conference
By Bob Davis
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
623 words
23 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- President Bush, in his first presidential news conference, vowed to fight any effort in
Congress to add special tax breaks to his tax-cut proposal, and he protested China's involvement in
improving Iraqi air defenses.
"I'm going to resist the Christmas-tree effect of tax policy," said Mr. Bush, referring to the lobbying strategy of
loading tax bills with additional cuts. "I don't want people putting ornaments on my plan." The Bush plan
would cut taxes $1.6 trillion over 10 years.
As for China, the president said, "It's troubling that they'd be involved in helping Iraq develop a system that
will endanger our pilots." He was referring to Pentagon reports that last week's airstrikes by U.S. and British
warplanes near Baghdad were designed to destroy optical-fiber communications lines being laid by Chinese
personnel. "Let me just tell you this," Mr. Bush added, "it's risen to the level where we're going to send a
message to the Chinese."
Later, Mr. Bush's national security adviser, Condoleezza Rice, explained that Secretary of State Colin Powell
had twice raised the issue with Chinese officials. "We have told them that we are concerned that there may
be violations of the sanctions regime," she said.
The questions at the news conference bounced among tax policy, budget issues, foreign affairs and former
President Clinton's pardons. Mr. Bush, who has been overshadowed recently by the controversy over
presidential pardons, avoided criticizing his predecessor. While he didn't break new ground, the session gave
him a chance to demonstrate his understanding of different issues and to get more media attention in
advance of his budget speech to Congress Tuesday.
Concerning his budget strategy, Mr. Bush said he would seek to "slow the rate of growth of the budget down."
He didn't give an overall spending number, but his advisers said that he would propose that discretionary
spending grow about 4%, a bit higher than the rate of inflation.
He said that under his plan, Medicare spending would rise by $21 billion in the fiscal year beginning Oct. 1, or
10%, from the current level of $216 billion. But it wasn't clear whether that reflected funding for his
prescription-drug proposal or merely an increase in legally mandated spending. Mr. Bush didn't mention any
programs that were due for cuts or for increases that didn't keep up with inflation.
On other issues, Mr. Bush said he was encouraged that some Russian officials are beginning to talk favorably
about certain forms of missile defense. He also said he would start to lobby Congress for fast-track trade
negotiating authority, by which lawmakers agree to vote yes or no on a trade pact, but can't amend it.
Leaders of Latin America and Canada, who want a hemispherewide trade zone, are certain to bring up the
fast-track issue when they meet with Mr. Bush in Quebec City in April. That is because trading partners don't
want to cut deals with the U.S. that can be changed by Congress.
The president chose the tattered, crowded White House briefing room for his first news conference rather
than the gold-leaf elegance of the East Room, where such sessions are usually held. That appeared to be in
keeping with his efforts to craft a presidential style that is less showy than his predecessor's. Occasionally he
would break into campaign-style jocularity, referring to one reporter as "Pancho," and interrupting a question
about Latin America with the Spanish "Si."

EUROPE/AFRICA
U.K. Livestock Crisis
Likely to Become
Hot Political Issue
British Prime Minister Tony Blair said that an outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease among livestock in Britain
was the "last thing farmers need" and the government would consider assistance for farmers. Mr. Blair, in
Ottawa on the first visit to Canada by a British prime minister since 1988, said London was working to contain
the outbreak, which has led to a world ban on imports of British livestock and animal products and plunged
the U.K. farm sector into a new crisis. The outbreak this week could become a hot political issue in British
general elections expected this spring. The opposition Conservatives said the Labour government may be to
blame for the reappearance of the highly infectious disease among livestock.
"The government should have acted sooner to prevent the risk of this disease entering Britain through
substandard meat imports," said Tim Yeo, a lawmaker and Tory spokesman on agriculture issues. The
government is anxious to make sure the disease is under control by the time the European Commission
reviews its ban on British exports on March 1.
Russia's Economy Grew 7.7% in 2000
Russia's gross domestic product last year rose 7.7% to 6.947 trillion rubles ($242.2 billion), the State
Statistics Committee said, confirming its earlier estimate. The committee revised upward the 1999 GDP
growth figure to 3.5%, from 3.2%. It also said the number of unemployed people in January fell 21% from a
year earlier to 6.9 million, or 9.6% of the work force, and was down 7.0% from December. The committee
estimated Russia's total work force at 71.8 million people, or about 50% of the country's total population, as of
Jan. 31.
Two Swedish Banks Plan to Combine
Swedish banks SEB and ForeningsSparbanken plan to merge in a $7.4 billion stock swap that would create
the Nordic region's second-largest financial-services group and which is likely to spur further consolidation in
the sector. SEB is swapping its stock for shares in ForeningsSparbanken to create a combined group to be
called SEB Swedbank. SEB will have a 51.5% stake in the new group and ForeningsSparbanken will have a
48.5% interest. The combined company will have more than 11 million customers, 35,000 employees, $131
billion in assets and a market value of about 150 billion Swedish kronor ($15.14 billion), the banks said. The
merger will result in cost savings of as much as three billion kronor a year, they said. The transaction is
subject to Swedish and European Union regulatory approval.
Reed Elsevier Chief Expects Profit Growth
Anglo-Dutch publisher Reed Elsevier reported pretax profit for 2000 at the top end of market expectations
and its chief executive, Crispin Davis, said Reed could achieve double-digit growth in profit this year if its
proposed $4.5 billion acquisition of U.S. publisher Harcourt General is completed quickly. The British
government this week said it will investigate the deal on competition grounds, but Mr. Davis expressed
confidence the transaction would go ahead. Reed reported that pretax profit last year, before exceptional
items and amortization, declined 2.8% to GBP 690 million ($996 million), mainly because of substantial
investments related to the Internet. Analysts had forecast pretax profit between GBP 670 million and GBP
691 million. Revenue rose 11% to GBP 3.77 billion. Reed's businesses include the Lexis-Nexis legal
database, numerous scientific journals and the entertainment trade paper Variety.

Page 104 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
South Africa's Gold Output Falls 4.7%
Gold production in South Africa, the world's No. 1 gold producer, fell 4.7% last year to 428.3 tons, the lowest
level since 411.7 tons in 1954, but the decline in production is expected to slow as new projects come on
stream, the South African industry's Chamber of Mines said. South Africa's mines yielded about 1,000 tons of
gold at their production peak in 1970, but output has fallen steadily since gold prices topped out at $850 an
ounce in the early 1980s.
BRIEFLY:
-- The euro zone's current-account deficit widened to 28.3 billion euros ($25.7 billion) last year, from 5.8
billion euros in 1999. But direct investment outflows, blamed for the euro's woes, fell 81% to 23 billion euros,
the European Central Bank said.
-- The European Central Bank has outgrown its headquarters and plans to construct a new complex outside
Frankfurt's high-rise banking district by about 2006.

Fuji TV Stake to Be Sold in Public Offer
Japan's Fuji Television Network said its second-largest shareholder, Nippon Cultural Broadcasting, plans to

Cultural's planned sale of 110,000 shares in Fuji TV would be valued at 75.8 billion yen ($650 million) at

Qantas to Trim Jobs as Profit Falls 22%
Australia's Qantas Airways said it will cut 5% of its 30,000 employees and suspend some international
services, after reporting a 22% drop in fiscal first-half profit that it blamed largely on increased domestic
competition. Qantas will cut 220 management jobs within two weeks and 1,250 more jobs over six months.
The state-controlled carrier, in which British Airways holds a 25% stake, said profit for the six months ended
Dec. 31 was 262.9 million Australian dollars (US$137.9 million), above analysts' estimates of A$220 million.
Revenue rose 13.1% to A$5.1 billion. Qantas attributed the profit decline to competition from no-frills upstarts
Virgin Blue and Impulse Airlines in addition to rising fuel costs and the weak Australian dollar. Qantas said the
job cuts are expected to save the airline A$100 million a year. Qantas said it will suspend "poorly performing
international services," including all services to China and Canada.
Beijing Promises Huge Olympic Outlays
Beijing promised Olympic inspectors that it would spend $20 billion if the city is awarded the 2008 Games -- a
construction frenzy that Beijing bid officials said would rival the building of the Great Wall, state media
reported. The spending on new subways and other facilities was announced as the International Olympic
Committee's 17-member team was in the capital to assess the city's bid. Reporters weren't immediately able
to confirm the bid plans.

Is Stagflation About to Stage a Comeback?---If a Few Economists Are Correct, Fed's Moves Could Be
Economy
Is Stagflation About to Stage a Comeback? --- If a Few Economists Are Correct, Fed's Moves Could
Be
By Jon E. Hilsenrath and Greg Ip
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
1,314 words
22 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Layoff announcements are mounting. The manufacturing sector has fallen near recession territory. And now
some inflation indicators are ticking up.
Yesterday, the Department of Labor said consumer prices jumped 0.6%, seasonally adjusted, in January
from December -- the largest increase since March 2000 and much faster than expected.
Doesn't that have the whiff of stagflation?
The term is broadly defined as flagging growth tied with rising inflation. Economists haven't given it much
thought since the 1970s, when consumers lined up along suburban streets to fill their cars with high-priced
gasoline and the jobless rate hit 9%. To many, the notion of a double-barreled problem like rising prices and
high unemployment seems absurd during a period marked by a jobless rate near 4%, high productivity
growth, and the perpetual decline in the price of computers and microchips. In fact, the majority of economists
think inflation will be lower by year end and the economy will be back on a stronger growth track.
Yet a few economists say recent data hint at inflationary pressures even as the economy's growth rate is
slowing. Besides crunching the disposable income of average consumers, stagflation could handcuff the
Federal Reserve. The central bank has been fighting economic slowdown by lowering interest rates. But
some economists say an overly aggressive Fed could engender inflation.
"I'm not pleased with those numbers we saw for January," said Robert Parry, president of the Federal
Reserve Bank of San Francisco, in an appearance before the National Association of Business Economists.
January's increase in prices was led by energy, but even excluding the more volatile food and energy
components, so-called core prices were up 2.6% from a year earlier -- a notable acceleration from the 2%
core inflation during the prior year. There are other signs of rising prices. Wholesale prices were up 1.1%
from December. And earlier this month, the National Association of Purchasing Management reported that
nearly a third of its members were seeing increasing price pressure, even as its index of output slumped near
recession levels.
Economists expect the rising energy prices to partly unwind in coming months, since natural-gas prices have
fallen to half their January peak. But Tom Robinson, managing director at Cambridge Energy Research
Associates, says gas prices are unlikely to fall much further because suppliers will need to rebuild depleted
inventories for next winter, constricting supply while summer power-generation needs are likely to underpin
demand.
Paul Kasriel, an economist with Northern Trust in Chicago, says if there is stagflation this year, it will be much
milder than what was seen in the 1970s. Still, he predicts that inflation will rise faster this year and next than
the economic growth rate.
His stagflation argument hinges on two essential points.
First, he says, while the New Economy -- in the form of rising productivity and falling microchip prices -- eases
price pressures, Old Economy energy prices are negating that. Some economists like to look at inflation
excluding energy costs, since it is such a volatile sector. But energy prices have risen briskly for nearly two
years, he says, and that is too long a run to ignore. He says many American energy suppliers underinvested
in refining, exploration and electricity-generation equipment during the 1990s. Add in production restraints
from the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, and you have a real supply shock, Mr. Kasriel says.

--- Page 107 ---
Page 107 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Second, he says the Federal Reserve has exacerbated the problem in recent weeks by pumping money into
the economy. By his calculations, bank deposits and money-market funds have grown at an annual rate of
11.8% in the past 13 weeks, far outstripping the 0-to-5% money growth that prevailed during much of the
early 1990s. Many economists expect that extra money will help boost consumer demand without feeding
price increases. But Mr. Kasriel has a different view: "If the economy's ability to satisfy demand for increased
goods and services is constricted because of energy, we are going to get less real economic growth and
more inflation," Mr. Kasriel says. "We used to call that stagflation."
Greg Jensen, an analyst at Bridgewater Associates, a money-management company, says his company has
been investing in inflation-indexed bonds in recent months, because it is concerned about the
double-pronged problem of inflation and slowing growth.
Of course, these stagflation views are still deeply in the minority. In testimony to Congress last week, Mr.
Greenspan said "the effects of the rise in energy costs does not appear to have had broad inflationary effects,
in contrast to some previous episodes."
Mr. Greenspan would find comfort in the experiences of Lynn Hedell, who manages procurement for Millard
Metal Services, an Omaha, Neb., maker of equipment for industrial beef and pork processors.
Mr. Hedell says globalization has been a much bigger influence on his company than the energy crunch. His
warehouse is filled with stainless-steel sheets from Brazil, South Korea, Japan and Spain. That competition
from global suppliers has helped to drive down his costs, he says. The slowdown in the auto industry has
sapped demand for steel, further easing price pressures.
A year ago, Millard Metal was paying about $1.35 for every pound of stainless steel it purchased. It now pays
about $1. Mr. Hedell says shippers have added a surcharge for higher fuel costs, but that adds only about a
penny a pound to the cost of the steel. "Our business is remaining pretty strong," he says.
Bruce Steinberg, chief economist with Merrill Lynch and a New Economy believer, notes that the price of
gold is down 16% in the past year, hardly a sign of inflation. He adds that manufacturers are operating at
just 80% of their capacity, again a sign that there is plenty of slack in the economy. "Inflation is really one of
the last things we need to be worrying about right now," he says.
Mr. Steinberg says that the Fed didn't have a grip on managing the economy during the 1970s. Back then,
real interest rates, which take into account inflation, were consistently negative, which helped to feed
further price increases. Last year, he says, real interest rates were closer to 5%, hardly a sign of a slack
central bank.
"We're not talking about the same universe as the 1970s," Mr. Steinberg says. "The economy was
dysfunctional then."

Economy Rebounds Slightly, but Outlook Is Mixed
Economy
Economy Rebounds Slightly, but Outlook Is Mixed
By Greg Ip and Russell Gold
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
1,224 words
20 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- What the economy has to fear most, it seems, is fear itself.
Reports Friday that housing starts and manufacturing output excluding autos both rose in January confirmed
that the economy rebounded a bit last month after plunging in December -- as a cautiously optimistic Federal
Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan indicated to Congress earlier in the week.
But the big question going forward is whether January was the beginning of a true turnaround or just a pause
before a February collapse. The pessimistic scenario gained weight as consumers' moods continued to
darken in the first half of this month. The University of Michigan reported Friday that its index of consumer
sentiment plunged to 87.8 in early February, its lowest level since 1993, from 94.7 in January. Low
confidence could prompt consumers to cut spending enough to transform the fear of a recession into reality.
"The announcements of layoffs are having a pronounced negative effect on consumer confidence," said
Joseph Abate, a senior economist at Lehman Brothers. Though "the fundamentals are weakening, I don't
think their current level . . . justifies the pronounced fear that people have regarding layoffs."
In fact, the gap between consumers' assessment of current conditions and their much more pessimistic
expectations of the future is "the widest ever measured," Merrill Lynch noted. "So if their worst fears are not
realized, the sentiment index should bounce back." (The University of Michigan's index of consumer
sentiment is different from its index of customer satisfaction.)
There are signs that could happen. Helped by milder weather and falling mortgage rates, housing starts
jumped a seasonally adjusted 5.3% in January from December, the Commerce Department said.
Single-family units rose 2.8%.
The Fed reported that industrial production fell a seasonally adjusted 0.3% in January from December, its
fourth consecutive decline. But much of that was because of a 6% drop in utility output as weather returned to
seasonal norms, and because of declining automotive production. Excluding the auto and parts sector,
manufacturing output rose 0.3% after falling 0.8% in December.
"The slowdown is easing," said Gordon Richards, an economist at the National Association of Manufacturers.
But for some, it isn't easing quickly enough. Joe Darling, the 64-year-old general manager of Midwest Metal
Coatings LLC in Granite City, Ill., laid off 12 workers -- the entire second shift -- last month as customer
orders dried up. He then decided not to trade in the three-year-old Buick Century his wife drives, even though
they have bought a new car every three years for as long as he can recall.
"I'm just not going out and spending a lot of money," he says. He expects coated-steel orders to pick up by
June, but he also is concerned about the prospect of layoffs at one of his suppliers, a nearby steel plant
owned by National Steel Corp., Mishawaka, Ind.
"I don't think it's any worse than when we've had serious downturns," Mr. Darling says. "But we've been riding
along on the wave for a long time. The wave has crested."
Gene Cottini, training manager for S&C Electric Co., a closely held manufacturer of high-voltage equipment in
Chicago, says there are ample signs the worst is yet to come. "In spite of the fact that Mr. Greenspan assures
us that we aren't going into a recession, we in the manufacturing sector sure see some signs pointing in that
direction," Mr. Cottini says. He's hearing caution and pessimism on the job, and among friends and
neighbors, too. "People are taking a fallback position -- a wait and see on major purchases," he says.

--- Page 110 ---
Page 110 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Other than consumer confidence, data for February are inconclusive. For example, the Federal Reserve Bank
of Philadelphia's index of business conditions improved only to -30.5 in February, from -36.8 in January,
suggesting manufacturing in that region continues to decline, albeit at a slower rate. But companies'
expectations over the next six months turned positive on balance for the first time in three months.
Philadelphia-based Technitrol Inc. has been aggressively cutting costs to maintain profit margins as the likes
of Nortel Networks Corp. and Cisco Systems Inc. cut orders for its electronic-filtering components. "We've
seen supply running ahead of demand a lot -- that has to correct itself," said spokesman David Stakun.
"We're confident that's going to take place in the first half of the year."
While layoff announcements have continued to mount, such as by Dell Computer Corp. and Nortel Networks
last week, Mr. Abate of Lehman says layoff announcements have historically been a poor leading indicator of
employment. Initial unemployment claims actually dropped by 11,000 in the week ended Feb. 10, after rising
a similar amount the previous week.
Retail sales, which were brisk in January, are mixed so far in February, with one early survey pointing up and
another pointing down. In housing, a decline in mortgage applications in early February suggested a
moderation in demand, but a rise in the home builders' index in February from January suggests the opposite.
"I've heard rumors there is a slowdown in the economy, but I'm not seeing it," says Jerry Pittman, an
accountant and landlord in Grapevine, Texas. His accounting practice is doing well and three office buildings
he owns in the suburban Dallas are at 96% occupancy. But Mr. Pittman says he noticed that he is getting
fewer calls asking for space and some competitors aren't filling up.
Spot natural-gas prices have plunged to half their peak level of early January, though they are still double
year-earlier levels. Mr. Greenspan has cited that drop as a reason for optimism.
Lower energy prices also will help contain inflation. The Labor Department said Friday that producer prices
leapt a seasonally adjusted 1.1% in January from December, the biggest one-month increase since 1990.
Excluding food and energy, they were up an equally unexpected 0.7%. But without tobacco, where litigation
costs are pushing up prices, and autos, prices would have been up just 0.3%. Furthermore, the Fed said
Friday that industry was using just 80.2% of its capacity in January, the lowest level of utilization since 1992.
"With both labor and manufacturing capacity more readily available, price increases will be even harder to
maintain," economist James Glassman of J.P. Morgan Chase said in a report.

The Gallery: Art for Spooks and Bureaucrats
LEISURE & ARTS
The Gallery: Art for Spooks and Bureaucrats
By Matthew Gurewitsch
1,376 words
7 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Washington -- WHILE THE NATIONAL Endowment for the Arts continues on starvation rations, our
government supports artistic endeavors more than many of us realize, as I found out on a postinaugural spin
through the capital.
Case in point. Of the many facets of the Central Intelligence Agency about which the public at large remains
in the dark, the existence of the agency's Fine Arts Commission may well rank among the most surprising.
But why? The CIA headquarters in Langley, Va., over the river and through the woods from the Capitol, has
(as one might guess) its share of halls and lobbies that cry out for dressing up. Not that Jane or John Q.
Taxpayer will ever see them.
Among the permanent holdings are large, abstract canvases in dusty hues, dubiously claiming kinship with
Monet, and knockoffs of Op Art: examples (we are told) of the Washington Color School, a movement
perhaps sufficiently obscure as to qualify as a covert operation. Far more resonant is the wall engraved with
77 gold stars, each commemorating an intelligence officer killed in the line of duty (some of their identities
are still classified) since the agency's creation -- a tribute as quietly eloquent as Maya Lin's Vietnam Veterans
Memorial on the Mall.
In the company of the curator Toni Hiley, an earnest Liza Minelli look-alike attached to the Center for the
Study of Intelligence, a stroll through the makeshift gallery space of the Old Headquarters Building (corridor
1D) is more fun than a trip to the Sharper Image. Discover the "Liberator" Pistol, produced at a cost of $1.72,
and air-dropped into such theaters as occupied Europe and the Philippines for freedom fighters, who would
use them once to "liberate" the superior weapons of their enemies. Learn how to make a microdot, which
reduces a full page of type to the size of the dot over the letter "i". Marvel at the myriad ways to deploy a
Minox, and shiver at the sight of the KGB's designated assassination arsenal, unique in the world. Get wise to
the nonfluorescence of Elmer's Glue and the uses of canine pheromones. All this courtesy of an extended
loan from H. Keith Melton, historian of espionage and collector.
The atrium of the New Headquarters Building has an installation that soars: a trio of one-fifth-scale wind-test
models from Lockheed for the A-12 Blackbird, the D-21 Drone and the U-2. Painted black with a
light-swallowing matte finish, they hover overhead like silhouettes raised to the third dimension. Nearby is the
modestly scaled CIA Exhibit Center, starring such legendary spooks as Virginia Hall, whose career in World
War II was undeterred by the loss of a leg. (Her dispatches on occasion made reference to Cuthbert, her
wooden leg.) And then there is the irrepressible, retired Col. William H. Pietsch, Jr., an architect of the U.S.
Special Forces, who at 21 crisscrossed enemy lines in Burgundy six times in a requisitioned Nazi staff
vehicle. Upon viewing his very own showcase, he had this to say: "When they display your socks, you know
you've had an interesting life."
"Have you seen the National Cryptologic Museum at the National Security Agency in Fort Meade?" Ms. Hiley
asks. "There's a wonderful collection of cryptographic equipment there, and it's open to the public."
Maybe next time. My next stop was the Federal Reserve, whose Board of Governors has a curator on staff,
too. She is Mary Anne Goley, whose temperament strikes a mischievous balance between enthusiasm and
irony. A scholar of 19th-century American painting, she has, somewhat improbably, already logged a quarter
century at the Fed, roaming far beyond her specialty. Much of the art in her care is parked in private offices
and meeting rooms, but she superintends public exhibits, too. The one she just has finished hanging, on view
through April 13, consists of American works on paper from the collection of Philip Brewer, M.D., ranging from
a magnificent Colonial portrait of one Thomas Hillen of Baltimore by Charles Saint-Memin (1770-1852) to the
sensitive autumn landscape "Hudson Highlands" by Jasper Cropsey (1823-1900) and the calligraphic "Study
for Tanktotems" by David Smith (1906-1965).

--- Page 112 ---
Page 112 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some collectors prize drawings and watercolors for their spontaneity, for their proximity to the thought of the
artist in the act of seeing. In that spirit, Dr. Brewer cites "the reliable expression of feeling and intent of the
artist" as the special attraction of drawings and watercolors; yet most of his purchases exhibit the finish and
perfection long associated with oil paintings. The best of them may be seen at the Federal Reserve Board
Gallery in the Eccles Building, at C street between 20th and 21st. (You will have to pass security, but
between 11 a.m. and 2 p.m. no appointment is required.) Meanwhile, a previous show of Ms. Goley's, "Money
Making: The Fine Art of Currency at the Millennium," has been picked up by the Trust for Museum
Exhibitions, in Washington, D.C., which will send it on tour in the fall. It is worth watching for. Among the
highlights are Steven D. Gagnon's and Ray Beldner's beautiful, and markedly dissimilar, flags made from
dollar bills. Barton Lidici Benes and Howard Meyer's $1 and $10 bills shredded and then strung back together
on a warp of thread are mesmerizing, too.
Scarcely more frequented by the public than the Fed, though a good deal more convenient, are certain art
oases on the Mall. At the Smithsonian's mostly underground National Museum of African Art, 950
Independence Avenue S.W., the only other souls you may encounter of an afternoon are those of the security
guards -- and of the mostly anonymous artists. Through Feb. 19, a fine show by the contemporary Egyptian
artist Chant Avedissian, whose large-scale stenciled and painted "scrolls" on cardboard -- part
Rauschenberg, part Sigmar Polke -- aim to perpetuate vibrations of a vanishing Egypt. An adjacent
workshop, equipped with colored paper, stencils, rubber stamps and pens, invites viewers of all ages to turn
the artist's techniques to their own purposes. From the sheets tacked up on the wall, they have been doing so
with remarkable freedom. (In the morning hours, it appears, the galleries are packed.)
The permanent installation "Images of Power and Identity," drawn from the collection and as lonesome as the
tomb, runs the gamut from archetypal, wholly figurative examples of mother and child to cult objects such as
a divination basket filled with bric-a-brac of enigmatic purpose. A shrine figure from Nigeria, no more than a
bundle of twigs and pieces of carved wood (including a pipe), is just one of the displays that stands in its case
emanating an aura of dark lightning, packed and set to strike at a clap of a shaman's hand.
Even the National Gallery of Art has its secret recesses, notably within its superlative and surprisingly unsung
20th-century galleries in I.M. Pei's East Building. A roomful of choice early Picasso canvases sits undisturbed
at a dead end on the second floor. Small, single-artist groups of the sculpture of Alberto Giacometti, Louise
Bourgeois and Constantin Brancusi give off a charge of mystery akin to those of the Smithsonian's sacred
pieces from Africa. But most magical of all is the gallery wholly occupied by the sculpture of Alexander
Calder, another dead end, this time on the concourse level. Modest in footprint but towering in height, closed
off by a back wall like a cyclorama, it is a chapel to the sheer play of the imagination. At the center, a little
menagerie sits on a dais, like smiling totems. Behind hangs a large, abstract piece, bold and radiant. In
niches, on the floor, on the walls, and suspended from above is an assemblage of stable and mobile
Calderiana spanning the spectrum of the artist's creation, at once impish and full of grace. Pinpoint spotlights
cast shadows that trace the walls like climbing vines in an enchantress's garden. If human skill can make
matter divine, here it has been done.

Coin Operation: Mint Sees Gold In Safeway Inc.
Coin Operation: Mint Sees Gold In Safeway Inc.
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,212 words
7 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
When was the last time a store gave you one of the new $1 coins the U.S. government began minting last
year? In fact, have you ever seen one?
For many Americans, the answer is rarely, if ever. Even though 700 million of the gold-colored coins
supposedly are in circulation, as many as 600 million are being hoarded by collectors.
It is a sore spot for the U.S. Mint. So what is the Mint's solution, considering that the government has been
unwilling to simply ban the paper dollar and force Americans to rely on the new coins?
The Mint's answer: Save us, Safeway!
Later this week, the Mint -- on the one-year anniversary of the coin's release -- will announce its latest
marketing effort to increase actual use of the coins. It plans to distribute 1.6 million so-called Golden Dollar
coins via Safeway Inc.'s more than 1,500 supermarkets. This comes after a similarly structured deal with
Wal-Mart Stores Inc. failed to establish the coin as conventional pocket change. In an announcement
yesterday, the Mint said the Safeway agreement is its way of "countering speculation that the new Golden
Dollar is not circulating."
On its face, the deal seems like small change. After all, the Pleasanton, Calif., supermaket chain is ordering a
relatively small number of coins compared with the total in circulation. But Mint officials are betting that
consumers already have hoarded all the coins they want, and that introducing the batch of new coins at one
of the nation's biggest food-and-drug retailers will make a noticeable difference.
The curious thing is that the $1 coins, which bear the likeness of Native American explorer Sacagawea, are
hard to come by even though they already are circulated by more than 500 businesses with a combined total
of 225,000 locations. Consumers may go months without seeing one, and then get one out of the blue from a
newsstand operator as change. Even a Safeway spokeswoman, asked if the coins have already filtered into
the chain's registers via customers' wallets, said that undoubtedly has happened, but "not in significant
numbers."
A year ago, the Mint ruffled some feathers when it launched the Golden Dollar (which isn't gold, of course; it
is made of manganese and other industrial metals) exclusively via Wal-Mart and that company's Sam's Club
stores. Problems started when the stores together ordered 94 million coins that reached consumers' hands
quicker than the simultaneous shipments the Mint sent to the Federal Reserve, which then trickled into local
banks. At the time, some tellers at banks that didn't yet have the coins were chagrined to find they had to
send customers to the local Wal-Mart to get the coins they wanted.
Mint officials say consumers have saved six times as many coins as they have spent. That means only about
100 million of the 700 million circulating $1 coins are actually being used to pay for bus fares, sodas, parking
meters and other things.
The U.S. government could give the coin a boost by eliminating the paper dollar. That is what Britain did with
its pound and Canada with its dollar. But jettisoning the paper dollar, U.S. government officials fear, would be
politically unpopular among citizens who like the tradition of the greenback, of which there are about 7.5
billion in circulation.
According to the Mint, the real problem is that the American public loves the coin too much, based on its
aesthetics. So anyone who gets one squirrels it away; they are popular as keepsake presents for children, for
example.
"What is cool about this is people love the design and love keeping it," said John Mitchell, deputy director of
the Mint. "The partnership with Safeway is the next big step toward getting people to use it in their everyday

--- Page 114 ---
Page 114 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
transactions. Hopefully, we'll reach a point where it's not even treated as such a novelty, just something
people are used to using everyday."
The Mint gets a marketing boost from deals like the Safeway one; the store will post signs promoting the new
dollar. After their initial purchases, the stores will restock the golden Sacagawea coins at their local banks
depending on demand, Mint officials said. "We think the customers will enjoy using and receiving the coins,"
said Safeway spokeswoman Debra Lambert. "Obviously, we also think it's going to be a nice draw to the
stores."
The Mint insists that, aside from the everyday-use problem, the new coin has been a success, by its
measure. The Mint says the one billion coins it has struck so far, including 300 million in reserve, reflect more
demand than it experienced for the unpopular Susan B. Anthony dollar. In 21 years, the Mint produced only
920 million of those coins, Mr. Mitchell said. "We've taken polls and found that we've got a 90% recognition
rate among consumers for the Golden Dollar," he said. "That means our coin and our marketing of it are
getting the message out."
Of course, it also means that even after a full year, one in 10 Americans still don't recognize a new
incarnation of their country's basic unit of currency. Mint officials concede that is because many still haven't
received a Sacagawea dollar in change after making an everyday purchase.
"What makes our decision-making process different is that in most countries, coinage decisions are made by
the central bank, which is supposed to be apolitical," said James C. Benfield, executive director of the Coin
Coalition, an industry association that favors eliminating the greenback. "But in our system, those decisions
are made by Congress, and changing that would take a constitutional amendment, which is difficult."
Thus, the new coin is left to fight for its place in the nation's pocketbooks. Right now, it has niches of ardent
fans. Franchisee Jim Opitz distributes only Sacagawea dollars as change at two Subway sandwich shops he
owns in Wisconsin. He said the stores, which inadvertently used to give away hundreds of dollars in
greenbacks that stuck together, overpaid less than $10 that way last year using coins.
Mr. Opitz said it is difficult to gauge the amount of additional sales the coins are generating, although three or
four times a week someone will stop in primarily to buy as much as $50 in Sacagawea dollars. That is
particularly true at his Madison store, situated near Interstate 94, a bingo hall and a McDonald's restaurant.
He said drivers and players used to patronize the McDonald's more before his store's reputation spread as
someplace people could get the dollar coins.
"I've never had someone come in and buy some dollars and not want to buy something else, too," he said.

Kangaroo Pounces on Europe to FosterFuzzy Feelings About Common Currency
Kangaroo Pounces on Europe to Foster Fuzzy Feelings About Common Currency
By G. Thomas Sims
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
693 words
6 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
B15A
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
FRANKFURT -- It has been hard getting Europeans to warm up to the euro. Maybe a cuddly mascot can
help.
One group thinks so and even thinks it has found the perfect animal: an obscure breed of kangaroo also
called the euro.
Euro-Point e.V., an association of German banks that plugs the euro, is bringing seven euro kangaroos to a
zoo near Frankfurt, an effort, the group says, to bring the fledgling currency "into the hearts of the people."
If Euro-Point has its way, the fecund beasts -- whose name has an aboriginal origin and is unrelated to the
euro currency -- will end up in zoos across the European continent.
Stuffed euro kangaroos, decked out in European Union blue and yellow, are already here. The 18-centimeter
dolls come complete with a pouch carrying a chocolate one-euro coin in a gold wrapper. Euro-Point is selling
them for as little as 4.75 euros ($3.75) each if you order 10,000.
European Central Bank President Wim Duisenberg already has his. Euro-Point dropped off a bunch of the
plush kangaroos at the ECB last week. The white-haired president took one home that evening -- partly
because the ECB is tight on space, the bank said.
Mr. Duisenberg wouldn't comment on the appropriateness of the kangaroo as a symbol for the euro, but
Euro-Point hopes to get him to pose next to a stuffed kangaroo.
"The kangaroo has a very special symbolic power," says Manfred Pohl, director of Euro-Point. Mr. Pohl didn't
mention that the Australian dollar slid even more than the euro last year.
Two male and five female kangaroos are set to arrive in April at the Opel Zoo. Each will be named after a
euro-zone country. It isn't yet decided which of the 12 euro countries will be left out.
But since the animals reproduce quickly, it won't be long before the herd exceeds the number of
euro-member nations, even if eastern European countries join the common-currency club. The Opel Zoo is
banking on other European zoos adopting the excess offspring.
"We hope the increase won't be inflationary," says Ulrich Groeschel, head of the management board staff at
Helaba, a Frankfurt bank sponsoring Euro-Point.
Thomas Kauffels, director of the Opel Zoo, is thankful the euro is an animal with positive connotations. "After
all, it could have been a poisonous snake, and the project probably wouldn't have worked," Mr. Kauffels says.
Luckily, euro kangaroos are very adaptable and should have no problem acclimating to Frankfurt, though it
might be too cold in Finland, which also is in the euro zone.
This isn't the first time the ECB's gotten suggestions for mascots. Lex Hoogduin, Mr. Duisenberg's top
adviser, wasn't thrilled to once receive a toy emu, another animal from Australia. But the emu buries its head
in the sand, hardly fitting the image the bank wants, says Mr. Hoogduin. "Personally, I would hope that people
could also find symbols a little bit closer to the euro area," he says.
Publicis Groupe's Publicis SA, an ad agency handling the ECB's own 80 million euro campaign, considered
coming up with a mascot but feared any one symbol would project different images in the 12 euro countries.

--- Page 116 ---
Page 116 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Although the ECB hasn't embraced the kangaroo concept, it did go along with with Euro-Point's last euro
campaign. The group placed a thousand 1-meter-tall (3.3 feet) euro coins around Frankfurt. The ECB's staid
17-member council even agreed to a photo shoot with the enormous coins.
The euro kangaroo isn't even that well known to average Australians. David Richardson, an Australian
software specialist who lives in Frankfurt, knows the eastern gray kangaroo, the red and even the wallaroo,
but has never heard of the euro.
Australians aren't all that crazy about kangaroos, either. They are a national symbol, Mr. Richardson says,
but farmers think they are pests.

Does Sharon Still Hold a Winning Hand?---He Triumphed in WarsBut Faces a New Israel,The Stakes Piled High
International
Does Sharon Still Hold a Winning Hand? --- He Triumphed in Wars But Faces a New Israel, The Stakes
Piled High
By Wall Street Journal reporters Stephen J. Glain in Jerusalem, Hugh Pope in Istanbul, Turkey, and Neil King
Jr. in Washington
1,568 words
6 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Israelis today are widely expected to elect as their leader for an uncertain future a man from a world that no
longer exists.
As much as any living Israeli, Ariel Sharon is a hard-boiled product of the Cold War, an era in which the Arab
states were allied in their aggression toward Israel and their military might was leveraged by support from the
Soviet Union. When Sharon the tank commander was leading armored columns to spectacular success in
Israel's wars of survival, the Jewish state lived and worked on a constant war footing and had a statist
economy sustained by agrarian and industrial collectives.
The Israel Mr. Sharon is expected to command after today -- polls show him with a 17%-21% lead over
incumbent Ehud Barak -- is a regional military power. In mute recognition of Israeli supremacy, no Arab
leader has put his forces on alert despite four months of instability in the Middle East. Israel has the world's
20th-largest economy, owing to its robust high-tech sector, and its world-class corporations are working to
overturn union and regulatory control of traditional industries.
Mr. Barak's missteps and the Palestinian uprising begun in September, however, have opened the way for
the 73-year-old hard-liner to take the helm. Many voters remain undecided, and Israeli polls are particularly
unreliable, so a Barak victory can't be ruled out altogether, yet his chances are slim.
Though few analysts actually fear for the survival of the Jewish state, the violence in the West Bank and
Gaza has seeped into Israel proper, where a growing number of Israelis have died in ambushes on roads or
in shops. Instinctively, Israeli voters appear to have turned to yesterday's man. "We're retreating to
pre-peace-treaties norm, focusing solely on immediate security needs," says Menachem Feder, a commercial
lawyer in Tel Aviv.
In the Arab states, where Mr. Sharon is deeply disliked, leaders are keeping a nervous eye on their people as
well as Mr. Sharon, and businessmen have all but given up hope for a "peace dividend." In Washington,
President George W. Bush's top advisers met twice last week to discuss the region and possible reactions to
a Sharon victory, and have called upon Arab officials to urge them not to overreact if the Likud Party hawk
wins. "We are urging restraint, not just in action but also in word," an administration official says.
Mr. Sharon's supporters say the fact that he has campaigned as one who can bring peace to the Middle East
suggests he believes at least that the Jewish state can co-exist with its neighbors. Mr. Sharon's party, they
add, has signed more peace agreements with Arab governments than have Mr. Barak's Laborites. They say
their man's persistent calls for a unity government, which Mr. Barak says he would never join, show he is
serious about working together with rival factions.
Sharon the tactician may indeed be able to deliver a peace agreement where Mr. Barak, who seems never to
have found his footing in Israel's labyrinthine politics, failed. Mr. Sharon "has been involved in politics long
enough to know that compromise is necessary," says Meir Rosenne, an ex-ambassador and lawyer and aide
to Mr. Sharon. "But there can be no talks while Israelis are under fire."
Mr. Rosenne says he was at the Israeli-Egyptian peace talks at Camp David in 1979 when Mr. Sharon
phoned then-Israeli prime minister Menachem Begin and guaranteed he would help dismantle Jewish
settlements on the Sinai Peninsula. Mr. Sharon's support, Mr. Rosenne notes, made the Israel-Egypt peace
accord possible.
The Sharon camp also emphasizes its commitment to economic reform. As the head of several economics
ministries, Mr. Sharon was an early proponent of privatization, a process that has sputtered under Mr. Barak.

--- Page 118 ---
Page 118 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Zalman Shoval, an important foreign-affairs adviser to the Sharon team, says a Sharon regime would step up
the sale of state assets and reduce interest rates to spur economic growth.
Mr. Sharon may be attuned to the new global economy, but it isn't clear that he has yet come to grips with the
Middle East's new geopolitics. Israeli Deputy Defense Minister Ephraim Sneh says Mr. Sharon will offer Mr.
Arafat something he knows the Palestinian leader cannot accept. Conceivably, this course could ultimately
provoke Arab states and Iran into confronting Israel with weapons of mass destruction, the only resource in
their outdated arsenals that still seriously concerns Israeli military planners.
Even allies of Mr. Sharon say many of the issues preventing a peace deal are insoluble. "Israel made
unprecedented compromises at Camp David [in July] and was given an insurrection as a thank-you note,"
says Dore Gold, a former ambassador to the United Nations who is widely expected to occupy a senior
foreign-ministry post under a Sharon regime. "The diplomatic gaps are unbridgeable."
Arab mass media have been trumpeting dire scenarios -- hardly inspiring for Arab businessmen, who have
spent the last half-decade in purgatory waiting for an end to the Arab-Israeli conflict and the dawn of
economic renewal. "It's the end of the peace process, and the reconstruction of Lebanon depends on peace,"
says Kamal Shehadi, a Beirut privatization consultant.
A Sharon government would pose different challenges to various Arab governments, depending on their
proximity to the Jewish state. Egypt, cushioned by a pacified Sinai, is comfortable with the `cold peace' it has
had with Israel since the 1979 Camp David accords.
Jordan, whose peace treaty was signed only in 1994, is much more closely entangled in the conflict; at least
half of its population is of Palestinian origin, and Mr. Sharon himself has talked in the past of Jordan's being
the real Palestine -- a direct challenge to Jordan's ruling Hashemite dynasty and its embrace of the peace
process. Tensions between Jordan's ethnic Palestinian and Jordanian populations are already on the rise.
Several months ago, a soccer team's rowdy fans were disciplined for chanting slogans disrespectful of the
king's Palestinian wife.
A Sharon administration could put other Arab regimes in a similar bind. Any impulsive escalation of the
conflict by Mr. Sharon could well widen the gap between the angry citizens of the Arab `street' and the often
authoritarian rulers who know that any move toward outright conflict with Israel's army would be disastrous.
The fear of a more aggressive Israel under Mr. Sharon is also shifting regional alliances in the Middle East,
buoyed by growing irritation with U.S. support for Israel, in the normally less antagonistic Arab states of the
Persian Gulf.
One result of the new polarities, directly counter to the stated desires of the incoming Bush administration in
Washington, has been to speed the regional rehabilitation of Iraq. Baghdad has portrayed itself as the
standard-bearer of Palestinian rights, and its flag is prominently displayed in the streets of the West Bank and
Gaza. When Israel warned Syria in recent months against attempts to assist Palestinian resistance groups,
Iraqi-Syrian relations warmed further.
Syria refuses to make peace with Israel until the Jewish state returns the Golan Heights it captured in 1967.
Syrians were angered last March when their late leader, Hafez al-Assad, met President Clinton in a Geneva
summit in expectation of a peace deal but came back empty-handed. So long as Syria remains unhappy, it
can bank on continued support from Iran. "Iran became much softer when Israel started talking to Assad,"
says Mohammad Haydar, editor of the independent Iranian newsmagazine Gozaresh. "Now it has gone back
to its previous position."
In Lebanon, where Mr. Sharon is identified with a 1982 massacre of Palestinian refugees, a Sharon victory
would play into the hands of the Muslim militia Hezbollah, which last spring forced Israeli troops to evacuate
almost all of southern Lebanon and is regarded as a model by Palestinian protesters. "Israel will pay the
price" of a Sharon victory, Lebanese Prime Minister Rafik al-Hariri said at a ceremony to reopen al-Jamhour,
a power station on the outskirts of Beirut destroyed three times by Israeli bombardment and rebuilt each time.
Officials in Washington say they don't expect Mr. Sharon to do anything provocative in his first months in
office, but they acknowledge they are concerned that rising street violence or terrorist attacks could inspire a
swift Israeli crackdown. Absent a major confrontation, the Bush administration has made it clear it will assume
a less conspicuous mediating role in the Middle East than did Bill Clinton, who hurled himself into the peace
process in his final months in office -- to the detriment of regional stability, some argue.
Secretary of State Colin Powell said in a television interview over the weekend that he wants to take a
broader approach to the region, firming up friendships in the Arab world. "We will always be committed to the
security of Israel," he said on Sunday, "but we also will be anxious to see what we could do in every way
possible to achieve the legitimate aspirations of the Palestinian people."

Mutual Funds Are Gaining Currency in Asia
Fund Track
Mutual Funds Are Gaining Currency in Asia
By Sarah McBride
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
755 words
6 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C25
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The biggest deal about the mutual-fund business in Asia is what a little deal it is. Across the continent, only
minuscule numbers of people invest in funds -- 8% of the population in Hong Kong, 10% in Japan and 14% in
Singapore. But that could change soon.
In the U.S., household savings are actually negative, as investors have been pulling money out of the bank to
spend or to invest. That wasn't always true. American households, half of which now invest in mutual funds,
used to keep a lot more money in the bank until runaway inflation in the 1970s broke them of the habit.
Better rates on money-market accounts got investors thinking in terms of mutual funds; from there, it was an
easy step to move cash into stock funds when the bull market of the 1990s came along.
"If you take the U.S. or Canada or the U.K., people there have been used to trusting institutions for decades,"
says Stuart Leckie, chairman of pension consultants Woodrow Milliman China. Savings rates are high in Asia,
ranging from 27% of gross domestic product in Taiwan to 52% in Singapore. Aside from their banks, Mr.
Leckie says, Asians tend not to feel so comfortable with financial institutions.
Not for long, he predicts. "Ten years from now, I think the mutual-fund industry in literally every country in
Asia will be much larger and more significant."
When they do invest, Asians like to pick the stocks themselves, but that attitude, too, may be changing. In
Hong Kong, almost 8% of adults now invest in funds, compared with 4% four years ago. Younger people and
those educated overseas are increasingly willing to pay for investment advice. "People don't have time to
manage their own portfolio," says Stephen Cheung Yan-leung, a finance professor at City University of Hong
Kong.
High fees in Asia have tended to put a damper on sales, hovering around 5% for an initial sales charge, and
1.5% yearly in management fees. Compare that with the U.S., where nearly a third of funds have no sales
commission, and yearly management and promotion fees are about 1.3%.
But fees are coming down in Asia. In Taiwan, for example, which with 1.4 million locally registered fund
holders has more than most other Asian countries, sales fees are closer to 3%. In another big market, Japan,
Fidelity Investments Ltd. says it also charges a 3% sales fee, in line with local practice. Citigroup Inc.'s
Citibank, one of Asia's largest fund distributors, is in the process of launching a line of funds that charge no
sales fee.
In addition, investors are starting to appreciate the diversification funds provide, says Jackie Kestenbaum, a
Fidelity spokeswoman in Japan. Fidelity's biggest funds in Japan contain about 120 companies each, she
explains. "People here realize it's hard" to find 120 good companies, she says, so "it's worth it to hire a
professional manager to do it for you." Besides, she says, "Think of what you'd be paying in [brokerage] fees"
if you went out and bought 120 stocks on your own.
Some Asian government moves could provide growth opportunities for the mutual-fund industry. Singapore's
Central Provident Fund, a mandatory retirement-savings program, allows its citizens to invest their CPF
money in mutual funds, introducing many people to a vehicle they might not otherwise have tried. Hong
Kong's equivalent, the just-launched Mandatory Provident Fund, also includes a wide range of mutual funds.
Another big incentive for mutual-fund investing is poor returns elsewhere. Traditional investments, such as
property, gold, or simply stashing money in the bank haven't done well during the past few years. Property in
Asia hasn't come close to recovering to 1997 levels, gold has been drifting downward since the late 1980s to
around $266 per ounce, and interest rates on regular bank deposits show no signs of picking up.

--- Page 121 ---
Page 121 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In Japan, for example, the value of assets in unit trusts declined 3.8% to just short of $425 billion at the end of
2000, due to stock-market declines and redemptions at money-market funds. Still, there were large inflows
into bond and stock funds -- 715 billion yen and 54 billion yen ($6.17 billion and $4.7 million), respectively, in
December alone. To fund managers, that is extremely encouraging.
