Cattle Futures Jump as Mad-Cow Fears Wane
Commodities
Cattle Futures Jump as Mad-Cow Fears Wane
By Daniel Rosenberg and Shella Calamba
Dow Jones Newswires
775 words
2 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
CHICAGO -- Cattle prices shot up the trading limit at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, posting two-week
highs as concern about bovine spongiform encephalopathy, or mad-cow disease, faded.
The market also got a lift from frosty weather forecasts and from steady cash prices. But the fading of BSE
fears, which had haunted the market for two weeks, played the biggest role in the rally.
Nearby February futures climbed the 1.5-cent daily limit to 79.05 cents a pound, the highest close since Jan.
18.
"The industry was thinking that the recent flurry of mad-cow stories had pretty much run its course," said Dan
Vaught, analyst at A.G. Edwards & Sons in St. Louis. "Unless some major event happens, the media isn't
going to have much to focus on as far as mad-cow in the U.S. goes."
Last week, the market sagged after the U.S. Food & Drug Administration quarantined a feedlot in Texas.
Cattle there had been fed a mixture of bone-and-meat meal banned in the U.S. The quarantine, along with
the rash of cases in Europe, made headlines across the country, and analysts and traders feared it would
dent beef demand. Nearby Chicago Mercantile Exchange cattle futures fell nearly 5% from their January high.
But the FDA later said none of the Texas cattle tested positive. BSE has never appeared in the U.S., and
many in the industry are confident it never will. Beef demand soared in recent months despite the scare in
Europe, a signal that U.S. consumers aren't too concerned about a crisis.
BSE wasn't the only factor in the rally. U.S. weather played a major role, traders and analysts said.
Temperatures fell below zero Fahrenheit in parts of Iowa, Nebraska and the Dakotas at midday yesterday.
Although readings stayed in the 30s and 40s in Kansas and Oklahoma, a drop into the teens had been
forecast for last night as far south as the Texas panhandle. Traders feared the cold could stress animals that
already have been through a lot this winter.
"Cattle quality is probably as poor as it's been in years," said Dave Maher, president of consulting firm Dave
Maher & Associates in Ely, Iowa. "Cattle are already in bad shape, so this cold won't help them any."
Cattle can die from the cold. Another less dramatic consequence is failure to thrive, marked by weight loss.
When cattle weigh less, they yield less beef.
The closely watched Texas cash market remained quiet through the session. Last week, most cattle there
traded at 78 cents a pound, and packing firms, which buy cattle from feedlots, reduced their bids early this
week. But cash turned steady in Nebraska at midday, giving the futures market a boost, traders said. They
began hoping for higher prices in the south, and feedlot owners there vowed to hang tough for a price of at
least 79 cents.
The steady cash came despite a declining beef market. Beef is down about eight cents from January's record
highs. Some analysts say beef prices fell due to a slowing U.S. economy. When people are laid off, or have
to accept smaller raises, they are less likely to go out for steak. But others say a downturn won't hurt the
market.
"The cattle market historically does well during a recession," said Glen Ring, president of Glen Ring
Enterprises LLC in Cedar Falls, Iowa. If people buy their meat at a grocery store rather than at a restaurant,
he said, they can get more beef for their money, resulting in a bigger volume of beef sales.
In other commodity markets:

--- Page 123 ---
Page 123 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
GOLD: Futures rose due to short covering, the buying of futures to balance out previous sales, by
speculative commodity funds. That buying was spurred by a humbled U.S. dollar after the Federal Reserve
Board's interest-rate cuts Wednesday, traders said. The April contract gained $2.70 to $270.70 a troy ounce.
SUGAR: World raw-sugar futures rallied on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange after the active March
contract broke through what traders saw as a signifcant level. Support also came from renewed demand in
the physical market and expectations of more as Asian nations return to the market after the New Year
celebrations. The March contract added 0.3 cent to 10.25 cents a pound, reached after rising above the key
level of 10.12 to 10.13 cents, where more buying came into the market.

The Fed (Finally) Picks Up the Tempo
The Fed (Finally) Picks Up the Tempo
By Brian S. Wesbury
1,076 words
1 February 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The shock and dismay at the rapid slide in the U.S. economy is palpable. Layoffs have accelerated
dramatically and the Federal Reserve's full percentage point reduction in the federal-funds rate in January --
with the latest installment coming yesterday -- seems almost a sign of panic.
Fed maestro Alan Greenspan has been in his post since August 1987 and his preferred tempo has always
been adagio. But in the past month he's accelerated to presto. The Fed has not cut rates this fast since
August 1982, when the U.S. economy was in the middle of the worst recession since the 1930s.
In this sense, January's rate cuts are amazing. They also raise some serious questions: Were they
necessary? Are things really that bad?
The answers are yes and no, respectively. Yes, dramatic rate cuts were necessary because the Fed raised
interest rates too far and held them there too long. But no, even though I believe the economy entered a
recession in the fourth quarter, things are not as bad as they were in 1982.
As Alan Reynolds and I wrote on this page last October, the Fed often misses the onset of recessions and
begins to cut rates too late. This is largely because it focuses on economic data such as real gross domestic
product growth, consumer confidence, layoffs and unemployment. These lagging indicators of economic
activity reflect recessions after the fact.
For example, just 10 weeks ago the Fed issued a statement saying that "the easing of demand pressures has
not been sufficient" to reduce pressures on inflation. The Fed kept its tightening bias in place because it
thought the risks of inflation were greater than the risks of recession.
Most economists, including many at major corporations, walked the Fed line and assumed the slowdown in
production was due to an inventory correction that would soon end. But they misread the economy. In
August, the National Association of Purchasing Management's manufacturing index fell below 50, a sign of
contraction. It has remained there for six consecutive months.
In addition, commodity and gold prices, the yield curve, the real federal funds rate and the value of the dollar
all suggested that the Fed was too tight and that a severe slowdown was on the way. Unfortunately, these
are supply-side indicators of monetary policy and are mostly ignored by the popular press.
As a result, the precipitous decline in U.S. economic activity caught a lot of people off guard. Now that layoffs
have surged, many have tried to make the case that something new is happening. A Newsweek cover story
this week suggests that corporations are, "preemptively jettisoning workers, before it's even clear the
slowdown will be long or deep."
Anxiety over the economy is understandable given that we've avoided a recession for 114 consecutive
months -- the longest expansion in U.S. history. While I have yet to meet a new economy supporter who
suggested the business cycle was dead, many investors and business leaders acted as though this were the
case.
As a result, companies hired too many workers and ignored the real shift the new era wrought. Today,
corporate America faces competition from new technologies and globalization. This is eroding profit margins
and leading to the most intensely competitive environment in U.S. history.
While the majority of Americans keep looking for inflation, new economy forces are creating deflation and
creative destruction is sweeping across corporate America. In the information age, news travels fast and
markets react at lightening speed. But the transformation of an entire economy from the industrial age to the
information age takes a long time and brings with it wrenching change.

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
For most of the long boom, the creative side of the equation was able to offset the destructive side. Over
time, these forces will tend to balance out and find equilibrium. But because the Fed was unwilling to let the
economy run, it engineered a slowdown that is now accentuating the destruction and slowing creation.
By knocking down stock prices, the Fed was attempting to level the playing field between new-era and
old-era firms. But tight monetary policy is an equal-opportunity destroyer. LTV Steel, Chrysler, General
Motors and Sara Lee have been hurt in the same way as Lucent, Motorola and the dot-coms.
As well, the drying up of initial public offerings and difficulties in the junk-bond market are signs of stress in
the new economy that signal a reduction in risk-taking. When incentives for risk-taking decline so does
economic growth, which hurts all players, not just the high-risk ones.
In addition to an overly aggressive Fed, there are two other factors that have created today's recession --
taxes and regulation. Each has crept onto the economic scene like a stealth bomber. Taxes have climbed
due to productivity-driven bracket creep and are now the highest, as a proportion of income, in more than 50
years. The resulting surpluses are a sign that government is taking too much from the private sector.
Regulation has also crept in. Last year the number of pages in the Federal Register rose to 83,000, the
second-highest total after the 87,000 pages of 1980. Environmental regulations are the root cause of the
energy crisis in California, and telecommunications regulations and antitrust actions are inhibiting the
proliferation of broadband Internet access.
The good news is that despite the recognition lag, the economy will come out of this mess in great shape.
With the Fed now correcting its mistake and the Bush administration moving rapidly on tax cuts and
regulatory rollbacks, the recession should be over by the fourth quarter of this year and the stock market will
be back on track for big gains in the years ahead.
However, the transformation toward the information age will continue. In 1973, manufacturing jobs made up
26% of overall employment; by last year that share was down to 14%. This trend will continue as creative
destruction runs its course. Sometimes this trend will come with much pain, as during the spate of recent
layoffs. But more often it will come in the smooth fashion that was behind the long boom of the 1990s.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens & Thompson in Chicago and author of "The New
Era of Wealth" (McGraw-Hill, 1999).

Markets Rally on Expectation of Fed Rate Cut---The L-U-V Connection:How Economy `Shapes Up'Presents Risks for Investors
Heard on the Street
Markets Rally on Expectation of Fed Rate Cut --- The L-U-V Connection: How Economy `Shapes Up'
Presents Risks for Investors
By Ken Brown
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,235 words
31 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
If everyone expects a V, what happens if we get a U? Or worse, the dreaded L?
Many investors and most Wall Street firms are firmly in the V camp, believing the economy is falling sharply
but will bounce like a ping-pong ball and head back up strongly in the second half of the year, producing a
V-shaped recovery. They believe the Federal Reserve, by beginning what appears to be a series of rate cuts,
has staved off a lengthy downturn.
So it shouldn't be surprising that the Nasdaq Composite Index is up nearly 15% so far this year. Markets
generally anticipate events, whether for the overall economy or for specific companies. Stocks dropped last
year well before the economy really slowed down.
This can be a risky game, though. If the market has priced in a soft landing and quick rebound, what happens
if the economy lingers at the bottom for a while, the so-called U-shaped recovery? Or how will things play out
if the economy stalls altogether -- the ugly L-shaped slowdown -- as it did at the beginning of the decade?
Either scenario could leave the market high and dry with no place to go but down.
For investors, a V-shaped move means buy now, and buy cyclical stocks that will benefit from a rising
economy, sectors such as capital goods, retailing and technology. If the trajectory of the economy forms a U
or an L, defensive stocks such as drug makers and consumer-goods manufacturers, whose earnings hold up
in a slowdown, are the place to be. Those are the stocks that soared in last year's fourth quarter but have
slipped so far this year as investors anticipate an economic recovery.
"In my view, the call of this year is, is this a V recovery . . . or do we have a long wait?" says James Paulsen,
chief investment officer at Wells Capital Management. He adds, "Since the Fed eased [rates on Jan. 3], I see
a situation where sentiment has coalesced together to say we will be out of this by the summer, but the
economy hasn't said anything yet."
Others say the economy has been giving hints that a rebound isn't far off, but warn that improvement may not
be apparent for several months. Impatient investors could send the market on a wild ride as they peer
through the mists for some confirmation that economic growth has resumed.
Here are some of the signs that analysts are watching to gauge the health of the economy:
GOLD RUSH: Mr. Paulsen, who remains worried that a recovery could take longer than most people believe,
says the fact that commodity prices didn't rebound after the Fed cut rates by half a percentage point on Jan.
3, is worrying. Normally commodities such as gold, copper and lumber surge during an economic rebound
because people believe demand will rise for these products, sending up prices. But that hasn't happened.
"The commodity market ain't buying an economic recovery," Mr. Paulsen says. A rise in commodity prices
would give him more confidence that a recovery is at hand.
PINK SLIPS: Mr. Paulsen also looks at the weekly unemployment-claims numbers, which give a quick
snapshot of the labor market, rather than the monthly employment reports, which tend to tell you how the
market was last month. Weekly jobless claims have declined in recent weeks, though some high-profile
layoffs may send that number up again.
BOND BLUES: Elizabeth MacKay, chief investment strategist at Bear Stearns, sees signals in the markets
themselves. "Just as the market recovered very quickly, the economy could recover fairly quickly," she says.
She looks at the spread between interest rates charged to low-risk and high-risk borrowers. That gap had
been huge last year as investors sold off risky bonds in fear that a slowing economy would prevent many of

--- Page 127 ---
Page 127 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
those borrowers from paying off their bonds. That drove down high-yield bond prices and pushed up their
yields, widening the spread between these so-called junk bonds and risk-free Treasurys, which investors
flocked to.
But that has changed in the past month as high-yield bonds have rallied, closing the gap between their yields
and those of Treasurys. If the junk-bond rally reverses itself and spreads widen again, that could signal
trouble ahead for the economy. "If all of a sudden spreads start to widen, uh-oh," Ms. MacKay says.
EXTRA GREEN: Christine Callies, chief U.S. investment strategist at Merrill Lynch, says the
mortgage-refinance index and the index of mortgage-loan applications are important indicators. An increase
in the refinance index, which tracks the number of people refinancing their existing home mortgages, says
that over the next few months, people's mortgage payments will likely go down, giving them more cash to
spend. The index of mortgage-loan applications is a gauge of consumer confidence. People don't buy homes
if they think they will lose their jobs. Bot these indexes have surged since the Fed cut rates, and analysts
expect them to improve again as more rate cuts occur.
"The data most clearly indicate consumers are not particularly depressed, their balance sheets are in decent
shape as a group," Ms. Callies says. "The money to borrow for consumption or education or vacation is there.
Those are the raw ingredients for a rebound in consumer activity, possibly before the second half."
RED-TAG SALES: Alan Levensen, chief economist at T. Rowe Price, says business inventories have grown
dramatically in the past year, and until production slows and these excess goods are sold off, the economy
will be weighted down. "The faster production falls, the faster we get this inventory correction out of the way,
the faster we then set the stage for production expansion that matches the strength in demand," Mr.
Levensen says.
He emphasizes that a healthy stock market acts as a booster to the economy, and an economic rebound
without a strong stock market is unlikely. "Recovery in the market is not a signal the economy is in recovery,
but it is necessary for the economy to rebound," he says.
One indicator not to watch, analysts say, is reported corporate profits, which lag behind economic activity.
The lousy earnings being announced every day this week are the result of weakness last fall, and few
analysts expect to see bright news on that horizon until this summer, at the earliest.
But future earnings are important, and some analysts worry that some expensive tech names might get
clobbered if technology spending continues to slow, hurting their earnings. "Have we discounted
disappointments in the second quarter or the third quarter; I suspect we haven't," said Ms. MacKay.
Mr. Paulsen says the big risk right now is investors are buying stocks and bonds on the assumption that a
V-shaped recovery will be well under way by the end of this year. He adds: "If it's an L that drags into 2002,
then you could lose your head."

Increase 179.01" -- WSJ Jan. 31, 2001)

Coal Stoves Are Hot Again---New Models Offer AlternativeAs Prices of Other FuelsSoar and Supplies Shrink
Coal Stoves Are Hot Again --- New Models Offer Alternative As Prices of Other Fuels Soar and
Supplies Shrink
By Robert Guy Matthews
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,391 words
30 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
ON CHILLY MORNINGS, most of Phil Mechnig's Providence, R.I., neighbors head for the thermostat to turn
up the heat. Not Mr. Mechnig. He tosses a baseball cap full of coal into his brand-new coal stove.
"It's beautiful," he says of the stove, which sits in the middle of his family room. Black and trimmed in gold,
with a big glass viewing window, it looks like a miniature fireplace. "I've had wood stoves for years, but
today's coal is much cleaner, and it gives steady, even heat," he says.
Not only that, but it's cheap and plentiful, and increasingly that's what counts. With skyrocketing energy costs
and tight natural-gas supplies, homeowners are looking for ways to save and conserve. That can mean
turning off the lights or taking public transportation. But for a small but growing number of people, it also
means exploring and investing in alternative heating sources, including coal.
Coal-stove dealers -- there are about 300, nearly all small mom-and-pop outfits -- say they expect
double-digit sales gains this year. Don Hysko, owner of Peoples Coal Co. in Cumberland, R.I., says he sold
about 80 coal stoves last winter and has sold some 300 this winter. "Last year, if you would have asked me if
business was good, I would have told you to stay away," says Mr. Hysko, who is one of the largest anthracite
coal dealers in New England. "Today the phone doesn't stop ringing."
Until recently, coal stoves tended to be dismissed as dirty and ugly -- and unnecessary given the availability
of clean natural gas and electricity. Coal companies didn't bother promoting home coal use because 94% of
the coal they mine goes to generate electric power. The only place the stoves seemed to make sense was
close to coal regions like Pennsylvania.
But a persistent and loyal group of coal-stove makers and dealers has steadily and quietly updated the
stoves. Thanks to the energy crisis, consumers are starting to pay attention and search them out.
Drew Stermer, who sells coal stoves and hot tubs through his Stermer Bros. Stoves & Spas in Lancaster, Pa.,
says his family has been in the business for 27 years. Most of his customers used to be long-time coal-stove
users. Now he's seeing new faces, people who have never operated a coal stove before. "Our market has
changed dramatically," says Mr. Stermer. "Some models have priced out the local people. There are more
white-collar people buying coal stoves."
Mr. Mechnig, 58, owner of Meckandil Tool Co., says that he was initially reluctant to consider coal stoves for
his three-bedroom, two-bath split-level home. When he was a boy, the family who lived one floor below had
one. It gave off a lot of soot and he could smell the faint scent of sulfur. "I remember going down to the
basement to stoke it," he says.
But when he visited a friend who had recently bought a coal stove, he decided that this generation of stoves
deserved a second look. They didn't make a lot of noise, set off smoke alarms or smell. One reason is that
the coal burned today is different from the kind used when he was growing up. Decades ago, homes used
bituminous coal, also known as soft coal, for heating and cooking. Between 4% and 15% of that coal is
foul-smelling sulfur, and it gives off potentially dangerous levels of carbon monoxide.
Today, home heating uses anthracite coal, know as hard coal, which has only about 1% sulfur. Stove dealers
still advise consumers to buy carbon-monoxide detectors, but Dave Ryan, a spokesman for the
Environmental Protection Agency, says that harm to the environment from burning anthracite coal appears to
be negligible.
Most of the stoves operate like wood stoves, heating a room or two at a time and expelling smoke through a
chimney. But in houses where duct work is already in place, a forced-air fan or blower system can circulate
the heat.

--- Page 129 ---
Page 129 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Top-of-the-line stoves, which cost about $2,700, are fully automatic, feeding rice-sized pieces of coal into a
blower fan that can warm a 2,500-square-foot house. A pricey model called the Alaska Stoker can go for two
days before the fire goes out. There are even coal stoves with a special device to grill food.
"With accessories, you can run it up to three grand in a hurry," says Todd Kauffman, a sales manager for
Harman Stove Co. in Halifax, Pa. Harman offers a variety of colors including "patriot blue," "honey-glo brown"
and "forest green," the most popular. Doors come trimmed in gold. Ceramic and hand-painted tiles are also
popular. "They're made to sit in the living room," says Mr. Kauffman.
The post-pot-bellied look is broadening the stove's appeal beyond the traditional markets of the mid-Atlantic
and Northeastern states, such as Virginia, Kentucky and Maine. "As we change the appearances of the
stoves, we are definitely selling more coal stoves in places like Detroit and Chicago," says Mr. Kauffman. He
expects to sell 30% more coal stoves this year than last.
Coal stoves aren't for everyone. They are less suitable for apartment dwellers and people living in densely
urban areas because stove owners must buy a ton of coal at a time and need nearby storage space. Stove
owners have to work out a deal with a coal-yard dealer, who goes directly to the coal mines and picks up the
coal. The dealer then resells the coal to the homeowner and delivers it. Coal stoves still aren't popular in
Colorado or California because there isn't a cheap enough way to distribute the coal.
Then there's the ash factor. A ton of coal produces about a half ton of ashes. Some people bury it. Others
bag it and set it outside for the garbage man.
The stoves also involve more work than just flipping a switch. Some of the newer ones have to be fed only
every nine to 12 hours. But with a cheaper model, owners could be shoveling coal throughout the day.
Nonetheless, the cheap price of coal makes the work worthwhile for many. A winter's worth of coal can cost
$200, depending on location. Dealers in New England sell a ton of coal for about $135, including delivery.
Closer to where the anthracite coal is mined, like Pennsylvania, the price per ton drops to about $85. That's a
huge savings when, on average, it takes only two or three tons of coal to heat a home for the entire winter.
Jim Ruthowski, a retired police officer who lives in Pawtucket, R.I., shudders when he thinks what his gas bill
would be if he didn't have coal. "It would be through the roof," he says. "We have had snow on the ground for
weeks." His suburban ranch home is equipped with natural gas, which he uses to run his clothes dryer and
cooking stove. Even with that limited use, his gas bills run about $40 a month.
Mr. Ruthowski says he has been using coal stoves to heat his home for about eight years. His stove burns
black egg-sized coal, called chestnut coal, that he keeps in a huge bin that was built to look like a doghouse.
He usually lights his stove around the end of October and keeps it running continuously until March. It takes
about 45 minutes to light, using wax and sawdust fire starter, charred wood, layers of coal and a sharp eye.
"Once the coal goes from red to white, you add a little more. Then 15 minutes later you throw on more," he
says. "It does take a little time to get a feel for it."
Twice daily, he adds more coal and shakes out the ashes. He says that he would love to upgrade to a fancier,
less time-consuming stove, but this one still works as well as it did when he purchased it new eight years ago.

Page 130 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
E-Business --Dot-Com Rarity:NextCard FindsOnline Ads Work
E-Business -- Dot-Com Rarity: NextCard Finds Online Ads Work
By Paul Beckett
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,259 words
29 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
SAN FRANCISCO -- Marketing and selling on the Internet is almost impossible, right? Business-to-consumer
sites are dead, aren't they? Don't try telling that to John Hashman, chief executive of NextCard Inc., an
Internet-only credit-card issuer.
"Very few people in America need another credit card," says Mr. Hashman. But that hasn't stopped the
41-year-old credit-card veteran from coming closer than most to achieving New Economy nirvana --
successfully selling products online.
How has he done it? By employing one of the most annoying, yet undeniably successful, ways of making
people buy stuff: direct marketing, but with an online twist.
The Internet may not, after all, be a revolutionary way of doing business; witness the problems at
Priceline.com Inc., whose "Name Your Own Price" system was supposed to be a new paradigm. And many
companies have seen response rates to online banner ads plummet.
But such advertising does appear effective for at least one thing: Persuading people to sign up for credit.
NextCard does so by applying, and improving upon, many of the lessons learned by the credit-card industry's
experience with direct mail, in which it stuffs about three billion solicitations a year into the nation's mailboxes.
"Everybody was talking about the Internet inventing a bunch of new business models, but that will not be very
true," Mr. Hashman says. "EBay is an online flea market, Amazon.com is the Book of the Month Club, and
we're really a credit-card company that exists on the Internet."
Still, NextCard, founded in 1997, does have many of the trappings of an Internet company. It works out of a
cramped office suite on Market Street here, where conference rooms have names like "Lion's Den." Veteran
employees pride themselves on their "single-digit" status, meaning they were among the first 10 to be hired.
And it even has a plummeting stock price. In 4 p.m. Nasdaq Stock Market trading Friday, its shares stood at
$9.06, down from their 52-week high of $36.87. It went public in May 1999 at $20 a share and closed on its
first day of trading at $33.50.
But unlike imploding dot-coms, NextCard says it expects to turn a profit by the end of this year, when it also
plans to have some $2 billion in credit-card loans outstanding, up from $1 billion reached in October.
For the fourth quarter, NextCard reported a net loss of $19.4 million, which narrowed from a net loss of $24.8
million in the year-earlier quarter. Revenue jumped to $71.7 million from $13.9 million and operating profit
rose to $62 million from $13.9 million the year before. Amid the layoffs elsewhere in tech-land, the company
has been hiring. Its staff rose to 820 in the fourth quarter from 287 a year earlier.
"This is a company that is really growing through all the noise going on around them," says analyst Stephen
C. Franco, who follows NextCard at U.S. Bancorp Piper Jaffray.
Several credit-card issuers, particularly Capital One Financial Corp. and Providian Financial Corp. -- where
both Mr. Hashman and NextCard founder Jeremy Lent once worked -- have built huge and successful
businesses using direct mail. They constantly tinker with the design of the mailings, altering the wording, the
type size, the letter color and the positioning of the MasterCard or Visa logo, to find what triggers the greatest
consumer response. But there is only so much you can do with an envelope. Results aren't known for six
weeks or more. And recently, direct-mail response rates across the industry have been dropping.
By contrast, NextCard runs about 100 different banner ads on the Web at any one time out of its stock of
about 2,000 designs. In a single month, its ads, on which it spends about $3 million a month, appear roughly
three billion different times on about 200 different Web sites. In a single year it conducts about 200,000 tests.
That gives it 200,000 opportunities to gauge, in as little as two weeks, what combination of design and

--- Page 131 ---
Page 131 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Web-site positioning is most likely to provoke a "click" and a card application -- and at what cost in advertising
dollars to the company.
NextCard also can monitor which banners generate not just the most customers but the most-profitable
customers, and whether those customers stay after any low introductory interest rates expire. Because
signing up for a credit card often is an impulse move, the company makes sure each potential customer is
approved or declined for credit within 30 seconds. And those approved are presented with at least two
options on terms, such as interest rates and rewards.
NextCard also is developing technology that will allow it to "pre-approve" customers -- a standard pitch by its
snail-mail competition -- that next year will allow it to display some banner ads only to individuals who it
believes will be good customers.
In short, NextCard has taken direct marketing to a level that makes even the most carefully-tailored mailings
"look like the Flintstones chiseling an offer out of stone tablet," says Jim Nail, a senior analyst at Forrester
Research Inc., a Cambridge, Mass.-based technology consulting firm.
NextCard views the various measurements it uses in deciding on banners and customers as sufficiently
unique that it has taken steps to protect them. Last fall, it filed suit in California Superior Court in San
Francisco alleging that two former employees and their new company, iLAB Inc., had stolen trade secrets in
setting up a system designed to optimize the effectiveness of banner ads. David Schwartz, a lawyer for the
defendants, says his clients deny the allegations. A hearing is scheduled for early next month.
How does the ad system work? Here's one example: In September, NextCard ran eight different banner ads
on a free Internet service provider for two weeks. Five were "controls," which had proven successful on
similar sites before. Three were new. One of the controls, titled "Dreamer," was a blue banner with a swirling
gold arrow, small "Visa" and "NextCard" logos and a window saying "30-second approval." Dreamer attracted
about the same rate of new accounts, measured as a percentage of all clicks on the banner, as one of the
new ads, which showed a woman's face on a green banner with the words "Susan saved $256." Dreamer's
response rate was 0.38%; Susan's was 0.35%.
For many companies, that might have been enough to declare success for the Susan ad. Not at NextCard. As
well as measuring accounts booked, NextCard also measures how much in credit-card balances each new
cardholder transfers from another card issuer to NextCard -- a leading indicator of how profitable customers
are going to be. Measured by the amount of ad dollars the company had to spend to get $1 in balances
transferred, Susan fell badly behind. It cost NextCard three cents to get one dollar in balances from
customers who clicked the Dreamer banner. But it cost 16 cents to get one dollar from those who chose the
"Susan" ad.
Three months later, new results confirmed the initial data: Customers booked under the Dreamer ad were
more than twice as profitable as those lured by Susan. So, says David Dowhan, group vice president for
credit-card acquisitions, "She was axed."

On Economic Cures
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
On Economic Cures
1,007 words
29 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Given that the economy seems to be slowing, the policy heads are furrowing their brows over which would be
better -- lower tax rates or lower interest rates. Well, it turns out that both would be best, though neither is
likely to run the economy on a dime.
-- Taxes. Will lower marginal tax rates spur growth? Sure. Low taxes are associated with rapid growth the
world around. The Reagan tax cuts, once fully implemented in 1983, ended "malaise" and started a boom
that persisted through 2000, except for a nine-month dip in 1990 and tepid growth as the economy digested
the 1990 and 1993 tax increases. We would not now be experiencing growth close to zero, as Alan
Greenspan described it in his testimony last week, if we had passed a tax cut last year, when opponents
warned that it would "overheat the economy," whatever that may mean.
And there, in timing, is precisely the rub in using tax changes as countercyclical policy. Given the political
delay in crafting tax legislation, tax cuts will arrive at the right time only through blind luck. What's more,
taxpayers watching the political tides anticipate tax changes, often in perverse ways. Recall, for example,
what happened with the investment tax credit. Expected passage of the credit brought business investment to
a halt while the players waited for it to go into effect, just as an anticipated suspension called forth a burst of
activity before the suspension was enacted. Both reactions merely aggravated the economic situation the tax
credits were meant to remedy.
It's better to think of tax policy in structural terms, designing it to produce incentives for growth in the long run.
In these terms, cutting marginal tax rates is a no-brainer for one reason -- taxes are way too high. Not only
did Mr. Clinton's tax increase create de jure brackets of 15-28-31-36-39.6%, but the disallowance of certain
deductions and his phase-outs of exemptions make the de facto brackets even higher. In high-tax states like
New York and browned-out California, the top combined federal-local marginal rate approaches 50%, a level
loaded with disincentives and distortions.
These higher marginal rates are especially punishing because real bracket creep -- what happens as wages
and salaries increase with economic and productivity growth -- pushes more people into higher brackets. As a
result federal tax revenue as a percentage of the economy is remarkably fat, at levels last seen in World War
II. And the evidence that taxes are way too high is hanging out there for all to see in the form of budget
surpluses.
The case for a tax cut proposed by President Bush, and endorsed by Mr. Greenspan last week, does not rest
on countercyclical arguments. The current slowdown certainly dispels the notion that the economy could go
gangbusters forever despite current tax levels. And as Mr. Greenspan suggested, a tax cut would provide
some insurance that the downturn won't turn into a chronic funk like Japan's. But the basic case is that the
Bush proposal represents a structural change that is necessary and salutary over the long term. And the
sooner it's done the better.
-- Interest rates. This is also (surprise!) a no-brainer: the Fed should cut short-term rates because they're too
high. And tomorrow's meeting of the FOMC seems like an excellent time.
Again, though, it's by no means clear that monetary policy can "fine tune" the real economy. The proper role
of monetary policy is to keep prices steady -- that means the Fed should defend the economy against both
inflation and deflation. We're currently having problems in part because, instead of sticking to its price-level
last, the Fed was trying to use monetary policy to slow the economy and cap the stock market. Monetary
policy changes can have an impact on the real economy, but it is mostly temporary and shows up only with a
delay, which optimists put at three months and pessimists put at two years.
In the longer view, the Greenspan Fed has been remarkably successful. The long boom has been partly due
to smart management by the Fed. As John Taylor, an economist at Stanford, observes: "monetary policy
has been credible, transparent and systematic -- strongly reacting to changes in the economy." As a result,

--- Page 133 ---
Page 133 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
inflation has been low and the real economy more stable. A recipe with the happy result that when the
inevitable mistake appears, recessions are less frequent and less severe.
Currently, there's some reason to believe that the Fed has been tighter than needed to preserve stable
prices. Our friend Robert Mundell, the Nobel Prize winning economist, keeps looking for price-level indicators
independent of the real economy. Commodity prices have fallen, the dollar price of gold has been down
since 1996, and the dollar has been strong in the foreign-exchange markets. Also, he adds, countries whose
currencies are fixed to the dollar (Hong Kong, China, Saudi Arabia and Argentina) have been experiencing
outright deflation.
So even if the Fed can't quickly reverse the current slack in the real economy, its fundamental job of
stabilizing prices gives it ample reason to ease. Mr. Mundell says it should be as easy as possible consistent
with avoiding a forex free-fall of the dollar, and that currently this calls for a reduction of one-half point in the
Fed's target interest rate.
-- Neither fiscal nor monetary action will prevent a recession if in fact that is where the economy is heading.
But that doesn't mean we shouldn't act. We can and should have a tax cut, which would result in a long-term
structural improvement for the economy, And we can and should have an interest-rate reduction, which would
counteract some of the current tightness in monetary policy. All the silly blah-blahing about choosing
between a tax cut or a reduction in interest rates is just that.

Even if Fed Cuts Rate, Dollar Strength Is Likely
Foreign Exchange
Even if Fed Cuts Rate, Dollar Strength Is Likely
By Jennifer Downey
Dow Jones Newswires
733 words
29 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar is likely to stick to a pattern of relative strength against the yen and euro this week
despite a gold mine of economic data and back-to-back central-bank decisions in the U.S. and euro zone
that could rouse other financial markets.
Effects on the foreign-exchange market will take longer to see because they depend on capital flows,
analysts said. So while U.S. stocks and Treasury bonds could suddenly rally from any interest-rate cut,
currency markets are likely to be less exuberant. Many traders expect the Federal Open Market Committee to
cut the 6% federal-funds rate after its meeting tomorrow and Wednesday.
The European Central Bank, which will announce its own rate decision Thursday, has insisted it will not
necessarily follow in the Fed's footsteps. "But if the Fed goes 50 [basis points], it would be tough not to do
something," said Frank Pusateri, vice president of corporate foreign exchange at Mellon Bank in Boston. The
ECB has kept its main refinancing rate at 4.75% since increasing 25 basis points, or a quarter percentage
point, Oct. 5.
On Friday, Bundesbank President Ernst Welteke, who is also a member of the ECB council, said the ECB
had interest rates on hold but would carefully consider its next move should the Fed cut rates by a half
percentage point. French Finance Minister Laurent Fabius later reinforced Mr. Welteke's comments, adding
that there is "absolutely no automatic link" between U.S. and euro-zone interest rates.
Late afternoon Friday, the dollar was buying 117.34 yen, up from 116.80 yen early in London and 116.81 yen
late Thursday in New York. The euro was trading at 92.44 U.S. cents, little changed from 92.28 cents early in
London and from 92.47 cents late Thursday in New York.
The British pound was trading at $1.4615, down from $1.4605 late Thursday in New York. Against the Swiss
franc, the dollar was trading at 1.6517 francs, up from 1.6507 francs late Thursday in New York.
The euro dropped significantly against the dollar after a surprise, intermeeting half-point Fed rate cut Jan. 3.
The common currency plunged over a cent and a half that day and has struggled to recover since.
But Mr. Pusateri said the Fed move expected this week has already been priced into the market because
traders have been hashing it over so long. The euro could then react positively as interest-rate differentials
between the U.S. and euro-zone will have narrowed.
Mr. Pusateri foresees a euro bounce up to 93 or 94 cents if the Fed cuts by a half percentage point, but
doesn't expect a substantial euro upswing to take hold over the week.
Michael Carey, economist at Credit Lyonnais in New York, expects currency markets to focus on the week's
data from Europe, including European Union jobless data, French and Italian producer-price indexes as well
as comments from ECB officials regarding the Fed decision.
Last week's trading was capped Friday by more bad news from Japan, which reported retail sales down 0.9%
and consumer prices down 0.4%. The bleak economic outlook could be compounded by jobless-rate and
household-spending figures to be released today.
Hopeful or dismal, the new data are unlikely to move currencies much this week, Mr. Pusateri said, because
the fundamental outlook on Japan remains bad. "Are people really going to change their portfolios on this
stuff?" he asked. He expects the dollar/yen pair to trade in a range of 118 to 119 yen over the week.
"Pressure from yen positioning ahead of the fiscal year will keep the currency confined," said Lisa Finstrom,
senior currency analyst at Salomon Smith Barney in New York.

--- Page 135 ---
Page 135 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The dollar's range could also be limited by worries about U.S. economic growth, she said, and any sentiment
of a U.S. recovery would have to weigh domestic data. Ms. Finstrom cited tomorrow's consumer-confidence
index and Thursday's NAPM business index as indicators to follow carefully, though an attention-getting
FOMC meeting falls between the two.
"The Fed decision is critical, but has to be in the context of the data," she said.

Corn, Soybean Prices Fall on Mad-Cow Fears
Commodities
Corn, Soybean Prices Fall on Mad-Cow Fears
By Dyanna DeCola
Dow Jones Newswires
495 words
29 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
CHICAGO -- Concerns that demand for U.S. feed grains could be crimped by the possible spread of
mad-cow disease, and heavy speculative selling, sent grain futures prices tumbling Friday at the Chicago
Board of Trade.
"Talk of mad-cow disease has the grain market terrorized with fear that feed usage will dwindle," said Steve
Bruce, a grain trader with ED&F Man in Chicago.
Though no U.S. cattle have been found to have the disease, the market was made more jumpy by a
quarantine of Texas cattle that were possibly fed animal parts, which is thought to be the way the disease,
called bovine spongiform encephalopathy, is spread. In Europe, there has been a growing furor over
mad-cow disease and a resulting decline in beef consumption. Scientists believe 83 people in Britain have
died from the human version of the disease, which they probably contracted by eating infected beef.
"If beef consumption decreases because of the mad-cow fear, that means less corn is needed to feed
livestock," Mr. Bruce said.
CBOT corn prices fell to four-month lows and soybean prices set new contract lows. March corn fell four
cents to $2.0950 a bushel; March soybeans plummeted 5.75 cents to $4.6025.
Traders and analysts said the fear is that a steep drop in meat consumption in Europe could spill over into the
U.S. The markets are extremely "export sensitive," with record corn and soybean supplies looming
world-wide.
"News stories about mad-cow disease in Europe have put the corn market on the defensive here in U.S.,"
said Mr. Bruce of ED&F. "Beef sales are down 35% in Spain and have dropped roughly 60% in Germany over
the last three months. The nervousness will likely keep the corn market on the defensive until the fear about
mad cow striking the U.S. dissipates."
The issues are expected to take their toll on the soybean meal market as well, traders say. Soymeal is made
from soybeans and is used to feed livestock, poultry and hogs.
COCOA: Prices bounded higher on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange in a two-leg shopping spree
initiated by trading houses and followed by speculators, participants said. The price of the March contract
rose $52 to $1,056 a metric ton, after registering a 16-month high of $1,066. "At $1,030, we had an
unbelievable size of buying orders ... but whether they were covering shorts or initiating new positions is yet
to be seen," said Al Abaroa, principal at Miami brokerage house Time Leverage Capital. Concerns over
supply availability helped support prices.
GOLD: Prices fell at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange on speculative selling. The
price of the February contract fell $1.90 to $262.90 a troy ounce.
---
Enza Tedesco and Mara Lemos contributed to this article.

EUROPE
E.ON Official to Quit,
After Comments
Denying Bid Talks
The deputy chairman of German utility E.ON's energy division, Otto Majewski, is stepping down, amid
complaints he made misleading comments to the media about E.ON's talks with Britain's Powergen. Mr.
Majewski, who was also E.ON Energie's chief financial officer, earlier this month denied to a newswire that
E.ON was in talks with the British power company. E.ON felt compelled to disclose immediately that
negotiations indeed were under way; it said Mr. Majewski spoke in "ignorance of the actual discussions" and
his remarks were "inconsistent with company policy not to comment on possible acquisitions or market
speculation." E.ON yesterday declined to comment on the reason for Mr. Majewski's resignation, effective
March 15. He was unavailable for comment, and didn't return telephone calls.
The move suggests that E.ON, formed in June from the merger of Veba and Viag, has learned from a fraud
case brought against it last autumn by the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission. The SEC charged that
Veba misled investors in 1999 when it repeatedly denied that it was in merger talks at the time with Viag.
E.ON signed a cease-and-desist order agreeing to abide by its policy either to communicate truthfully with the
public on such matters or not to comment at all. E.ON's American depositary shares were at $55.69, down 88
cents, in 4 p.m. composite trading on the New York Stock Exchange.
EADS Forms Bid for RAF Jet Tankers
European Aeronautic Defense & Space, known as EADS, launched a joint bid with four other companies to
win a GBP 9 billion ($13.2 billion) order for new midair-refueling jet tankers from Britain's Royal Air Force. The
joint venture, called Air Tanker, also includes Rolls-Royce and Cobham of Britain, France's Thales and
Halliburton of the U.S. The Future Strategic Tanker Aircraft program would replace the RAF's VC10 and
Tristars. EADS said the partners would take equal stakes in the Air Tanker consortium, and each company
would contribute its own expertise if the group is awarded the contract. The RAF has invited tenders for the
program, which may also include managing and maintaining the aircraft, an EADS spokesman said. Britain's
Defense Ministry will decide on the winner of the contract in 2002. Britain's BAE Systems and Serco Group
are also seen as bidders.
Investors Defy Hungarian Regulator
Investors who regulators allege are linked to Russian oil and gas company Gazprom defied Hungary's market
regulator and seized control of the board of Hungarian chemical company BorsodChem. The move is
expected to worsen an ownership crisis that has beset the company for months. The shareholders, who deny
regulators' assertions that they are acting in concert to subvert Hungary's takeover laws, voted at an
extraordinary shareholders' meeting to remove five of BorsodChem's seven board members. Chief Executive
Laszlo Kovacs and Zoltan Bordas, another executive member of the board, were spared, but the new board
voted after the meeting to replace Mr. Kovacs as chairman with Heinrich Stahl, a representative of Vienna
Capital Partners of Austria. Mr. Stahl said the board changes aimed to restore shareholders' confidence and
lift the uncertainty around Hungary's sixth-largest publicly held company.

Economic growth this year in the euro zone may not be as strong, and inflation not as high, as the European
Central Bank thought just weeks ago. But the central bank for the 12 countries that use the euro still sees

ASIA/PACIFIC
PetroChina Unveils
Major Gas Discovery
In Inner Mongolia
PetroChina, China's largest oil producer, said it has discovered a natural-gas field that could prove to be the
country's largest. Its president, Huang Yan, said the Sulige field, in the Ordos Basin in the Inner Mongolia
region, already has proven reserves of 220.4 billion cubic meters, of which 163.2 billion cubic meters are
recoverable. "Accumulative proven original gas in the place could be over 700 billion cubic meters, which
ranks Sulige gas field [as] the largest gas field in China," he said. Eight trial wells produced gas at an average
daily rate of 600,000 cubic meters, 10 times the average for other Chinese wells.
Mr. Huang said gas from Sulige could be channeled immediately to a pipeline leading to cities in eastern
China, and PetroChina has contracted Royal Dutch/Shell Group to help it study building a new pipeline from
Shaanxi province to Beijing.

As North Korean leader Kim Jong Il wrapped up a six-day visit to China, North Korean media and China's
government reported his stay for the first time Saturday. Mr. Kim, accompanied by military and ruling-party
officials, paid an unofficial visit at the invitation of Chinese President Jiang Zemin, North Korea's offficial
overseas news agency said. China's Foreign Ministry said Mr. Kim praised China's free-market reforms after
getting a firsthand look. It said both sides agreed on the timing of the visit's announcement, but it didn't
explain the reason for the secrecy. South Korean experts say North Korea keeps its leader's overseas travels
a secret because of concerns about security.

Canada's Manulife Financial said it will pay 147 billion yen ($1.25 billion) to acquire the policies outstanding of

Daihyaku said 145 billion yen of a total 292 billion yen paid for the policies would be provided by an
insurance-industry fund. Daihyaku had a negative net worth of 320 billion yen at Sept. 30, he said. Japanese
financial regulators ordered Daihyaku to halt most of its operations last May. Manulife had been expected to
take over Daihyaku's policies because the Canadian company had set up Manulife Century Life Insurance, a
joint venture with Daihyaku, in April 1999.

Bangkok and Singapore have overtaken Bali, Indonesia, as the Asian-Pacific region's most attractive
hotel-investment markets, according to a survey by investment bank Jones Lang LaSalle Hotels. It said about
18% of 750 large hotel owners and investors surveyed had responded. More than half of the respondents
also indicated an intent to invest in Sydney. In general, the short-term view was more pessimistic than in the
previous survey, in October, and less upbeat than for hotel investment in Europe and the Americas.

Mr. Soong put funds into his personal accounts in the early 1990s, they were used to finance Nationalist
campaigns and party projects. Opposition leader Chen Shui-bian won last year's election, with Mr. Soong,
now head of the People First Party, finishing a close second and Mr. Lien a distant third.
BRIEFLY:
-- Taiwan's industrial output in December shrank 2.07% from a year earlier, the first decline in 15 months. For
all of 2000, output gained 7.73%, the government said. Export orders rose 11.7% to $12.5 billion, the lowest
monthly level since last April.
-- Hong Kong's Global China Technology Group and a unit of state-run Xinhua news agency agreed to form a
joint venture to provide online financial information. Global China will hold a 55% stake and Xinhua 45%. The
venture, Xinhuaonline, will require total investment of 100 million yuan (US$12.1 million) over three years.
-- Indonesia's central bank said it doesn't plan to impose capital controls to defend the ailing rupiah, but
added that controversial foreign-exchange rules imposed last week are aimed at curbing offshore -- and often
speculative -- trading in the rupiah in Singapore and other financial centers.

EUROPE/AFRICA
Bid Talk Pushes Up Gold Fields' Shares
Speculation that South Africa's Anglo-Gold, the world's largest gold producer, could bid for Gold Fields, the
country's No. 2 gold miner, pushed Gold Fields shares up 5.1% in Johannesburg trading Friday. Talk of a
merger involving Gold Fields gained momentum after Anglo American PLC, AngloGold's London-listed
parent, raised its Gold Fields stake to 17% last month in a stock swap with a South African bank. Anglo
American owns 54% of Anglo-Gold. Gold Fields said Friday that it wasn't aware of any offer. Anglo-Gold and
Anglo American declined to comment.
Pace of Europe's PC Shipments Weakens
Fourth-quarter shipments of personal computers in Europe fell to their lowest level in at least four years,
according to preliminary figures from U.K. market-research firm Context. Fourth-quarter PC shipments rose
6.9% from a year earlier to 10.7 million units, compared with an 11.7% increase in sales a year before and
more than 20% growth in the 1998 and 1997 final quarters. Context said unit growth for all of 2000 was 7.9%.
The fourth-quarter figures are subject to revision because PC sales reported to Context by Dell Computer are
estimates, based on Dell's fiscal quarter that ends Jan. 30. According to the provisional figures, Compaq
Computer led with a 13.8% market share, followed by a joint venture of Germany's Siemens and Japan's
Fujitsu, with 9%, and Dell, 8%.
EM.TV Is in Talks on Formula One Stake
German media company EM.TV & Merchandising, burdened by debt after buying half of the Formula One
auto-racing circuit, has opened talks about selling its Formula One stake with the same parties it was involved
with when it bought the interest last March -- Formula One impressario Bernie Ecclestone and San Francisco
investment firm Hellman & Friedman, people with knowledge of the discussions said.

Nafta Partners Accelerate End of Levies
The U.S., Mexico and Canada have agreed to accelerate the removal of certain trade tariffs cited in the North
American Free-Trade Agreement, or Nafta. The reduction in tariffs was to have been gradually implemented
through 2008, said former U.S. Trade Representative Charlene Barshefsky, whose term ended last week with
the close of President Clinton's administration. Instead, the U.S. will remove tariffs on goods accounting for
$867 million in trade among the three countries, effective Jan. 1. Items included in the tariff eliminations
include footwear, chemicals, pharmaceuticals, auto parts and batteries, she said Friday.
Nike Probes Mexican Factory Violence
U.S. shoe and sports-apparel company Nike said it is investigating reports of labor unrest and violence in
Mexico, in which strikers allegedly were beaten at a South Korean-owned sweatshirt factory in Puebla state,
about 65 miles from Mexico City. Nike said it has been a major customer of the Kukdong factory, but currently
has no orders placed there. The factory makes apparel for several universities. On Jan. 11, about 200 state
police officers beat and dragged away some of the 850 employees occupying the grounds to protest the firing
of five workers, according to allegations last week by workers-rights group Global Exchange. The group said
15 people were hospitalized. Nike said it deplores violence and didn't initiate the calls to law-enforcement
officials. "Nike supports the rights of workers to organize and collectively seek fair and independent
resolutions to disputes such as these," it said. Factory management and unions plan to meet with a mediator.
Nike said.

Sears Net Falls 40% After ChargesAnd Disappointing Holiday Sales
Marketing & Media
Sears Net Falls 40% After Charges And Disappointing Holiday Sales
By Calmetta Coleman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
593 words
19 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Sears, Roebuck & Co. said fourth-quarter net income plunged 40%, dragged down by disappointing holiday
sales and previously announced charges related largely to store closures.
Like most other retailers, Sears saw sales suffer last month as bad weather kept some people away from
stores and as concerns about the economy, such as rising heating oil prices, kept some shoppers from
spending more. As consumers tighten their purse strings amid a slowdown in economic growth, the retail and
credit-card company said it expects 2001 to be a challenging year, particularly in the first half.
"People are clearly focused on what's going to happen with the consumer," Chief Executive Alan Lacy said in
an interview, noting that the company expects net income for the first quarter of 2001 to be lower than a year
ago. "We're taking a pretty conservative stance."
For the fourth quarter, net income was $442 million, or $1.32 a diluted share, down from $740 million, or
$1.98 a share. The latest results include a charge of $197 million, or 59 cents a share, for the closure of 89
stores and a value adjustment for a termite and pest-control business.
Absent the charge, profit would have been $639 million, or $1.91 a share, ahead of a First Call/Thomson
Financial 14-analyst consensus of $1.87 a share.
A stock-repurchase program during the quarter helped boost the per-share results. Sears had 334.9 million
shares outstanding at the end of the quarter, down from 373.9 million a year earlier.
Revenue rose 2.4% to $12.4 billion from $12.1 billion.
Sales at U.S. stores open at least a year inched up 0.9% as Sears continued to post weak sales of apparel,
which were offset by strength in items such as appliances, electronics and sporting goods.
Domestic credit revenue slipped 5%, reflecting lower balances on the Sears Card. Those were partially offset
by growth in the company's Gold MasterCard, which has been offered at low, introductory interest rates.
Some analysts were encouraged by Sears' performance during a tough quarter for retailers overall. Wayne
Hood, an analyst at Prudential Securities, noted that the charge for the store closures, announced earlier this
month, was in keeping with Mr. Lacy's turnaround plan to improve long-term performance in Sears' retail
business. "Alan Lacy has taken a very hard look at return on capital, and we haven't seen Sears do that
before," Mr. Hood said.
To bolster the retail business, Sears, based in Hoffman Estates, Ill., will soon begin implementing some of the
strategies that have been successful in test stores, including offering mattresses at 400 stores by the end of
April.
Sears, the nation's second-largest retailer behind Wal-Mart Stores Inc., expects same-store sales to rise 1%
to 3% in 2001. It expects operating income to increase in the mid-single digits and earnings per share to rise
in the low double digits for the year.
Sears' stock slipped 26 cents to $34.49 at 4 p.m. in New York Stock Exchange composite trading.

BIS Shareholders Sue J.P. Morgan, Arthur Andersen
International
BIS Shareholders Sue J.P. Morgan, Arthur Andersen
By John Carreyrou
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
554 words
19 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
BRUSSELS -- Belgian shareholder-consulting firm Deminor sued J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. and Arthur
Andersen, alleging that they didn't fairly appraise a buyout offer made by the U.S. Federal Reserve and the
French and Belgian central banks to the minority shareholders of the Bank for International Settlements.
Deminor, which is representing a group of individual minority shareholders of the BIS, filed the lawsuit in a
civil court in Paris yesterday. The Belgian firm has asked the French court to strip the bank and the consulting
firm of their mandate as advisers in the buyout deal and replace them with a legal expert, who would be
charged with issuing a new fairness opinion.
The BIS serves as a coordinator for the world's central banks. Deminor believes the buyout offer that the
Federal Reserve, which serves as the U.S. central bank, the Bank of France and the National Bank of
Belgium have put to the BIS's minority shareholders is undervalued. Acting through the BIS, the three central
banks have offered minority shareholders 16,000 Swiss francs ($9,725) for each of their shares, a price that
Deminor says is 53% below the BIS's net-adjusted asset value.
Now a modern international clearing house, the BIS was founded in 1930 to manage the reparation money
owed by Germany to France and its allies after World War I. Its ownership structure has been little changed
since then. The world's major central banks own 86.27% of the BIS, with the rest belonging to individual
shareholders. The remaining 13.73% is owned by French, Belgian and American holders, whom the central
banks of the U.S., France and Belgium are now seeking to buy out.
Last March, the BIS, based in Basel, Switzerland, launched a capital increase in a bid to grant ownership
stakes to the central banks of developing countries. The BIS issued the new shares at 23,000 Swiss francs
apiece, 7,000 Swiss francs more than the price it is now offering to pay individual minority shareholders to
buy them out.
In its suit, Deminor accuses J.P. Morgan Chase of being biased in favor of the central banks. J.P. Morgan
Chase has a large position in derivatives indexed to the price of gold, which makes it vulnerable to any
sudden buying or selling of large quantities of gold by the central banks, the world's largest holders of the
precious metal, Deminor alleged. "J.P. Morgan can't hence be considered independent," Deminor said in a
statement.
A spokeswoman for J.P. Morgan Chase, Hakim Faz, said the bank is aware of the lawsuit and believes there
are no grounds for it. "We don't believe there's a conflict of interest. We're acting in the best interest of our
client, BIS, but [also] as an independent adviser."
Deminor alleges Arthur Andersen didn't do its job properly in failing to second-guess J.P. Morgan's fairness
opinion. Arthur Andersen "just copied the J.P. Morgan opinion," said Fabrice Remon, a partner with Deminor
in Paris. Officials at Arthur Andersen's European unit couldn't be reached for comment.
---
Konstantin Richter contributed to this article.
