The Luxury Slump
WEEKEND JOURNAL
The Luxury Slump
By Wall Street Journal staff reporters Danielle Reed, Elizabeth Bernstein and Michelle Higgins
3,390 words
19 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
[The shaky economy has hit some high-end homes with a vengeance. We take a cross-country tour of our
`Houses of the Week' and find slashed prices and desperate sellers. Will the rest of the market be far behind?
]
It is the house that has everything: four sprawling bedrooms, five fireplaces, a heated four-car garage and
views of the Rocky Mountains. It even has a celebrity pedigree, with Cher as a former owner and pop-star
Mariah Carey renting just this month.
The only thing it doesn't have: a buyer. Owner Robert White, who put the Aspen, Colo., house up for sale in
August, says lookers drive by all the time -- but so far, not a single bid. "I'm astonished about how many
people don't even get out of the car," he says.
The big news of the cooling economy has been the surprising resilience of the housing market. But there is a
sign of trouble ahead: sluggish sales of luxury properties. While higher-end homes have typically been
insulated from hard times, brokers from Boston to the Bay area are reporting fewer offers, a virtual end to
bidding wars and price cuts up to 20% on some of the market's crown jewels. And that could ultimately affect
all homeowners, because so much of the '90s boom was fueled by growth at the top of the market.
To get a clearer sense of where things are, we took a look back at our own database -- the homes featured
as Weekend Journal's "House of the Week." We visited brokers, neighbors, even many of the houses
themselves. The results were surprising: Of the 52 homes featured in 2000, only 11 have sold. In 1998 and
1999, about a third sold. "The frenzy is gone," says Kathy Steinberg, a New York broker specializing in this
niche. "The buyers want to wait and see."
Indeed, our cross-country house-hunting trip provided a snapshot of some of the trials and tribulations of
trying to sell houses in this changing real-estate market. So concerned are the owners of a historic house in
Salem, Mass., listed at $999,900 since March, they are considering transforming it -- into a
bed-and-breakfast. You'd think nothing could be wrong with a $20 million mansion with views of San
Francisco -- it's just that the one we saw also has views of the freeway. (Still, owner Karen Smart says, "it's
pretty obvious that the person who lives in this house is successful.")
Just imagine buying a place with 74 art-glass windows to clean. Apparently not many other people can either:
The Frank Lloyd Wright house we saw in Riverside, Ill., has had trouble finding a buyer, despite price cuts
totaling 21%. Then there is the Los Angeles mansion once owned by actress Jayne Mansfield. "It's just crazy
that someone hasn't snatched that up," says local agent Cecelia Waeschle.
These sorts of woes aren't just issues for upper-end homeowners. Already, there are signs they are making
their way into the mass market. While prices have generally held up, sales of existing homes slowed last year
for the first time since 1998 -- down 3.9% to 5.2 million homes, according to year-end projections from the
National Association of Realtors. The number of new houses being built also fell 4.8% and is projected to fall
another 3.8% this year. In the Midwest, despite pockets of growth, housing prices actually fell slightly last
year.
Unlike previous downturns, which were driven heavily by unemployment, the stock market is fueling much of
the current slowdown. That's why caution is appearing in the luxury market, especially in high-tech corridors
that have suffered. One example: a contract that fell through on one of our Houses of the Week -- a home in
Telluride, Colo. -- after the bidder got slammed by the plunging Nasdaq Composite Index.
In our search, we hit eight homes in some of the priciest markets in the country. We found owners -- who
might have once expected bidding wars -- doing everything from putting in new "doubled glazed" windows to

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
including the dining-room table in the deal. (The catch: It's an extra $75,000.) One frustrated owner in North
Carolina told us he was "ready to move on" after failing to sell our July 7 House of the Week. Here they are.
The Pink Palace, Los Angeles
Asking Price: $5 million
Actress Pamela Anderson looked into this seashell-pink home, owned by '60s crooner Engelbert
Humperdinck who bought it for about $1 million in 1975. "It would be perfect for her," says Bradley Dorsey,
Mr. Humperdinck's son. "It needs to have a star to make it shine again."
It sure does. When we visited, it was clear the house -- once home to Jayne Mansfield -- had lost much of its
luster. The decor is still 1970s, it lacks a modern kitchen and we got hit by a ceiling leak in the foyer. ("That
will be fixed of course," Mr. Dorsey says.) Indeed, the Sunset Boulevard house has been yanked on and off
the market for years, including a period in 1997 when it was marketed on home-shopping network QVC.
Mr. Dorsey, 26 years old, lives in the house gathering inspiration for an album of his own. And there's plenty:
the piano on which Gershwin composed "Rhapsody in Blue," Mr. Humperdinck's gold and platinum albums
and the kennels that housed Mansfield's lions (now home to the family dog, Heiko). Everything in the house is
negotiable, though Mr. Dorsey thinks his father wants to keep the piano.
Local brokers predict Mr. Humperdinck ultimately will have to come down in price. It would be "an excellent
investment" closer to $4 million, says Ms. Waeschle, the Beverly Hills agent. (A house on a smaller lot across
the street sold recently for that price.)
Lindsay Manor, Tiburon, Calif.
Asking Price: $20 million
What can you say about a house with a "children's wing" and a formal name for the closet in the master
bedroom? The three-year-old manor, just 15 miles from downtown San Francisco, has 28 rooms -- many of
them, including the playroom, with panoramic views of the bay and Golden Gate Bridge. Owner Mrs. Smart, a
portrait painter, says she feels like "queen of the castle" in the 800-square-foot master bedroom.
The price, eye-popping as it is, isn't a record in southern Marin county. A nearby $23 million estate with two
swimming pools sold in just two weeks in November. No such luck for Lindsay Manor. Decked in mahogany
and Italian marble, it went on the market eight months ago and has drawn regular inquiries but only two bids,
which "weren't acceptable," says Mrs. Smart's husband, Preston, a financial consultant.
What are its prospects? "It's a little too early in the year to tell," says listing agent Nan Allen. One drawback:
The house is visible from the freeway, something buyers in the $20 million-range usually try to avoid. One
local broker says she has driven by with several buyers who saw it from the road and didn't want to go in. "If
you can see the bridge, they can see you," says Mr. Smart. "It's a give-and-take."
White Horse Springs, Aspen, Colo.
Asking Price: $8.75 million
With the ski business flat for much of the decade, it is real estate that helped fuel Aspen's boom. The average
sale price there jumped to $3.8 million last year, up 48% from 1999. So while well-above average, this $8.75
million house is hardly a rarity. The reddish-stucco Santa Fe-style house went on the market six months ago
and has yet to draw a bid.
"There's something about Santa Fe that turns people off," says its owner, Mr. White, who has watched
potential buyers drive up, see the adobe facade and turn around. And that's before they've seen the cowhide
leopard-print sofa in the breakfast nook -- a hand-me-down from Cher, who sold the house to Mr. White for
$3.25 million six years ago. He says his young daughters hate the sofa because its coarse hair is scratchy. (It
is. We felt it.)
Since buying the house, Mr. White, chairman of management-consulting firm ARC Worldwide, says he has
put in another $2.25 million, including a living room with views of the Rockies and a caretaker's apartment. He
says he just rented the house out at "six figures" for a month to singer Ms. Carey. One highlight:
leather-braided staircase banisters.
Local brokers say the upper end of the Aspen market has slowed some lately, with buyers moving more
slowly as they wait to see how the economy shapes up. Brian Hazen, a realtor at a rival firm to the listing
agent, says the house is priced reasonably for the market and he expects it to sell by the end of this summer.
Stratford Towers, Asheville, N.C.

--- Page 148 ---
Page 148 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Asking Price: $1,550,000
The ad for this house might read "No air conditioning. 1950s fixtures. Price reduced." The property has been
listed for a year; in the past three months, agent Gregory Sullins (godson of the late owner) has shown it only
three times. The result: This month, he slashed 9% off the original $1.7 million price. "You have to listen to
the economy of the nation," says Mr. Sullins.
Owned by the estate of local ceramist and philanthropist Grace Joan Love Schneider, who inherited it, the
house is stately. It has walnut woodwork, French chandeliers, mountain views and a door -- taken from a
castle in Scotland -- that opens onto a foyer with a double staircase. On the other hand, the stairs are
covered by a faded purple rug, all 5u baths need updating and the kitchen seats only two people. There is
also an eight-head shower upstairs, though we weren't sure whether that is a pro or a con.
Mr. Sullins says the price takes refurbishing costs into account and notes that Stratford, at about $215 per
square foot, is priced well below the area's $277 per square foot average price.
The Tomek House, Riverside, Ill.
Asking price: $975,000
On the plus side, this Frank Lloyd Wright home is a national historic landmark. On the down side, owner
Maya Moran Manny's sons used to cover its signature art-glass windows and play floor hockey in the billiard
room. Otherwise, though, Ms. Manny says she has taken pains to preserve the place. "I don't own the home.
I curate it," she says.
The home has been on and off the market in recent years, slashed from $1,235,000 to its current price. Even
at that level, it's at the high end for Riverside, which has yet to see a sale crack the $1 million mark, including
another Wright estate that sold last spring for $975,000.
Ms. Manny says the husband of one couple who toured the house wanted to buy it, but his wife quickly nixed
the idea. Not because of its unusual Wright design, but because she hated the idea of washing its 74
decorative windows. "It will take a unique buyer," Ms. Manny says.
None has shown up so far. Local brokers say the market in Riverside, just outside of Chicago, remains strong
but that this house is a bit overpriced given its condition. With the exception of the main living area and dining
room (which has lighted Tiffany glass on the ceiling) the house needs a lot of work. "We're talking several
hundreds of thousands of dollars," says Carm Merola, an interior designer who toured the house recently.
But there's a lot here for anyone willing to spend the money, including vistas of a park landscaped by
Frederick Law Olmsted, of Central Park fame. The windows alone are worth almost twice the asking price of
the whole house. The hitch: You can't sell them. The deed prevents structural changes, and many of them
have been donated to a Wright conservancy.
The Joseph Story House, Salem, Mass.
Asking Price: $999,900
An area law firm recently looked into converting this Federalist mansion into a think tank. That isn't a bad idea
-- it could take a think tank to figure out exactly what to do with this historic but somewhat disjointed home. It
has four separate apartments, four staircases, four entrances -- and in fact two addresses, each on a different
street.
When we visited the place, once home to U.S. Supreme Court Justice Joseph Story, we were allowed to view
only the main residence and a studio upstairs. Its 190 years were evident: Some of its 12 fireplaces are
boarded up, and the kitchen and baths need serious updating. Still, details such as the early American
mantels and stairwells are in excellent condition.
Some local realtors believe the house should sell for around $850,000. Owner Kathleen Ward, whose
grandmother bought the house in the 1930s, says there have been a few bids since the house last went on
the market in March, but none high enough. Recently, she has begun considering other options, such as
turning it into a bed-and-breakfast or allowing it to be used as a showcase house for interior designers.
Ms. Ward says the price reflects the home's history. Revolutionary War Gen. Marquis de Lafayette once slept
in what is now her bedroom. This summer, Ms. Ward says her son discovered a passageway from the cellar
to the street, bolstering the theory that Justice Story, who delivered the famous Amistad slavery decision,
might have participated in the Underground Railroad.
The Hoffman House, Darien, Conn.

--- Page 149 ---
Page 149 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Asking Price: $6.95 million
A year ago, this house might have had a shot at breaking Darien's $6 million barrier for properties not on the
Long Island Sound. But local agents say the five-acre property is now unlikely to sell close to its asking price
-- and that is after the seller slashed it by $1 million. (Owner Glenn Hoffman says the current price is "very
close" to where the town appraised it.)
Mr. Hoffman, a marketing executive, concedes the 1930 house is a tough sell, even in better times: "This is
not a cookie-cutter house." Not only is it old for Darien, where even 20-year-old houses regularly get torn
down, but it is also a Mediterranean-style chateau in a town of conventional New England architecture. For all
its 16th-century stained glass and carved wooden doors (it even has a wrought-iron gate in the dining room)
the house has no garage. Another mixed blessing: the highly polished wood stairs were slippery when we
visited -- the agent who showed us the house almost took a tumble.
"The right person is out there. They've got the money," says Mr. Hoffman. "But how do you attract that
person?"
The John Penn House, Philadelphia
Asking Price: $1.25 million
George Washington slept at this site -- but at this price, owner Fred Simeone has yet to find anyone else who
wants to. In eight months, he has gotten only a few bids, despite dozens of showings. So he is now
considering reducing the price or taking the five-story Society Hill house off the market to fix it up.
"It's going to take somebody with the ability or the vision to see what the house could become," says Judy
Hartl, who lives in a similar house next door. That's because the 9,000-square-foot home needs an estimated
$150,000 of restoration work. Most of that is cosmetic, but attending to the fine details of a historic home can
be complicated.
The brick building, which dates from about 1830, has eight working fireplaces and original wood floors and
hearthstones. The previous house on the site was the residence of John Penn, grandson of Pennsylvania's
founder. Washington used that building during the American Revolution as his military headquarters.
Its most prominent characteristic may be its size, with cavernous rooms (there is a ballroom on the second
floor) and high ceilings. The house is so huge that the idea of restoring it makes "some buyers feel
intimidated," says listing agent Steve DiFrancesco. A slightly smaller house around the corner that was listed
at $1.3 million -- reduced from $1.8 million -- recently sold for $1.1 million
---
And for the Rest of the Market . . .
The same forces being felt in high-end housing have already begun to
appear in the broader market.
After seeing
interest rates cut and prices drop on houses for sale in
her area, Elizabeth Neid of Denver says she and her husband are kicking
themselves over the $265,000 house they bought in May. Indeed, most
brokers say caution is in order for both buyers and sellers. For one
thing, the stock market's declines are only now hitting housing
because real estate typically lags about six months behind Wall
Street. While some experts see price declines as a possibility, Case
Shiller Weiss, a research firm that tracks real estate for The Wall
Street Journal, sees slowing rates of appreciation in most markets.
Of course, the news isn't all bad. Many analysts believe interest
rates will decline further, helping cushion the blow. Prices also
aren't likely to be hit as hard as they were in the early '90s, when
speculation and overbuilding made the market more vulnerable. But,
says Mark Zandi of consulting-firm Economy.com, "there is a bubble,
and it will be deflated."
Here are last year's price changes and predictions for 2001 in 10
different markets.

When a Town FallsBetween Two Nations,It Rains Lots of Money---Campione Lies in Switzerland,But It Is a Rich Part of Italy;Grim End to the Fa...
When a Town Falls Between Two Nations, It Rains Lots of Money --- Campione Lies in Switzerland,
But It Is a Rich Part of Italy; Grim End to the Fairy Tale?
By Yaroslav Trofimov
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,475 words
16 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
CAMPIONE D'ITALIA, Italy -- Squeezed between snowcapped Alpine peaks and beautiful Lake Lugano, this
town looks like a scene from a fairy tale. And, thanks to a quirk of history, for many years it truly was a
financial fairyland.
An Italian town with a population of 2,800, Campione is oddly situated well within the borders of Switzerland.
It became one of the wealthiest towns in the world, minting prosperity because of its limbo between two
nations. Since Italy is a half-hour drive and a customs checkpoint away, people here use Swiss money,
license plates and telephones, even as they live under Italian law.
Its isolation started bringing generous privileges from Rome in 1933 when Mussolini decided to transform
Campione, then a destitute fishermen's village, into a showcase of Italy's prosperity. His recipe: opening here
one of Italy's four legal gambling casinos and showering Campione's burghers with unusual tax and
exchange-rate subsidies.
The perks, which earned Campione a listing in international tax dodgers' handbooks, were the envy of regular
Italians -- and a major annoyance. Now, fed up with complaints about abuses and shady deals here, Italy's
Parliament is finally jerking Campione's leash.
"The main problem with Campione? They simply have too much money," says Adria Bartolich, a member of
Parliament who sponsored legislation to bring Campione to heel.
As of this month, Italy has abolished for Campione's wealthiest residents -- and curtailed for the rest -- a
complicated exchange-rate subsidy that had nearly halved income-tax bills. The old system artificially valued
the Swiss franc so a Campione resident earning $200,000 in Swiss francs was taxed at a rate applied to
income of about $37,000 in lire and thereby avoided the pain of Italy's high marginal tax rates. The same
unreal exchange rate, little changed since the 1930s, was also used to turn Campione's lira-denominated
pensions into huge sums in Swiss francs.
On Campione's manicured lakefront promenade, where furs and Rolexes abound, this is a very ill wind. "We
aren't nearly as rich as these people want to think," says Luciana Medici, who heads the citizens' association
and works for the casino, which generates $32.8 million of Campione's $37.2 million municipal budget.
"People in Italy are convinced that our streets are paved with gold," but they are wrong, says Mayor Roberto
Salmoiraghi, who is also one of the two practicing physicians here. His championing of Campione's "rights"
won him 85% of the votes in the last election.
In doing so, the 51-year-old Dr. Salmoiraghi, who has been mayor for nearly seven years, has made enemies
among Italian politicians, who also assail his record.
Chief among them is Sen. Felice Besostri -- a former small-town mayor who crafted some of the new laws.
"On visits to Campione, I've been taunted as a short, obese and bearded creature," says the 56-year-old Mr.
Besostri, a member of the dominant party in Italy's government, the Democratici di Sinistra, or Democratic
Left. "Obviously, some people there don't want me poking around."
The mayor, a wiry man with piercing eyes, believes that the enclave's rights are justified by its cost of living.
He says it would be unfair to pay full-fare Italian taxes since people here can't readily shop in cheaper stores
in Italy and must pay Switzerland's higher insurance and utility rates. If all the perks are withdrawn, "everyone
here will just pick up and leave," Dr. Salmoiraghi threatens.
Distressed, Dr. Salmoiraghi last summer wrote Mr. Besostri, in Italian, that his gravestone ought to read:

--- Page 152 ---
Page 152 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
To the man -- or, better, to the hero, shall we say
Who, moved by the leftist sense of justice, chose to slay
Of Campione's good taxpayer any capacity to pay.
The senator was quick to respond, with a 32-line poem of his own called "Adventure in Campione," alleging
abuses of Campione's international status and accusing Dr. Salmoiraghi of monopolizing medical services,
among other things.
He also wrote to Italy's Ministry of the Interior, which runs Campione's temporary casino administration. The
confidential letter, which discussed salaries, pensions and alleged improprieties in Campione, was quickly
leaked, photocopied and stuffed in mailboxes all over town.
Dr. Salmoiraghi, who oversees a budget 10 times that of comparable towns in Italy, denies wrongdoing by his
administration. "The mayor's seat here is a very uncomfortable one to occupy," Dr. Salmoiraghi says. "It's a
tiny town that involves enormous interests and lots of money."
For its anomalous status the town has to thank a local lord, known as Totone, who bequeathed the land to
the St. Ambrose abbots of Milan back in the year 777.
Being Italian citizens, the people of Campione don't have the right to work in Switzerland -- which limits their
employment opportunities mainly to lucrative jobs with the local government or the casino. Local youths
without a university education can earn as much as $10,000 a month as croupiers -- after taxes.
"We enjoy some of the world's highest per capita incomes, and it's not like this is a town of highly educated
professionals," says Giorgio Olmeda, a municipal inspector. Dr. Salmoiraghi's "power is based on providing
jobs and dispensing favors," he says.
Involved in a Mafia money-laundering scandal in the 1980s, the casino has been managed by an
Italian-government caretaker since the early '90s. Dr. Salmoiraghi, however, expects soon to conclude an
agreement that would let his administration own 46% of a new casino-management company and the right to
appoint its executives.
While Campione's streets are usually deserted until early afternoon, when Italian gamblers arrive for an
all-nighter at the casino, a few dozen folks can be found on most days huddling in Dr. Salmoiraghi's medical
clinic just up the road from City Hall.
Because Italy's clinics are at least 10 miles away, the Italian government allows Campione to sign
health-insurance agreements with Swiss plans instead of Italy's national health service. That has been a boon
to Dr. Salmoiraghi.
In Italy, a Mayor Salmoiraghi couldn't continue to work as a physician at all. Doctors in the national
health-insurance system are civil servants barred from holding elective office.
Though Swiss insurance usually lets a patient choose his own doctor, that doesn't exactly apply in Campione.
Dr. Salmoiraghi invokes an Italian law intended to keep health spending in check that lets a town the size of
Campione have just two publicly funded doctors. Most people here are patients of Dr. Salmoiraghi's.
According to Mr. Besostri, who maintains that the arrangement is illegal, Dr. Salmoiraghi earns about
$620,000 a year. And because many citizens say they have trouble getting an appointment to see Mayor
Salmoiraghi, they take minor ailments to Dr. Salmoiraghi and use some of their time with him to ask him
questions they want to ask the mayor. "His medical clinic has become a sort of employment agency," scoffs
his patient Santo Ferrau, a retired interior decorator.
Dr. Salmoiraghi, who won't discuss his income, says he is insulted by suggestions that he needed a political
career to further his medical practice. "I'm sure I'm not the best-paid doctor in Switzerland," he says.
But he's certainly one of the most highly paid people in Campione. Oddly enough, few really rich outsiders
have taken up residence in this little town, even though Campione is prominently featured on Internet sites
dedicated to tax avoidance, second passports and hiding assets. "Switzerland's secret semitropical tax
haven," is how one such site touts it. Campione is "like Monte Carlo in 1952, before it became `in' and
developed into a mini-Manhattan," says "The Campione Report," a $100 handbook, available online from a
British publisher for those who might wish to move here to avoid taxes.
Dr. Salmoiraghi complains that politicians in Rome have rejected his demands to turn Campione into an
offshore financial center or to grant the right to issue postage stamps. He is still pursuing one ambitious
project: a $50 million skyscraper that, by 2005, will house a new casino and entertainment center, adding a

--- Page 153 ---
Page 153 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
bit of Manhattan to the Lake Lugano skyline. That's a risky game because Switzerland and Italy are
considering opening casinos nearby to challenge this town's regional monopoly.
But Monte Carlo it isn't and probably won't become, Dr. Salmoiraghi acknowledges. "With Italian mentality,
that's just impossible -- this country is too bureaucratic," he says.

Slowing Economy, Safety Concerns Zap Growth in Laser Eye Surgery
Slowing Economy, Safety Concerns Zap Growth in Laser Eye Surgery
By Laura Johannes and James Bandler
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
1,303 words
8 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Jack Dunn, fed up with his itchy contact lenses, considered joining the millions of Americans who have cured
their nearsightedness with laser surgery.
But then his ophthalmologist disclosed the risks, which include a slim possibility of permanent damage to the
eyes. "It frightened me enough to say `I don't want to do this,'" says Mr. Dunn, a 39-year-old college
administrator from Milton, Mass.
People like Mr. Dunn are one reason that growth in the laser eye-surgery market has come to a screeching
halt after five giddy years of expansion. Concern about side effects and efficacy are mounting. Also to blame
is the slowing economy. The surgery generally costs from $1,000 to $2,500 an eye and isn't covered by
insurance. "Clearly we're seeing consumers saying that since it's an elective surgery, postponing it is
something they can do," says William M. Carpenter, chief executive of Bausch & Lomb Inc.
The Rochester, N.Y., eye-care giant announced Friday that its fiscal fourth-quarter earnings would fall short
of analyst forecasts, largely due to lower-than-expected demand for its laser eye-surgery equipment. It now
expects net income of 69 cents to 71 cents a diluted share, not including charges -- well below the 73 cents
predicted by analysts. Bausch & Lomb said it will cut 350 jobs, bringing the total number of cuts announced
since October to 800 out of about 13,000 employees, and take pretax charges of $50 million, mostly in the
fourth quarter.
The nation's top laser eye-surgery equipment maker, Visx Inc., of Santa Clara, Calif., warned Wall Street last
month that it expects a disappointing fourth quarter due in part to the slowing economy. Its stock fell 25% to
about $9 a share on the news, down from more than $100 in 1999.
Mr. Carpenter firmly believes the market for eye-zapping equipment will rebound as the economy improves.
Irving Arons, of Spectrum Consulting, Peabody, Mass., predicts the number of surgeries will begin growing
again soon and finish the year up by 50%.
Still, even the most bullish estimates acknowledge that the dizzying growth phase of this field is over. The
surgery, in which a beam of light is aimed at the eye to reshape the cornea, is used to correct
nearsightedness, farsightedness and astigmatism. After its U.S. introduction in 1995, the number of
procedures doubled every year until 1999, when 950,000 were performed.
But last year, the rate of increase slowed to 53%, as the number of procedures reached 1.4 million, according
to Market Scope, a St. Louis research firm. Nearly all the year's growth occurred in the first half. The second
half was nearly flat. In the third quarter, 375,000 procedures were done, up a paltry 1% from the second
quarter, and the number inched up 2% in the fourth quarter.
The sudden leveling off is particularly painful to the industry because the market is getting crowded. In the
mid-1990s, only two companies, Visx and Summit Technology Inc., shared a booming market. Now, five
major equipment makers are jostling for a share. They include Summit, which merged with rival Autonomous
Technologies Corp. and subsequently was acquired by Nestle SA, LaserSight Inc. of Winter Park, Fla., and
Japan's Nidek Co.
Meanwhile, about a dozen operators of surgery-center chains are falling over each other to offer lower prices.
Lasik Vision Corp., a Vancouver company which took the U.S. by storm in 1999 with rock-bottom prices,
announced earlier this month that it was restructuring amid widening losses.
John Klobnak, chief executive of Laser Vision Centers Inc., a St. Louis company that runs a chain of some
320 surgery centers, says he believes an industry shakeout is due. "We think there is only room for two or
three corporate competitors, and the consolidation is going to happen within the next 12 months," says Paul
Doneshrad, chief executive of Aris Vision Inc., a closely held Los Angeles chain of surgery centers. Aris has

--- Page 155 ---
Page 155 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
agreed to buy Icon Laser Eye Centers Inc. of Toronto and a controlling interest in Gimbel Vision International
Inc., another Canadian chain with U.S. operations, at undisclosed terms.
In the 1990s, the laser eye-surgery market looked like a gold mine. Visx and Summit, under a revenue and
patent-sharing pact, collected $250 for every vision-correcting laser surgery in the U.S.
But in December 1998, Nidek thumbed its nose at the Visx-Summit patents and stormed into the U.S. market.
It undercut the established players with its vow never to charge per-procedure royalties. Visx unsuccessfully
tried to get the federal International Trade Commission to bar Nidek imports, and sued Nidek in U.S. District
Court in San Francisco for patent infringement. That suit is pending; Bausch & Lomb also announced Friday
that it settled litigation with Visx, agreeing to pay undisclosed royalties for each use of its machines. Nidek,
which still offers free use of its machines, says that advantage has made its sales immune to the recent
slowdown.
In February, Visx slashed its per-eye royalty rate to a mere $100 an eye, a discount immediately mimicked by
competitors. The price cut fueled a price war among doctors. Dave Edmiston, a Sacramento ophthalmologist
who had charged $2,000 per eye, says he was shocked when a new Lasik Vision center offered an
introductory promotion of both eyes for $999. Dr. Edmiston halved his price to meet Lasik's permanent price
of $999 an eye.
The average price of eye surgery was about $1,700 an eye last year, down from $1,800 to $1,900 in 1999,
says Spectrum's Mr. Arons. However, the discounts sometimes came at a cost of poorly trained doctors and
less follow-up care. A growing number of patients suffered poor results, such as blurry vision that is
uncorrectable even by glasses or contacts. Malpractice lawsuits mounted.
Amid growing reports of botched procedures, the U.S. Food and Drug Administration posted an online
warning on Dec. 1. It urged consumers to beware of "slick" advertising and eye-surgery deals that "sound too
good to be true." The agency highlighted the list of rare but serious risks of the surgery, including blurred
vision, night-time sight reduction and even "irreversible blindness."
R. Doyle Stulting, a professor of ophthalmology at Emory University in Atlanta, says that at the best centers,
serious complications occur in only a half-percent of patients. However, in the hands of ill-trained operators,
as many as 15% of patients will be unhappy with the results, he says.
Even people who see the best practitioners can suffer a common side effect: reduced night vision. Candy
Gold, who had her eyes lasered last March, says she still needs glasses for nighttime driving. In romantically
lit restaurants, the Newton, Mass., writer says she needs "to take the candle and move it so close that it's
practically dripping wax on the menu because I can't see what I'm reading."
Advances now under way at several companies may improve the surgery. Bausch & Lomb plans to offer
soon new diagnostic technologies that better tailor a laser to a person's eyes. "It's the difference between
buying a one-size-fits-all dress or having it tailor-made," says Barbara Kelley, a Bausch & Lomb
spokeswoman.
Lehman Brothers analyst Kenneth Goldman predicts that after an industry shakeout, the players left standing
will enjoy strong demand. In five years, he says, "there's no reason to think that someone 30 years old is
going to be wearing a pair of glasses with Coke-bottle lenses."

Thinking Things Over:A Photo-Op Presidency: The Finale
Thinking Things Over: A Photo-Op Presidency: The Finale
By Robert L. Bartley
1,166 words
8 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A33
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
In a better world this column would be strictly about the economy, much in the news with the manufacturing
sector in outright decline and the Fed reaching for the emergency cord. But as with everything else in the
administration, the economy is inextricable from presidential character, never more in evidence than in the
final days of Bill Clinton's tenure.
Item A is not in fact the economy, but the compulsive grandstanding in the Middle East. The Clinton plan to
have Israel give up the Temple Mount for the Palestinian's abandoning the "right of return" is a non-starter on
two counts. One, Israel happens to be a democracy and Prime Minister Barak does not control the Knesset,
which is not about to give up the Temple Mount. Two, the Palestinian mini-state and most Arab nations
happen to be dictatorships, depending on their grievance against Israel to bolster internal legitimacy; they are
not about to deed legitimacy to the Jewish state.
Given these realities, what is all this fuss about? Well, it's about one big final photo-op for Bill Clinton.
President-elect Bush says he appreciates this effort "so very much," and indeed it may demonstrate that the
land-for-peace formula is unworkable and help refocus policy on the military balance of power. But Mr.
Clinton's clear intent is to wrap up a deal with Mr. Barak and Mr. Arafat that neither can or will deliver, letting
the incoming president pick up the pieces after his own peace-in-our-times photo-op.
We've already had the Esquire-interview photo-op (see photo), showing the same taste as the Mussolini walk
at the Democratic National Convention. We've had the first-black-President photo-op, with Mr. Clinton
shredding promises to the Senate about recess appointments so he can name one more black judge, at least
for a year. We've had the war-crimes photo-op, adding the International Criminal Court to the long list of
treaties Mr. Clinton has signed despite no chance of Senate ratification.
But then, we've had rescission of the 1993 ethics photo-op, with Mr. Clinton repealing the rules he issued in
the first hour of his presidency about a five-year moratorium on lobbying by former executive branch
employees.
On the economy, we've rehearsed the old Clinton ploy of sending a minion out to turn the tables on your
critics and pose as the victim. Of course, chief economic adviser Gene Sperling did not exactly talk of
hundred-dollar bills and trailer parks. Instead, he complained that "what you're seeing is President-elect Bush
and his team actually talking down our economy" and injecting "fear and anxiety." They'd observed that a
recession risk has suddenly appeared, and that their proposed tax cut looks like a better idea than ever.
By now it should be evident even within the White House that recession risk is not something dreamed up in
Austin. After all, the economy is so perilous that the Federal Reserve felt it had to cut its target interest rates
by a full 50 basis points only two weeks after it left them unchanged. It's preposterous to assert, let alone
believe, that this was caused by a few remarks by the incoming administration much milder than some
already heard along Wall Street.
Only a minority of economists, to be sure, has concluded that we are experiencing a recession, two quarters
of contraction. We can still hope, as I certainly do, that the consensus prediction of a "soft landing" proves
correct. But it's under severe test from the abruptness of the downturn and its seeming acceleration. Nor is it
clear that the Fed's sudden change of heart will make a quick difference; monetary policy affects real
economic variables like production and employment only with a lag -- some say as long as two years.
The "talking down the economy" line is a rather pathetic effort to deflect blame for any serious problems
forward into the Bush administration. Any sensible person will see that any recession starting with the current
sourness would not be the Bush recession but the Clinton recession.
Some of course are already seeing a Greenspan recession; the hunt for policy errors starts with monetary
policy. But back in the summer and fall I agreed that Mr. Greenspan had one big inflationary sign to worry

--- Page 157 ---
Page 157 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
about, namely the price of oil, which I do not dismiss as casually as some of my friends do. Yes, the dollar
was strong and the gold price low; perhaps Mr. Greenspan could have eased sooner. But with oil soaring to
$35 a barrel, this was far from clear at the time.
Even in the Greenspan era, you cannot expect monetary policy to be perpetually errorless, so you also have
to look at the rest of the policy mix. On the fiscal side, the federal government is taking more of economic
output in taxes than it ever has since World War II; should we be surprised that a touch on the monetary
brakes might have an untoward effect? Nor is running a surplus to pay down debt a stimulative policy; where
are the Keynesians when we could use them?
Add to this the Clinton regulatory morass. A big lawsuit against Microsoft is another good photo-op, but not
exactly the way to keep the productivity surge rolling. The broadband and wireless revolutions have been
stopped in their tracks by the antitrust division and the FCC. The Clinton veto of tort reform opened the field
for his plaintiff lawyer friends/contributors to suck blood from American business.
While the price of oil has now declined, natural gas prices have quadrupled and electricity has become
scarce. Why? Because environmental photo-ops have stopped energy investment. California has not sited a
power plant for 15 years, and suddenly the long run is here.
Mr. Clinton, similarly, has spent the eight years of his presidency going from photo-op to photo-op. Beyond
discovering the virtue of paying down the debt after the election of a GOP Congress in 1994, his
administration has seldom thought beyond the next news cycle. We will be better off if his luck holds into a
soft landing. But it now seems possible that the long run is arriving before he gets out of town.
So how is he spending his final days in office? With some 450 photo-op orders and proclamations, nearly
every one an economic drag. The safety photo-op burdens business with new ergonomic standards. The
public lands photo-op locks up 58 million acres in 39 states from logging or energy development. The Clinton
EPA is pouring out new regulations, and the Landmark Legal Foundation has filed suit for an injunction
against destroying relevant documents.

Cut Rates Fast
Cut Rates Fast
By Marc A. Miles
1,049 words
4 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Yesterday the Federal Reserve cut the federal funds rate by half a point -- to 6% from a nine-year high of
6.5%. It was the first rate decrease in two years, and the first time the Fed has cut rates between scheduled
meetings since October 1998, when it battled the Asian currency crisis. But unless the Fed moves further and
fast, it may make a bad situation even worse.
In the first half of last year the economy was humming along at 4%-6% growth, and suddenly, "poof," the
steam was gone. Growth started tumbling, companies started cutting back, unemployment started to rise, the
Nasdaq Stock Market fell to its lowest level in almost two years, and retailers began slashing prices as
holiday sales fell short of expectations.
Who did this to us? The answer is simple: the folks at the Fed.
Return to the mid-'90s. Inflation and interest rates are low and stable. There is no indication that this
environment will change any time soon. Making decisions is easy, for future conditions are likely to be much
like the present. Not surprisingly, the economy responds by growing at rates far in excess of what was
experienced in the inflationary '70s and early '80s. Some suggest the economy is simply making up for
growth lost during the inflation times.
There's only one problem. The Fed has nothing to do. In fact, except for the reaction to the Asian crisis in the
fall of 1998, the Fed funds rate is stable between 1995 and 1999. Great economy, but the Fed doesn't seem
to be a player.
Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan tries shaking things up a bit with his "irrational exuberance" speech at the end
of 1996. The speech receives much publicity, but the stock market shrugs off the suggestion, and continues
to climb at a torrid pace. Something more potent is needed to gain attention.
That void is filled by the fear of inflation. Not that inflation is a problem. Inflation rates in 1997, 1998 and
1999 average only around 2%. But who knows what price increases lurk around the very next corner? With
growth so consistently strong, and unemployment the lowest in decades, the Fed embraces prevailing
Keynesian theories like the Phillips Curve and NAIRU that warn inflation is the logical next repercussion.
Armed with these questionable justifications, the Fed is ready to go on the attack. The Fed funds rate rises by
a quarter point in June 1999, followed by similar increases in August and November.
There are two things we can say with conviction about participants in the economy. They respond to
incentives, and they will do their best to avoid higher taxes or costs. Rising interest rates ignite both
responses. Higher interest rates mean higher costs. Expectations of a rate increase exhort participants to
scurry to complete investment and consumer purchases before higher costs hit. The classic example is
buying a car before loan rates rise. As expectations of even higher rates emerge, the scurrying intensifies.
Plans for the future become purchases of today. Economic activity from later periods shows up in current
statistics.
But the impact of these rate rises seems to be just the opposite of what the Fed envisioned. As future Fed
funds increases become more predictable, quarterly growth rises rather than falls. Economic growth jumps
from about 2.5% in the quarter before the first rate increase, to nearly 6%, then over 8% in the third and
fourth quarters, as the specter of even higher rates looms. In the first two quarters of this year, growth still
averages over 5%.
Then the Fed signals it has had enough. Once the fear of even higher rates evaporates, the scurrying stops.
But, since some purchases planned for today have already happened, the scurrying is replaced by a stillness
in the economy. Economic growth declines. Fed funds rise by 50 basis points in May 2000, and by June it is
apparent that the increases are over. In the next quarter, the economy slows to about 2%.

--- Page 159 ---
Page 159 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
So here we are in the grasp of the slowdown. Where do we go from here?
True to type, the Fed has suddenly realized that the drop in growth has gotten out of hand. It thinks
yesterday's half-point drop will surely jump start the economy.
But while this opinion is popular, it is inconsistent with incentives and behavior. Prospective rate drops will
only increase the length and depth of stillness as market participants wait for the better deals down the road.
Why refinance today when better terms are only a few months away?
In other words, now that the Fed has turned 180 degrees and embarked on a course of lower rates, the
economic slowdown will remain that much longer. How long and how deep? That will depend in part on the
speed with which rates decline, and the ultimate drop. The faster the drop, the sooner growth returns to a
more normal 3+ %. The bigger the drop, the bigger the rebound. But if the decline is gradual, George W.
Bush will preside in his first two years in office over the kind of delayed rebound that cost his father
re-election.
And, what about the fear of inflation? With gold prices again in the $270 range, with oil prices falling, with
productivity rising, inflation is not a problem. In fact, during this whole period of rising interest rates inflation
has remained tame.
Does the Fed deserve credit for keeping inflation in check? That question is best answered by the old story
of the man in New York with the elephant whistle. He shows the whistle to his friend and tells him how it
keeps elephants away. The friend, of course, protests that there aren't any elephants in New York. To which
the man replies, "See how well it works!"
Rising rates are like the whistle on inflation. They kept away something that was never there. But the
increases did induce the current slowdown. The Fed has already stolen the Christmas season of 2000. Let's
hope they'll drop rates far and fast so they won't spoil Christmas 2001 too.
---
Mr. Miles is an economic consultant and financial planner in Newton, Mass.
