Year-End Review of What Was News 2000---The Events That Took UsOn a Roller-Coaster Ride
Year-End Review of What Was News 2000 --- The Events That Took Us On a Roller-Coaster Ride
10,481 words
2 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
R12
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
FOR INVESTORS world-wide, 2000 was quite a roller-coaster ride, marked by events that often weren't what
they seemed at first glance.
For starters, the year-2000 glitch had a barely noticeable impact, thanks in part to hundreds of billions of
dollars spent on preventive measures. With that out of the way, Nasdaq rolled early in the year to surpass
5000 on the back of technology stocks. But a subsequent washout contributed to a more than 50% drop in
the index's value and helped turn the year into its worst ever. The Dow Jones Industrial Average set records,
but it too proved vulnerable late in the year as profit warnings spooked investors.
The Internet gained wider acceptance as a tool for communicating, investing and shopping, but also provided
several sobering examples of its pitfalls. Hackers penetrated -- and paralyzed -- Amazon.com and eBay,
while a computer virus nicknamed the "love bug" crippled e-mail systems around the world.
Meanwhile, Microsoft was ordered split in two in its antitrust trial, though that was put on hold pending
appeals. The courts also were busy untangling whether Elian Gonzalez should be returned to his father in
Cuba (he was).
Throughout, consolidation continued, with AOL announcing it would buy Time Warner, and a host of pairings
among banks and brokerage houses.
And there was the presidential election. Uncertainty began when TV news stations called Florida first for Al
Gore, then for George W. Bush, and chaos followed when a recount was needed and both candidates filed
lawsuits. The U.S. Supreme Court ultimately foreclosed a hand count of chad-impaired ballots and Texas
Gov. Bush was declared the winner.
Here's a summary of the news events as chronicled on the front page of The Wall Street Journal. In most
cases, the dates reflect when the items were published.
---
Compiled by Juston Jones, Rob Rossi and Sheila Courter
---
JANUARY
3 The world dances past the 2000 milepost unbruised, celebrating in
peace. Analysts estimated spending in the U.S. on repairing
Y2K-related problems totaled between $100 billion and $600 billion.
Russia enters the post-Yeltsin era following a surprise New Year's
Eve resignation. The nation's first democratically elected president
adds to his legacy of unpredictability by abruptly handing the reins
to Premier Putin.
5 President Clinton nominates
Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan to a fourth
four-year term, making official what markets had been expecting and
politicians had been endorsing.
Inmates injured at Attica state prison are to share an $8 million
settlement New York has agreed to. Many were beaten or tortured after
the 1971 uprising and police counterattack that killed 32 prisoners
and 11 hostage guards.
6 Cuban boy should go home, the U.S. rules in an international
custody spat. Elian Gonzalez is ordered sent back to his father by
Jan. 14, but an appeal is filed by his Florida relatives. The

--- Page 161 ---
Page 161 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
six-year-old boy touched off a tug-of-war between the Castro regime
and U.S. Cubans when he was saved on Thanksgiving after a shipwreck
off Florida that killed his mother.
10 The Dow Jones industrials soared to a record 11522.56 on Friday,
propelled by drug stocks, consumer staples, utilities and REITs. The
Nasdaq composite recouped a record 155.49 to 3882.62.
11 The Cuban boy's case will be heard March 6 by a Florida judge, who
issued an order blocking immigration authorities from returning him to
his father in Cuba by Jan. 14. Florida relatives want custody of Elian
Gonzalez.
14 A record pollution penalty is imposed on Koch Industries, which
reached agreement with the EPA to pay $30 million to settle lawsuits
involving 300 oil spills from its pipelines and other facilities in
six states. It also will pay $5 million for a range of environmental
work.
Microsoft's Bill Gates steps aside as CEO 25 years after founding the
software concern, handing its daily operations to longtime lieutenant
Steve Ballmer just as the company faces an array of legal and
competitive challenges. Gates will remain chairman and take on the new
role of "chief software architect."
17 The Dow Jones Industrial Average jumped 140.55 Friday to 11722.98
-- a record high for the year.
Glaxo Wellcome will acquire SmithKline Beecham in a $75.7 billion
stock swap. The merger will form one of the world's largest drug
makers with annual sales of $24.9 billion and 107,000 employees
world-wide.
18 Tyco plans to build and operate an undersea cable network spanning
the Atlantic and Pacific at a minimum cost of $4 billion in the first
phase.
26 Chase agrees to stop sharing information about its depositors with
other businesses unless it has received permission. Observers say the
settlement will set a powerful precedent.
27 Elian Gonzalez finally meets with his grandmothers from Cuba for
11/2 hours at a college official's home in Miami Beach. The boy was
animated during the reunion and the women wept when he left, a person
present said.
Coke says it is slashing 20% of its work force, or 6,000 employees,
and would take $1.6 billion in charges. The firm also posts a $45
million quarterly loss, its first in at least a decade.
28 American Home settles a wrongful death lawsuit in Massachusetts
over its former diet drugs for an amount believed to be about $10
million.
31 Genetically modified goods must bear labels identifying them as
such when they are shipped across borders, according to rules
established by 131 nations at a U.N. conference in Montreal. But in a
concession to the U.S., the labels don't require specific details to
be given.
FEBRUARY
1 Alaska Air Flight 261 crashes off Southern California, killing all
88 on board. Investigators later say the MD-83 was cruising at 31,000
feet toward San Francisco when pilots reported what investigators
called a "jammed stabilizer" and tried to land in Los Angeles.
Puerto Rico and the Navy reach an agreement that will permit limited
training at a bombing range on the island of Vieques. The accord calls
for a referendum that could end such training in 2003, two years
earlier than the Navy wanted. Exercises were suspended after a
civilian was killed in April.
The economic boom today becomes the longest in history, with 107
months of expansion. Economists credit the capital markets with being
a crucial factor, but they also warn of possible risks of new
financial tools.
2 GM's board approves a plan to offer shareholders $8 billion in
tracking stock in its Hughes unit and to contribute an additional $7
billion of the securities to retirement plans.
4 Mannesmann agrees to a $180.95 billion merger with Vodafone. The
deal is the biggest ever and creates the world's largest mobile-phone

--- Page 162 ---
Page 162 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
operator. Under the all-stock transaction, Vodafone shareholders would
hold a majority of 50.5%. The accord is expected to force rivals to
merge and hasten the arrival of cellphones that work anywhere.
8 Pfizer confirms it would pay $93.4 billion to buy Warner-Lambert.
The deal creates one of the world's largest drug firms with $28
billion in revenue and $4.7 billion in annual R&D spending.
Yahoo's Web portal is shut down for nearly three hours because of
what the company called "a coordinated attack designed to take down
the site."
9 Hackers stage more attacks on some of the biggest sites on the
Internet, temporarily shutting down Amazon.com, Buy.com and eBay.
10 Computer hackers continue to disrupt some of the world's biggest
Web sites. The latest targets include online brokerage firm E*Trade,
which was shut down for a short time, and technology- news publisher
ZDNet. Other Web firms are gripped by fear of becoming targets of
"denial of service" attacks.
Japan's Nikkei index closes above 20000 for the first time in 21/2
years, with tech stocks continuing to surge despite signs of economic
weakness.
14 Charles M. Schulz, 77, creator of the "Peanuts" comic strip died
Saturday, in Santa Rosa, Calif., after battling colon cancer.
15 Crude-oil prices top $30 a barrel, the highest since 1991, raising
concerns about a summer gasoline shortage and energy-induced inflation.
22 Two top Sotheby's executives quit unexpectedly amid a Justice
Department investigation of possible price-fixing in the fine-art
industry.
The Broadway show "Cats" is to close in New York June 25 after a
record-breaking 7,397 performances. The musical opened at the Winter
Garden theater in October 1982 and has grossed $380 million at that
site.
23 Chase Manhattan announces an earlier version of the bank helped
the German Nazi government exchange marks that may have been seized
from Jewish refugees. Banks' wartime practices recently have come
under heightened scrutiny.
The Microsoft case returns to the courtroom for closing arguments,
with the judge questioning company claims that copyright protection on
software overrides antitrust law. Gates is said to have met secretly
with the judge overseeing settlement talks.
24 Seven Wal-Mart workers in Texas vote to unionize in the first
successful union vote at a U.S. Wal-Mart.
25 HIV-positive patients will be allowed to receive Medicaid payments
in Maine in a one-state experiment by the federal government that could
lead to greatly expanded benefits. Previously, patients had to have
full-blown AIDS to qualify for the program.
28 GM, Ford and DaimlerChrysler agree to create a single auto-parts
exchange on the Internet. Once fully operational, the exchange is
expected to handle $240 billion in annual world-wide spending by the
three car makers. The exchange will be an independent company with
plans for an IPO.
29 Citigroup's John Reed will retire in April, leaving Sanford Weill
as the financial-services firm's sole chairman and chief executive.
Weill agrees to yield his CEO post within two years.
MARCH
3 An ex-Democratic fund-raiser is found guilty by a federal jury on
five felony counts for arranging more than $100,000 in illegal
contributions during the 1996 campaign. Maria Hsia had accompanied
Gore on a visit to a California Buddhist temple that year.
The Commerce Department unveils a new index to track electronic
commerce separately from overall retail sales. Online retail sales
totaled $5.3 billion in the fourth quarter.
Palm shares soar on their first day of trading, giving the maker of
hand-held organizers a market value nearly twice that of parent 3Com.
6 The head of Bob Jones University drops its ban on interracial
dating Friday. It became an issue because of Texas Gov. George W.
Bush's visit in South Carolina's race.
7 MGM Grand agrees to buy Mirage for $21 a share, or $4.4 billion,

--- Page 163 ---
Page 163 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
and assume roughly $2 billion in debt. The deal tops MGM's initial bid
of $17 a share.
VeriSign is buying Network Solutions for $17 billion in stock, in a
bid to become the leader in verifying identities of online buyers and
sellers.
9 Deutsche Bank and Dresdner Bank unveil their $29.55 billion merger.
The combined firm plans to shed its retail branches and focus on
serving wealthy clients via the Internet.
10 The Elian Gonzalez case opens before a federal judge in Miami with
demonstrators snarling traffic outside. Florida relatives are
challenging immigration officials' decision to return the boy to his
father in Cuba, and are seeking political asylum for him.
The Nasdaq closes above 5000 for the first time, climbing 149.60
points to finish at 5046.86. The index reaches the milestone a little
more than two months after first crossing 4000. The Dow Jones
industrials regain the 10000 level, climbing 154.20 to 10010.73.
13 I2 Technologies agrees to acquire Aspect Development for $9.3
billion in stock, in what would be the largest software deal to date.
Both boards have approved the transaction. The deal is likely to
significantly boost i2's position in the booming business-to-business
e-commerce market.
The Nasdaq closes at its all-time high of 5048.62.
14 Tribune Co. agrees to buy Times Mirror for $5.9 billion in cash
and stock, or $84 a share. The deal would create a multimedia giant
with 22 TV stations, 11 daily newspapers --
including the Chicago
Tribune and Los Angeles Times -- four radio stations, a strong
Internet presence and the Chicago Cubs baseball team.
15 Wall Street is rocked by a major insider-trading case involving 23
corporate deals leaked over the Internet by a former temporary employee
of Goldman Sachs and CS First Boston.
16 A Stephen King electronic novel drew 400,000 download orders in
its first 24 hours, a record even for the top-selling horror writer.
"Riding the Bullet" cost $2.50 a copy at some Web sites, but was free
at others.
17 The Dow Jones industrials soar 499.19 points -- by far their
biggest point rise ever -- as Old Economy stocks returned to the fore.
20 As many as 470 cultists died in Ugandza in an apparent mass
suicide Friday, police said in raising the toll from initial reports.
Members of the millennialist Movement for the Restoration of the Ten
Commandments of God feasted, burned possessions, locked themselves in
a church and set it on fire.
Time Warner says it will close Life magazine, in part to divert
resources to the six magazine launches the publisher has planned for
this year.
21 Philip Morris and R.J. Reynolds are found guilty of
misrepresenting the dangers of cigarettes. A California jury orders
the firms to pay $1.7 million in damages to an ex-smoker dying of
cancer and to her husband.
22 The Supreme Court rules the FDA lacks authority to regulate
tobacco. The 5-4 decision by the court's conservative majority is a
major blow to the Clinton administration's efforts to curb smoking,
and leaves the Justice Department's lawsuit as its most-potent weapon
for extracting concessions from the industry.
The Elian Gonzalez case is dismissed by a federal judge, setting back
efforts by Florida relatives to block the U.S. from returning the boy
to his father in Cuba. An appeal is planned. Gov. Bush backs keeping
the six-year-old in the U.S.; Vice President Al Gore says the decision
is up to the courts.
24 A gene-mapping milestone is passed as Celera researchers announce
they have deciphered the entire genetic blueprint of the fruit fly,
the most complex organism decoded so far. The flies are widely used in
lab work and share a surprising number of genes with humans.
28 Philip Morris and R.J. Reynolds are told by a California jury to
pay $20 million in punitive damages to a sick smoker, underscoring
cigarette makers' vulnerability in the state.
Cisco Systems surpasses Microsoft to become the company with the

--- Page 164 ---
Page 164 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
highest market value in the world, at $555.44 billion, as the
networking- equipment maker's stock price rose.
30 Seagate, the world's biggest disk-drive maker, will become private
as part of a nearly $20 billion stock-and-cash deal with Veritas and
others.
31 Elian Gonzalez's case grows more tangled and forces Gore into
taking sides. The vice president asks Congress to give permanent
resident status to the Cuban boy and six family members, breaking with
the Clinton administration on an issue that could sway a battleground
state in the fall. Elian's father and an entourage applied for visas
to come to the U.S. and take custody of the boy before a May court
appeal.
APRIL
4 The Nasdaq plunges 349.15, or 7.64% -- its heaviest point drop
ever, and its biggest percentage drop since Aug. 31, 1998 -- as the
collapse of efforts to settle the Microsoft suit spurs a tech
sell-off. The Dow Jones industrials, meanwhile, soar 300.01.
6 Vanguard 500 surpasses Fidelity's Magellan as the largest mutual
fund, according to various estimates.
SBC and BellSouth confirm they will merge their U.S. wireless assets
to form the nation's No. 2 wireless firm.
Elian Gonzalez's father, Juan Miguel Gonzalez, arrives in Washington
and demands his son's return.
7 Celera completes a major step toward deciphering the entire human
genetic blueprint. The biotech company says it sequenced 99% of all
the human genetic material in seven months, and will now try to figure
out how the DNA fragments fit together to form the human genome. Celera
shares surge 20% to $137 on the news.
10 North and South Korea agree to a June summit after 52 years of
division.
11 The Nasdaq suffers its second-biggest point drop ever, as blue
chips advance strongly. The tech-heavy Nasdaq composite falls 258.25
points, or 5.81%, to 4188.20, while the Dow Jones industrials advances
75.08 to 11186.56.
A Florida judge sets May 15 as the start of the next phase of the
tobacco trial, in which jurors will determine the amount of punitive
damages. That comes a day after a Miami jury awarded $6.9 million in
compensatory damages in a suit involving the nation's top four tobacco
firms.
13 The Nasdaq slumps 286.27 points, or 7.06%, to 3769.63, dragging
the index into bear-market territory. The tech-heavy Nasdaq is now
25.3% below its record close last month. The Dow Jones industrials
drop 161.95 to 11125.13, hurt by steep losses in H-P, IBM, Intel and
Microsoft.
Vodafone AirTouch's $180 billion bid for Mannesmann is cleared by the
EU, but the merged entity must grant rivals access to its network and
shed Mannesmann's Orange unit.
14 President Clinton says he won't seek a pardon from his successor
if he is indicted by the new Whitewater prosecutor after he leaves
office.
17 Stocks plunged Friday, with the major indexes enduring their
biggest point drops ever and among their steepest percentage losses in
a decade. The consumer-price report indicated inflation may be
resurfacing. The Nasdaq Composite Index dropped 355.49 points, or
9.7%, to 3321.29. The Dow Jones industrials sank 617.78 points to
10305.77.
NASD members vote to sell the Nasdaq market to brokerage firms and
the corporations that trade on it.
19 The Nasdaq scores its biggest point gain ever and its second
biggest ever in percentage terms. The composite index soars 254.41
points, or 7.19%, to 3793.57 and erases all of Friday's steep decline.
The Dow Jones industrials rally 184.91 points to 10767.42.
24 Images generated by Saturday's predawn seizure of Elian Gonzalez
promise political pain for Attorney General Janet Reno and Vice
President Al Gore. The boy was returned to his father and the family
remains at an Air Force base near Washington.

--- Page 165 ---
Page 165 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
25 Microsoft's stock plunges 16% as investors were shaken by the
possibilities of slower earnings growth and a breakup of the company,
including a spinoff of its Office software suite.
26 A bill allowing gay "civil unions" passes Vermont's Legislature
and the governor signs it a day later. It confers most benefits of
marriage, and the first ceremonies are to take place in July.
28 New York Mayor Giuliani discloses he has been diagnosed with
prostate cancer and says it was caught early and is curable.
MAY
1 MP3.com infringed on copyrights of five big record companies with
its My.MP3.com service, a federal judge rules. Shares in the Internet
music firm, based in San Diego, fall 40%.
2 South Carolina's governor approves observance of the Martin Luther
King holiday, the last state to do so. But he drew an NAACP protest
for also approving a holiday for Confederate Memorial Day on May 10.
3 Time Warner Cable reaches a temporary cease-fire with Disney that
restores ABC programming to cable subscribers in several big cities.
Time Warner had abruptly dropped Disney's ABC network from its system,
leaving 3.5 million households in parts of New York, Los Angeles and
elsewhere without the network. The media companies are fighting over
the fee Disney charges Time Warner cable systems to carry the Disney
Channel.
Unilever offers to buy Bestfoods for about $18.4 billion, but
Bestfoods' board rejects the proposal. The bid comes eight months
after Bestfoods aborted merger talks with Heinz.
Cardinal John O'Connor, 80, who championed Vatican positions on birth
control, abortion and homosexuality in 16 years as New York's top Roman
Catholic cleric, dies in New York, after 1999 brain-tumor surgery.
4 The FCC finds that Time Warner violated federal rules by pulling
Disney's ABC network signal off its cable-TV systems during a sweeps
month.
5 A computer virus nicknamed the "love bug" cripples e-mail systems
around the world, quickly spreading from Asia to Europe and the U.S.
Many companies, such as Ford and Microsoft, as well as the U.S. Army
and Navy, are forced to shut down their e-mail while technicians
scramble to delete infected files. The computer virus that infected
more than a million computers is later traced to a 23-year-old in the
Philippines.
9 WPP Group reaches a pact with Young & Rubicam to buy the New York
firm for about $4.7 billion in stock.
10 Sen. John McCain (R., Ariz.) endorses Texas Gov. George W. Bush
and takes himself out of the vice presidential race.
11 Five drug companies agree to slash the cost of HIV drugs for
people living in poor nations. The agreement, which was negotiated by
the U.N. with Bristol-Myers, Glaxo, Merck, Boehringer and Roche, comes
in response to the African AIDS crisis.
12 New Mexico wildfires sweep through the abandoned streets of Los
Alamos, burning more than 260 homes and forcing 7,000 in a suburb to
flee. Flames scorch part of the nuclear-weapons facility, but
officials say the materials there were safe. The National Park Service
official who ordered the initial "controlled" burn is placed on leave.
The Vatican names Bishop Edward M. Egan, head of the Bridgeport,
Conn., diocese, to succeed Cardinal John O'Connor as head of the New
York archdiocese. Egan, 68, is expected to be elevated to cardinal
soon.
Xerox CEO G. Richard Thoman resigns after only a year in the job.
Chairman Paul Allaire orchestrated Thoman's departure by gathering
support from the board and then confronting him.
17 The Fed intensifies its campaign to cool the economy with a
half-point rate increase and warns that rates could keep climbing this
summer.
Spain's Terra Networks will buy Lycos in a $12.5 billion deal that
marks the largest-ever combination of a telecom provider with an
Internet portal.
Mattel will appoint Robert A. Eckert chairman and chief executive.
The Kraft executive succeeds Jill Barad, who was forced out in

--- Page 166 ---
Page 166 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
February.
18 WorldCom's buyout of Sprint should be blocked, federal antitrust
enforcers are recommending, because the $115 billion deal would
violate antitrust law, according to lawyers and industry executives.
19 Columbia/HCA will pay the government $745 million to resolve
several Medicare-fraud allegations, including its handling of home
health care and its billing of laboratory claims.
New York Mayor Giuliani drops his U.S. Senate campaign for health
reasons.
24 South Carolina's governor signs legislation to remove the
Confederate flag from the statehouse dome, where it has flown for 38
years. The flag will be removed July 1, and a version of it will fly
at a nearby monument.
UAL, parent of United Airlines, agrees to acquire most of US Airways
for $4.3 billion in cash and $7.3 billion in assumed debt and leases,
creating an enormous carrier with 6,500 daily flights and multiple
hubs on both coasts.
25 Microsoft's antitrust trial comes to an abrupt close with Judge
Jackson signaling that he is prepared to support a breakup or other
sweeping sanctions against the big software maker.
JUNE
6 Philip Morris will pull ads for Marlboro and its other cigarette
brands from 40 to 50 magazines with large numbers of young readers.
7 Goldman Sachs ex-chief Jon Corzine wins New Jersey's Democratic
Senate primary, trouncing former Gov. Jim Florio in a double-digit
victory. Corzine spent a record $33 million, nearly all of it his own,
in the race.
Unilever clinches Bestfoods with a sweetened bid of $20.3 billion
plus the assumption of $4 billion in debt. The deal would make
Unilever the second-biggest food company after Nestle.
8 A federal judge orders Microsoft to be split in two and imposes
sweeping restrictions to stop antitrust abuses. The Justice Department
takes the rare step of asking the Supreme Court to review the order.
9 Health coverage for same-sex partners is to be extended by the Big
Three auto makers, a breakthrough in a long national debate that has
seen more and more large corporations providing such benefits. A total
of 96 of the Fortune 500 companies now offer domestic-partner benefits
or plan to do so, a gay-rights group says.
12 MP3.com reaches settlements with Warner Music and BMG over charges
of copyright infringement. The two big labels also agree to license
their catalogs of recordings to MP3.com.
16 AT&T completes its $44 billion bid for MediaOne, becoming the
nation's largest owner of cable-TV systems.
19 Tiger Woods won the 100th U.S. Open golf championship at Pebble
Beach, Calif., over the weekend by a record 15 strokes. His overall
score of 272 tied the record for the Open, and he was the first player
ever to finish at 12 under par.
20 The Supreme Court bars student-led prayers at school football
games.
Vivendi seals an agreement to acquire Seagram in a deal valued at
$34.4 billion. Vivendi also is purchasing the 51% stake it doesn't
already own in Canal Plus. Boards of all three companies approved the
transactions.
Pfizer concludes its takeover of Warner-Lambert, creating the world's
largest drug company, after the FTC approved the $116 billion deal.
21 The restrictions placed on Microsoft's business practices are
suspended by U.S. District Judge Jackson while the company's appeal of
the antitrust ruling is pending.
22 Martin Frankel is sentenced to three years by a German court for
not paying taxes on smuggled diamonds and carrying falsified passports.
26 Philip Morris agrees to acquire Nabisco Holdings for $55 a share,
or $14.91 billion, plus the assumption of $4 billion in debt, beating
a rival joint bid by Danone and Cadbury Schweppes.
27 Scientists decipher the human genetic code, a historic step in the
understanding of human biology and a powerful new tool to find cures
for diseases. The still-rough sequencing of the human genome is

--- Page 167 ---
Page 167 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
expected to accelerate the development of drugs and therapies to
combat disease.
29 In a New Jersey case, Supreme Court justices rule 5-4 that forcing
the Boy Scouts to accept gay leaders would violate the group's rights
of association and expression.
JULY
3 Mexico's voters swept opposition candidate Vicente Fox Quesada into
the presidency in a stunning landslide victory over the weekend. The
58-year-old ex-Coca-Cola executive and leader of the center-right PAN
beat the ruling party's Francisco Labastida and ended 71 years of PRI
rule.
The Nasdaq composite finished down 13.3%, or 606.72 points, to
3966.11 for the second quarter, while the Dow Jones industrials slid
4.3% to 10447.89.
6 Goldman Sachs unveils plans for a stock offering of 40 million
shares, currently valued at about $3.8 billion.
10 JDS Uniphase reaches an agreement to acquire rival fiber-optics
firm SDL for about $41 billion in stock.
11 An FBI e-mail surveillance system is stirring alarm by privacy
advocates. Called Carnivore, it secretly searches millions of e-mails
a second for messages from crime suspects. The bureau says such
wiretaps have been used in fewer than 100 cases, and require a judge's
order.
13 UBS's $10.25 billion bid for PaineWebber pairs a large mainstream
brokerage firm with a global financial-services giant. UBS will gain
access to 2.7 million private clients in the fast-growing market of
advising the wealthy.
14 Sprint-WorldCom merger is officially called off and Sprint's CEO,
William Esrey, says he isn't talking to any potential buyers and
insists that the company doesn't need a big merger partner.
Vietnam and the U.S. sign a historic agreement liberalizing trade
between the former foes after ironing out last-minute snags. The
accord leaves North Korea as the last big Asian nation that isn't
making moves to join the WTO.
17 Tobacco companies vow to appeal after a Florida jury delivers a
$144.87 billion punitive-damage award against them. The cigarette
firms say the penalty could bankrupt them, but a state law may force
it to be reduced.
18 A Nazi slave-laborer accord is signed by representatives of
Germany, the U.S., Russia, Israel, four Eastern European nations and
groups representing victims. Under it, a $5 billion compensation fund
is to be set up by German industry and government.
Florida and Georgia insurance regulators order 28 insurers to stop
collecting any higher premiums from African-Americans based on their
race.
24 Tiger Woods won the British Open golf tournament at St. Andrews,
Scotland, over the weekend with a record 19-under-par performance. At
24, he assumes Jack Nicklaus's mantle as the youngest person to win
all four of golf's top events.
26 A Concorde crashes after takeoff outside Paris, killing 113
people. The supersonic Air France jet with 109 aboard was bound for
New York. France and Britain immediately order the world's fleet of 13
Concordes grounded.
Mideast talks fail over the issue of who will have control of
Jerusalem. U.S. officials say the summit had seen extraordinary
progress on the issues of the shape of a Palestinian state and the
return of refugees.
Bush picks Richard Cheney as his running mate in a move viewed as
cautious. The former defense secretary is known for judgment,
discretion and loyalty, but is untested as a national campaigner,
carries the baggage of a history of heart trouble and, as Halliburton
CEO, adds to the Bush campaign's image of closeness to the oil
industry.
27 Napster is ordered to stop making much of its music available for
downloading by the weekend, thus shutting the most popular service of
the controversial Internet site. But the music-sharing site gets a

--- Page 168 ---
Page 168 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
reprieve from immediate closure a few days later that gives it a
chance to find ways to withstand an injunction that might remove
recordings from big record labels from its service.
A Holocaust settlement is approved by a federal judge in New York,
paving the way for $1.25 billion in reparations by Swiss banks to
victims of Nazi persecution. But any of the more than 550,000
claimants could file an appeal, which could hold up payments for many.
28 Bank of America plans to cut 10,000 jobs, or 7% of its work force,
as part of a restructuring.
31 FPL Group and Entergy agree to combine in a $7 billion stock deal,
plus $6.9 billion of debt, in an effort to create the U.S.'s largest
electric company.
The Nasdaq composite crumbled 4.66%, or 179.23 points, to 3663.00 on
Friday, leaving it down 10.5% for the week. The Dow Jones industrials,
less exposed to tech stocks, slid 74.96 points to 10511.17.
AUGUST
1 Prime Minister Ehud Barak, under fire for concessions he offered to
make at Camp David, survives a confidence vote, but his candidate for
Israel's president loses.
4 George W. Bush, accepting the Republican Party's presidential
nomination, vows to be "a president with a purpose" and to seize a
"unique moment" of prosperity to cut taxes, rebuild defenses and
reform Social Security, Medicare and education.
Microsoft is formally accused by the European Commission of seeking
to corner the global market for software that runs powerful "server"
computers. The action comes as the software giant still faces a
two-way breakup ordered by a U.S. judge.
7 The unions representing 87,200 employees of Verizon call a strike
after the two sides failed to reach a contract agreement for the
unionized workers of the former Bell Atlantic. But Verizon and its
unions remain at the bargaining table following a last-minute proposal
from the company that addresses organizing wireless workers.
8 Arthur Andersen is told to break up with Andersen Consulting,
ending a bitter, nearly decade-long dispute. An international
arbitrator also orders Andersen Consulting to pay about $1 billion to
Arthur Andersen and to give up its name. Andersen Worldwide, the
parent of both firms, is strongly criticized by the arbitrator for
failing to supervise its units.
Gore selects Sen. Joseph Lieberman of Connecticut to be his running
mate. Lieberman, 58, is the first Jew chosen to run on a major party's
presidential ticket.
9 Chile's high court strips Gen. Augusto Pinochet of his immunity
from prosecution. The decision provokes fierce reactions by allies and
foes of the ex-dictator.
10 Bridgestone/Firestone offers to replace 6.5 million tires, mainly
on Ford light trucks and SUVs, in the second-largest tire recall ever.
Bridgestone/Firestone admits it doesn't readily have that many tires on
hand. Parent company Bridgestone will record a $345 million charge to
cover the recall's costs.
11 The SEC approves a rule intended to make corporate information
more readily available to individual investors.
14 News Corp. agrees to acquire the TV-station empire operated by
Chris-Craft Industries for more than $5.3 billion, beating out rival
Viacom. The deal likely sounds the death knell for Viacom's UPN
television network.
15 Russia scrambles to try to save 116 men trapped in a sunken
nuclear submarine. The Kursk went down in about 354 feet of water
during naval exercises in the Barents Sea over the weekend. Some of
the crew is believed to have survived the initial explosion.
16 Viacom offers $15.17 billion in stock to acquire the 5.7% of radio
giant Infinity Broadcasting that it doesn't already own.
All Concordes are grounded putting the supersonic jets' future in
doubt. Britain and France have decided to suspend the airworthiness
certificates for the entire fleet. The planes will remain in the
hangar until the safety of tires and wheels can be upgraded after last
month's crash of an Air France Concorde.

--- Page 169 ---
Page 169 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
18 Al Gore accepts the Democratic presidential nomination "as my own
man." In a list of proposals outlined over a long, rousing address
that capped the party's convention, he appeals to middle-class swing
voters as well as his own base and used issues ranging from tax cuts
to school vouchers to Medicare to draw contrasts with Republicans.
22 Russia declares the crew of the Kursk dead as Norwegian divers
entered the sunken submarine and found it completely flooded. The
Russian submarine sinking has hurt President Putin's image of can-do
professionalism as his countrymen watch a chaotic rescue effort and
receive conflicting official statements on the situation.
24 Verizon reaches an accord with a holdout CWA division covering
35,000 workers in the mid-Atlantic region, ending an 18-day strike.
The deal is almost identical to a pact reached with 37,000 CWA members
in the Northeast.
25 Lernout & Hauspie's CEO, Gaston Bastiaens, agrees to step down,
and John Duerden will succeed him. The voice-recognition software
firm's rapid growth has drawn investor skepticism.
28 The SEC and other agencies are investigating who was behind a hoax
news release warning of bad news at Emulex. Its shares fell 60%
following the "news," slicing the firm's market cap by $2.45 billion,
but its stock later recovered. It is discovered that an ex-employee of
Internet Wire is allegedly behind the hoax.
Cheney pledges that if he is elected he will ensure he doesn't
benefit from rises in the value of Halliburton stock through options
that have yet to mature.
29 A federal judge backs American Home Products' plan to pay as much
as $3.75 billion to settle injury claims over its once-popular
"fen-phen" diet pills.
Ford Motor Co. is facing new questions from Venezuelan authorities
about the safety of its Explorer sport-utility vehicle, even as it
rushes to line up more replacements for recalled Firestone tires
mounted on its U.S. SUVs.
30 Credit Suisse First Boston agrees to buy Donaldson, Lufkin &
Jenrette for $11.5 billion. A deal would narrow the gap between CSFB
and its two big U.S. rivals, Goldman and Morgan Stanley, while giving
DLJ a greater global reach. DLJ would be transferred from Paris-based
AXA, which owns 70% of DLJ, to Zurich's Credit Suisse Group, parent of
CSFB.
Sweden's OM Gruppen launches a hostile $1.19 billion bid to buy the
London Stock Exchange. The LSE, which rejected the offer, is forced to
delay a planned vote by its holders on whether to merge with
Frankfurt's exchange.
31 Indonesia's Suharto went on trial for graft, but his lawyer says a
medical team pronounced him too ill to attend. The ousted president is
accused of diverting at least $570 million from charities he ran.
SEPTEMBER
1 Concorde-crash investigators release a preliminary report on the
July 25 crash that blames debris damage from a tire ruptured by a
piece of metal on the Paris runway.
5 In Venezuela, Firestone agrees to recall its Wilderness tires on
models other than Ford Explorers. Separately, Firestone and its
steelworkers union reach a tentative accord averting a strike by more
than 8,000 workers.
7 Bridgestone/Firestone compiled evidence of tire safety problems at
least eight months before its huge recall in the U.S., but failed to
take any action. Internal Firestone documents, which chart a sharp
rise in damage and injury claims involving the tires in the late
1990s, are released during congressional hearings.
Citigroup agrees to buy Associates First Capital, the U.S.'s biggest
public consumer-finance firm, for $29.83 billion in stock. The
purchase gives Citigroup control of Associates' international
franchise catering to individuals.
11 An FTC report details the entertainment industry's marketing
practices. The yearlong study finds that music, movie, computer and
video-game companies routinely promote products that contain parental
warnings in venues where children are likely to see the advertising.

Page 170 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Russia's Marat Safin beat Pete Sampras, 6-4, 6-3, 6-3, in the men's
final of the U.S. Open tennis championships over the weekend. On
Saturday, Venus Williams captured the women's title that her sister
Serena won last year, by defeating Lindsay Davenport, 6-4, 7-5.
12 Oil prices spike to a new post-Gulf War high of $35.14 a barrel,
despite OPEC's promise to raise output by 800,000 barrels a day.
Feeling pressure from the U.S., some OPEC officials said they agreed
to boost production even though their own experts say there is enough
oil already on the market.
Goldman Sachs is set to become the New York Stock Exchange's biggest
specialist firm after it confirmed it would buy Spear Leeds for $6.5
billion.
13 Protests over high fuel taxes have crippled transportation across
Europe, but British Prime Minister Tony Blair insists the government
won't follow France in granting concessions. The top five gasoline
retailers in Britain say more than 4,000 of their 6,000 stations had
either run dry or were rapidly running out of supplies.
Hillary Clinton wins the New York Senate Democratic primary, becoming
the only U.S. first lady to be a candidate in a national election.
Chase Manhattan agrees to acquire J.P. Morgan for roughly $36 billion
in stock, a deal that would bring together two institutions with rich
histories. The new entity, to be called J.P. Morgan Chase, would have
a combined $675 billion in assets.
14 Wen Ho Lee is released from jail after pleading guilty to one of
59 counts. The Los Alamos research scientist suspected of espionage
received an apology from a federal judge after pleading guilty to
unlawful possession of defense information. Lee, accused of stealing
nuclear secrets, was facing life in prison. The judge says Lee's
treatment had embarrassed the country in a scathing criticism of "top
decision makers" at the Justice and Energy departments.
15 Bush agrees to three October debates with Gore, breaking an
impasse. The agreement, originally proposed by the bipartisan
Commission on Presidential Debates, includes one debate for their
running mates.
British protesters dismantle their blockade of the country's oil
refineries. They claim a moral victory by drawing attention to the
issue of fuel taxes, though no immediate concessions from the
government are won.
18 Peru's president says he will call new elections and won't be a
candidate. Fujimori's announcement came amid international outcry
following the release of a video showing his intelligence chief
bribing a congressman.
19 The European Commission drafts a preliminary recommendation to
block AOL's merger with Time Warner, partly because of concerns over
Time Warner's own plans to create a music joint venture with Britain's
EMI Group. The EMI venture also faces antitrust objections from the FTC.
Oil prices soar to a new post-Gulf War high, climbing 96 cents to
$36.88 a barrel.
20 Ford documents suggest its popular Explorer was designed with
little safety margin as to how incorrect tire size, tire pressure or
even tire tread could increase the SUV's rollover risk. Federal safety
regulators receive reports of 15 more deaths linked to faulty Firestone
tires, bringing to 103 the number of U.S. fatalities connected to the
investigation.
The Nasdaq composite stages its biggest one-day percentage gain since
early June, rising 139.12 points, or 3.73%, to 3865.64.
The Senate passes the China trade bill in a foreign-policy victory
for Clinton. The historic vote, 83-15, gives China permanent normal
trade relations and ends America's 20-year annual review of China's
trade status.
21 The Whitewater probe ends without charges against the Clintons.
Independent counsel Robert Ray, who succeeded Kenneth Starr in the
record $52 million investigation, says there was "insufficient
evidence" to prove "beyond a reasonable doubt" that either the
president or Hillary Clinton had committed a crime.
The SEC nabs 15-year-old Jonathan G. Lebed, of New Jersey, for using

--- Page 171 ---
Page 171 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
the Internet to manipulate small stocks, reaping $272,826. It is the
first time the SEC has alleged that a minor committed stock fraud.
22 President Clinton says he will release 30 million barrels of oil
from the U.S.'s emergency reserve. Oil prices dropped $1.67 to $32.73
a barrel. A day earlier, Gore proposed tapping emergency oil reserves
to push down fuel prices. Bush denounced the plan.
Bridgestone/Firestone urges Ford to boost the recommended tire
pressure on its Explorer SUV in the name of customer safety. A
Firestone official says Ford's recommendation of 26 psi leaves an
inadequate margin of safety. Ford later reverses itself and says it
would recommend that owners inflate their 15-inch Firestone tires to
30 psi instead of 26 psi.
Nearly all of Lernout & Hauspie's revenue last year in Singapore came
from 19 companies that the voice-recognition software concern helped to
create. An SEC probe is examining L&H's rapid sales growth in Singapore.
25 The U.S. joins Europe, Japan and Canada in a coordinated effort to
prop up the euro, buying $3 billion to $5 billion of euros. The
intervention halts a 21-month slide of the common currency, which rose
to 87.84 cents to the dollar.
Sotheby's and Christie's agree to pay $512 million to settle claims
by art buyers and sellers that the auctioneers colluded on prices and
cheated them, with nearly one-third of the tab to be paid by Sotheby's
former chairman.
U.S. sprinters Marion Jones won gold in the women's 100 meters and
Maurice Greene won gold in the men's 100 meters on Saturday at the
Olympic Games in Sydney.
26 Milosevic refuses to accept defeat as rival Vojislav Kostunica
claims the Yugoslav presidency. He later concedes.
27 A "Carnivore" inquiry will be conducted by a nonprofit research
group associated with the Illinois Institute of Technology. It is to
study whether the FBI e-mail surveillance device has adequate
safeguards against abuse.
The Supreme Court decides against an early review of the Microsoft
antitrust case, delaying a final judgment for as long as two years and
freeing the company to enter new markets unshackled by the courts. The
case will be reviewed by the federal appeals court in Washington,
D.C., which ruled in Microsoft's favor in an earlier case.
28 Ford plans to equip the bulk of its next-generation Explorer SUVs
with Michelin tires, abandoning its decade-long reliance on tires made
by Bridgestone/Firestone.
29 An Indonesia court ends the graft case against Suharto, ruling him
too ill for a lengthy trial.
The abortion pill RU-486 wins FDA approval after a 12-year battle,
provoking antiabortion groups' ire.
Elian Gonzalez's Miami relatives sue Attorney General Reno over the
April 22 raid on their home that reunited the boy with his Cuban
family. The suit says agents had a faulty warrant and used excessive
force.
The Dow Jones industrials rise 195.70 points, or 1.84%, to 10824.06,
for the best point or percentage gain since May 30. The Nasdaq
composite jumps by 122.02 points, or 3.34%, to 3778.32.

led the medal count, winning 39 gold and 97 overall. Russia is second
with 32 gold and 88 overall, then China with 28 gold and 59 overall.

in Exxon Valdez damages.
Texas Gov. George W. Bush and Vice President Al Gore clash on tax

in Boston.
4 The Federal Reserve lets rates stand, reflecting summer's economic
slowdown. But rising energy prices and tight labor markets prevent

in Kentucky.

Priceline.com's founder is closing his WebHouse service for grocery
and gasoline sales after running out of money, raising questions about

foreign affairs and sensitive social issues in soft tones in their
second presidential debate.

that some contain genetically modified corn.
The European Union gave AOL and Time Warner the green light on their
$117.8 billion merger on the condition that they scrap some ties with

13 The Dow Jones Industrials plummet 379.21 points, or 3.6%, to
10034.58, in the index's fifth-biggest point drop ever amid upheaval

third-period earnings. The Nasdaq composite sinks 93.81, or 3%, to
3074.68, 39% off its high set in March. Crude-oil prices top $36 a

Seventeen U.S. sailors are killed and dozens injured in an apparent
suicide-bombing attack on the USS Cole, a Navy destroyer docked in
Aden, Yemen.
16 South Korea celebrated the awarding Friday of the Nobel Peace
Prize to Kim Dae Jung. The 74-year-old president survived years of
house arrest and assassination attempts to steer his nation toward
reconciliation with North Korea.
Texaco's board approves Chevron's $35.1 billion stock proposal. The
deal would create the world's fourth-largest oil company, to be called

17 Texas Gov. George W. Bush and Vice President Al Gore jab and
circle in St. Louis in their last and most fiery debate. The debate
comes a day after Missouri Gov. Mel Carnahan's death in a plane crash
that also killed his son and an aide.
19 The Dow Jones Industrials slide 114.69 points, or 1.14%, to
9975.02, after falling as much as 435.07 early in a frenzied day of
trading. It is the blue-chip index's first close below 10000 since
March 14.
25 AT&T's board approves a proposal that would keep its core consumer
long-distance and business services together and create new firms for
cable and wireless. The plan would eventually break up the
telecommunications giant, with all four businesses trading under
different stock symbols.
31 The widow of Missouri Gov. Carnahan agrees to serve if her husband
wins a Senate race posthumously.
NOVEMBER
1 Stocks climb on strength in the technology sector and the end of
tax-driven selling by mutual funds. The Nasdaq surges 178.15, or 5.6%;
the Dow Jones industrials rise 135.37, or 1.3%.

Milosevic.
3 The international space station opens for business as the first

They christen their new home Alpha, but the name may not be permanent.
Bush takes a late blow as a 1976 drunken-driving conviction surfaces.
6 Two major tobacco companies, Lorillard Tobacco and Liggett Group,

against them in nearly a dozen lawsuits.

9 The election is stuck in a historic limbo as a Florida recount
proceeds. Soon after conceding defeat, Gore learned the decisive
Florida contest had narrowed to 1,725 votes and, in a testy phone

10 Lernout & Hauspie says it expects to revise its financial
statements for 1998, 1999 and the first half of 2000 to make up for

13 Austria tries to recover skiers' bodies after a tunnel fire that
killed about 170. The cause of the blaze, which broke out in a cable
car that climbs to the Kitzsteinhorn glacier near Kaprun over the
weekend, isn't yet known.
14 The Nadaq closes below 3000 for the first time in over a year. A
morning plunge is sparked by election uncertainty and a weak earnings
report by H-P, which trades on the Big Board but affects Nasdaq tech
stocks.
15 DaimlerChrysler plans to oust James Holden as head of Chrysler,
replacing him with Dieter Zetsche. Though the combination of the auto
makers was once billed as a "merger of equals," the move makes it
clear Chrysler is now a subordinate unit to Daimler's German
headquarters.
16 Florida's top electoral official, a close Bush ally, says she
won't amend her tally despite hand counts under way in at least two
counties. The official earlier lost a bid to get the state's high
court to halt the recounts. The move comes after a judge's admonition
to use "discretion" in deciding whether to accept late-running hand
recounts.
The SEC approves rules requiring Wall Street to disclose how stock
orders are filled. The move could help investors calculate when they
are charged more than they realize for trading.
17 Coke agrees to pay $192.5 million to settle a race-bias suit and
says it would submit its employment practices to a high level of
outside scrutiny.
Chrysler will fail to meet its profit target for 2000 and is likely
to report a fourth-quarter loss.
21 Time Warner strikes a deal with EarthLink that lets the Internet
provider offer its services on the media giant's high-speed cable
lines. The deal is a crucial step in getting government approval for
the AOL merger. The FTC must clear the Internet deal before it will
consider approving the merger.
22 Florida's high court orders hand tallies accepted under a tight
deadline. Only those given to the secretary of state by 5 p.m. Sunday
will be folded into the state's total.
24 Gore is set back when Miami-Dade, citing time constraints, ended
its manual tally Wednesday and Florida's high court, on Thanksgiving,
refused to compel one.
Cheney suffers a slight heart attack, his fourth.
27 Florida certifies a Bush victory, but state suits and the Supreme
Court await. The Republican gives what amounted to an acceptance
speech after Gore, despite gains in manual tallies, officially falls
537 votes short.

move makes the 44-year-old head of GE's medical-systems business heir
to Chairman and CEO John Welch, who said last month that he will stay
on the job as long as the end of 2001 to oversee the Honeywell
acquisition.

7 Newspaper publishers are facing a weak fourth-quarter advertising

11 The jobless rate is beginning to rise after hovering near a

20 The Federal Reserve abandons its anti-inflation stance, declaring

21 Stocks plunge, virtually ensuring that 2000 will end up as the

Year-End Review of Markets & Finance 2000---A Year of Living Dangerously---Though Nasdaq Was Massacred,Dow, S&P 500 Declines MissedMeasur...
Year-End Review of Markets & Finance 2000 --- A Year of Living Dangerously --- Though Nasdaq Was
Massacred, Dow, S&P 500 Declines Missed Measuring Stick for Bear Market
By Greg Ip
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,986 words
2 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
INVESTORS HAVE JUST lived through one of the most extraordinary years in stock-market history.
Last year's massacre of technology and Internet stocks marked the end of what many consider the U.S.'s
biggest financial mania of the last 100 years. Yet it was a massacre that left much of the market relatively
unscathed.
The Nasdaq Composite Index plunged 39.3% to 2470.52, the worst year since it was created in 1971, giving
back almost all of 1999's record 86% rocket ride. Its peak-to-trough 54% plunge represented a loss of $3.3
trillion in paper wealth, equivalent (in dollars if not in effect) to one-third of the houses in America sliding into
the ocean.
But the Dow Jones Industrial Average fell only 6.2% to 10786.85 for the year. Though that broke a nine-year
winning streak and represented its worst calendar year since 1981, the Dow's peak-to-trough decline of just
16% was less than that of 1990, failing to meet the 20% bear market rule of thumb. The Standard & Poor's
500-stock index lost 10.1% to 1320.28 last year, its worst since 1977. But excluding its technology
components, according to ABN Amro strategist John Mullin, the index was down just 4%. Indeed, the Dow
and the S&P 500 have given up less than a sixth of what they made when they tripled between 1995 and
1999.
"We've proven we can liquidate the excess and still not destroy the entire market," marvels Jim Paulsen, chief
investment officer of Wells Capital Management.
By contrast, the 1973-74 bear market clawed everything. The Nasdaq's 60% slide then was the only time it
has fallen further than last year. The Dow lost 45% and the S&P 500 48%, ending 1974 at their lowest levels
since 1963.
The new year begins with investors wondering if Nasdaq's bloodletting is the vanguard of a broad-based bear
market. The answer depends mostly on whether the economy's downshift in recent months is a pause in the
longest expansion in a century or the first stage of a recession.
So far, much of Wall Street opts for the former view. Just as the Federal Reserve's raising interest rates
through last May eventually sank stocks and braked the speeding economy, economists expect it to lower
rates soon and propel stocks and the economy forward again.
UBS Warburg strategist Edward Kerschner thinks we are at one of the five most-attractive opportunities to
own stocks in 20 years, and he predicts the S&P 500 will gain 30% this year. Indeed, strategists as a group
are the most bullish they have been in the 16 years Merrill Lynch has surveyed them. Merrill's head of
quantitative research, Richard Bernstein, finds this enthusiasm both ironic, given that cash and bonds both
trounced stocks last year, and sobering, since markets usually bottom at the point of maximum pessimism,
not optimism.
Indeed, there are some gloomier forecasters who think last year set the stage not for a big rally but for
several years of miserable stock performance. That is because the eight-year boom in technology investment
that boosted productivity, kept inflation low and drove up stocks -- especially technology stocks -- also
created enormous production capacity and has drawn in new competitors that could weigh heavily on profits
for years to come.
Most of last year, it looked less like a bear market and more like a bubble deflating. From a low of 1419.12 in
October 1998 after the debt markets crisis to its closing high of 5048.62 last March 10, Nasdaq climbed
256%. Based on the magnitude of the rise, the disconnect between valuation and economic reality, the
number of ordinary investors drawn in and the sheer money involved, many market historians rank the

--- Page 177 ---
Page 177 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
technology/Internet bubble ahead of earlier U.S. manias such as radio and investment trusts in the 1920s,
conglomerate stocks in the late 1960s and early 1970s and oil stocks and gold in the late 1970s and early
1980s.
Yahoo! rose from below $30 in fall 1998 to $250 last January when it was valued at $133 billion -- more than
Ford Motor and General Motors combined. At year end it was back to $30.06. And Yahoo is a profitable
company. Web-page technology provider Akamai Technologies lost $56 million on sales of $4 million in 1999,
went public in October that year, and was valued at $32 billion by January -- more than Texaco. It since has
fallen 94%.
"The first half of the year was the blowing off of speculative excess in the Net sector," says Henry Blodget,
Merrill Lynch's Internet analyst, adding, "From 1995 to 1998, most investors actually underestimated the
power of the Internet. In 1999, people began to overestimate it. But what was interesting in mid-2000, was the
excesses moved into other sectors: business-to-business, then infrastructure, then jumped into optics."
But Mr. Blodget also points out that as Internet valuations deflated, many solid, profitable New Economy
companies proved highly exposed to the Internet bubble. "The average Net start-up raised about $100
million," he says. "Of that, $5 million went to Scient or another professional services firm, $10 million to Yahoo
or America Online, $5 million to Vignette or other software providers, a couple million to Exodus or another
[Web] hoster, a couple million to Sun [Microsystems], a couple million to Dell."
As the stock market turned off the spigot to money-losing Internet companies and the junk-bond market did
the same to capital-hungry telecommunications companies, the likes of Yahoo and Lucent Technologies
began to feel the pinch. Investors began questioning not just the price being paid for technology companies'
earnings [the P/E ratio], but the earnings themselves. Mr. Blodget had thought Yahoo would ring up $1.8
billion to $2 billion in sales this year, "Now I'll be happy if they could do $1.5 billion," he says. In August, Wall
Street expected Lucent to earn more than $4 billion in its current fiscal year, which ends in September. Now it
expects Lucent to lose more than $700 million.
But outside technology, most stocks were unscathed, and some even boomed. Old Economy stalwarts like
Minnesota Mining and Manufacturing and Exxon Mobil were up 23% and 8%. Utility and health-care stocks
were even more buoyant, with the Dow Jones Utility Average rising 45% and Merck up 39%.
To be sure, wealth has been lost, but more important, it has been shuffled. At the end of March, Microsoft,
Cisco Systems and Intel were the first, second and fourth most-valuable companies in the U.S. At the end of
the year, Cisco was fourth, behind General Electric, Exxon Mobil and Pfizer; Microsoft sixth, behind Wal-Mart
Stores; and Intel 10th, behind Citigroup, American International Group and Merck.
It was a humbling year for bulls. Lehman Brothers' chief investment strategist Jeffrey Applegate, whose
bullishness on stocks, and technology in particular, had served his clients well through the late 1990s, titled a
report in November, "Mea culpa." In March, he considered pulling back on his technology recommendation,
but "I just decided I'm a rotten sector rotator, so why bother." But since March, excess greed has turned to
excess fear, and Mr. Applegate thinks technology will come back.
Valuations have been corrected, and the high-priced stocks still standing, like Cisco and EMC, both now at
about 100 times trailing earnings, can remain high-priced for years just as International Business Machines,
Xerox and Polaroid did for most of the 1960s, he argues. Mr. Applegate thinks overall corporate profits will
grow 7% this year, and he predicts demand for technology will again outpace overall economic growth.
If he is right, then the long-term bull market that many think began when inflation was tamed in the early
1980s is still intact. [The bear market in 1990 was one of the shortest on record.] Between 1980 and 1999,
the Dow had only three down years -- 1981, 1984 and 1990 -- as did the S&P 500 -- 1981, 1990 and 1994.
The year following each witnessed double-digit returns.
But there is a school of thought that last year might mark the beginning of a long stretch of dismal stock
returns. The market's price/earnings ratio rose from 14 in 1990 to 28 in 1999, which means the profit
investors demanded for every $100 in stocks fell to $3.50 from $7 over the same period. At the same time,
corporate-bond yields fell. Combined, this meant the cost of capital plummeted. That, argues Ben Inker,
director of asset allocation at Boston money manager Grantham, Mayo, Van Otterloo & Co., meant rational
companies made investments that posted ever-lower returns.
"If P/Es remain high, it means companies have a permanently low cost of capital [which] is only consistent
with a permanently low return on capital. It's how capitalism is supposed to work."
In fact, returns on investments -- whether in specialty retailers, sport-utility vehicles,
semiconductor-fabrication plants or broadband networks -- were quite high because demand has been so
strong. But late last year, overcapacity began popping up throughout the economy and demand slowed
sharply, and sector by sector, profit margins are being squeezed. "Certainly AT&T and Lucent are the

--- Page 178 ---
Page 178 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
casualties today," says Mr. Inker. "But all the way along we are seeing companies finally coming to the
realization that if you have huge increases in investment and therefore capacity, that is horrific for profit
margins."
For the current decade, Mr. Inker is bullish on the economy, but thinks stocks overall, in part because of their
still historically high P/E ratios (especially in technology), will return next to nothing in the next 10 years, after
inflation.
And what if the economy does stumble? The falling stock market has pinched household wealth and raised
the cost of capital, endangering both consumer spending and investment. Mr. Bernstein of Merrill thinks
profits could actually fall this year, although declining interest rates could offset the impact on stock prices.
In fact, rate cuts are historically one of the most consistently bullish signals. According to Ed Keon of
Prudential Securities, stocks have returned 20% annualized on average during 12 easing periods since 1953,
and in only two were stocks lower six months after the first rate cut.
Furthermore, the bear market in small and "value" stocks -- those with low prices relative to earnings or book
value -- began back in 1998. They hit bottom early last year, just as the technology bear market got going.
"Knowing the market's tendency to return to the mean, value investors will be the ones who make the most
money in the next few years," says Robert Farrell, Merrill's senior investment adviser.
But technology stocks won't recover until their current owners give up on them, he says. At the peak, some
75% of the money in Fidelity Investments' sector funds was in technology, and that had only dropped to 58%
by mid-December, he says. "I would look for that to go down maybe to 30% or 40%" to represent a bottom in
sentiment.
Another sign of capitulation will be when Wall Street tells people to avoid technology stocks. On that front, Mr.
Farrell has spotted some early signs. On Friday, Dec. 15, eToys warned holiday sales were dismal and said it
might not survive on its own. The following Monday, ABN Amro analyst Kevin Silverman put a sell
recommendation on the stock -- at 28 cents, down 99.7% from its all-time high.

Five-Year Views: Blue Skies or Storms?
Getting Going
Five-Year Views: Blue Skies or Storms?
By Jonathan Clements
859 words
2 January 2001
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2001, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
STOCK INVESTORS SHOULD EXPECT success and hope they are wrong.
The optimism is justified, because history shows that stocks rise over time. (Indeed, I suspect shares will post
modest gains in the year ahead.) Still, continued turmoil wouldn't be bad, because it gives us the chance to
buy shares at lower prices.
So what will it be, blue skies or stormy weather? I asked five prognosticators to forecast how Standard &
Poor's 500-stock index will perform over the next five years, which is often considered the minimum time
horizon for anybody venturing into stocks.
Below are our pundits' forecasts, starting with the most bullish:
Finding Favor: Over long periods, stocks have outpaced inflation by seven percentage points a year, notes
Jeremy Siegel, a finance professor at the University of Pennsylvania's Wharton School.
"The return on the S&P 500 in the future probably won't be higher than that," he says. "Valuations are still
high from a historical standard. But there are some favorable factors."
Mr. Siegel points out that the capital-gains tax rate is low, which is good for stock investors. Indeed,
companies are taking advantage of that low rate, by buying back shares rather than paying dividends. Mr.
Siegel also notes that inflation is subdued and that the cost of buying and maintaining a diversified portfolio
has fallen, thanks to plunging commission rates and the availability of low-cost stock funds.
"My feeling is that we might get a real rate of return between 5% and 8%," Mr. Siegel says. "If you add 3%
inflation, you might get between 8% and 11%. That's not fantastic. But it's better than government bonds."
Split Decision: "It's probable that the market will be higher five years from now," reckons Peter Bernstein, a
New York-based economic consultant to institutional investors. "Is the case for a higher market so compelling
that we can expect stocks to be higher by enough to compensate for the probability that my forecast will be
wrong? I'm uncertain, because we start from a base that is still high."
Mr. Bernstein, author of "The Power of Gold: The History of an Obsession," figures stocks will deliver three or
four percentage points a year more than inflation over the next five years.
He says that Treasury-bond investors will probably earn comparable returns, and that high-yield junk bond
investors could do even better. "If conditions are good enough for stocks to be higher in 2005, how about junk
bonds that give us double-digit yields right away?" Mr. Bernstein asks.
Speculating on Speculation: "There are only two sources of stock-market return," says John Bogle, founder of
Vanguard Group in Malvern, Pa. "One is investment return, which is relatively predictable. The other is
speculative return, which is highly unpredictable."
Mr. Bogle calculates the likely investment return by adding together the starting dividend yield, currently just
over 1%, and annual earnings growth, which Mr. Bogle pegs at 8%. Combine those two numbers and you get
just over 9%.
But for investors to earn that return, stock valuations would have to remain unchanged. Mr. Bogle thinks that
is unlikely. He looks for the market's price-earnings multiple to fall below 20 over the next five years, which
would knock six percentage points a year off the market's return. Result? Investors will collect just 3% a year.
"You should be getting yourself emotionally prepared for much lower returns than we've had in the past," Mr.
Bogle says.

--- Page 180 ---
Page 180 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Thanks for Nothing: Like Mr. Bogle, money manager Robert Arnott starts by analyzing earnings, dividends
and possible changes in price-earnings multiples. But Mr. Arnott reaches an even more dire conclusion, in
part because he thinks annual earnings growth will be just 4% or 5%.
"Our expectation is that the return for the S&P 500 over the next five years should be zero or less," says Mr.
Arnott, managing partner of First Quadrant in Pasadena, Calif.
He suggests going light on stocks and heavier on bonds, especially inflation-indexed Treasury bonds. "I
would be at least 10 percentage points below what your normal [stock] allocation would be," Mr. Arnott says.
Still, he finds some stocks attractive. "If you look at the 20% of the market that has the lowest price-earnings
ratio, you are looking at average P/E ratios of around eight," Mr. Arnott says. "Value stocks across the board
look awfully interesting."
Look Out Below: If Mr. Arnott seems pessimistic, listen to Robert Shiller, an economics professor at Yale
University and author of "Irrational Exuberance."
"Depending on which measure you use, stocks are at maybe twice or more the value they would normally
have based on fundamentals," Mr. Shiller says. "It's quite possible that we could have negative returns for the
next five or even 10 years."
But he also notes that stock performance is hard to predict, especially over short periods. "It's a crazy
market," he says. "The day-to-day and year-to-year performance is largely a psychological phenomenon."

EUROPE/AFRICA
Euro-Zone Inflation,
At Seven-Year High,
May Have Peaked
Consumer prices in the euro zone in November surged 2.9% from a year earlier, the quickest pace in nearly
seven years but most likely a recent peak as energy prices ease. But December inflation figures, due today
from German states and Italian cities, are expected to paint a more benign picture, with a decline in crude-oil
prices and fears of mad-cow disease reducing the price of beef. Economists expect a decline in year-to-year
German inflation to 2.2%, from November's 2.4%; Italian inflation is expected to slip to 2.7% from 2.9%.
The European Central Bank aims to keep inflation below 2% in the euro zone's 11 countries.
The ECB today will also publish for the first time macroeconomic projections compiled by the central bank's
staff. The ECB will announce a range for euro-zone inflation for the next two years. It will also announce a
range for growth in gross domestic product and break it down into four components: exports, imports,
consumption and investment. The forecasts will be based on constant foreign-exchange and interest rates.
The forecasts will show why the ECB continues to believe the risks to price stability are still on the upside.
Nigeria Awards Oil-Exploration Blocks
Eight companies won bids for highly sought Nigerian oil-exploration blocks. The allocations were the first
through an open-bidding tender in line with Nigerian President Olusegun Obasanjo's drive for transparency.
The eight blocks, some in deep offshore waters, are also crucial to Nigeria's hopes of raising its oil production
to four million barrels a day, from just over two million barrels currently. Chevron of the U.S. won the bid for
the most sought-after block, OPL250, a deep-water acreage, and will operate it with Royal Dutch/Shell
Group, said Mr. Obasanjo's energy adviser, Rilwanu Lukman. Other major winners included Exxon Mobil and
Brazil's Petrobas. Italy's Agip won a bid on a block that it will operate on behalf of Nigerian Petroleum
Development, a unit of state oil company NNPC. Phillips Petroleum of the U.S. and Nigeria's Obekpa also
won bids. "Based on the estimates of premium payments expected from the companies to which the offers
were made, a total of about $900 million is expected to accrue to government," Mr. Lukman said.
Portuguese Mobile Licenses Awarded
Portugal's three current wireless operators and the OniWay consortium, led by national power company
Electricidade de Portugal, won licenses for third-generation mobile-phone services. Portugal's
telecommunications regulator, after assessing seven bids, awarded licenses to Telecel, controlled by Britain's
Vodafone Group; Portugal Telecom's TMN unit; Portuguese industrial conglomerate Sonae's Optimus unit, in
which France Telecom also holds a stake; and OniWay, the consortium led by EdP and including Norway's
Telenor. The winners will each pay 100 million euros ($89.4 million) for the 15-year concessions. The four
winners must now focus on getting services up and running by 2002 and financing what is expected to be a
2.5 billion euro investment for each company.
Energis to Buy Control of Germany's Ision
Britain's Energis agreed to acquire a 75% stake in German Internet company Ision in a cash and stock deal
valued at 812 million euros ($725.7 million). Energis in 1993 began offering telecommunications services to
businesses that piggybacked on the electricity-distributing network of Britain's National Grid Group, which
holds a 33% stake in Energis. But some observers had doubted Energis could mount a successful
pan-European strategy. Analysts said the acquisition of the 75% stake in Ision from Switzerland's Distefora

Holding for 210 million euros in cash and new Energis shares valued at 602 million euros would go a long
way to addressing those concerns.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Nisshin Steel Plans Further Staff Cuts
Japan's Nisshin Steel plans to cut its work force by 1,000 employees over the three years from April 2001 to
March 2004. a company spokesman confirmed. Nisshin Steel plans to cut its work force to 3,500 by March
2004, down from about 4,500 expected by next March 31, and in line with its current plan calling for the work
force to be cut from 5,620 employees in March 1999. "The move is part of the three-year business plan,
which we will probably announce early next year," he said.
U.S., Singapore Trade Pact Draws Fire
Hopes that a U.S.-Singapore free-trade agreement could be signed before the Clinton administration leaves
office are dimming as Republican lawmakers raise objections and influential trade lobbies argue over its
content. Senate Majority Leader Trent Lott (R., Miss.), Speaker Dennis Hastert (R., Ill.) and leading members
of congressional trade committees have advised President Clinton against incorporating "controversial labor
and environmental provisions" in the agreement. In a letter to Mr. Clinton, they registered their support for the
trade-expanding intent of the Singapore agreement but warned that introducing such provisions could result
in "costly setbacks" in negotiating the agreement and "severely undermine the ability of the next president to
craft a bipartisan trade program."
Japan's Trade Surplus Shrinks Again
Japan's trade surplus in November narrowed to an unadjusted 598.5 billion yen ($5.36 billion), from 663.3
billion yen a year earlier, shrinking for the fifth straight month, the Finance Ministry reported Wednesday.
Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had forecast a surplus of 778.2 billion yen. Adjusted for
seasonal factors, the November trade surplus widened to 624.5 billion yen from 609.7 billion yen. The U.S.
and other countries have urged Japan to hold down its trade surplus by bolstering domestic demand for
imports. But Japanese consumer demand remains sluggish. The trade surplus with the U.S. widened 18% to
644.1 billion yen.
