Petrodollars Staying Under Mattresses---Chastened OPEC NationsUse Windfall to EaseFiscal, Economic Woes
International
Petrodollars Staying Under Mattresses --- Chastened OPEC Nations Use Windfall to Ease Fiscal,
Economic Woes
By Hugh Pope
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,737 words
18 December 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
DOHA, Qatar -- High oil revenues again are pouring into the treasuries of the eight states that straddle the
world's biggest oil reserves in and around the Persian Gulf -- a total of $170 billion this year, 75% more than
last year.
The windfall for the Gulf states, which pump out more than two-thirds of the total oil produced by the
Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, is greater than most had expected. But this oil boom is
different from the bonanzas of the past, which sent petrodollars washing back into the coffers of international
banks.
Shocked by their brush with financial ruin when oil prices sank to as low as $7 a barrel during 1997-1999, the
Gulf states are keeping budgets cautious and are quietly repairing the damage to their external financial
positions. More-diversified local economies and capital markets also are absorbing more of the income.
Even though oil prices started rising to their current level of more than $30 a barrel more than a year ago,
sending annual growth rates in oil-related parts of some Gulf economies soaring to 20%, expansion in nonoil
sectors remains a modest 2% to 3.5%. Regional stock markets have stayed in the doldrums and no
governments are going on spending sprees.
"It reflects tightness in government capital spending, leading to a slow pickup in private-sector confidence,"
said Mohammed al-Shorooghi, Middle East director for Citibank, a unit of Citigroup Corp. "The excess oil
revenue is being used to either bolster reserves or buy back debt."
Oil income is likely to be $73 billion more than last year's $97 billion in Iran, Iraq and the six states of the Gulf
Cooperation Council: Saudi Arabia, the United Arab Emirates, Kuwait, Qatar, Oman and Bahrain. There is
some recycling of petrodollars -- Iran, the U.A.E. and Kuwait sent about $3 billion each back into the
international banking system in the first half of this year. Indeed, Kuwait now expects a $4 billion budget
surplus for the nine-month period through the end of March, instead of a projected $5 billion budget deficit.
But the picture isn't all rosy. Surprisingly, Saudi Arabia actually withdrew $1.3 billion from the world's banking
system during the first half of 2000, according to the Bank for International Settlements. The BIS -- the
central bank of the world's banking system -- says it is possible Saudi Arabia could be buying American
assets, because the U.S. doesn't supply detailed country-by-country breakdowns of incoming investments.
But the oil powerhouse that is scooping up 40% of the extra cash has fiscal housecleaning to do. In 1999, the
Saudi budget deficit still was equal to 7% of its gross domestic product, compared with 10.1% of GDP in
1998. In 2000, it expects its first budget surplus in 18 years. "The international banking system's role in
recycling petrodollars may not be as important today as it was in the 1970s," the BIS concluded in its
November quarterly report.
There aren't as many petrodollars to go around as before, either. At constant prices, U.S. Department of
Energy statistics show the Gulf region's oil revenues in 2000 are generally worth only half the value of the
record they set in 1980. And oil revenue per person also has dropped substantially: In Saudi Arabia it has
fallen to 14% of its 1980 level, at the same time that once-vast foreign-exchange reserves have turned into
public debt of more than $150 billion.
Better off than most, the 522,000 people of the small Gulf Arab state of Qatar already enjoy one of the
highest per-capita incomes in the world. But although last year's $70 million budget deficit is turning into a
surplus of $1 billion to $2 billion this year, Qatar is taking a long view. "Part of the new income will be used to
rebuild foreign reserves, which were exhausted," said Fawzi al-Khateeb, chief economist at the Qatar

--- Page 185 ---
Page 185 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
National Bank. "And part will be used instead for a planned $1 billion bond to finance the latest expansion of
the natural-gas export terminal."
As yet, the new petrodollars have made little impact on the gleaming malls of Gulf consumer life. At the
luxurious Toyota/Lexus dealership in Doha, the Qatari capital, men in white robes glide between shining
four-wheel-drive vehicles and sleek limousines. But the commercial manager, Mizanur Ramazan, expects to
end 2000 with sales of 6,000 vehicles, the same as last year and down from 7,000 in 1998.
"We still haven't seen the fruit of the oil-price rise. It hasn't reached us yet," Mr. Ramazan said. "Maybe the
government is thinking conservatively. They don't know how long this high price will go on."
Like many Gulf states, Qatar also plans to spend more on infrastructure -- in its case, secondary roads,
holiday resorts and perhaps a new airport. A major spending goal for states that mostly are 90% dependent
on oil revenues will be to diversify economies and create jobs. Many are trying to follow the example set by
Dubai, part of the U.A.E., which has made up for relatively small oil income by successful diversification into
container terminals, banking, trade, information technology and hotels. Even traditionally closed Saudi Arabia
is experimenting with opening up to tourism.
In Iran, the government is conservatively budgeting for an oil price of $20 a barrel for its next fiscal year
beginning March 21. The extra $9 billion expected during this financial year so far has been spent funding the
deficit, lubricating areas of the economy drained of foreign currency during 1997-1999 and regularizing the
foreign debt. The main effect of the new dollar inflow so far has been a strengthening of the value of the
Iranian rial, according to Paris-based Iranian economist Ali Rahnema.
Mr. Rahnema doubts there would be any sudden liberalization or opening up to imports, because many
Iranians blamed their foreign-debt trouble on a previous experiment with such policies a decade ago.
"Where they're really going to put the money, God knows. It isn't physically there yet. A good part is going to
go to private industry in Iran in order to provide currency needs for inputs of capital goods," Mr. Rahnema
said. "Perhaps half will go to this new reserve fund, but nobody has said yet if it will be put in portfolio
investments, European capital markets, or what."
Such reserve funds could amount to nearly $1 trillion in the eight Gulf countries, but all are managed
secretively. Mostly they avoid taking possibly controversial positions in big Western companies. The U.A.E.
has a fund valued at about $150 billion. Diplomats say Saudi Arabia could have $50 billion in public coffers
and several hundred billion dollars more in private hands.
Other member nations of OPEC are facing similar questions about what to do with their oil windfalls. Two of
OPEC's three African members -- Nigeria and Algeria -- each are burdened with $30 billion of external debt
and are trying to restructure their economies, lately with the help of higher oil revenues. The oil-price collapse
had left Nigeria in such a perilous state that it was only this past June that the state oil company was able to
pay back arrears of $2 billion to foreign oil companies operating in Nigeria as part of joint ventures where the
state oil company has a 60% interest.
The situation is better in Libya, where external debt is relatively low at close to $4 billion, and oil-export
revenues are expected to rise to about $12 billion this year from nearly $7.5 billion in 1999. The U.S.
continues to sanction Libya, though the United Nations suspended its sanctions last year. Oil accounts for
about 98% of Libya's export earnings, and the country has targeted infrastructure spending and investment in
agriculture and fisheries to reduce its dependence on petroleum.
Meanwhile, Venezuela has received between $26 billion and $30 billion of oil revenue this year, up from $16
billion in 1999. Much of the money has gone to increased fiscal spending, which jumped 36% in nominal
terms for the nine months ending in September from the year-ago period, according to the Venezuelan
Central Bank's Web site. Thanks to the fiscal stimulus, economists expect the Venezuelan economy to grow
3% or more this year, after declining 7.2% in 1999.
Rising oil receipts helped Mexico bring in additional income of $2.84 billion during the first nine months of the
year. Of that, the country set aside $615 million for a new oil-stabilization fund aimed at covering shortfalls in
future budgets when the price of oil falls. It also used another $926 million to amortize public debt, the
majority of which went to debtor-support programs stemming from the 1995 peso crisis.
Economists say the tough part is finding out what happened to the rest. Mexican Finance Ministry officials
said in November that about $724 million went to spending, and another $398 million to state-run agencies
for oil, electricity, social security and workers' housing. The remainder hasn't yet been assigned.
---
Bhushan Bahree in Paris and Jose deCordoba and David Luhnow in Mexico City contributed to this article.

The Vulture Culture
WEEKEND JOURNAL
The Vulture Culture
By Eileen Daspin and June Fletcher
2,016 words
15 December 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
[Frustrated by a decade of insane prices for everything from art to real estate, many Americans see a silver
lining to the wobbly economy -- the chance to finally swoop in on some deals. Eileen Daspin and June
Fletcher report.]
With the economy slowing and prices softening, Washington Redskins fan Paul Sawyer has one wish: that
things will get just a little bit worse.
It's the only way Mr. Sawyer figures he'll ever move up the 45,000-name waiting list for Redskins' season
tickets. The more the stock market wobbles, the harder he scours the classifieds for down-on-their-luck ticket
holders. Not to mention other bargains he's eyeing if the economy keeps stumbling.
"I hate to prey on anyone's misfortune," says Mr. Sawyer, who has been reduced to watching games on
television. But "I won't feel too awful if someone loses their luxury."
After a decade of watching prices go insane for everything from art to real estate, a lot of Americans see an
upside to the economic downturn: a chance to finally swoop in on some relative deals. The result is a kind of
vulture culture, as Americans at almost every income level see some potential relief in every hiccup of the
stock market. The hopes are especially high for bargains on big-ticket items that saw frenzied demand as the
New Economy soared: homes, waitlisted cars, high-end electronics and spa vacations -- the very things that
often suffer first in a slump.
In many ways, this is more a reflection of psychology than economics. Apart from digital cameras, hotel
prices and scattered other bargains, economists say most prices aren't likely to drop more than a couple of
percentage points. But don't tell that to the vultures, many of whom have been holding out for years in the
expectation that saner prices were surely around the corner. While even the most affluent consumers have
put off some purchases, the anticipation of a cheaper era is especially keen for those who missed out on the
windfalls of the '90s.
"There's a sense of frustration out there," says David Stewart, professor of marketing at the University of
Southern California. And now that people see the dot-commers faltering, "there's a sense there is justice in
the world, after all."
Of course, second-guessing the economy can be a dangerous game. Lots of people got burned in the last
recession buying art that looked like a bargain but never really regained its value. Still others are kicking
themselves for waiting for a real-estate deal, only to find that prices kept racing even higher.
But Elizabeth Canning Blackwell, who works in publishing in Chicago, is ever hopeful. After watching her
upwardly mobile life start to slow by comparison during the decade-long boom, she says she can't help but
wish for bad news. The 31-year-old, who has been buying fake Fendi bags at street fairs and staying away
from trendy restaurants, says she had just one thought when she heard retailers were struggling this
Christmas: "Great," she says. "That means the January sales will be better than ever."
Indeed, analysts say there's a good reason people feel frustrated. Previous U.S. economic booms were
usually much shorter, so prices and salaries leveled off more frequently. But despite remarkably low inflation,
prices of certain goods have skyrocketed. Last year, real estate jumped 25% in San Francisco, while simple
three-bedroom starter homes in Chicago fetch as much as $400,000 today. The same goes for some
restaurants: While prices overall have been fairly stable, the costs at the fanciest spots are eye-popping --
$250-a-head for the prix fixe dinner at French chef Alain Ducasse's new eatery in New York.
Even those who can afford all this can't always stomach it. Janis Spindel, who has spent the last eight months
hunting for a luxury home in Manhattan, says she just can't bring herself to pull the trigger. The holdup? "Out
of control" asking prices in the $6 to $8 million range, she says. While the matchmaker for well-to-do singles

--- Page 188 ---
Page 188 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
isn't betting that prices will fall, hope springs eternal. She plans to start looking again at the beginning of next
year.
Of course, a big part of being a vulture is knowing when to swoop in. So where do analysts recommend
looking for bargains? Prices for some electronics have already started to come down and more could be on
the way if the sluggishness continues. That's largely a function of the fact that electronics tend to get cheaper
as technology improves, but it's also a highly competitive industry that's especially sensitive to slowdowns.
In fact, despite aggressive pricing and rebates, sales of personal computers slumped 8% in the first two
weeks of November compared with the year-earlier period, according to PC Data Inc. and NPD Intelect Inc.
But other big-ticket electronics may be slower to come down in price. Two prime examples: hand-held
wireless devices and hot items like the PlayStation 2. "It's crazy to spend the money now," says Tim
Heffernan, a New Yorker who has his sights on a $2,400 36-inch Sony Wega XDR television.
Likewise in the world of real estate. To be sure, there are some signs of hope. One Chicago builder,
Gallagher and Henry Homes, is offering free extras worth about $5,000 on its more expensive models. Some
sellers are also already starting to throw in other freebies, like paying for drapes or termite inspections. In
addition, brokers say bidding wars in some parts of the country have begun to abate.
But full-fledged deals are still rare, and most experts don't expect that to change anytime soon. The reason:
Housing inventory in much of the country is tight. "People waiting for a big bargain on real estate are going to
be disappointed," says Mark Vitner, an economist with First Union Corp., Charlotte, N.C.
Besides fueling the economy in recent years, the Internet has also played a significant role in prodding the
development of the vulture culture. With so many products and prices posted online, consumers can more
readily see where the bargains are -- and realize that everything from cars to masterpiece paintings can be
haggled over.
Some businesses, too, will benefit if the economy stumbles. Bob Diener, president of Hotel Reservations
Network in Dallas, runs a hotel-consolidation business, buying rooms in bulk from the big chains and
re-selling them at cut-rate prices to consumers. Before hotel prices started softening in November, flush hotel
chains sometimes didn't even return his company's calls, let alone give HRN access to their inventory.
But in the last month alone, his company has picked up 60% more hotel rooms, including some at hot new
hotels in Las Vegas. "We've been innundated," says Mr. Diener. "The slowdown has been very good news for
us."
Obviously, there are limits to this. If the economy totally crumbles, consumer spending will probably plummet
in all these areas. Not to mention that today's bargain can be tomorrow's white elephant. In the early '90s,
when the art market collapsed, attorney Marshall Fogel snapped up some photos of old pro-baseball teams
for $1,000 a piece. He thought he'd hit the jackpot; only a year earlier, they'd sold for almost three times that
much. Today, he's still trying to unload his "bargain" photos. "Frankly, if I get my money back, I'll be really
lucky," he says.
And, as Los Angeles arts executive Jonathon Glus can attest, holding out for a bargain isn't necessarily a
recipe for happiness. He's chickened out of the housing market twice in the last year-and-a-half, hoping
something cheaper might materialize. The upshot: He's still in the same tiny studio apartment. "It makes me
nuts," says Mr. Glus, 37 years old. A few weeks ago, he was ready finally to take the plunge on an apartment
-- until he discovered to his fury that it had sold for 75% less just two years earlier.
Still, for some consumers the prospect of leaner times ahead remains tantalizing. Mr. Sawyer, the
Washington Redskins fan, admits that he has been chafing at the rising fortunes of executives at companies
like America Online and other dot-coms in his area. "I've stopped using AOL entirely," he says. But revenge is
sweet: "I'll be standing by," says Mr. Sawyer.
---
Where Bargains Could Surface
Do you feel your inner vulture growing? Keep in mind that all
bargains in a softer economy won't be equally juicy. We talked to boat
brokers, art dealers, realtors, economists and retailers to find out
where prices might -- or might not -- slide.
Area --
Art
Where To Look -- Contemporary art and photos
Where Not To Look -- Masters like Picasso, Cezanne and Modigliani
Comments -- Careful. In the art bust of the early '90s, contemporary
art plunged -- and some of it never came back. Possible bargains
ahead: lesser-known "outsider" artists, who are self-taught and hot

--- Page 189 ---
Page 189 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
for now.
Area -- Boats
Where To Look -- Small boats, older used boats
Where Not To Look -- Custom-made yachts
Comments -- Eighteen-month back orders for $300,000-plus yachts will
keep those prices afloat. But smaller vessels (less than 25 feet) and
pre-1995 refurbished boats could see values sink.
Area -- Cars
Where To Look -- Some upscale American-made cars
Where Not To Look -- Upscale foreign-made models and collector cars
Comments -- In general, most car makers don't roll back prices when
times get tough; they offer rebates and financing deals instead.
Still, Cadillac has already lowered the Seville's sticker price 4% to
$41,935.
Area -- Electronics
Where To Look -- Cathode-ray and projection televisions
Where Not To Look -- DVDs, Personal Digital Assistants
Comments -- Among the hottest gifts this season, DVDs and PDAs are a
bad bet for bargain-hunters. But expect prices of old-style
televisions, already heavily discounted, to plunge over the next year
or two.
Area -- Furniture
Where To Look -- Mass-market reproductions
Where Not To Look -- High-end, high-style furniture
Comments -- To preserve their image, many upscale wholesalers would
rather sell fewer models than discount. But mass marketers are making
deals. Expect the best bargains on styles trending out, like deco and
'30s retro.
Area -- Furs
Where To Look -- Novelty and refurbished furs
Where Not To Look -- New furs
Comments -- With fur back in style and sable coats by Michael Kors
fetching up to $70,000, forget any deals for now. But novelty furs
made abroad can be downright cheap.
Area -- Homes
Where To Look -- Vacation homes
Where Not To Look -- Mini-mansions and custom homes
Comments -- While the overall real-estate market is still
out-of-control, there's hope for buyers in the second-home market --
especially at the seashore, where bad weather can raise maintenance
fees and add investment risks.
Area -- Jewelry
Where To Look -- White
gold and platinum day jewelry
Where Not To Look -- Yellow gold
Comments -- Not all diamonds are forever. New York jewelry dealer Guy

15% to 20% recently -- but may come down next year.
Area -- Travel
Where To Look -- Thailand, Vietnam
Where Not To Look -- Spas
Comments -- Pampering has gone bananas -- a six-hour package at the
Plaza Spa in New York now costs $1,375. Sorry. That won't change much.
For a bargain, check into Bangkok's five-star Peninsula Hotel, at $115
a night. Vietnam is a deal, too.
Area -- Restaurants
Where To Look -- High-powered business restaurants
Where Not To Look -- Flavor-of-the-month hotspots
Comments -- With entrees having broken the $50 barrier in some
cities, it would seem that prices have nowhere to go but down. Just
don't tell Alain Ducasse, whose $250-a-head prix fixe menu has shocked
even jaded New Yorkers.

Broken Ballot -- America's Dysfunctional Voting System---Down for the Count:Election Day BecameA Nightmare, as Usual,For Bernalillo Count...
Broken Ballot -- America's Dysfunctional Voting System --- Down for the Count: Election Day Became
A Nightmare, as Usual, For Bernalillo County --- Localities Administer Voting, But Most Lack
Resources, Staff to Do the Job Right --- Florida Was Far From Alone
By Bryan Gruley and Chip Cummins
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
4,950 words
15 December 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Corrections & Amplifications
THE VEHICLE driven by Robert Lucero, the coordinator of elections for Bernalillo County, N.M., was an
Aerostar minivan, made by Ford Motor Co. A page-one article Friday misidentified the vehicle as an Astro,
which is a Chevrolet product made by General Motors Corp. (WSJ Dec. 18, 2000)
[First in a series]
ALBUQUERQUE, N.M. -- Robert Lucero couldn't believe the printouts. He tried again and again, but the vote
counts wouldn't add up. As midnight neared on Tuesday, Nov. 7, the man who ran the election in Bernalillo
County began to fear something had gone wrong.
Reporters and political-party officials kept calling him in his cluttered office in the county building downtown.
"These numbers look a little funny, Robert," one caller said. Absentee- and early-voting tallies appeared to
show that thousands of voters had cast ballots without bothering to vote for a presidential candidate.
I'm on it, Mr. Lucero told the callers. He knew what they were thinking: Bernalillo County has blown another
election. He hunched over his computer, snacking on peanuts, running more printouts. He told himself he
would have a final tally for the local morning newscasts.
It was Mr. Lucero's first presidential election as chief of Bernalillo County's elections bureau. He and his
full-time staff of 20 had hired and trained more than 2,300 poll workers, arranged to have 1,100 voting
machines installed at polling stations, and weathered an Election Day snowstorm. He himself had
programmed the balloting system that now was giving him trouble. He thought he had done everything right.
Until that night. By the time Mr. Lucero left his office just before dawn, the county had withheld about 66,000
absentee and early ballots from its final tally. That placed in limbo the tight race between George W. Bush
and Al Gore for New Mexico's five electoral votes. And, for Bernalillo County, it began three frantic and
embarrassing days of recounting and second-guessing.
Most years, no one notices if elections are run by people who lack relevant experience or with voting
machines that malfunction. This year, with a photo finish between Messrs. Bush and Gore, the whole country
noticed. The fiasco in Florida, with a decisive 25 electoral votes at stake, received most of the attention. But
the lesson America learned from the historic election of 2000 was that, from Palm Beach to Bernalillo, the
nation's voting system is deeply dysfunctional.
If there's one key reason for the mess, it's the American tradition, going back two centuries, of delegating
responsibility for running elections to the nation's 3,067 individual counties. Guided by few national standards,
most of these local governments devote modest resources to elections and hire poorly trained staffs for the
massive job of recording and counting a total of more than 100 million votes for president, as well as millions
more for lower offices.
The result is a jumble of different technologies and methodologies -- an invitation to disaster. This year, there
was chaos all over the country.
In Iowa, a hard-to-read fax from Scott County caused election officials initially to give Vice President Gore an
extra 2,006 votes. In Outagamie County, Wis., a typo in a tally sheet threw Mr. Bush hundreds of votes he
hadn't won. And in Multnomah County, Ore., a new system under which voters could mail in or drop off
ballots led to fears of misconduct, as unidentified people collected ballots from voters on the street in Portland

--- Page 191 ---
Page 191 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
during the Election Day rush. The mysterious ballot collectors put their haul in cardboard boxes and
disappeared, witnesses said.
The U.S. Supreme Court ended the election drama with a proclamation that more recounting in Florida would
violate the constitutional doctrine of "equal protection" because standards would vary from county to county.
By that logic, the entire nation arguably engaged in an unconstitutional free-for-all on Election Day, as
standards varied within and among many states.
The truth is that the enormous job of running accurate elections just isn't a high priority in most counties --
and it shows. With roads, parks, police, jails, sewers and other necessities to worry about, county
governments typically allocate no more than 3% of their budgets to the clerk's office, says Jane Hague,
president of the National Association of Counties and a member of the governing council of King County,
Wash. A chunk of that is spent on filing and recording of public documents, leaving little money to keep voting
machinery up-to-date, train poll workers adequately, or hire professionals experienced in running elections.
Most counties figure it's less of a risk to postpone buying voting equipment than "to have criminals on the
street, backlogs in the courts, or overflowing jails," Ms. Hague says. Likewise, low salaries for election
supervisors make it hard to attract top talent -- but until this year, that wasn't a problem many voters
complained about.
In recent weeks, a movement has begun in Congress to make voting more modern and uniform. Sen.
Charles Schumer, a New York Democrat, for example, has sponsored legislation to pay for research on the
best voting methods. He and others are considering federal matching grants to fund nationwide
improvements.
In Bernalillo (pronounced burn-uh-LEE-oh), New Mexico's most populous county, with the 530,000 residents
of Albuquerque and surrounding areas, County Clerk Judy Woodward has presided over three elections in
recent years marred by delays in counting absentee ballots. Even fellow Democrats have blasted her as
incompetent, a charge she vehemently rejects. Critics point to high turnover in the clerk's office, where
everyone agrees salaries are modest.
The current elections chief, Mr. Lucero, is 43 years old, a compact man with a cherubic face and short, dark
hair dusted with gray. Prior to joining the office shortly before the 1998 general election, he had spent 22
years working in public and commercial television -- doing everything from writing scripts to appearing on
camera -- and four years as an elected Albuquerque school-board member. His county job pays only $36,000
but lets him continue adding to the government pension he began accumulating while working in public TV.
And it sounded fun. "The job really reminded me of television," he says. "You do everything." Formerly a
registered Democrat, he dropped his party affiliation when he took the elections post.
After the polls closed on Election Day, Mr. Lucero pulled his gold Ford Astro minivan into the county
building's parking lot. His rounds that day had included checking polling places and being interviewed on TV.
A morning snowstorm hadn't hurt turnout much.
To record and count regular votes on Election Day, the county had used 1,100 bulky touch-screen machines
made by Shoup Voting Solutions Inc. of Rosemont, Penn. The Shouptronics had performed well, even though
many of the touch-screen units dated to the mid-1980s. The problematic absentee and early votes, by
contrast, had been cast on paper ballots which were then fed into Accu-Vote optical-scanning machines sold
by Global Election Systems Inc. of McKinney, Texas.
Now, around 8:30 p.m, Mr. Lucero took the credit-card-size memory chips that contained the absentee and
early votes up to his sixth-floor office for tabulating. Local reporters and party workers were watching election
returns in the county commission's meeting room, an amphitheater decorated with paintings of cowboys and
grazing buffalo. Ms. Woodward, who is 73 years old and wears a silk scarf woven through her
snow-and-ash-colored hair, busied herself with sandwiches for election workers, relishing a smooth Election
Day. It looked like everything would be wrapped up once the absentee and early ballots were counted.
The roughly 66,000 absentee and early ballots are crucial in Bernalillo, where this year they accounted for
more than a third of the total votes cast for president. The county allows voters to vote early, and in person,
during a 20-day period prior to election day, or to send in absentee ballots by mail. In neither case must
voters assert that they will be out of the county on Election Day. Many states and counties have similarly
liberalized voting rules in an effort to boost turnout.
Although Bernalillo County overall tends to favor Democrats, Republicans usually win more absentee and
early votes because the GOP aggressively promotes these options. That's why state GOP Chairman John
Dendahl was puzzled when the national TV networks projected Mr. Gore as the winner in New Mexico early
in the evening of Nov. 7. "That's premature," he told fellow Republicans partying in the Marriott Hotel
ballroom. The statewide race between Messrs. Gore and Bush was close, as expected. Mr. Gore had

--- Page 192 ---
Page 192 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
dominated regular polling in Bernalillo County, but the absentee and early votes were likely to help Gov. Bush
of Texas.
Partial totals from absentee and early voting had been tacked to the wall of the cavernous warehouse south
of downtown, where those categories of votes are counted. Reporters there were having trouble making
sense of the returns. The paper tallies, resembling grocery-store receipts, seemed to show that many more
ballots had been cast overall than were cast in individual races. For example, tallies later that night would
show that, of about 38,000 early ballots cast, only 25,000 were cast for Mr. Gore or Mr. Bush.
Around 9 p.m., county computer staffers noticed the discrepancy and pulled the early-vote totals from the
county Web site. Ms. Woodward took a call from a befuddled party official. "Judy," he said, "we have a little
problem here."
It wasn't her only problem. She had just learned that poll workers at the vote-counting warehouse had been
sent home, leaving about 1,800 ballots -- a combination of early and absentee -- to be counted the next day.
These ballots had to be counted by hand because the Accu-Vote optical-scanning machines had refused to
read them. The reasons for this glitch aren't clear. Some voters may have made stray pencil or pen marks
that confused the scanners. Other people may have voted for two candidates in the same race or spilled
coffee on their ballots.
Furious, Ms. Woodward telephoned Aggie Lopez, the absentee-voting supervisor. Only the county clerk could
dismiss those workers, Ms. Woodward scolded her. "You should have kept them there."
"I'm sorry," Ms. Lopez said, "but the team was so tired." Only 57 of the 80 scheduled workers had shown up
for the one-day, $6.50-an-hour assignment. Many were elderly, and the warehouse lighting was poor. Each
ballot listed more than 30 races and issues, in English and Spanish. Three people were required to review the
ballot during the hand count, a process that could take as long as five minutes for each ballot. Ms. Lopez told
her boss she had sent workers home because she feared they might start making mistakes.
But Ms. Woodward was determined to get those votes counted immediately. In 1998, counting of absentee
votes had been delayed a day after she allowed poll workers who hadn't finished their work to go home. Now,
she went on radio and TV to plead that workers return to the warehouse.
A swarm of reporters gathered at the cinder block-and-stucco warehouse. A few workers did return, and Mr.
Dendahl, the state GOP chairman, had collected some fellow Republicans to help count. But when Ms.
Woodward arrived and assessed her meager manpower, she reluctantly decided it would be futile to try to
finish counting that night.
It was nearly midnight, and local TV and radio stations were running a familiar story: Bernalillo County would
be late.
Worse trouble was brewing back at the county building. Mr. Lucero had spent the evening feeding the
memory cards containing the bulk of the absentee and early votes into his office computer and printing the
results. He couldn't understand how such a gap could exist between the number of ballots cast and the
number of votes for individual candidates.
The memory cards came from the 13 Accu-Vote scanners. The scanners, which resembles fax machines,
had worked well in nearly a dozen elections since the county had purchased them for a total of $97,000 in
1997.
Ms. Woodward had once argued publicly for getting rid of the Shouptronic machines used for regular voting at
all polling places on Election Day and instead conducting the entire county's election with paper ballots that
would be counted by Accu-Vote scanners. She contended that the smaller Accu-Vote devices were cheaper
to store and transport and were just as accurate as the Shouptronics. But she didn't formally propose the
purchase of more Accu-Vote scanners because, she says, "the money just wasn't there."
So Bernalillo has operated with a mixture of voting machines, using the Accu-Vote devices solely for
absentee and early ballots.
During election years, the county typically allots about 3% of its budget -- about $3 million this year -- to the
clerk's office, which spends about half of that on election expenses. New Mexico counties can borrow at no
interest from a state voting-equipment fund. But total lending by the fund is limited by law to $2 million for the
whole state, not enough to help each of the 33 counties to buy new machines when they need them.
Ms. Woodward says she has worked diligently since being elected in 1992 to keep her budget tight. "The
[state] legislature is what's looking at you," she says. "If they see you spend too much, they just think you're
sloppy."

--- Page 193 ---
Page 193 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Back at the county building, the performance of the Accu-Vote machines was frustrating Mr. Lucero. It was
clear that the computer program he had installed in those machines was flawed. He called technicians with
the vendor, Global Election Systems, and begged them to e-mail him a fresh program, so he could re-run the
memory cards. He desperately wanted to avoid spending another day having each ballot re-fed through the
scanners.
The company technicians, who had more election experience than Mr. Lucero, pointed out to him that fiddling
with the software now could invite charges of vote tampering. Anyway, even if they sent him a new program,
the paper ballots would have to be fed through the machines again, one by one.
When Mr. Lucero returned to his one-story brick home for a shower at 5 a.m., he didn't know the Florida
election had also been a squeaker. He hadn't been following the national returns. But he figured, based on
his experience in TV, that local, and maybe national media would be clamoring for Bernalillo County's results.
"It's going to be a long week." he told Shannon, his wife of 22 years.
Two hours later, he was back at his desk, without having slept. An election headline in that morning's
Albuquerque Journal read, "Mistakes Lengthen the Wait." Mr. Lucero prepared to go before the county
commission to accept responsibility. But he wasn't so sure it was all his fault.
Back in September, he had toiled in his office for four 14-hour days with Don Biszmaier, a Global Election
technician. Together, they customized basic software provided by the company to fit Bernalillo County's
needs. The resulting program would tell the Accu-Vote machines how to count the absentee and early ballots.
The two men made no fewer than 114 versions of ballots to reflect a multitude of local races and issues in
different parts of the county.
As they worked, Mr. Lucero's computer screen repeatedly displayed a command window offering a pull-down
menu. From the menu, the two men should have clicked on "straight party." Either they didn't make the
crucial click, or they did and the software failed to work. As a result, the Accu-Vote machines counted a
straight-party vote as one ballot cast, but didn't distribute any votes to each of the individual party candidates.
To illustrate: If a voter filled in the oval for straight-party Democrat, the scanner would record one ballot cast
but wouldn't allocate votes to Mr. Gore and other Democratic candidates.
While Mr. Lucero and Global Election officials agree this was the problem, they disagree about how it
happened. Mr. Lucero, whose computer training consists of a few classes at the University of New Mexico,
says he customized the program properly, but the software failed.
Frank Kaplan, Global Election's western-regional manager, says there wasn't a flaw with the company's basic
software, but he can't explain what went wrong. (Mr. Biszmaier declined to be interviewed.) With 850 counties
and municipalities across the country as customers, Global Election says it has kinks from time to time, but
nobody has had software problems like those in Bernalillo County.
Whatever its origin, the error was detectable. Global Election explains to customers in written training material
how to run a series of tests to make sure a program counts properly. These tests are vital, akin to a driver
tapping the brakes after having them repaired, the company says.
Mr. Lucero says he thoroughly tested the Accu-Vote machines and determined they were working properly.
But he neglected to check the individual vote totals against the overall number of ballots cast. "I didn't do the
math," he says.
No one checked Mr. Lucero's work. His supervisor, Deputy County Clerk Lena Gonzales, who attended a
four-day training session with him at Global Election in Texas last spring, didn't look at the test results, she
says. A retired school administrator who joined the clerk's office two years ago to supervise elections, she
had no experience running a voting operation. She says she left everything concerning the Accu-Vote
machines to Mr. Lucero.
The county employs 75 computer technicians, some of whom could have helped Mr. Lucero. He says he
didn't seek their assistance because "they're [information-technology] people, not elections people."
Bob Ashmore, director of the county's information-technology department, says, "Robert was the one
responsible for getting [the computer programming] done with the vendor, and he had a hundred other things
to do." In the future, Mr. Ashmore says, his people will be more involved.
The day after Election Day, Mr. Lucero rewrote the computer program with help from Global Election, but it
was midnight before a recount could start. At the urging of Ms. Woodward and Mr. Lucero, a local district
judge, Theresa Baca, named a committee of observers, one each from the Republican, Democrat and Green
parties. With the judge presiding, the panel spent a good part of the evening holed up in a conference room at
the clerk's office. Fueled by coffee and pizza, they tested hundreds of sample ballots, doing the math Mr.

--- Page 194 ---
Page 194 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Lucero had neglected weeks earlier. By the end of the night, they were satisfied that the problem had been
fixed.
The recount of the absentee and early votes began Thursday morning at the warehouse. Workers had hauled
dozens of black-plastic ballot boxes out of storage and stacked them in the building's main room, among
tables draped in stars-and-stripes tablecloths. Thirty poll workers, now in their third day on the job, sat in a
row, feeding ballots through reprogrammed Accu-Vote machines.
Watching from a few steps away were Mr. Lucero and Ms. Woodward, District Judge Baca's committee, a
phalanx of lawyers from both parties, two investigators from the state attorney general's office, and Ms.
Lopez, the absentee-voting supervisor, with her two assistants. Yellow tape roped off a section where
journalists listened to Mr. Dendahl, the state GOP chairman, rail at Ms. Woodward: "Once again, the county
clerk and her machinery have distinguished themselves with incompetency." Ms. Woodward came over to the
reporters to defend herself: "This glitch did not surface until all the ballots were counted. How we reacted
shows competence."
Late that afternoon, most of the crowd left the warehouse and headed in a caravan of vehicles up Broadway
to the county building. Sheriff's deputies escorted the Accu-Vote memory cards containing the recount of the
26,000 absentee votes. The observers and lawyers from all sides crammed into Mr. Lucero's small office. He
ran the memory cards again, as he had on Tuesday night. This time, it worked.
All the votes were there, and all had been distributed properly to the candidates. Mr. Lucero felt momentarily
relieved. And, as the Republicans had expected, Mr. Gore's statewide lead of about 10,000 narrowed to
6,800.
Next came the recount of the 38,000 early votes. Around midnight, Ms. Baca, the district judge, and the
others were back in Mr. Lucero's windowless office. Outside, local reporters were now joined by journalists
from New York, Washington and Los Angeles, as the country remained uncertain about who would win the
election.
At one point, Pat Rogers, a local attorney for the Republicans, asked that TV cameras be turned off for an
important announcement. What county election officials had discovered, Mr. Rogers reported gravely, "was
the Wen Ho Lee tapes." The reporters roared with laughter at the joking allusion to the Los Alamos, N.M.,
scientist whose missing tapes filled with sensitive nuclear weapons data have been the subject of an
intensive Federal Bureau of Investigation search.
Inside, Mr. Lucero faced his PC. To his left stood a half-empty jar of peanuts, untouched since Election Day.
He clicked his computer mouse for printouts from the laser-jet on his right. The room fell silent as everyone
scanned the numbers. "I don't believe this," said Green Party representative A. Eric Prowten. He tapped on a
calculator. "We're 252 ballots short."
The printouts showed the recount had tallied 38,072 early votes. On Tuesday, the count had been 38,324.
Shock and disbelief creased the faces in the room. This is humiliating, District Judge Baca thought. We're
going to have to tell the press that we can't even keep track of our own ballots. She and the others figured the
ballots probably had been misplaced, but they couldn't dismiss the possibility that someone had tried to
manipulate the vote. District Judge Baca ordered a search of poll workers and 24-hour police security at the
warehouse.
The group of observers, lawyers and officials in Mr. Lucero's office briefly discussed telling the journalists
nothing for now. "No," Judge Baca said, "we have to tell the press, and we have to tell them right away."
Sometime after 2 a.m., she faced the reporters, with Mr. Lucero standing nearby. One journalist asked if the
missing ballots could compromise the election. "I think there has been a compromise already," the judge said.
The next morning, four days after the election, Judge Baca reconvened the recount assemblage at the
warehouse. The building had been searched, but the missing ballots hadn't turned up.
While the group considered its next move, Nel Finberg, a Global Election technician, flipped open a door on
one of the podiums that had been used at polling places to hold the Accu-Vote machines. Inside, he found a
ballot. Now, only 251 were unaccounted for.
There was no simple way to track the missing ballots because no one kept a precise inventory of the ballot
boxes. So Judge Baca ordered a hand inventory of the ballots in the warehouse. The group had just begun
that laborious task when Lou Melvin suddenly appeared with a scuffed, knee-high ballot box at her feet. "I
think I have found your ballots," she said.

--- Page 195 ---
Page 195 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ms. Melvin, who is 70 years old and wears glasses with purple frames, is the secretary of the New Mexico
GOP and a Republican elector. She would cast her electoral vote for Mr. Bush if he won the state. She also
was a veteran polling-place worker.
Election experts say it's unusual but not unheard of for party officials like Ms. Melvin to be deeply involved in
the voting process. "If you have a party official involved in the tabulation process itself, that is suspect as
heck," says Kim Brace, president of Election Data Services Inc., a Washington, D.C., consulting firm. Ms.
Melvin says she has never been questioned about a conflict of interest. "When you're counting these ballots,
there are no thoughts of partisan politics," she says.
Ms. Melvin told those gathered in the warehouse she had spoken that morning with a friend, a Democrat, who
had supervised an early-voting station. He had heard about the missing ballots on the news and remembered
having a box containing about 250 ballots. Sure enough, Ms. Melvin said, she found the stray container sitting
off to the side in the warehouse, its outer label turned to the wall. She told the others she had found it locked.
Someone produced bolt-cutters and snipped off the locks. "Don't touch those," said Mr. Rogers, the
Republican lawyer. He thought that any fingerprints on the locks might be important if the ballot box wound up
in court.
Out came a one-inch stack of ballots -- 257 of them. With the one Mr. Finberg had unearthed, the group now
had six more ballots than it was looking for. Close enough, they decided.
It still wasn't over, though. There remained 379 early ballots. These were from the batch of 1,800 that the
Accu-Vote scanners had failed to read, and which hadn't been hand-counted on Election Night because tired
poll workers were mistakenly sent home.
The Democratic lawyers at the warehouse argued that under state law, early ballots that scanners fail to read
shouldn't be counted at all. Republicans angrily insisted the votes had to be counted.
When scanners won't accept ballots, voters in Bernalillo normally are asked to redo them. But this year, some
frustrated voters who had waited in long lines had given up. Rather than cancel their ballots, Ms. Melvin, the
GOP activist, and other poll supervisors placed them in locked compartments and promised voters the ballots
would be hand-counted.
Now, Ms. Melvin argued that state law did allow for a hand count of those ballots. She had promised voters. "I
gave them my word," she said.
Ms. Woodward knew her fellow Democrats wouldn't be pleased if she sided with Ms. Melvin. But she said the
Republican had done the right thing. "I think we should count them," the county clerk concluded.
Diane Denish, chairwoman of the state Democratic party, says Ms. Melvin's argument "looks suspicious. Lou
Melvin ended up playing an integral role in Bernalillo County, and she's [a GOP] elector. I think that's a
conflict."
The Democrats had reason to be nervous. After the 38,000 early votes had been tallied, including the stray
piles of 258 and 379 ballots, Mr. Bush had passed Mr. Gore in New Mexico by just four votes: 285,644 to
285,640.
The lead wouldn't hold. The following Monday, Dona Ana County, about 200 miles south of Albuquerque,
disclosed that it had shorted Mr. Gore by 500 votes. Someone had mistaken the figure "620" for "120" in a
voting tally. On Dec. 5, the state canvassing board certified Mr. Gore as the winner in New Mexico.
Ms. Woodward couldn't run for re-election this year because New Mexico limits countywide officials to two
terms. As for Mr. Lucero, his cheerful manner disappears when asked how he feels about the election. "This
has been the worst thing that ever happened to me," he says. "But we made it right. I can look at friends and
neighbors and say, `Your vote counted.' "
Mr. Lucero has heard that Ms. Woodward's successor plans to replace him, and he's looking for a new job.
---
Voting by County
Many of the problems with the country's dysfunctional voting system
stem from the fact that the 3,067 individual counties run elections,
with few national standards and typically inadequate budgets. Here are
some of the varieties of voting machines used around the nation:
Optical Scanner
Advantages: High-speed and generally accurate count.

--- Page 196 ---
Page 196 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Disadvantages: High printing costs; machines can be overly or
insufficiently sensitive, depending on setting. If voter circles a
choice, machine won't count it.
Punch Card
Advantages: Machines are inexpensive, take up little space. Ballots
can be examined and recounted.
Disadvantages: The ambiguity of "hanging" or "pregnant" chads. The
device must be periodically cleaned out to prevent chads from
accumulating.
Lever
Advantages: Fast, potentially less-confusing count.
Disadvantages: Heavy and large, so relatively few fit in a single
polling place, leading to longer lines. Expensive to purchase, store
and transport. If something goes wrong, votes can be lost.
Paper Ballots (Manual Count)
Advantages: Humans determine voter intent; no machine errors.
Recounts possible.
Disadvantages: Arduous counting process; potential for human error
and fraud.
Optical Scanner
39%
Punch card
20%
Lever
15%
Paper ballots
(manual count)
13%
Other methods
13%
Source: Federal Election Commission, Office of Election Administration

EUROPE
U.K. Proposes Plan
For Restructuring
Television Industry
The British government unveiled plans to create a new communications regulator and clear the way for
consolidation in the television industry as part of an overhaul of the country's archaic media laws. The
government, in a blueprint for future legislation, proposed to remove prohibitions on a single company running
ITV, Britain's most-popular television channel, and said it would consider changing U.K. rules on radio
ownership. Dubbed Ofcom, the new watchdog would for the first time bring together regulation of television,
radio, telecommunications and the Internet, replacing a mass of regulators including the current telecom
regulator Oftel and the Independent Television Commission.
While the white paper provided few surprises in its proposals for a single regulator and TV-ownership rules,
some industry experts criticized the government for skirting key issues such as cross-media holdings and
regulation of the publicly funded British Broadcasting Corp.
U.K. Inflation Is Expected to Moderate
Higher oil prices pushed Britain's retail inflation rate to 3.2% in November, from 3.1% the month before, as
crude prices led to a rise in the price of gas at the pumps, the Office of National Statistics said. The
underlying inflation rate, which excludes mortgage interest and is used to set government inflation targets,
rose to 2.2%, from 2.0% in October. The underlying rate has now come in below the Bank of England's 2.5%
target for the past 20 months. Oil prices have come down by about 20% from a month ago, and economists
expect the rise in U.K. consumer prices to slow in coming months. Britain's subdued inflation has led to
growing expectations that the central bank will move to cut interest rates from their current 6.0% level next
year. The Bank of England has kept rates unchanged since February.
Euro Zone's 2.8% Growth Outpaces U.S.
Weaker consumption resulting from high oil prices, combined with declining exports, cut economic growth in
the 11-nation euro zone to an annualized 2.8% in the third quarter, from 3.2% in the second quarter, the
European Union's statistics office said. The data confirm that while the euro zone's economy is slowing, it
grew at a faster pace than the U.S.'s 2.4% expansion in the third quarter, the first time that has happened in
more than two years. But most economists expect U.S. growth to rebound slightly in the fourth quarter, while
the euro zone's activity continues to slow.
Sitcom Cancels IPO, Accuses Chase
Sitcom, an Italian content provider for digital television, canceled its initial public offering one day before the
shares were to begin trading on Italy's Nuovo Mercato exchange, alleging that it fell victim to changes
resulting from Chase Manhattan's planned merger with J.P. Morgan & Co. Sitcom said planned staff cuts at
investment bank Robert Fleming, the co-sponsor of the offering with Italy's Banca Aletti, negatively affected
the outcome of the offering because the bankers involved were no longer motivated to support the
transaction. Chase, which this year acquired Robert Fleming and acted as the offering's global coordinator,
denied the allegations. It said the decision to withdraw the IPO was the result of the offering's poor reception
by investors. Sitcom had offered 1.63 million ordinary shares, or about 26% of the company. The price was
set at 16 euros a share, valuing the IPO at 26 million euros ($22.8 million).

--- Page 198 ---
Page 198 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
EU Details Mad-Cow Prevention Plan
The European Union's Beef Management Committee, in a further step toward fighting the spread of Europe's
mad-cow crisis, gave more details about its plan to purchase and destroy old cows that either test positive for
the disease or haven't been tested at all. The "purchase-for-destruction" plan is designed to help farmers
destroy all cows over 30 months old that haven't cleared the test for bovine spongiform encephalopathy, or
BSE. The European Commission has indicated it would cover 70% of the cost of purchasing the animals.
Finland, Austria and Sweden were exempted from the program for their domestic markets. The program is
scheduled to begin Jan. 1 and end June 30.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Japan's Tankan Survey Fails to Improve
In a new danger sign for the Japanese economy, the Bank of Japan's closely watched survey of business
sentiment failed to show improvement for the first time in eight quarters. The so-called diffusion index for
large manufacturers, which had risen steadily since March 1999, remained unchanged at plus 10. The tankan
survey asks companies whether business conditions are good, bad or middling; the index for the
October-December quarter showed that the number saying "good" was 10 percentage points higher than
those saying "bad." Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had forecast a reading of plus 11. The
survey also showed companies expect demand for their products to weaken over the next three months.
Japan has seen several signs of a slowing economy. Growth for the fiscal first half ended Sept. 30 was 1.0%
at an annualized pace, and price deflation has worsened as exports have fallen off. The problem is especially
severe because Japan's heavily indebted government can't afford to spend its way out of recession.
Glencore Offers to Buy National Steel
Switzerland's Glencore International said it offered to acquire ailing National Steel, the Philippines' largest
steelmaker. "There is interest from us but only if we can close with the proper local partner," an executive of
Glencore Far East Philippines said. "Based on our technical due diligence, we value the plant lock, stock and
barrel, including all the assets, at $150 million." The Philippine Securities and Exchange Commission ordered
the liquidation of National Steel in October.
Taiwan's UMC Plans Singapore Plant
United Microelectronics Corp., in a blow to Taiwan's efforts to keep its chip makers from venturing abroad,
said it will build a $2 billion wafer-fabrication plant in Singapore with a foreign partner. UMC's chairman told
reporters the group chose to build the Singapore plant, which will make 12-inch wafers, because the
Singapore government supports the "knowledge industry" better than Taiwan. He said UMC will have a
majority stake in the venture, but declined to identify the foreign partner. Details will be released Friday in
Singapore. UMC is the world's No. 2 microchip foundry after Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing.
BRIEFLY:
-- China's trade surplus in November narrowed to $520 million from $2.6 billion a year earlier, the official
Xinhua news agency reported, citing customs figures. Exports rose 14% to $22.16 billion, while imports
increased 28% to a single-month record of $21.64 billion. For the first 11 months, exports rose 30% to $227.2
billion, while imports jumped 37% to $203.7 billion.
-- Japan's Financial Services Agency said it will allow banks to sell insurance products, even of nonaffiliated
firms, starting April 1, when a ban on banks' entry into the insurance sector will end. Banks will be allowed to
sell one form of life insurance and three types of non-life insurance.

THE AMERICAS
Oil Firms Look Beyond Arctic Gas Study
Major oil companies planning to tap vast natural-gas reserves in the Canadian Arctic said they are moving
past the study stage and could soon begin preparing a regulatory application for a multibillion-dollar pipeline.
The group, which includes Imperial Oil, Gulf Canada Resources, Shell Canada and Exxon Mobil, began
studying this year how to develop about six trillion cubic feet of gas reserves in the Mackenzie Delta region of
the Northwest Territories. Surging gas prices have sparked a huge resurgence of interest in developing the
reserves. Meanwhile, oil companies led by Britain's BP Amoco are also formulating plans to develop reserves

--- Page 199 ---
Page 199 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
at Prudhoe Bay in northern Alaska. BP Amoco, Exxon Mobil and Phillips Petroleum last week said they had
joined forces to study construction of a pipeline to tap 35 trillion cubic feet of Alaskan gas reserves.
AES Raises Offer for Chile's Gener
U.S. power company AES said it is raising its offer for the American depositary shares of Chile's Gener to
$16.50 per ADS. AES also extended the expiration date of the offer to Dec. 26. AES also increased its offer
to purchase 3.47 million shares of Gener's common stock in Chile to the Chilean peso equivalent of 24.3
cents. Meanwhile, Gener shareholders lifted the company's 20% cap on ownership by any single party,
clearing the way for a takeover.
Peru Bank Consolidation May Continue
The weeklong wave of consolidation and closures in Peru's financial sector could continue, as people in the
industry said Peru's fourth-largest bank, Interbank, was on the verge of acquiring ailing state-run Banco
Latino. One person close to the deal said negotiations were at a "very advanced" stage. Both banks declined
to comment. On Monday, the country's banking regulator said it placed the small NBK Bank under its
administration because of liquidity problems, marking the second banking casualty after the closure of Banco
Nuevo Mundo last week. The regulator on Monday also announced it took over the administration of La
Vitalicia Cia. de Seguros, a small insurer.
BRIEFLY:
-- Canadian gold miner Barrick Gold, citing the current market environment for gold and silver, said it won't
begin full-scale construction immediately on its Pascua-Lama project in Chile and Argentina.

There's Still Time to Avoid a Hard Landing
There's Still Time to Avoid a Hard Landing
By Brian Wesbury
1,098 words
5 December 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Pundits are predicting the ultimate winner of the presidential election will have a hard landing in the Oval
Office. He will have no honeymoon, no mandate and will face a divided Congress. But these may be the least
of his problems. Despite the market's rally after yesterday's unanimous Supreme Court ruling for George W.
Bush, the economy is closer to a recession than it has been in over a decade and we could be in for a hard
landing.
In the third quarter, real gross domestic product grew just 2.4%, its slowest pace in four years. Investors are
pulling back as the economy slows; the Nasdaq Stock Market just finished its worst month since October
1987. Lower stock prices push up the cost of capital and inhibit the investment and "creative destruction"
necessary for building our amazing economy.
This comes at a bad time. The opportunity cost of a slowdown at this juncture is very high. What the next
president does to address this problem will decide whether the U.S. remains the leader of the information
age.
Some argue that uncertainty over the election is the culprit behind the latest drop in the market. After all, the
Nasdaq is down 23% since Nov. 7. But this argument doesn't make sense. Is it really possible that
uncertainty is any greater today than it was during the week before the election? In that week, the Nasdaq
surged 7%.
A better explanation is that stocks shot higher the week before the election because investors believed Mr.
Bush would win and enact his across-the-board tax cuts. Now that markets know the nation has elected an
evenly divided Congress, the probability of tax cuts has been reduced and investors' spirits have fallen.
Still, to focus on just the election is to miss the biggest negative force in today's economy: a misguided
Federal Reserve policy. During the past 18 months, the Fed boosted rates six times under the mistaken belief
that strong growth, rising stock prices and low unemployment cause inflation.
The Fed is getting its way; growth has slowed and stock prices are lower. The only soldier left standing in the
Fed's army of imaginary enemies is a low unemployment rate and even that is about to change. Initial
unemployment claims rose during Thanksgiving week to 358,000 -- the highest level in almost two and a half
years -- suggesting that unemployment is about to rise.
The Fed's decision to do nothing at its Nov. 15 meeting was a mistake. Most damaging was its statement that
it was ignoring signs of slower growth because it believed unemployment was still too low. The market knows
that unemployment is a lagging indicator of economic activity and that by the time it starts to rise it may be too
late to stop the economy from sliding into a recession.
The wrecking ball of Fed rate hikes swings through the economy in a typical pattern. Manufacturing and
housing are the first to take a hit, then employment, and finally consumption. Viewed this way, the economic
problems this year are a typical Fed-induced slowdown. Housing activity peaked last year and manufacturing
activity is slowing rapidly. Non-high-tech manufacturing output fell 1.7% at an annual rate during the six
months ending October.
But even the high-tech sectors of the economy are having problems. In the past three months, the output of
semiconductors and related components climbed at a 50.9% annual rate. While this may seem strong, it is a
significant slowdown from the sector's 127% second-quarter growth. With Gateway's announcement of weak
sales, high-tech output may slow further.
For a year now the markets have been launching signal flares, but the Fed chose to ignore them. Sensitive
indicators -- gold, non-oil commodity prices, the yield curve and especially the dollar -- have all warned that
the Fed was too tight and that inflation wasn't a problem. But the Fed's focus on Phillips Curve-based

--- Page 201 ---
Page 201 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
models blinded it to these signals. Its models said that low unemployment and rising oil prices would boost
inflation.
However, in the current economic environment of high productivity and intense competition, higher input
costs do not produce higher retail prices. Moreover, when economic activity begins to slow, prices come
under immediate downward pressure. Auto manufacturers and retailers are already cutting prices in an
attempt to boost volume. It is clear that inflation isn't currently a threat.
To add to the economy's woes, taxes are too high. During the 12 months ending October, personal income
rose 5.8% but personal tax payments shot up 12.8%. During this period, Americans' total personal income
rose by $459 billion. At the same time, total taxes paid increased by $149 billion. In other words, the marginal
tax rate on income averaged 32.4% in the past year, and taxes now take up the largest share of personal
income since 1944.
This higher tax burden was key to the market's rally both the week before the presidential election and after
yesterday's Supreme Court decision. The market knows that Mr. Bush's tax cuts are necessary to get the
economy and stock market moving again. This is especially true given that Germany, France and Italy are all
scheduled to cut taxes in 2001. If the U.S. doesn't cut taxes, not only will our economy continue to suffer, but
Europe will begin to gain the upper hand in competitiveness.
The U.S. has an unbelievable opportunity to create wealth over the next 10 to 20 years. New technologies
have boosted productivity and increased our potential growth rate to at least 5%. Every day that U.S. growth
is less than its potential because of high taxes or misguided Fed policy, the economy loses billions of dollars
in future gains.
When the next president takes office, his first order of business should be to move ahead with an
across-the-board tax cut and then to fill the two vacancies on the Federal Reserve Board with people who do
not subscribe to the Phillips Curve. If he doesn't do these things, an unavoidable hard landing will hurt his
administration, while a slower recovery will reduce budget surpluses and make it even more difficult to get
anything done in the years ahead.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief economist of Griffin, Kubik, Stephens & Thompson in Chicago and author of "The New
Era of Wealth" (McGraw-Hill, 1999).
