Page 202 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Glut Check:Did Corporate AmericaGo a Little OverboardWith Capital Spending?---Despite New-Economy Tools,Overcapacity May LoomIn Key Economi...
Glut Check: Did Corporate America Go a Little Overboard With Capital Spending? --- Despite
New-Economy Tools, Overcapacity May Loom In Key Economic Sectors --- Old Navy Just Got Closer
By Wall Street Journal staff reporters Greg Ip in New York, Robert L. Simison in Detroit and Jacob M.
Schlesinger in Washington
2,742 words
4 December 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Vehicle factories from Detroit to Newark, Del., sit idle as the industry struggles with bloated inventories. On
Friday the Big Three auto makers reported their weakest sales so far this year. A broader survey of
purchasing managers released the same day shows that U.S. manufacturers ranging from textile makers to
paper mills slowed their pace in November for the fourth consecutive month.
Leather pants and jackets, meanwhile, are piling up on shelves of Gap Inc. stores around the country. And
scores of fledgling telecommunications companies, faced with insufficient sales to cover their costs, are
suddenly scaling back ambitious plans for new networks.
As the nation's record-long economic expansion slows after five years of go-go exuberance, company after
company is finding it has more capacity than it needs. That raises a fundamental question: Did corporate
America overdo it with its capital spending binge of the 1990s?
If so, that would argue for some sober rethinking of the New Economy's vaunted invulnerability -- and bode ill
for the nation's prosperity in coming months.
During past booms, business executives tended to get carried away, building too many new stores and
factories even as demand for their goods softened. That worsened the blow when the bust finally came and
they were forced to shutter their unused capacity.
This time around was supposed to be different. Newly prominent capital markets were supposed to do a
much better job than once-dominant bankers did at pulling the plug on financing before companies got too
far. New technologies, such as sophisticated software programs that track sales, inventory and supply lines,
were supposed to give companies better, more timely information about their markets. That was going to let
them fine-tune production to demand, and thus smooth out -- or even eliminate -- the old boom-bust cycles.
Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan, for one, has repeatedly espoused this theory.
"Twentieth-century business decision-making had been hampered by pervasive uncertainty," he observed
earlier this year. That ambiguity, he said, has been significantly reduced by "the remarkable surge in the
availability of more timely information."
And the economy does seem to be performing more smoothly along those lines. American industry as a
whole isn't saddled with an unusual amount of unused capacity. Despite slowing demand, the Fed says that
all manufacturers, mining companies and utilities combined are currently using about 82% of their total
capacity, right in line with the average level of the past two decades. Capacity even remains tight in many
important sectors of the economy. Airlines are flying with their planes near their fullest loads in more than two
decades. Oil companies have been careful in expanding drilling and refining capacity, one factor keeping oil
prices high lately.
Yet, new pockets of overcapacity emerge each passing week, giving rise to concern that -- if consumer
demand slows sharply -- excess inventories will become a nationwide problem. It sounds a bit hauntingly like
Japan, where corporate titans confidently expanded through the 1980s, proclaiming a new era of higher
growth. When financial and property markets collapsed and consumer demand plunged in the early 1990s,
companies were stuck with far more capacity than needed, helping deepen an economic quagmire from
which Tokyo has yet to emerge. "I don't think we're seeing an overcapacity problem," says Goldman, Sachs &
Co. economist William Dudley. "But you don't know until the bust -- Japan looked OK until it didn't look OK,"
he adds.
The sectors now struggling with excess capacity show that information about future demand may not be
perfect after all. Last week, for example, Altera Corp., a San Jose, Calif., maker of customizable

--- Page 203 ---
Page 203 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
semiconductor chips, warned that its revenue in the current quarter is likely be flat, rather than growing in
double-digits as originally forecast. Vadim Zlotnikov, an analyst at Sanford Bernstein, says Altera's sales have
slowed because many of its chip customers overordered in past periods in anticipation of new demand that
didn't materialize and are now stuck with excess inventories.
Sometimes, the problem is simply that old-fashioned corporate decision-making hasn't caught up with new
Information Age tools. "A lot of the industry looks at pricing, volume and incentive decisions in three totally
different meetings" inside their companies, Van Bussman, corporate economist for DaimlerChrysler AG's
Chrysler Group told an investor group recently.
Indeed, better market information by itself can't always prevent excessive buildups, especially in the case of
potentially profit-rich new technologies. There, the lure of outsized returns seem to justify the risk of
overinvesting. "People are excited about various technologies, they put capital in place and keep doing so
until the profits slip," says John Makin, economist at the American Enterprise Institute, a Washington think
tank. "Then, there's too much capacity."
That's not entirely bad news. Oversupply means deep discounts for consumers, even by the low-price
standards of recent years. Some Lexus dealers are advertising a $1,500 price cut on options for Lexus
RX300 sport-utility vehicles -- a luxury line that just a few weeks ago was commanding premiums above its
sticker price. Jeep dealers are going Lexus one better, offering some options, such as air-conditioning, power
locks and windows or automatic transmission, free of charge. Ford is countering with 0.9% financing on
leftover 2000 model Explorer, Expedition and Excursion SUVs, well below rates of as much as 7.9% for its
latest models.
But all this discounting can also pinch profits, forcing companies to lay off workers and slash capital
expenditures in an attempt to scale down to an appropriate size. On Friday, Ford Motor Co. lowered its profit
forecast. The same day, auto-parts maker Delphi Automotive Systems Corp. said it would temporarily lay off
1,700 workers, or 3% of its work force. Chrysler, the maker of Jeeps, is working on plans to cut its five-year
capital spending by 20%, or $9 billion.
That marks a departure from the late 1990s and the first half of this year, when American companies seemed
to conclude that the economy would keep shifting into ever-higher gears. Since 1995, U.S. factories, mines,
and utilities have increased capacity by 5% a year -- more than double the pace from 1980 through 1994,
according to the Fed.
Retailers have been even more enthusiastic, embarking on a specialty-store building spree. The new
specialty stores -- outlets geared to a particular market segment, such as sportswear or lingerie -- will
increase the industry's nationwide floor space by about 30% by next year, compared with 1998, according to
a survey of 75 leading specialty merchants by Lazard Freres & Co. analyst Todd Slater. He says that buildup
is coinciding with a slowdown in the growth of consumer spending.
Since 1995, San Francisco-based Gap alone has more than doubled its overall number of Gap, Old Navy,
and Banana Republic stores to 3,500. In the coming year, it plans to add as many as 630 more, an 18%
increase.
A big unanswered question: Will those stores increase the chain's business or cannibalize existing sales? In
Livingston, N.J., an affluent bedroom community about 15 miles west of New York City, construction workers
are busy outfitting an Old Navy story in the Livingston Mall -- a mere two miles south of an existing Old Navy
in a Route 10 strip mall.
Pat Martino often stops by the Route 10 Old Navy to shop for friends' children but says she will probably favor
the more convenient Livingston Mall outlet when it opens. There's also an Old Navy a few miles from the
Union, N.J., university where she works as a secretary. "I don't know how they all exist, there are so many of
them," she says. But she welcomes their deep discounts: On a recent morning, she picked up five fleece
jackets at about a third off their regular price of more than $20 each.
"I think this will be one of the best Christmases on record for consumers," says Lazard's Mr. Slater. "But don't
expect record results from retailers."
Indeed, Gap's expansion has been so aggressive that the economy doesn't need to collapse for Gap to take
a hit. Consumer spending just has to stop growing quite as swiftly as it has in recent years. Companywide,
Gap's sales for the fiscal third quarter ended Oct. 28 were up 12% from a year earlier, but its sales per square
foot of store space fell 16%.
"We'll concede that we built too fast," says Gap spokesman Jack Dougherty. "But we disagree that we've built
too many" stores, he adds. "We have less than 6% of market share, and we think there's a lot of opportunity
out there."

--- Page 204 ---
Page 204 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
It isn't just giddiness that may have driven American companies to overinvest in recent years. The
warp-speed world of global competition also means that any profitable new business quickly draws a flood of
hungry new entrants.
Consider the evolution of the market for sports utility vehicles, which is still highly profitable -- though
decreasingly so. In 1995, North American factories were cranking out about 1.7 million SUVs. Production was
divided among 30 different models. Since then, the nation's SUV-production capacity has doubled, and
consumers now have 52 nameplates to choose from, according to Autodata Corp., a Woodcliff Lake, N.J.,
auto-industry research firm. Goldman Sachs estimates that over the next four to five years manufacturers are
planning another 44% increase in SUV capacity, with 49 new models.
To be sure, a good portion of that increase has been justified by Americans' voracious appetite for the
mammoth vehicles. "This year SUV sales are up 10% in a market that's up 4%," says George Pipas, a sales
analyst at Ford. And even with total vehicle sales expected to sink by 5% next year, he says he is "pretty
confident" that "there are segments within SUVs that will grow at double-digit rates again."
Still, only the most starry-eyed analysts expect demand to keep pace with the surge in supply. Goldman
analysts see the percentage of SUV-production capacity actually being used plunging to 81% in 2005 from
92% today. The result, they say: profit margins will drop by half.
Already, "it's gotten so competitive it's unbelievable," complains Houston Jeep dealer Alan Helfman. "I just
lost two customers because they had already owned our product and wanted to try one of our competitors',"
he adds. Last month, to work off unwanted inventories, Chrysler closed Jeep factories in Detroit and Toledo,
Ohio, for a week and plans to idle them for another week later this month.
American auto makers -- having suffered their share of painful downturns -- say they have actually tried to
exercise some restraint in recent years in building up capacity for SUVs and other vehicles. But Japanese
and German manufacturers have been flooding the market pioneered by the Big Three. Over the past few
years, Germany's Bayerische Motoren Werke AG and Mercedes-Benz AG, have added their own North
American SUV production. So have Nissan Motor Co. and Toyota Motor Corp. Both those Japanese
companies are planning expansions, while a third, Honda Motor Co., will soon join them.
Toyota, for one, expects to sell 60,000 of its new Sequoias next year into a market it expects to grow by, at
most, 40,000 units, says Alan DeCarr, Toyota's vice president for sales in the U.S. He says Toyota is banking
on grabbing share from Ford's Expedition and GM's Tahoe and Yukon models.
The auto industry has always had its sharp ups and downs, so perhaps it's no surprise that Detroit once again
faces an overcapacity problem. The more jarring development is a glut in high-tech sectors of the economy,
where demand was expected to keep up with whatever supply came onstream.
Companies selling into new market segments have no historical markers to guide them in predicting sales
patterns. Rapidly increasing demand and the potential for sky-high rates of return encourage technology firms
to bet on the high side when building plants or laying cables. Financial markets can also get swept up in a
herd mentality that exaggerates the unguarded enthusiasm of the industry pioneers.
A 1996 telecommunications deregulation law -- combined with growing awareness of the remarkable new
business frontiers to be opened by the blending of computers with phones -- unleashed one of the information
industry's greatest gold rushes. Hundreds of new telecommunications companies were launched, intent on
competing with the entrenched regional Bell operating companies by offering everything from consumer voice
and long-distance services to high-speed Internet service for business.
By paying premium prices for industry start-ups, executives at AT&T Corp. and WorldCom Inc. sent a clear
early signal that they thought the potential for growth was huge. After that, nearly 40 so-called competitive
local-exchange carriers were able to raise funds during the late 1990s in the heady market for initial public
stock offerings, according to Merrill Lynch & Co. Many more raised money through bond offerings to the point
where telecom start-ups currently carry $74 billion in debt, according to Lehman Brothers.
So far, however, these newcomers have barely made inroads against the old local phone companies, which
Merrill estimates still control 94% of the local business phone market. None of the upstarts has yet to report a
profit. Industry enthusiasts say the problem isn't overcapacity or lack of demand, which is high, but some
bottlenecks and glitches, such as insufficient capital to finance the networks. Skeptics, however, think
investors are now correctly cutting off financing after concluding that most of the upstarts won't ever find
enough demand to justify their spending. Saddled with unfinished or underused fiber-optic lines, these
companies say they will slash capital spending by 23% next year from this year's $8 billion level, according to
Paul Sagawa of Sanford Bernstein. Just four years ago, such spending totaled only $634 million.
FirstWorld Communications Inc. of Greenwood Village, Colo., began building and operating fiber-optic
networks in the mid-1990s, including a major one for the city of Anaheim, Calif. In 1998, the company decided

--- Page 205 ---
Page 205 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
to expand from voice service to Internet access, Web integration consulting and hosting of Web sites for other
companies. Enthusiastic investors helped FirstWorld finance these ambitions, as the company raised $600
million in stock and junk bonds. In March 2000, it went public at $17 a share.
But many of those same investors also financed countless competitors. And all of those new companies
found the market tough to crack. In the Los Angeles area, for example, FirstWorld was just one of 10 new
telecom companies attempting, with limited success, to strip business away from the established players,
Verizon Communications and SBC Communications Inc., says Terry Smith. His firm, Call Control Systems, of
San Clemente, Calif., advises businesses on phone and data service.
"They're the new kids on the block, they don't have any track record," he explains. He says he has steered
some clients who did business with the start-ups back to the local phone company because of service or
maintenance problems.
Just four months after going public, FirstWorld warned investors that sales would be below expectations and
a month later said it was putting its telephone-service and Internet-access businesses up for sale. That could
include selling its interest in the underused Anaheim fiber-optic loop. Only about six of the loop's 60 strands
that FirstWorld operates currently carry traffic. FirstWorld's stock now trades at about $1 a share.
FirstWorld is devoting its remaining physical assets -- mainly nine data centers -- to hosting Web sites for
small and midsize businesses. "The opportunity there in our minds is endless -- this market is going to
explode," says Scott Chase, FirstWorld's senior vice president of corporate and external affairs. But the
company has a ways to go to fill the space it has already built. The portion of FirstWorld's Web-hosting
capacity currently in use: 13%.

Borrowing Trouble: Credit Tightens, Posing A Risk to Weak Firms And Maybe Economy --- Even the Strong See Costs Climb, and Buyout Money...
Borrowing Trouble: Credit Tightens, Posing A Risk to Weak Firms And Maybe Economy --- Even the
Strong See Costs Climb, and Buyout Money Becomes Harder to Raise --- Batting Down the `Air Ball'
By Paul M. Sherer and Jathon Sapsford
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
2,755 words
1 December 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright © 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.
Corrections & Amplifications
LAIDLAW Inc., a Burlington, Ontario, transportation company, has defaulted on much of its debt, and is
conducting a restructuring that it says will include a prepackaged reorganization filing under Chapter 11 of the
U.S. Bankruptcy Code. Safety-Kleen Corp. , which is 44%-owned by Laidlaw, filed for Chapter 11 protection
in June. A page-one article Friday about credit conditions incorrectly stated that Laidlaw had already filed for
Chapter 11 protection. (WSJ Dec. 5, 2000)
For three years, video-rental chain Hollywood Entertainment Corp. has opened almost one new Hollywood
Video superstore per day. In 2001, it won't open a single one. It can't raise the money.
Even though operations have been improving, bringing Hollywood its first quarterly profit in two years,
bankers have told the company they want a tighter repayment schedule. The pressure this has created
helped spur Moody's Investors Service to downgrade Hollywood's credit rating. Now, further borrowing is
effectively out of reach for the company.
It's the kind of squeeze more and more U.S. businesses are encountering. Across the country, growing
numbers of companies that until recently could readily borrow all they wanted now meet a chillier reception
from lenders. And companies with top credit ratings are finding that although plenty of credit remains
available, its cost has stiffened.
It can't be called a credit crunch, at least not yet. It isn't nearly as bad as the debt crisis of 10 years ago, when
the U.S. was hit with a convergence of problems that compounded one another: a recession, the Persian Gulf
crisis, the collapse of savings-and-loans and the downfall of the main junk-bond underwriter, Drexel Burnham
Lambert Inc. But if the current credit tightening deepens, it could damage the U.S. economy -- already
showing signs of a slowdown -- as businesses respond by curbing their spending and expansion plans.
"In an environment where we had access to capital, we'd open more" stores, says Hollywood's chief financial
officer, David Martin. "But if the capital isn't there, you don't grow."
Riskier companies, always the first to feel a credit tightening, have suffered most of the pain so far. They
have managed to issue only $41 billion in junk bonds so far this year, way below last year's $95 billion and
1998's $140 billion, according to Credit Suisse First Boston. Syndicated loans to firms with single-B,
below-investment-grade credit ratings total a mere $225 million so far in the fourth quarter, vs. $5 billion in the
like period last year, says S&P Portfolio Management Data.
But a broad swath of stronger businesses is affected as well. A Federal Reserve survey shows that 44% of
U.S.-based banks have tightened standards for commercial and industrial loans in the past three months, in
part because regulators are pushing them to curtail risky lending. Many of the largest and most profitable U.S.
corporations have seen their borrowing costs go up.
The caution has hit some household names with relatively strong credit ratings, though mainly those facing
tough business challenges. Xerox Corp. in October found itself shut out of the market for commercial paper,
or short-term corporate debt, and had to draw heavily on $7 billion of backup credit lines. Levi Strauss & Co.,
which faces declining sales and has a credit rating just below investment grade, had to cancel a $350 million
junk-bond issue in October.
Why the squeeze, after years of robust economic growth and easy credit? Simply put, many see the U.S. as
headed into the downward phase of the economic cycle. The U.S. economy grew at just a 2.4% annual rate
in the third quarter -- less than half the second quarter's pace -- and capital spending, PC sales and auto
sales all are coming under pressure. Any sign of slowing makes lenders less confident of borrowers' future

--- Page 207 ---
Page 207 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
earnings, and therefore ability to repay. They start to deny credit or demand bigger compensation for taking
the chance of extending it.
Moreover, lenders are discovering that during the long boom years they gave money to lots of companies
that, in retrospect, shouldn't have been funded nearly so richly. The lending was indeed lush: In June, total
borrowing by nonfinancial U.S. companies stood at $4.6 trillion, up 61% from five years before, Moody's says.
Now, more debt is going bad. The junk-bond default rate is above 5%, the highest in nearly a decade. And
3.3% of syndicated loans were considered troubled as of the end of March this year, up from 1.3% two years
earlier, according to bank regulators' latest Shared National Credit survey.
Frothy financing deals done in 1997 and 1998 -- for overleveraged "roll up" companies on acquisition sprees,
and telecom companies with no earnings -- have turned around to bite investors. Chapter 11 bankruptcy-law
filings this year include firms such as AmeriServe Food Distribution Inc., whose $1.5 billion in debt was more
than nine times its annual cash flow. Rattling investors further: Some of the year's biggest Chapter 11 filers
have been companies that had investment-grade credit ratings, such as grocer Pathmark Stores Inc. and
Laidlaw Inc., a transportation company.
How tight credit has become is a matter of debate. Peter Gleysteen, who runs syndicated lending at Chase
Manhattan Corp., says the U.S. isn't in a credit crunch. But, using Darwinian terms, he adds that "as the
economy loses some of its momentum, it's not surprising that if there's less oxygen in the atmosphere, some
companies with smaller lung capacities will gasp for breath and some won't survive. It's the nature of our
free-market economy."
At Morgan Stanley Dean Witter & Co., corporate-bond strategist Steven A. Zamsky agrees it is "premature to
say we are in the middle of a full-blown credit crunch" but adds that "that description would be a fair
representation of what is already happening in parts of the market."
Today, in theory at least, a dispersal of credit risk reduces the danger to the economy. A decade ago, U.S.
banks had weaker balance sheets, and risky lending was much more concentrated. Regional banks in New
England that lent heavily in the booming real-estate markets of the 1980s were battered when property prices
collapsed at the end of the decade. Banks weren't merely unwilling to lend, but often unable to.
Now, big syndicated loans are often shared by dozens of banks and institutional investors. And individuals
have indirectly become huge lenders to corporations through mutual funds, which invest in corporate bonds,
syndicated loans, commercial paper and asset-backed securities.
Yet some contend that this shift of lending from the banks to debt-securities markets magnifies the danger.
Banks have relationships with the borrowers, but bondholders don't, and they tend to act quickly and all on
the same information. That's what happened in August 1998, when Russia defaulted on part of its debt and
lenders suddenly didn't want to own anything but supersafe Treasurys.
These days, mainstream borrowers are finding their creditors wary once more. Consider Hollywood
Entertainment. Throughout two years when the company was unprofitable, the credit spigot flowed freely.
Lately, results have been improving. Revenue at the Portland, Ore.-based retailer rose 15% in its fiscal third
quarter ended Nov. 2 to $306 million, and it recorded a profit of two cents a share.
But that was below analysts' expectations. And Hollywood's total debt of $595 million had started looking
worrisome to its lenders. So Hollywood's money-raising options all grew bleaker at once. Bankers turned
stingy. Bonds would have to carry such a high interest rate that selling them would choke off Hollywood's
cash flow. And selling more stock wouldn't do because the shares had fallen steeply. Seeing this, the rating
agencies downgraded Hollywood's credit, making it even harder to raise cash.
So far in the fourth quarter, Moody's has been lowering the credit ratings of 3.9 corporate borrowers for every
one it raises, the worst ratio since the fourth quarter of 1990. Tighter capital markets, says Moody's Senior
Vice President Dan Gates, are reason enough to re-evaluate any company that relies on debt to fuel growth
plans. "Diminished access to funding has become a big factor" in rating creditworthiness, he says.
For Hollywood, it is a "vicious cycle," says Mr. Martin, the chief financial officer. "We will pull through it. But
this adds some challenges."
A sharp line has been drawn between the corporate haves and have-nots. Companies that have strong credit
ratings or are in sectors currently in favor can still get plenty of money. At oil-driller Swift Energy Co., Chief
Financial Officer Alton Heckaman gets a constant stream of cold calls from bankers and investment bankers
offering to arrange financing. "From a standpoint of credit, we've actually seen quite an improvement in our
sector over the past nine to 12 months," he says.
And the commercial-paper market, which supplies short-term credit to corporations with the highest credit
ratings, grew 17.2% year-over-year through October, says John Lonski, chief economist at Moody's. "We're

--- Page 208 ---
Page 208 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
not seeing a contraction in the availability of credit that appears to be hampering business activity in a
broad-based manner," Mr. Lonski says. Financing for small businesses also has been readily available
through the end of October, according to a survey by the National Federation of Independent Businesses.
But even the strongest companies are finding it costlier to raise capital. When Citigroup Inc. issued $3 billion
of 10-year bonds in early October, it offered investors 1.47 percentage points more interest yield than
comparable Treasury notes. Yesterday, the company sold $1.25 billion more of the same bonds but had to
pay about 1.89 percentage points over Treasurys, a huge increase for bonds with a strong single-A-plus
credit rating.
So the "risk premium" that investors demand for holding even blue-chip corporate bonds has risen sharply.
The 5,000 corporate bonds in Salomon Smith Barney's Broad Investment Grade Index are trading at 1.9
percentage points over Treasurys, compared with 1.2 percentage points in January. Though the declining
supply of Treasurys caused part of that increase -- by pushing Treasurys' prices up and yields down -- in the
past two to three months much of the rise "could be ascribed to true fundamental credit risk," says Dennis M.
Adler, the high-grade corporate-bond strategist at Salomon Smith Barney.
Now, step across the credit divide. For any company or deal that looks risky, the money supply has been
largely cut off. If the would-be borrower has negative cash flow or "if we're funding an unproven business
plan, we're not interested," says Mark L. Gold, managing director and portfolio manager at TCW Group, a big
investor in syndicated loans.
The collapse of New Economy stocks earlier this year cut off credit to many upstart telecom companies that
had raised tens of billions of dollars from junk-bond investors. Unlike the profitless Internet companies, which
were financed mostly with equity, telecom start-ups could borrow because they were spending their money
on hard assets like fiber-optic cable. Also, lenders perceived them as likely to be acquired by bigger, stronger
companies someday.
In October, Deutsche Bank AG backed away from a commitment to lend $1 billion to MainStream PCS
Holdings LLC, a start-up that wanted the funds to buy wireless properties in the U.S. Midwest. After
MainStream was unable to sell $435 million in junk bonds, Deutsche Bank got out of its loan commitment by
invoking a rarely used clause citing a "material adverse change" in market conditions.
Bank regulators have long been pushing for tighter lending standards, but banks seemed to ignore them until
recently. Now, "banks are more tight with their credit controls and with the amount of money they want to
lend," says Nolan North, chairman of the Association for Financial Professionals, a forum for corporate
financial officers. He says it's partly because of "jawboning by regulators."
The regulators are looking to restrain so-called enterprise-value financing, also known as "air ball" lending.
The "air ball" is the portion of a loan not backed by the hard assets, such as equipment, but simply based on
expectations of business growth. Several now-beleaguered industries, including telecom, movie theaters and
health-care facilities, have been big recipients of air-ball lending. The Fed, the Office of the Comptroller of the
Currency and the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. are discussing new guidelines to curtail it, say officials
involved.
The regulators don't want to come down too hard. The air-ball loan has become important to the service
economy, where businesses don't have smokestacks to pledge as collateral. "What we're trying to do is pull
banks in without denying [loans] to companies who deserve it," says Dave Gibbons, a Comptroller of the
Currency official.
Even as regulators pull the reins on banks, consolidation in that industry has thinned the ranks of active
lenders. The number of banks involved in 10 or more "leveraged loans," or syndicated loans to riskier
companies, has plunged to 42 so far this year from 110 last year, according to Babak Varzandeh, a director
of S&P Portfolio Management Data. That decline, "along with a more guarded credit posture of those that
remain, has clearly made it harder" to syndicate the leveraged loans he says.
In turn, the tighter market for these loans and for junk bonds has slowed the U.S. merger boom, as some
companies and leveraged-buyout funds struggle to raise the capital to do deals. Those buyouts that the debt
markets continue to finance must now be done with a lower percentage of debt.
On Nov. 17, Century Aluminum Co. and Southwire Co. announced that "unsettled conditions in today's
financial markets have disrupted Century's financing plan" for a $460 million acquistion of Southwire's
aluminum-smelting assets. Century had been trying to raise $260 million in the syndicated-loan market and
the rest through a junk-bond sale. It cancelled the loan deal after failing to sell the bonds. The acquisition has
been delayed.
What some view as a credit squeeze, others see as a return to sanity. In 1997, buyout firms were able to
finance deals by saddling acquisition targets with debt equaling six times their annual cash flow. "That's a lot

--- Page 209 ---
Page 209 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
of leverage to put on companies," says Stephen Berger, chairman of Odyssey Investment Partners LLC, a
New York private-equity firm. "There's just so much you can load the saddlebags [with] before the horse
starts to buckle, I don't care if it's Man o' War."
When Odyssey did a deal to acquire Dayton Superior Corp. for $315 million in June, the total debt was four
times the target's annual cash flow, with a third of the money in equity. Had the deal for the Ohio maker of
construction products been done in 1997, it would probably have had debt of 5.5 times cash flow and only
20% equity, says Mr. Berger.
Those riskier companies still getting deals done must pay up. In May, battery maker Exide Corp. announced
plans to acquire competitor GNB Technologies Inc., intending to pay with a $250 million syndicated loan. But
talks with investors in August showed they were losing their appetite for companies, such as Exide, with less
than investment-grade credit ratings. (Its is single-B-plus.)
So Exide's investment bankers at Credit Suisse First Boston and Salomon Smith Barney told Exide's chief
financial officer, Kevin Morano, that he needed to sweeten the offering. First, he raised the interest rate to a
steep 4.5 percentage points above the benchmark London Interbank Offered Rate. He added a promise to
pay penalties if Exide repaid the loan ahead of schedule. And then Exide did something very rare in the
syndicated-loan market: It offered the lenders warrants to buy its stock. The acquisition went through.

Coffee Prices Fall to New Lows on Selling Spree
Commodities
Coffee Prices Fall to New Lows on Selling Spree
By Mara Lemos
Dow Jones Newswires
604 words
1 December 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- A failure to move beyond recent highs combined with an upward revision of a Brazilian crop
forecast set the scene for a coffee-selling spree that sent the nearby contract on the Coffee, Sugar and
Cocoa Exchange to fresh lows.
The most active March contract ended down 2.85 cents, or 3.8%, at 71.7 cents a pound after touching its
lowest level since listing at 71.3 cents. Coffee prices are hovering around seven-year lows and have been
under bearish domain for one year and in a long-term downtrend since mid-1997.
An attempted rally to break the high of 75.1 cents a pound failed for the second consecutive day, which fed
the bearish sentiment, said Michael McDougall, vice president of the Latin America desk at Fimat USA Inc., in
New York.
"We had bottom-pickers, bargain-hunters, all that just provided more food to the bears [because] it failed to
push the market above 75.1 cents so people bailed out again," said Mr. McDougall.
Compounding the bearish sentiment was the revised U.S. Department of Agriculture coffee-production
forecast for the world's leading exporter, Brazil. The USDA said late Wednesday that Brazil will pick 32.6
million 60-kilogram bags of coffee in the current 2000-01 season, started in July, up sharply from its previous
estimate of a 28.1 million bag crop.
Although the market has been working with a 2000-01 Brazil output of around 33 million bags anyway, the
USDA's adding 4.5 million bags to its forecast "obviously didn't help for the bulls," said Arthur Stevenson,
analyst with Prudential Securities.
These mounting bearish signs led analysts to predict coffee prices will fall below 70 cents in coming days.
"We are looking at the fact that it made new lows and failed at a previous high, so it strongly suggests to me
that the market will make another bout lower," said Ann Prendergast, analyst with brokerage Refco Inc. in
New York.
Prudential's Mr. Stevenson echoed that view, predicting the market will drop below 70 cents "in the next few
days."
The USDA is expected to release a world coffee-crop report in December, in which it is expected to also
revise upward estimated production in Vietnam and Central America, raising what already is a record high for
coffee production.
In other commodities markets:
ENERGY: Nearby January crude-oil prices fell 81 cents to $33.82 as United Nations and U.S. federal officials
discussed plans to counter Iraqi threats to halt exports. Also, natural-gas prices registered a new contract
high at the New York Mercantile Exchange due to forecasts of cold weather and expected reductions in
inventories. The January contract leapt 40.8 cents to $6.589 per million British thermal units, after reaching a
high of $6.75. The potential of colder weather, plus an almost 500 billion-cubic-feet deficit to year-earlier
storage levels, is driving prices upward, one trader said.
GOLD: Prices climbed at the Comex division of Nymex as a humbled U.S. dollar spurred another burst of
short covering, or buying to cancel previous sales, traders said. February gold ended $3.70 higher at
$273.30 a troy ounce, regaining ground lost on Wednesday. Market watchers said they wouldn't be
convinced that gold could hold its gains unless the euro steadied and climbed a few cents higher, which
would encourage more consumer buying of gold in Europe.

Drifting Apart:Indonesia Faces CrisisAs Separatism SpreadsWith Nation's Turmoil---Short on Cash and Leadership,Jakarta Sees Rich PapuaJ...
Drifting Apart: Indonesia Faces Crisis As Separatism Spreads With Nation's Turmoil --- Short on Cash
and Leadership, Jakarta Sees Rich Papua Join Secessionist Drive --- Hoping `to Muddle Through'
By Jay Solomon
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,595 words
29 November 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
JAYAPURA, Indonesia -- In May, tens of thousands of Irian Jaya residents flocked to their provincial capital
here. After several days of speech-making and spear-brandishing as police stood silently by, they cheered
the hoisting of their own flag.
With that, Irian Jaya declared its desire for independence and threatened Indonesia with the loss of one of its
richest and largest provinces. Now Irian Jaya is a tinderbox in the world's fourth most-populous country at the
worst possible time. After years of economic exploitation and military repression, Indonesia finds itself nearly
broke and rudderless just when it needs to show its provinces that things will be getting better. And the
rumblings of tribal, religious and ethnic divisions, heard dimly for years here, are now producing new
eruptions.
In the Maluku islands, Christians and Muslims are waging a religious war with bow and arrow and machete,
killing thousands and causing migration on a scale similar to the Balkans in the 1990s. The Indonesian
military is fighting a bloody battle against separatist guerrillas in Aceh province. In the wealthy province of
Riau, some local leaders want a secession vote. In East Timor, which is now independent following its 1999
vote to secede, militias backed by the Indonesian army have attacked United Nations security forces.
Hundreds of people have died, including 10 U.N. personnel, and tens of thousands have been displaced
since the secession vote.
"People here now expect freedom and are demanding their sovereignty back," says Theys Eluay, an Irian
Jaya tribal elder and the elected chairman of the independence movement's official body, the Papua
Presidium.
Things have been heating up in the province in a way disturbingly reminiscent of East Timor, with rising militia
movements, allegations that the military is instigating violence and a growing number of deaths, including one
bloody backlash over showing the independence flag. Many fear that matters could boil over Friday when
Papuans gather to commemorate the day in 1961 when they declared their independence from Dutch rule.
The military has warned of a stern crackdown at any new declaration of independence.
Indonesia's founding father, Sukarno, unified his disparate populace -- with 220 million people speaking 600
languages on 17,000 islands -- with a galvanizing call to throw off the yoke of colonialism. Suharto, his
successor, used economic growth and a repressive military to keep the islands united. Asia's financial crisis
in 1997 destroyed one element of that equation, and the collapse of Mr. Suharto's 32-year regime in 1998 left
a leadership vacuum and began eroding the military's power.
The country has shown remarkable resilience as the upheaval of recent years rocked its economic, social
and political systems. Last year, Indonesia staged its first democratic election in more than 40 years. The
economy is on the mend, recording third quarter growth of 5.1%, compared with a year earlier. But it's not
clear whether Indonesia can sustain that growth. The government remains desperately short of funds and
President Abdurrahman Wahid has received so far only low marks for leadership in an increasingly fractious
political environment.
With religious and political conflict has come a growing lawlessness that touches the rest of Asia. A
nationwide rise in illegal logging and slash-and-burn clearing threatens to destroy large swaths of one of the
world's largest rain forests and bring back the choking haze that blanketed Southeast Asia two years ago.
Cash-strapped Indonesian soldiers are turning to piracy in the global shipping lanes that carry vital oil and
natural gas to Japan and others. Terrorism is on the rise. In September, 15 people were killed when a car
bomb exploded at the Jakarta Stock Exchange building. The U.S. Embassy in the capital recently reopened
after a two-week closure owing to what it called "credible" threats to safety.

--- Page 213 ---
Page 213 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Dewi Fortuna Anwar, chief foreign-affairs adviser to former President B.J. Habibie, also fears the instability
may undercut Indonesia's traditional role as a counterweight to China. "What happens in Indonesia colors the
whole region," she says.
Irian Jaya, also known as Papua, presents a strange stage for geopolitical drama. Its mangrove swamps,
equatorial glaciers and many indigenous peoples -- such as the Asmat, who only recently gave up
headhunting -- are far removed from the world at large. Yet the independence drive here, and the men
leading it, reflect the political forces at play across Indonesia in the wake of Suharto's fall.
Mr. Theys is 63 years old and a chief of the Sentani tribe. Before taking up Papua's independence movement,
he was a three-term legislator for Mr. Suharto's political machine, known as Golkar,
His landholdings as a chief make him relatively wealthy, and some days he can be found at his extensive
home handing out cash to needy members of his tribe. A generally stolid man, he possesses a Don King-like
shock of white hair and a colorful wardrobe that give him a certain flair. For a meeting in his de facto office in
Jayapura, a hotel coffee shop, Mr. Theys wore a green jacket, green-and-blue-plaid trousers and a tie
featuring the countenance of the Statue of Liberty in red, white and blue
Another key figure in the independence movement is its chief fund-raiser, Yorrys Raweyai, 49. A native
Papuan of Chinese descent, he made his name and fortune overseeing protection rackets, collecting debts
and running nightclubs. He eventually became a top official with one of Mr. Suharto's strongest
social-engineering tools, the Pancasila Youth group. With as many as seven million members, it espoused
the state ideology of "unity through diversity" and acted as the president's paramilitary wing. When Mr.
Suharto fell, however, Mr. Yorrys and his youth group -- he remains its secretary general -- fell out of favor.
Earlier this year, his Suharto ties caught up with him when Mr. Yorrys was jailed for a time on charges that he
orchestrated a 1996 attack on the headquarters of Mr. Suharto's chief political opponent at the time,
Megawati Sukarnoputri, the current vice president.
Both sides are playing for high stakes in Papua. The province is home to the world's biggest copper and gold
deposit, mined by Freeport-McMoRan Copper & Gold Inc. of Louisiana, and to one of the world's largest gas
fields, being developed by Atlantic Richfield Co., a unit of BP Amoco PLC. Many Indonesians worry that
independence for Papua could set off a chain reaction among other restive provinces, some of which also
possess lucrative natural resources.
In addition, Mr. Theys's independence movement is stoking fears that Papua will follow East Timor down the
path to carnage. The death toll from East Timor's decades-long fight for independence ranges from the
Indonesian military's count of 60,000 to Timorese groups' figure of 200,000. Nothing on that scale has
happened in Papua, but last month saw one of its worst incidents. After Indonesian soldiers tore down some
Papuan flags, militiamen nominally led by Mr. Theys's 31-year-old son and security chief, Boy, took revenge
on non-Papuans, killing more than 30.
Already, there are signs that nonnative residents of Papua are leaving the province because they are worried
about attacks by native Papuans. This is what happened in East Timor before the 1999 secession vote. In
another echo of East Timor, there is concern that Jakarta could foment violence between anti-independence
militias and pro-independence militias -- there have been reports of both in Papua -- and between the various
tribal militias. The presence of Mr. Yorrys also unnerves many independence backers because he has a
history of instigating unrest.
Military commanders in Papua deny that they are inciting unrest. Mr. Yorrys denies that he instigated the
attack on Ms. Megawati's political headquarters.
Mr. Yorrys discovered and joined the independence movement while he was in jail. Upon his release this
summer because of insufficient evidence, he was invited to help the movement raise funds and to serve as its
international ambassador. He recently hosted a lavish party in his Jakarta mansion to celebrate a diplomatic
success: a pledge by the South Pacific nations of Nauru, Vanuatu and the Solomon Islands to back the
movement's cause. "We feel very happy, as the Papuans' views are now known to the world," says Mr.
Theys, chatting with Mr. Yorrys near photographs of Mr. Suharto, his children and paramilitary youth gangs.
Also nearby, a group of Papuan women sings Christian hymns, one legacy of centuries of colonial missionary
work in Papua.
Papuans say they shouldn't have been part of Indonesia in the first place. After the Dutch pulled out of what
was then known as Netherlands New Guinea, Indonesia annexed it in 1963. In 1969, the U.N. offered Papua
an independence vote, but only 1,000 tribal leaders handpicked by Jakarta officials were allowed to
participate. Willy Mandowen, a negotiator for the independence movement, notes that Papuans differ in
religion, ethnicity and culture from Jakarta's mainly Muslim bureaucrats.
The Papua Presidium has a simple and highly popular basis for rule: kick out the Jakarta bureaucrats, stamp
out prostitution and drugs, and replace the laws imposed by the central government with those of adat, or

--- Page 214 ---
Page 214 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
tribal custom. Answering that call, Papuans have built an elaborate government-in-waiting here, including a
tax system, an elected 32-person executive body and an adat legal structure characterized by co-ordination
among the territory's tribal councils.
Another popular concept underlies the idea of independence, as it is known through the Indonesian word for
it, merdeka. Beyond its political meaning, the term is steeped in religion and suggests a supernatural state,
one of nirvana, and one that many believers expect to attain on Dec. 1, which is the province's independence
day.
The gathering here last May and into June was for the Second Papuan National Congress. The first occurred
on Dec. 1, 1961, when the Papuans declared independence from the Dutch, though without success. It was
then that they first raised their Morning Star flag, which represents, through Papuan mythical symbols, the
people's struggle for liberation from colonial oppression.
President Wahid told the independence movement earlier this year that Papuans could display the flag in
front of their buildings, provided it flew next to and below the Indonesian flag. Mr. Wahid's concession led to
flags being displayed all over the province and not necessarily in the manner he prescribed. The president
subsequently banned the flag, though it is still seen in many parts of the province.
One consequence of the Presidium's rise -- and of the institutional decay since Mr. Suharto's fall -- is that the
central government's civil-service infrastructure here is withering from disuse. In the town of Sentani, demand
for everything from identification cards to marriage certificates has evaporated, says Marthen Ohee, the vice
mayor, because people are turning to the Presidium for services instead of the central government. He once
oversaw a staff of 20, but most have stopped showing up for their shifts. "My employees have lost their desire
to work," Mr. Ohee says, looking out from his nearly empty office at a nearly empty parking lot.
A drive through Papua shows how well-established the Presidium's taxation and security apparatus is. Many
towns and villages are dotted with small wooden command posts flying Morning Star flags. In Sentani,
several militiamen from one command post patrol the streets. Clad in black uniforms and T-shirts bearing the
countenance of Mr. Theys, they station themselves at key intersections and tax the passing trucks. The
payments, ranging around $2, are voluntary and will be used to fund the operations of the Satgas, as the
militia is known.
The central government's men in Sentani say they're unable to halt these operations. Luther Totumbu, the
district police commander, says he fears any crackdown "could bring retribution against our families."
In any case, Jakarta's law-enforcement system here has its own problems. In July, members of Indonesia's
police force, which has been given more control under government reforms, got into a shootout in the town of
Timika with soldiers from the army's crack Strategic Reserve unit. The soldiers killed a policeman after he
barred a soldier from entering a sporting event where the police were providing security. The next day, at a
memorial ceremony for the slain man, a 21-gun salute by the police turned into another firefight with the army
unit. A similar incident in Jayapura resulted in the death of a subdistrict army commander.
The disarray leaves Papua's militias free to take a sometimes-violent stance against vice. Prostitution, alcohol
and gambling are their targets, and punishment can range from push-ups to canings and Chinese water
torture. "If someone has repeatedly broken the law, we might even have to beat them up," says Mr. Boy,
wearing black battle fatigues with a Nike swoosh.
Irfan Nuhuyanan, a bootlegger, says Satgas militiamen operating in Merauke district beat him until he was
unconscious and destroyed the apparatus he uses to make coconut wine. "The militias are now stronger than
the police and military," Mr. Irfan says.
Both sides in the independence battle are turning up the heat. The Presidium has launched a globetrotting
diplomacy campaign to gain a secession vote. In September, Mr. Theys and his team traveled to New York to
drum up support among U.N. members at the Millennium Summit there. The Indonesian military is taking a
hard line toward the Presidium. Susilo Bambang Yudhoyono, Indonesia's coordinating minister for security
and political affairs, said recently that a declaration of independence on Dec. 1 would be considered "an act
of treason and the government will take stern actions." Meanwhile, Papuans report an influx of Indonesian
troops across the province and, ominously, the rise of pro-Jakarta militias.
Given Jakarta's weak hand, few people here expect a clear resolution. Instead, they fear a steady, simmering
breakdown of Indonesian central control that fosters a warlord environment where men such as Messrs.
Theys, Boy, and Yorrys can flourish. One model for the future Indonesia often cited is China in the early 20th
century: the anarchic period after the collapse of the Ming dynasty in 1911.
In places like Papua, says Wimar Witoelar, a spokesman for Indonesia's president, "we'll have to accept that
most of the power will be in their hands now, not Jakarta's." He says broad autonomy is possible as long as it
doesn't include controlling defense, foreign affairs and monetary policy.

--- Page 215 ---
Page 215 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A federal system is one solution. Indeed, under the International Monetary Fund's economic-reform program,
Indonesia is embarking on an ambitious financial decentralization program that will give much greater power
to provinces. In a globalized economy, many Indonesians believe the centralized government can no longer
work.
Can Jakarta regain the goodwill of its provinces? Mr. Wahid's waffling on the Morning Star flag hasn't set a
good precedent. When asked what the president's plans were to keep Papua on board, Mr. Witoelar offers an
ominous admission: "On the bright side, you could say the government hopes to muddle through. But a
pessimist would say there's no cohesive policy."

View / Music:A High-Voltage Composer Moves Beyond the Fringe
WEEKEND JOURNAL
View / Music: A High-Voltage Composer Moves Beyond the Fringe
By Matthew Gurewitsch
1,253 words
17 November 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
W13
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
New York -- After the revolution, everything may be different. For now, you would expect the world premiere
at Carnegie Hall of an evening-length string quintet by an avant-garde performer-virtuoso to draw at best a
sparse and mostly scruffy crowd. But on Oct. 17, Giovanni Sollima's "Viaggio in Italia" ("A Journey Through
Italy"), a piece of just this description, brought out a nearly full house graced by plenty of half-dressed, heavily
bedizened beauties, many on the arms of sleek elder plutocrats.
"Did anybody pay to be here tonight?" one cynic of the third estate wondered, eyes raking the boxes.
"Yes!" a colleague shot back. "Gianmaria Buccellati."
Yes, indeed. All present were the guests of the paterfamilias and reigning designer of Italy's deliberately retro
house of peerlessly crafted jewelry and objets de vertu. He had arranged this surprising musicale to celebrate
the opening of the exhibition "Buccellati: Art in Gold, Silver and Gems" at the Smithsonian National Museum
of Natural History, in Washington, D.C., where it continues through Feb. 25. And why not? "This exhibition --
for our family, it is like a coronation," the portly, white-haired, disarmingly approachable Mr. Buccellati said
both in public and in private. The clan was very much in evidence, down to the gaggle of grandchildren who
brought on bouquets after the Sollima premiere.
Inevitably, a few dignitaries had to be heard from before we could listen to the music. Italian diplomats and
cultural officials heaped praises and a medal on Mr. Buccellati. He, however, wanted only to share his
excitement about the new piece. "At the end of the evening, no one will say, `Bravo, Buccellati!' You will be
saying, `Bravo, Sollima! And thank you for this wonderful music.'" He spoke as one whose passion for playing
the violin has lasted a lifetime -- and who says he could imagine only one career other than the one his
pedigree predestined him for: that of a conductor.
Mr. Sollima, for his part, was born into a family of musicians, who brought him up playing the cello. Now in his
late 30s, he could well have gone the route of the classical virtuoso. But from the beginning, his antennae
picked up music of every clime and period. As a performer, he looked beyond the standard repertoire, and
eventually, he started composing, too. Onstage with his instrument, in the grip of the music, dressed all in
black, behind wire-rim glasses, he can look like a mad professor. In photographs, his smoldering, doleful eyes
and broad-cut lips give him the aura of a rock balladeer.
Like the culture of his native Sicily, Mr. Sollima's art is fed by influences from east and west, north and south,
Christian, Arab and pagan. He plays as if on his own heartstrings, in tones that sound ancient and somehow
exotic. He has been known to perform with two bows simultaneously, one actually woven into the strings of
his cello, and his ability to conjure up, by himself, the sonority of a whole string orchestra verges on black
magic. Time-suspending, haunted adagios are as much a trademark as his flights of polyphonic
prestississimo bravura.
Mr. Sollima is no newcomer to New York. Three years ago he spent six months here, at work on the
scintillating "Aquilarco," inspired by themes of flight. Recorded on Point Music/Universal, the new-music label
controlled by Philip Glass, the piece featured not only the composer on cello but also Robert Wilson speaking
texts by Mr. Wilson's autistic protege and longtime collaborator Christopher Knowles. Performances at such
offbeat venues as the Knitting Factory and Merkin Hall, as well as radio appearances on NPR, WNYC and
WBAI, brought Mr. Sollima's name and his eclectic, high-voltage style to the notice of a mesmerized fringe
audience.
Hardly, one would think, Mr. Buccellati's clientele, whose predilections run to oversize Renaissance- and
baroque-inspired baubles so massive that (as one of the guests at Carnegie Hall remarked, pointing to her
own parure of diamond earrings and matching necklace) they almost look fake. But Mr. Sollima's music is
definitely catching on. Next year, Yo-Yo Ma introduces audiences in Tokyo to "Violoncelles, vibrez!" for two

--- Page 217 ---
Page 217 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
cellos and string orchestra. Also due next year is a new symphonic work, commissioned by Riccardo Muti for
the outstanding concert series of the orchestra of Milan's Teatro alla Scala. The world premiere, in Ravenna,
of a first opera, based on Shakespeare's "The Tempest," has been announced for 2002, although Mr. Sollima
has scarcely begun to work on it.
"Giovanni," says Lucia Ostali, of Casa Musicale Sonzogno, Mr. Sollima's publisher, "is the composer we have
been waiting for for years." Quite an endorsement from the house that has prospered for decades on such
operatic staples as "Cavalleria Rusticana," "I Pagliacci" and "Andrea Chenier," now approaching or even past
expiration of their lucrative copyrights.
"Viaggio in Italia," meanwhile, a suite in 14 movements running nearly 70 minutes, is not a quintet in the
conventional sense. There are indeed five musicians: a string quartet -- in this case the American, all-female
Lark Quartet, playing as if their lives depended on every note -- and a solo cello (Mr. Sollima), who on
occasion joins the quartet but also performs several movements alone. Variously inspired individual
movements evoke in turn the political and intellectual history of Italy, its art, landscape and dominant
personalities. The first and final movements memorialize the German-born Frederick II (1195-1250), Holy
Roman Emperor and King of Sicily, in a wild, mildly dissonant hoedown. "A very modern political figure," Mr.
Sollima says, "open to the world and different cultures. He's in our DNA."
Elsewhere, Mr. Sollima conjures up -- though never literally -- Giotto, Leonardo, an earthquake at sea, the
architecture of Pisa, the paintings of Arcimboldo and Giorgione, an imaginary city out of Italo Calvino. Other
sections involve singing or a sort of chant. A love poem by Michelangelo is set as a jaunty madrigal, in stark
contrast to excruciating deathbed utterances of Borromini and the philosopher Giordano Bruno, stitched
together in part from research sources on the Internet. At Carnegie Hall and in a subsequent performance at
the Smithsonian, Mr. Sollima delivered these passages himself in a chain-smoker's raucous croak, wracked
with emotion, embracing the neck of his instrument as if to commit murder.
Would these focal points not better be left to a classically trained singer or a pop musician? Either would be
appropriate, the composer conceded the other day from his studio in Palermo, his pencil audibly sketching
musical diagrams for the Muti commission as he spoke. But the need to express these sentiments in his own
voice was a little overpowering. "The voice of the cello is always close to song. My voice comes from the guts
of the instrument, with a primitive urgency, like a fever. To put it that way -- e una concessione poetica alla
brutezza della voce. It's making poetic allowance for the ugliness of my voice."
Is there any secret correspondence between Mr. Buccellati's art and Mr. Sollima's?
"We have different ways of re-reading the past," Mr. Sollima replies. "He sees and interprets the surface, so
he can be more `faithful.' Music is immaterial, like the air, so I must search for the soul. Our ways are
dialectically opposed."
His beaming patron gives a simpler answer.
"No."

The Immortal Metal That Once Ruled the World
Bookshelf
LEISURE & ARTS
The Immortal Metal That Once Ruled the World
By Stanley W. Angrist
1,044 words
9 November 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
THE POWER OF GOLD
By Peter L. Bernstein
(Wiley, 432 pages, $27.95)
IN 1511, KING FERDINAND of Spain coined immortal words: "Get gold, humanely if possible, but at all
hazards -- get gold." And so the Spanish did -- getting lots and lots of it from the New World. But Ferdinand's
words are more than the expression of one sovereign's desire; they are a kind of credo by which people have
lived since gold's discovery thousands of years ago.
In "The Power of Gold," Peter Bernstein tells the story of this beautiful metal, which has been used for so
many intricate purposes during the course of history: decorating the first sanctuary where Jews worshiped
God, providing an underlying value for the currencies of nations, enhancing the circuit boards on space
shuttles, not to mention launching a thousand metaphors without which nearly every language on earth would
be the poorer. As odd as it may sound, Mr. Bernstein has turned this story -- not an obvious golden
opportunity for even a writer of Mr. Bernstein's skills -- into a real page-turner.
Though gold has been mined for thousands of years on every continent, there is not much around, as Mr.
Bernstein reminds us. If you melted all the gold ever mined and poured it into a cube, it would weigh about
125,000 tons, he notes, and fit "aboard any of today's great oil tankers." The U.S. steel industry turns out that
amount of steel "in a few hours." And yet steel, from which skyscrapers, automobiles and washing machines
can be built, is valued at about 2 cents an ounce, while gold, whose uses are now primarily decorative, is
currently valued at about $270.
Steel, because it is not inert, may combine with other elements, such as oxygen, and become rust, while gold
goes on being gold. Its scarcity, durability and everlasting glow have made it an obvious choice as a "store of
value" -- a substance that will be worth something when all else fails (governments, currencies, stocks, the
real-estate market).
Reflecting its earlier use as a monetary standard, the vaults of the New York Federal Reserve Bank in
Manhattan still contain thousands of ounces of gold in bars weighing about 30 pounds and sitting in closets
with the names of different countries printed on the doors. From time to time, bars are shifted from one closet
to another in response to central-bank transactions.
Isaac Newton has traditionally been assigned either the credit or blame for inventing the gold standard in
1717, even if that was not his intention. As England's master of the mint, he recommended temporarily
freezing the value of gold guinea coins to establish an appropriate ratio between the prices of gold and silver
and their supply. But "the English never looked back," Mr. Bernstein writes, continuing to "manage their
currency with gold as the standard" even after the guinea had disappeared from circulation.
It was during the Victorian and Edwardian eras that the idea of an international gold standard evolved into
sort of a religion. Its creed rose out of the British Bullion Committee of 1810, which pronounced that it was
folly to let governments print as much money as they wanted and not expect inflation to result. The countries
that joined the gold standard -- according to which the amount of printed money derived from gold reserves
-- found that their currencies were accepted everywhere.
The standard held sway for more than 150 years, sometimes limiting the choices available to finance
ministers when the money supply was badly in need of adjustment and locking currencies into a fixed relation
to one another despite changing economic realities. During much of that period the British pound ruled the

--- Page 219 ---
Page 219 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
world as the standard of exchange. (Even though the name "pound sterling" implies a silver standard, its
value was defined in terms of gold.)
The gold standard began to lose force in the 1930s as prices and industrial output plummeted. President
Roosevelt began untangling the mess by issuing an executive order requiring all persons to deliver their gold
coin, gold certificates and bullion to the banks in exchange for paper currency or bank deposits, and for the
banks to deliver the gold to the Federal Reserve. More gold in the government's hands meant a greater
freedom to print money and lift prices.
In January 1934, Roosevelt devalued the dollar by raising the price of gold 69% to $35 an ounce, a dramatic
step that caused gold to flow into various monetary reserves. Mr. Bernstein comments: "Although many other
factors were at work, it is worth noting that from 1933 to 1937 industrial production jumped by 60% and
wholesale prices climbed 31% while unemployment fell from 25% to 14%."
In the early 1970s, when the U.S. faced soaring inflation, President Nixon imposed wage-and-price controls
for a short time and also announced that the gold window was closed -- that the Treasury would no longer
sell or buy gold to and from nations and central banks at $35 an ounce. The dollar weakened, making
American exports more attractive and foreign imports less so. (Ultimately, inflation was broken in 1980 when
Paul Volcker, chairman of the Federal Reserve, pushed interest rates to historically high levels.) For better
or worse, currencies are now generally subject to the vagaries of supply and demand and are not
redeemable, as they once were, for gold.
Which is not to say that gold has lost its luster. History suggests a never-ending cycle of desire. The
legendary Midas, the emperors of Byzantium, the kings and queens of Spain, and the gold bugs of the 1980s
(who counted on a stupendous price rise that never happened) -- all were fools for gold. They chased an
illusion because they believed that gold occupied an almost metaphysical realm as a hedge against the
uncertainties of life. But "the pursuit of eternity," Mr. Bernstein wryly observes, "is not to be satisfied by gold,
or by anything else we choose to replace gold -- dollars, euros, whatever."
(See related letter "Letters to the Editor: All That Glitters Here Really Is Gold" -- WSJ Nov. 24, 2000)

Page 220 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Commerce, Drama Duke It Out in Big Brazilian State---Governor Franco HasToo Much Fight in HimFor Some Businessmen
International
Commerce, Drama Duke It Out in Big Brazilian State --- Governor Franco Has Too Much Fight in Him
For Some Businessmen
By Jonathan Karp
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,246 words
23 October 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
BELO HORIZONTE, Brazil -- Amid the gardens and wide boulevards of the capital of Minas Gerais state, a
quiet economic powerhouse hums away. The home to multinationals such as Fiat SpA and agribusiness giant
Cargill Inc., Minas boasts an economic output exceeding that of several Latin American countries, including
Chile and Peru.
But as has often happened during the past two years, the business news in Minas has been overshadowed
by the latest political soap opera starring Gov. Itamar Franco. Last month, in a showdown with the federal
government, the theatrical populist ringed the governor's mansion here with police sharpshooters and,
feigning an imminent attack by the army, he compared himself to former Chilean leader Salvador Allende,
who was overthrown in a 1973 coup.
It wasn't the first time the antics of the 70-year-old Mr. Franco, a former Brazilian president, have brought
unwanted attention to Minas. Last year, he helped trigger Brazil's traumatic currency devaluation by declaring
a debt moratorium. He ousted two U.S. investors from a state electricity utility and threatened to divert rivers
to block the privatization of a federal power company.
Now, as Mr. Franco grandstands ahead of another run at the presidency, some Minas businesspeople feel he
is not only hurting the image, but also the economy, of Brazil's third-richest state. Elsewhere in Brazil, capital
flows are surging. But evidently not so in Minas. A survey by the independent Federation of Industries of the
State of Minas Gerais estimates that investment in new factories will fall 57% in 2001, compared with 2000.
Meanwhile, existing industries will invest 30% less in expansions.
Some investors are clearly unnerved by the bitterness of Mr. Franco's rivalry with President Fernando
Henrique Cardoso, his successor. When Mr. Franco recently tried to remove federal troops protecting a
presidential family farm from peasant activists, a worried European executive phoned Stefan Salej, president
of the Minas industrial federation. "Is this a new Chiapas?" he asked, referring to the strife-torn Mexican state.
Meeting with investors in Miami recently, Luiz Fernando Figueiredo, a central-bank director, was asked:
"How many other Itamars do you have in Brazil?"
Mr. Salej finds himself working overtime to contain the damage and to promote a state whose central
location, abundant natural resources and educated work force should make it a preferred destination for
multinationals. He invites Mr. Franco's administration along on investment roadshows, but the centerpiece of
his strategy is a McKinsey & Co. plan to attract capital in areas that don't rely on help from the Minas
government.
"This is the private-sector answer to political adversity," Mr. Salej says, choosing his words carefully. (Mr.
Franco recently summoned and upbraided a Minas steel magnate who had criticized him.)
Minas has long hosted multinationals, and established investors have recently expanded operations here.
The most prominent newcomer is DaimlerChrysler AG, which assembles Mercedes-Benz cars in Mr. Franco's
hometown. However, the $820 million project was signed and almost completed before he took office in 1999.
And, Mr. Franco ordered a contract review, settling eventually for minor rescheduling of state loan payments.
Unlike the southern state of Rio Grande do Sul, which lost an investment by Ford Motor Co. after a freshly
elected left-wing governor demanded contract changes, Minas hasn't had a defection under Mr. Franco. But
neither has it capitalized on investor interest. Mr. Franco refuses to join the so-called "fiscal war," in which
states compete for projects by offering financial incentives. Ford considered relocating to Minas, state officials
say, but chose the northeastern state of Bahia.

--- Page 221 ---
Page 221 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In a similar vein, when every state recently waived a certain tax on Internet sales of General Motors Corp.'s
new car for Brazil, Minas dissented, calling the move unconstitutional.
No statistics exist, but "there is a general perception that Minas has missed out on investments that have
come to Brazil," says John Mein, president of the American Chamber of Commerce in Sao Paulo. "It would
obviously be helpful if they had a pro-active, pro-business government."
The predicament is a far cry from the state's historic role as an economic pioneer. Gold first brought
prosperity, but steel turned Minas into the seat of Brazilian heavy industry. Minas was among the first states
to promote itself to foreign investors and issue bonds abroad. Minas accounts for 10% of Brazil's economic
output.
But Mr. Franco prefers to draw inspiration from Minas's political heritage, as the cradle of Brazilian
nationalism. "Itamar's actions strike a very open nerve, a resentment against federal government dating back
to the days when Portugal extracted gold and taxes from Minas," says David Fleischer, a political scientist at
the University of Brasilia.
They also personalize a 300-year-old rivalry between Minas and neighboring Sao Paulo, Mr. Cardoso's home
state. Mr. Cardoso served as finance minister under then-President Franco in the early 1990s and was
considered by many the power behind the throne. (As president, Mr. Franco was perhaps best remembered
for being photographed at Carnival with a model wearing no underwear.) Mr. Cardoso received the credit for
launching the economic plan that quashed hyperinflation during Mr. Franco's presidency. Later, he scuttled
Mr. Franco's 1998 presidential bid, bumping him down to state politics.
After defeating a Cardoso loyalist in Minas, Mr. Franco came out swinging. In his first week as governor, he
announced a halt on debt payments, likening the federal government to a "loan shark." Then to foil Mr.
Cardoso's plan to privatize power company Furnas, he mobilized 2,500 military police near Furnas's
hydroelectric plants in Minas and vowed to drain the rivers they drew from.
Investors and the U.S. government howled when Mr. Franco succeeded in stripping a group of minority
investors, including AES Corp. and Southern Co., of operating control of the Minas electricity utility, known as
Cemig. Mr. Franco said the shareholders agreement, signed by his predecessor, was illegal. The dispute is
trapped in various courts, and Cemig's shares are valued at one-third of the $1.05 billion that AES, Southern
and a Brazilian partner paid for them in 1997.
Instead of trying to assuage investors at a well-attended meeting in New York earlier this year, Mr. Franco
alienated them. He criticized the U.S. partners in Cemig, referred to foreign investors as colonizers, and, says
Alexei Remizov, a vice president at J.P. Morgan & Co., he was "not willing to give any assurances about a
level playing field or stable regulatory framework."
Minas officials defend Mr. Franco's investment policies, saying they are legal and fiscally responsible, unlike
states whose lavish tax breaks could burden future governments. Kenneth Albernaz of Minas's
investment-promotion board denies that foreign capital is being scared away but says that "it's more difficult
to work in this situation" of political tension.
Tension is bound to rise as the 2002 presidential election nears. Opinion polls rank Mr. Franco between
second and fourth among opposition hopefuls, though with only as much as 15% support, his chances are
remote. Yet despite being denounced by adversaries as "insane" and "authoritarian" in his latest challenge to
Mr. Cardoso, Mr. Franco sees his popularity rise with each new skirmish.

Copper Prices Fall on Stock-Market Drop
Commodities
Copper Prices Fall on Stock-Market Drop
By Marvin G. Perez
Dow Jones Newswires
624 words
19 October 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Copper futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange fell as the stock
market's slide added to fears that demand for the metal will slow next year, traders and analysts said.
The December futures contract dropped 1.10 cents to 87.20 cents a pound, bringing its loss since the middle
of September to about six cents.
The metal began under pressure from weak stocks in Europe. When U.S. stocks dropped more than 400
points in the first 30 minutes of trading, local traders and speculative commodity funds started selling
contracts, traders said.
U.S. economic data compounded the worries of a slowdown in the economy. The Commerce Department
reported that housing starts in September grew 0.3% to 1,530,000, below expectations for a figure of
1,570,000 starts. The consumer-price index rose 0.5% in September, above the 0.4% expected by the
market.
David Meger, senior metals analyst with Alaron Trading in Chicago said the data confirmed that "concerns for
lower [copper] demand for next year are real."
He expressed disappointment that copper didn't rebound along with the recovery in stocks seen later in the
day, nor on the recent retreat of crude-oil prices from their highs. Rising crude prices also have kept copper
under pressure, because of concerns that higher oil prices will raise costs and prevent interest rates from
falling.
Mr. Meger, like many metals traders and analysts, expects the stock market to continue dictating the course
for copper.
One floor trader in New York, timid about predicting where the copper price is heading, said that "as long as
stocks spiral down, copper will go down."
Alberto Arias, Latin American metals analyst with Goldman Sachs in New York said that the effect of falling
U.S. equities would be mitigated by the continued decline in copper stocks at the London Metal Exchange's
warehouses, which fell by 175 tons yesterday to 388,100 tons.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures rose at the New York Mercantile Exchange as the front-month contract neared
expiration and the American Petroleum Institute announced another weekly drop in U.S. inventories.
November futures, which expire tomorrow, jumped 49 cents to $33.48 a barrel. The December contracts,
which now cover more open positions as traders have begun to shift their attention from November,
registered a more modest five cent increase to $32.47 a barrel. The gains came after the American
Petroleum Institute reported U.S. crude stocks dropped 3.1 million barrels to 280.7 million barrels, near their
lowest levels since 1976. Department of Energy data showed an even bigger crude stock decline of 4.5
million barrels. The reports offset otherwise bearish remarks by the president of the Organization of
Petroleum Exporting Countries, Ali Rodriguez. He said the cartel will increase output by 500,000 barrels per
day before its Nov. 12 meeting if crude prices remain above $28 per barrel.
WHEAT: Futures dropped at the Chicago Board of Trade wheat as rain in the Southern winter-wheat belt
improved the outlook for the crop there. The December contract dropped 1.25 cents to $2.64 a bushel.
GOLD: Futures weakened on the impact of weaker currencies against the dollar. The December contract fell
80 cents to $272.60 a troy ounce. The South African rand and Australian dollar remained weak, reinforcing

worries about further selling by producers. South Africa and Australia are, respectively, the No. 1 and No. 3
gold producers and a strong dollar against their currencies makes selling gold more attractive while also
making it more expensive to buyers.
