Gold Prices Sink as Stocks Rally, Oil Prices Fall
Commodities
Gold Prices Sink as Stocks Rally, Oil Prices Fall
By Marvin G. Perez
Dow Jones Newswires
681 words
16 October 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold prices declined sharply Friday as U.S. stocks recovered and crude oil fell, reversing
some of the moves they made the previous day following the eruption of violence in the Middle East.
With the easing of Thursday's market panic about the Middle East, gold lost most of the boost it got from
alarmed investors looking for something safer to hold. Gold was also softened by a further weakening of the
Australian dollar.
The December contract at the New York Mercantile Exchange's Comex division fell $4 to $274.80.
Gold had risen $5.80 on Thursday on the heels of a sharp drop in U.S. stocks and a rise in oil prices,
triggered by the renewed and more violent confrontations between Israelis and Palestinians, and an attack
against a U.S. Navy ship in Yemen, suspected to be a terrorist act.
But when tensions in the Middle East didn't escalate further on Friday, many in the market began to question
the role of gold as a haven against world economic and political uncertainty, and started to sell futures and
lock in profits on the rally, traders and analysts said.
Leonard Kaplan, president at Prospector Asset Management in Evanston, Ill., said that initially gold got a lift
from its lows after the U.S. producer-price index for September showed a rise of 0.9%, above the 0.5%
expected by the market. Gold usually is used to hedge against inflation.
But participants also cautioned that because most of the recent rallies in gold have been fueled by short
covering -- the buying of futures to cancel out previous sales, and not by fresh outright buying, many had
doubted the gains of Thursday could be sustained.
Bridget Fraser, managing director of MacQuarie Inc.'s metal and mining division in New York said that a weak
Australian dollar added to the pressure Friday.
"That produced a lot of selling out of Asia [overnight] which put a cap in the market," said Ms. Fraser, noting
that heavy selling was seen around $277.50 a troy ounce for the December contract.
Weak foreign currencies, in addition to the Australian dollar, have recently driven the gold market. As the
currencies lose value, gold producers try to sell off inventory to lock in at higher dollar prices.
For next week, many players expect developments in the Middle East and currency movements to dictate the
course of gold. Many see the metal languishing in its current range, barring dramatic events in Israel and
Palestine.
In fact, many market watchers said that were it not for the Middle East crisis, gold would be drifting lower
because recent sales from European central banks have kept the market oversupplied.
Frank Homes, chief investment officer of U.S. Global Investors, a fund management company in San Antonio,
said that barring unexpected swings, gold will continue trading at its current levels and will close the year at
around $280 a troy ounce.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures fell at the New York Mercantile Exchange as speculative-commodity funds took profits
on the rally Thursday following an escalation of violence in the Middle East. The November contract dropped
$1.07 to $34.99 a barrel after rising $2.81 on Thursday. "We're seeing a little bit of the fear factor eased" by
the growing likelihood of peace talks, said Tom Bentz, an analyst at Paribas Futures.

--- Page 225 ---
Page 225 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
WHEAT: Futures slid at the Chicago Board of Trade wheat futures as forecasts of rain alleviated concerns
about late plantings, traders said. The December contract fell 6.25 cents to $2.6725 a bushel. The National
Weather Service's long and short-term forecasts called for above-normal rain in the Southwest winter wheat
belt. "That's bearish for wheat, if it's correct the rains will go a long way in improving things for wheat
farmers," said Victor Lespinasse, a grain trader with A.G. Edwards.

The Bull Turns 10, but Will It Reach 11?---Taking Stock,Our Man LooksBack (Humbly)
Getting Going
The Bull Turns 10, but Will It Reach 11? --- Taking Stock, Our Man Looks Back (Humbly)
By Jonathan Clements
845 words
10 October 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The past decade's bull market didn't just fatten my portfolio. It also changed my thinking.
Over the past 10 years, in one Wall Street Journal article after another, I argued that stocks were the best bet
for the long haul. The problem was, others came to like stocks even more, and soon the market was trading
at unprecedented valuations.
By the mid-1990s, I was worried -- wrongly, as it turned out -- that future stock returns would be modest and
that a horrendous crash might be imminent. But I also figured stocks could still deliver better long-run results
than bonds and money-market funds.
So what should I tell readers? The key, I decided, was to stick with stocks, but ensure you captured as much
as possible of the market's potentially more meager gains.
That meant abandoning efforts to beat the market and shunning actively managed stock funds. Instead, I
badgered readers to slash investment costs, diversify broadly, favor market-tracking index funds and shut
their eyes to the market's daily turmoil.
Here's how I arrived at that Spartan investment strategy:
Oct. 16, 1990: "Fund Investors Are Urged to Stare Down the Bear," read the headline on my article. A jeering
Wall Street analyst called and offered to get the story framed, so that I would have a constant reminder of my
foolishness. But luck was with me: The bull market had begun five days earlier.
May 7, 1991: I quoted an investment adviser suggesting that a 65-year-old "could justify a 50% equity
exposure." My editor hastily called a meeting, where we discussed whether the Journal could print such
radical advice.
Feb. 11, 1992: My story was headlined "Why It's Risky Not to Invest More in Stocks." A few months later,
Forbes magazine would mock that headline in a story that concluded "this is not the time to be putting new
money into the stock market."
March 3, 1992: "Mutual fund strategists have long argued that fund investors should boost their holdings of
foreign-stock funds," I opined in a three-part series devoted to foreign investing. "And many say now is an
excellent time to start taking this advice."
It wasn't. But despite disappointing returns, I kept pounding the table for foreign stocks. It was a question of
insurance: I didn't know which part of the global market would shine next, so diversification seemed like the
only prudent choice.
Dec. 9, 1994: By now, even I was fretting about the lofty market. "Stocks are going to generate lackluster
returns," I wrote. "Consider buying more."
It was a great suggestion, backed up by faulty analysis. Stocks went on to deliver an astonishing five
consecutive years of 20%-plus returns.
Nov. 28, 1995: "If you want to retire rich, your best bet is to save like crazy, invest in a broad collection of
stocks, then close your eyes, clench your teeth and don't hold your breath while you wait. Sound easy? For
lots of investors, it isn't."
That Getting Going column was the first of many devoted to investor psychology. Who could figure out this
crazy market? Maybe, at least, we could get a grip on ourselves.

--- Page 227 ---
Page 227 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
June 11, 1996: As the ebullient market confounded naysayers and left active managers in the dust, my
fondness for index funds grew. As I argued that day, "Index funds are brilliant, top-performing, tax-efficient
investments that should play a major role in the stock-fund portfolio of every rational investor. Yup, I like
them."
Feb. 10, 1998: "Spooked by the stock market? Disenchanted with lowly bond yields? Consider taking some of
your investment dollars and using them to pay down your mortgage."
Sure, stocks were the best bet for long-run growth. But in that column and others, I cast around for possible
defenses, suggesting that U.S. stock investors might bring some balance to their portfolios by holding a little
cash, paying off debt or buying inflation-indexed bonds, foreign shares and real-estate investment trusts. In
one moment of weakness, I even ventured a kind word about gold.
Meanwhile, I repeatedly urged readers to focus on things they could control, such as how much they pay in
investment costs and taxes, how much risk they take, how much they save and how they react to the
market's ups and downs.
And still stocks kept rising.
July 18, 2000: I wrote the column with trepidation. "After a decade of waiting for the stock market's "bubble" to
burst, it's time to face reality: Maybe all those stock-crazed small investors haven't been so crazy. Maybe
stocks will never revert to historical norms, where shares trade at 14 times earnings and yield 4%."
Heretical thoughts? No longer. I expected a truckload of dissenting e-mails. I got none.
(See related article: "In '90s Surge, Stocks Became National Passion" -- WSJ Oct. 10, 2000)

Quarterly Mutual Funds Review---Rough Market Boosts Standout Stock Pickers---As Many Funds Struggle for Gains,Top Performers Will Likel...
Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Rough Market Boosts Standout Stock Pickers --- As Many Funds
Struggle for Gains, Top Performers Will Likely Win The Biggest Chunks of New Cash
By Ken Brown
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,886 words
9 October 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
It is hard for portfolio managers to get noticed in the crowded field of mutual funds. That was especially true in
a year like 1999 when a triple-digit performance barely earned a second glance from investors.
But do well in a lousy year and suddenly you are the genius who can do nothing less than defy gravity.
With almost all the major indexes mostly down and the average mutual fund barely above water through the
end of the third quarter, a handful of fund firms are putting up performance numbers that shout, "Look at me."
If the fourth quarter doesn't pull the market out of its dreadful funk, these companies are likely to be big
winners in the sweepstakes for new cash over the next few months.
"I feel better about this year than last year," said Robert Turner, manager of the Turner funds who has been a
top performer this year and in 1999. "Last year, my five-year-old daughter could have picked stocks that
outperformed the market."
For investors and fund companies, the fallout from 2000 could be dramatic and widespread. Unlike in years
past when investors showed some patience for weak funds, today they are quicker than ever before to dump
an underperforming fund. The net result: Investors will be on the prowl for new funds to replace sluggish
holdings and a new group of hot funds will emerge, leaving some of the biggest fund winners of recent years
lagging behind.
The shift may not happen immediately, but when 2000's results are averaged in to three- and five-year
records, the standouts will emerge. "If you can throw in a real good year in a mundane year [for the overall
market], it really helps your three- and five-year average," said Garrett Van Wagoner, who runs five funds that
bear his name.
Among the likely beneficiaries are fund companies with good small-cap and midcap portfolios whose
performances have shined as the market has broadened. One thing that hasn't changed is investors are still
hot for growth funds and tepid on value offerings, meaning growth funds that hold up well should continue to
rake in the cash. While value funds outperformed growth funds in the third quarter, it would take a prolonged
trouncing for investors to abandon their appetite for growth investing.
Some likely candidates for big cash flows in coming months include growth managers Turner Investment
Partners and Oak Associates, both of which soared last year and are trouncing their benchmarks in 2000.
Turner is now selling its funds through Vanguard Group and Merrill Lynch, so the fund group may become a
leading seller. And there are more mainstream candidates, including recent winners such as Vanguard,
Amvescap's AIM Distributors and Invesco units, and Allianz's Pimco, which have solid performance, as well
as T. Rowe Price Associates, which could bounce back after a couple of lackluster years.
Pimco dominates the bond-fund world these days, at least on the load, or broker-sold, side of the business,
so it would benefit as bonds bounce back from the awful stretch. Vanguard should do well on the no-load
side. T. Rowe gets the steady-as-she-goes award; while investors punished the firm for lagging behind its
more aggressive peers these past few years, T. Rowe stuck to its guns and its risk-averse style has allowed it
to put up solid double-digit gains in many of its equity funds, particularly its small-cap and midcap offerings.
Holding up performance in a down market carries a lot of weight with investors. "There is no question it
definitely enhances your reputation," said Mary Lisanti, who manages several funds for ING Group's ING
Pilgrim unit. "To be able to deal in a couple of different markets really helps." Ms. Lisanti's Small Cap
Opportunities Fund is up 14% for the year, slightly ahead of the average for her peers, after a 145% gain in
1999.

--- Page 229 ---
Page 229 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"What you wanted to own in 1999 is not necessarily what you wanted to own in 2000," said Ms. Lisanti. She
points to one theme common to most managers that did well last year and this: They dumped their best
performers early this year as valuations catapulted beyond the outrageous.
Some fund managers didn't do that and ended up riding their big winners way down this year. Among the big
fund names that have tumbled this year are Putnam OTC Emerging Growth, down 18% through Sept. 30,
Janus Venture, down 22% and Kinetics Mutual Fund's Internet Fund, down 35%.
Of course, being cautious all the time won't get you anywhere either, but Ms. Lisanti argues that there is a
time and a place for everything. The fall of 1998, when global markets were falling and stocks were
inexpensive, was the time to take chances. "There are certain markets when you want to be pedal to the
metal," she said. "Your concern is not risk because risk has been priced into the market, your concern is,
`What is your upside?' "
One way to see the impact that a standout performance can have on a mutual fund in a dull year is to look
backward. In this case, the closest recent parallel to 2000 is 1994, when the market ended the year flat as the
Federal Reserve increased interest rates. In both 1994 and today, investors were worried first about
inflation, then about whether the Fed could engineer a soft landing and finally how a weaker economy would
affect earnings.
And like today, there was some speculative fervor in the market. Gold and emerging markets posted amazing
returns in 1993, and both sectors stumbled in 1994. Today, the market is coming off the best run in its history,
with technology having been the big leader. This year, of course, it has been tech that got hurt.
Back in 1994, Mr. Van Wagoner was manager of Govett Smaller Companies Fund, which was launched in
1993 and quickly hit the top of the charts. It bucked the trend in 1994, returning 29% and finishing third
overall. The fund ended the year with $76 million in assets.
Twelve months later, the fund had $519 million, and Mr. Van Wagoner was gone, off to start his own fund
company. "It definitely helped, as we moved through '94 and into '95, people saw that we came through '94
without getting blown away," Mr. Van Wagoner said. He isn't getting blown away in 2000 either; all five of his
funds are up better than 25%.
Unlike 1999, when 180 distinct funds topped 100%, funds had a great year in 1994 if they finished with
double-digit gains. The top fund in 1994, Seligman Communications & Information Fund, returned just 35%
and only 12 broke the 20% barrier. But those that did well in 1994 got big boosts in the following year or two,
propelling them into the top ranks of the industry.
Two of the best-known winners among fund families were T. Rowe Price and Fidelity Investments, both of
which have solid reputations as stock pickers. Back then, Fidelity was on a roll. Its stellar long-term record
was further solidified by its 1994 performance -- nearly 60% of its domestic equity funds beat Standard &
Poor's 500-stock Index. The result: Fidelity took in 25% of all new cash in the fund industry in the next year.
This year, things aren't going so well for Fidelity. While performance has slipped only slightly from recent
years the firm no longer dominates the cash-flow game as it did. Fidelity's market share has fallen to 3% from
14% last year.
T. Rowe Price has also lost some of its glitter, but the firm is hoping that its more conservative style will lead
to top-ranked returns this year. Already some of its small-cap and midcap offerings, as well as its sector funds
are doing quite well. "Firms in this year's environment that are a little bit more risk-sensitive and a little bit
more valuation-sensitive, they are probably doing better," said Brian Rogers, manager of the T. Rowe Price
Equity-Income and T. Rowe Price Value funds. "The big question for me is what it will take for the cash flows
for the more value-oriented funds to move into positive territory," said Mr. Rogers. "That's the $64,000
question."
Whatever the answer, it is certain that cash is moving more quickly than ever and it is rushing toward top
performers. In a study done through May, Financial Research, a Boston consulting firm, found that investors
this year had put $164 billion into funds rated four or five stars by Morningstar Inc., whose ranking system
rewards the recent top performers. Meanwhile, investors withdrew $74 billion from one-, two- and three-star
funds.
And investors also appear to be willing to take fliers on untested funds if they soar right out of the box. In the
past, investors usually were inclined to wait for funds to build the three-year track record required for a rating
from Morningstar and other fund-rating firms. According to the May Financial Research study, funds that were
less than three years old were on pace to take in $123 billion in new cash this year, a 28% jump from 1999.
The fast-money game benefits -- and hurts -- sector funds the most. Every year one or more sectors top the
overall performance charts, and until recently tech has been the big winner. This year, health care, which

--- Page 230 ---
Page 230 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
sports five funds with triple-digit gains, and natural resources are gathering the cash, much of which, if history
is a guide, will flee as soon as another sector emerges at the top.
That willingness to jump on a hot performer has benefited small firms like Oak Associates, whose White Oak
Growth Stock, Pin Oak Aggressive Growth Stock and Red Oak Technology Select funds were stars last year
and this. While Oak Associates has been around for years, it had just $1.1 billion in fund assets at the
beginning of 1999; today it has more than $9 billion in mutual-fund assets, and $33 billion in assets overall.
Oak Associates ranked as the third best-selling fund group in August, ahead of such giants as Fidelity, AIM
and AXA Group's Alliance Capital.
The three Oak funds were up better than 40% for the year through Sept. 30, in part because they avoided the
blowups of the market and took advantage of the cash coming in to buy tech names that had been slammed
in the spring, said Jim Oelschlager, the funds' manager. One example: Brocade Communications Systems,
which fell to $100 a share in May and is now back to $225.
"I wish I could tell you we're brilliant, but we're not, we've just been fortunate," Mr. Oelschlager said.

West Side Story:For Better and Worse,Affluence SurroundsA Housing Project---As Trump and Tishman BuildTowers, Poor NeighborsGet a Chance ...
West Side Story: For Better and Worse, Affluence Surrounds A Housing Project --- As Trump and
Tishman Build Towers, Poor Neighbors Get a Chance to Move Up --- Dishwashers for a Lucky Few
By Paul M. Barrett
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
3,414 words
9 October 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Raynell France longed for a real home. She was 26 years old, pregnant and had just split
bitterly from the father of her eight-year-old son. She shuttled between a tiny apartment in Harlem and her
grandmother's place in the public-housing project where she grew up.
Her grim situation seemed reflected by the very walls around her. Hallways at the Amsterdam Houses project
were dark and dirty. The elevator to her grandmother's seventh-floor apartment often stank of urine.
But something was changing in the neighborhood of her childhood. The housing project, located on
Manhattan's West Side, had become an island of poverty in a surging sea of prosperity. Rising on all sides
were massive new apartment buildings, and more would soon follow, boasting some of the glitziest names in
real estate. To the northwest: Donald Trump's mega-development, projected to have 16 luxury towers. To the
southwest: Daniel Brodsky's triad of skyscrapers. To the west: Tishman Speyer Properties Inc.'s glassy
506-unit colossus where once there had been a vacant lot.
"Maybe there's some way I can get in there," Ms. France mused. The gleaming spires loom just across the
street, but to most Amsterdam Houses tenants, they might as well be in the Emerald City. Rents on
two-bedroom apartments are advertised at as much as $6,500 a month -- more than a third of the average
Amsterdam household's annual income of $16,000. In the past few years, however, the new buildings have
come to have a profound impact on the lives of Ms. France and some of her neighbors.
Across the country, an unprecedented real-estate boom is bringing riches and renewal to long-abandoned
urban cores. But what's going on in this unusual section of New York's West Side isn't classic gentrification.
Developers and yuppies are moving in, but they aren't pushing out the poor. Amsterdam residents mostly are
staying where they are, in one of the city's oldest public-housing projects. Only now they're in the shadows of
some of the most expensive new buildings in America.
The development has brought with it both promise and disappointment to the 2,700 mostly black and
Hispanic residents of Amsterdam Houses. For a handful of them, the soaring new buildings mean direct
economic benefit: One building in the Trump Place compound has hired three maintenance men from
Amsterdam Houses. The Western Beef supermarket on the bottom floor of Mr. Brodsky's West End Towers
has employed about a dozen workers from the project.
For others, the glassy edifices suggest opportunities denied. "I just don't see where those buildings get us,"
says Shirley Jones, a 59-year-old Amsterdam tenant and retired school aide who sells beauty products on the
sidewalk near the project. "The new people are doing fine. Things here are bad and worse."
Perhaps most strikingly, for some Amsterdam tenants the new development offers something none had ever
imagined: a chance to move up simply by crossing the street. In exchange for tax benefits and other
government-sponsored financing perks, Mr. Trump and the other developers have offered hundreds of
apartments to low-income renters throughout the city at huge discounts.
Such programs have gained popularity across the country, as the federal government has turned away from
subsidizing new public housing. The results on the booming West Side are especially dramatic: A
one-bedroom unit in the new Tishman Speyer building that would normally rent for $2,800 a month, for
example, is available at just $465.
When the first of the new buildings began to open about five years ago, developers distributed fliers about the
program through the local community board and took out ads in city newspapers. The program quickly
became the talk of Amsterdam's macadam courtyards.

--- Page 232 ---
Page 232 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Many residents leapt to apply for the bargain-rate apartments. Others, like Marcus Young, balked. A
46-year-old office-lobby receptionist, he says he considered applying to the Tishman Speyer building but
dropped the idea when he learned the tenants would be chosen by lottery from thousands of applications.
"It's like the fix is in," he says.
Amsterdam Houses is one of the few reminders of a historically poor and neglected neighborhood. The area
inspired Leonard Bernstein's musical "West Side Story," and the 1961 film was shot a few blocks from
Amsterdam Houses. In the late 1950s, as part of an urban-renewal campaign, city officials knocked down
acres of shabby West Side tenements to clear the way for the Lincoln Center theater complex. Residents of
Amsterdam Houses immediately sensed a bitter symbolism even in the architecture itself: Their view of the
gleaming white-marble center across the street was of its garage and rear wall.
More recently, as the neighborhood's fortunes gradually rose, local businesses banded together to form a
privately financed street-cleanup and security committee -- but they defined its borders to stop just outside
the Amsterdam project.
Still, when Ms. France spotted an advertising poster on the side of Mr. Brodsky's just-completed West End
Towers five years ago, she wrote down the phone number and called for an application to the reduced-rent
program. When a detailed questionnaire arrived at her grandmother's apartment a few months later, Ms.
France jumped on it.
The odds of hitting the jackpot are long, with dozens of applicants from all over the city typically angling for
each "affordable housing" unit that goes on the market. In the case of West End Towers, Mr. Brodsky had
allocated 20% of its 1,000 units, or 200 apartments, scattered widely, but not including the most desirable
units.
Ms. France's application was rejected. But she decided to walk across the street from the project and try
again. She told a secretary at the rental office about her dreams of providing a better home for her young son
and the baby she was carrying. The secretary gave her a tour of the building.
Ms. France was bowled over by the contrast. Amsterdam's 14 red-brick buildings, most of which date to the
1940s, show the dark stains of age. At West End Towers, doormen stand guard 24 hours a day, and the
elegant wood-paneled lobby gets swabbed and dusted daily. The elevators sparkle. There's central
air-conditioning. Best of all, the model apartment she saw had a dishwasher. "I had prayed for a nice place
with a dishwasher," she says. "I believe in God." The rental-office secretary encouraged Ms. France to appeal
the rejection.
A few months later, she got good news: Her initial attempt had failed because she had mistakenly counted
her grandmother's government benefits in her income calculation. Her own pay from part-time jobs as a
hospital patient-services representative and cleaning-service worker came well within the program's $21,000
annual-income cap.
In mid-1995, Ms. France moved into a two-bedroom apartment with panoramic views of the Hudson River --
for $468 a month. That's about one-sixth of what the place would normally cost. Rents at Amsterdam Houses
range from almost nothing to more than $500, depending on a family's circumstances. At West End Towers,
Ms. France's rent increases by 2% a year, and she is guaranteed the apartment for 30 years, even if her
income rises. (This reporter lives in the West End Towers building adjacent to Ms. France's.)
The front door of the 14th-floor apartment where she lives with her two sons is hung with a large cardboard
sign listing vocabulary words that five-year-old Jayvon must learn. Ms. France now works full-time at her
hospital job and attends community-college classes several nights a week, in hopes of moving up in medical
administration. Staying close to Amsterdam Houses has been critical to her maintaining the work-and-school
juggling act. When she is at night classes, Jayvon and 13-year-old Clyde stay with her grandmother, who still
lives at the project.
While she never had doubts about moving, Ms. France says there were repercussions. Some former
Amsterdam neighbors gossiped that she must have bribed or slept her way into the building -- a falsehood,
she says, but one that embarrassed her grandmother.
She is, in fact, well known at Amsterdam Houses. At mention of her name, some residents murmur with
evident pride about how well they hear she's doing. Others have a more ambivalent reaction. "It's nice for her,
sure," says Ms. Jones. "Not too many others, though." Ms. Jones says she herself applied unsuccessfully to a
Trump tower.
In her new building, Ms. France, who is black, says some of her white neighbors seemed to keep their
distance -- not that unusual in New York, regardless of race. But in the lobby one day, Ms. France says, she
heard a white woman whisper loudly to a companion: "I see they are moving up in the world." Ms. France
says, "It made me feel bad, but I just walked by."

--- Page 233 ---
Page 233 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
More recently, while riding the building's free shuttle bus to the subway, she says she surprised another white
neighbor when conversation turned to their upbringings. The neighbor "couldn't believe I grew up in the
projects," Ms. France says.
Clyde, who sometimes wears an apple-shaped rhinestone earring and likes to write short stories on
basketball themes, attends a public junior-high school that requires students to take a competitive entrance
exam. Ms. France sends Jayvon not to the nearby elementary school, which is dominated by children from
Amsterdam, but to kindergarten at a better-performing school many blocks to the south. Like countless
enterprising New York parents in a system that allows some children to go to school in other neighborhoods,
she pulled strings: An appreciative patient from her hospital job is married to a school-system administrator.
"I feel kind of badly about it, because I went to [the neighborhood school], and this is my community," says
Ms. France. "But moving out was an opportunity, and I have to push for every opportunity for my son."
She still worries a lot. For a visitor's benefit, she prods Clyde to say that his best friends are from school. He
responds that, no, his best friends live across the street, at the project. "That's where he hangs out," his
mother acknowledges with a sigh.
Ms. France wasn't the only one whose dreams of moving away from Amsterdam Houses were fed by hopes
of a new apartment across the street. This summer, Minette Thomas had a falling out with her mother, in
whose Amsterdam apartment Minette and her three daughters had been living. "We're like oil and water --
eventually we separate," explains Ms. Thomas, who is 36 and single.
She badly wanted to stay in the neighborhood. She grew up in the Amsterdam Houses project. Her younger
girls go to school nearby. But Ms. Thomas earns $1,600 a month as a customer-service representative for a
cable-TV company, and the entire sum wouldn't pay the rent for a one-bedroom apartment on the West Side.
"I'd need two or three jobs," she says.
Ms. Thomas graduated from high school while pregnant with her oldest daughter, Shaniqua, now 17. She
dreams of one day moving to Virginia, where she has visited, and of owning her own house and hairstyling
business. Today, the only tangible link to those goals is her skillful arrangement of her own delicate,
gold-highlighted braids.
Her middle daughter, Raushida, a poised 10-year-old who says she would like to be a lawyer because she is
good at settling fights, doesn't go to the school associated with Amsterdam Houses. She attends the
more-integrated and better-regarded Public School 199, five blocks north of the project.
"A child should see the differences in life," Ms. Thomas says. She herself recalls receiving opera tickets as a
child from a family friend who sang at Lincoln Center. "I loved imitating the opera, I guess because I'm a
loud-speaking person," Ms. Thomas says, laughing at the memory.
One hope for staying in the neighborhood was the tan brick-and-glass twin-tower building going up on West
End Avenue and 64th Street. The developer, Tishman Speyer, announced last fall that 104 of the 506 units
would be discounted. Ms. Thomas, eager to get out from under her mother's roof, says she submitted a
written application. But she never heard anything back. That silence, she says, prompted her to wonder, "Is
this application for real, or do they know who they want?"
Her skepticism is common among project residents. And indeed, the affordable-housing application process
is complicated and a bit cryptic.
About 5,500 people applied for discounted apartments in the Tishman Speyer building. Settlement Housing
Fund Inc., the nonprofit group that the developer hired to screen applicants, says it randomly assigns a
number to each application and then begins reviewing them in order, starting with No. 1. There is no
guarantee that every application will be reviewed, and applicants who pull high numbers may not hear
anything for as long as a year, which breeds confusion.
Ms. Thomas also applied to get her own apartment in a public-housing project, requesting Amsterdam or
another site in Manhattan. But so far, the housing authority, which oversees 181,000 units that have an
average vacancy rate of only one-half of one percent, has offered her apartments in the Bronx -- a long
subway or bus ride away. Ms. Thomas turned them down.
Disheartened by her failure to find a low-rent apartment in her neighborhood, Ms. Thomas ended up moving
in late August with her daughters to an aging one-bedroom place at the northern tip of Manhattan, near the
Bronx. She pays $725 a month, or 40% more than Ms. France does for her two-bedroom with the river view.
Ms. Thomas says her daughters miss the new attractions in the neighborhood surrounding Amsterdam
Houses. Five-year-old Diamond remembers splashing in the sprinklers in the small park that is part of the
West End Towers development. "I'm like a fish," explains the slender little girl with large brown eyes. There

--- Page 234 ---
Page 234 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
aren't any similar facilities near the family's present apartment, so Diamond and her sister have spent most
weekends indoors, watching "Brady Bunch" videos and tending to their pet turtle, Simba.
Soon after the Thomases moved into their new apartment, a stove-pipe gas leak sickened Diamond, who was
treated at a hospital for carbon-monoxide exposure. The leak has been fixed and the stove replaced, but Ms.
Thomas, fearing a recurrence, now cooks with a borrowed two-burner electric hot plate.
The bathroom ceiling is crumbling into the bathtub. Raushida says she has trouble concentrating on her
homework because of the noise and rattle of the elevated subway line that runs directly in front of their
building. "I miss where we used to be," she says.
Ms. Thomas sets out by subway at 6 a.m. each weekday to deliver the two younger girls to school on the
West Side, before going on to work. Shaniqua, the oldest, has moved back to her grandmother's apartment at
the project.
Ms. Thomas is far from alone in her dissatisfaction. Deborah McDonald received a form letter in June from
Settlement Housing, informing her that her gross income exceeded the $18,690 limit for the low-price
one-bedroom in the Tishman Speyer building. The limit, derived from government regulations requiring that
beneficiaries demonstrate "continuing need" for housing assistance, is 50% of New York-area median income
for a single person.
"I was very frustrated," says Ms. McDonald, who is 49 and wants to move out of her mother's apartment at
Amsterdam. Her $24,000 official salary as a municipal clerk on paper made her ineligible. But diabetes and
other health problems have limited her actual wage-earning hours. She has W-2 forms from 1998 and 1999
indicating gross salary within the affordable-housing guidelines. She didn't appeal, she says. "I just got
disgusted."
Susan Cole, associate director at Settlement Housing, makes no apologies. "This is the best deal in town,"
she says, "and we want tenants who fit the criteria" for income, credit history and other factors. Mr. Brodsky's
West End Towers and the Tishman Speyer building each have at least three former Amsterdam families, Ms.
Cole says. Charles Reiss, a senior aide to Mr. Trump, says he doesn't know of any ex-Amsterdam tenants
living in the sole Trump Place building so far offering rentals and "seriously doubt[s]" if any do.
For Patricia Ryan, the self-described "mayor of 65th Street," the housing lottery presented a difficult choice.
Ms. Ryan's actual title is president of the tenant association of the 27-story Amsterdam Addition, which was
built in the mid-1970s. A native of the project, she grew up there in the 1960s, moved to the Bronx and then
returned to Amsterdam in the late 1980s, mostly for the relative peace and quiet.
In the Bronx, says Ms. Ryan, 48, "we went to sleep by the gunfire music." She rests easier now and says she
supports herself on disability insurance from an ankle injury suffered years ago as a hotel worker.
In the Addition, as her building is known, Ms. Ryan wields heavy influence. She helps organize everything
from street festivals to the tenant patrol. On a warm summer evening, she presides over a group discussion
in the tenant-association common room, telling stories to a group of friends and handing out flavored ices to
small children who scamper in and out. The developers, Ms. Ryan tells the group, aren't all bad. Her
audience is skeptical.
Later, she explains that her 30-year-old daughter, Marie Torres, lives in a reduced-rent apartment at One
Columbus Place, a Brodsky tower just southeast of the project. Ms. Ryan herself had thrown in an application
for the Tishman Speyer building and got lucky. Her finances passed muster, and Settlement Housing sent her
a letter inviting her to an interview to discuss a one-bedroom apartment, she says.
But this presented a dilemma. She already had a two-bedroom apartment at Amsterdam that was larger. The
rent would be comparable -- in the $500 range. But across West End Avenue, she wouldn't be the mayor of
anything.
The elevators in the Amsterdam Addition sometimes are balky, Ms. Ryan says. When plumbing breaks down,
the housing-authority maintenance workers don't always show up promptly. These and myriad other problems
end up on her doorstep. People count on her to get things done, even if most of them are small things.
"Pat is a pain because she has to be," says Muriel Cartier, another veteran resident of the Addition. "We love
her for it."
In either place, Ms. Ryan says, she would have easy access to the neighborhood's amenities -- the
museums, the free performances at Lincoln Center. Then again, she adds, she rarely gets to those
attractions because she prefers "meetings, tenant business and so forth." Most days, she visits or speaks to
her mother, Ellareet, who also lives in the project.

--- Page 235 ---
Page 235 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ms. Ryan enjoys representing her constituents, such as Minette Thomas's daughter, Shaniqua. Ms. Ryan
recently helped negotiate an after-school job for the high-school senior at the new upscale Food Emporium
that has opened at 64th Street. The store asked the teenager to fill an opening in a different branch, on the
East Side. Ms. Ryan thought that was a good idea. "This way," she says, Shaniqua's "little friends from the
neighborhood won't come in and get her in trouble."
Had she moved across the street to the fancy new building, Ms. Ryan says, she would lose this
intermediary's role. So she decided she wouldn't even respond to the invitation to interview for the new
apartment.
Looking around the cracked concrete courtyard over which she presides, Ms. Ryan says, "I like it here
better."

Gold Falls Amid Weakness in Foreign Currencies
Commodities
Gold Falls Amid Weakness in Foreign Currencies
By David Bogoslaw
Dow Jones Newswires
631 words
9 October 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold prices continued their descent Friday with no relief in sight as weakness in foreign
currencies relative to a potent U.S. dollar hammered away at global purchasing power.
Dollar-denominated gold appears more expensive in terms of local currencies when they lose value against
the U.S. currency. That encourages producers to sell forward to lock in higher prices and damps consumer
demand.
December gold on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange fell $1.30 to $272.10 a troy
ounce.
The Australian dollar, which closely tracks the euro, fell to US53.73 cents on Wednesday, pushing gold in
Aussie dollar terms to A$507, "a level not seen in quite a while and pretty attractive" to producers, according
to Bridget Fraser, managing director of metals trading at Australian bank MacQuarie Inc. in New York.
"Weakening in the currencies is destroying purchasing power around the world," said Ian MacDonald,
manager of precious metals trading at Commerzbank in New York. "Jewelry demand has certainly been
impacted. Every time the currencies go down, gold has to adjust."
Autumn traditionally is a strong season for physical demand as a confluence of holiday buying in cultures
world-wide brings consumers to the market. With the euro under constant pressure, European demand has
been minimal, and a weak rupee has been part of the reason India has abstained from buying, said Leonard
Kaplan, president of Prospector Asset Management in Evanston, Ill.
There had been faint anticipation of a price spike on the first anniversary of the Washington Accord on Sept.
26, under which 15 European central banks agreed to limit their gold sales over the next four years to no
more than 400 tons a year. Prices jumped briefly to $340 a troy ounce last year following the announcement.
Neither did the European Central Bank's 25-percentage-point interest rate increase Thursday help lift the
euro, and with it the price of gold, because Europeans "have no confidence in the currency," in the view of
Commerzbank's Mr. MacDonald. "[The ECB] isn't doing anything to ensure fiscal discipline. People are afraid
to own it."
All of which proves that gold remains inextricably linked to the fortunes of the dollar, which has held firm
despite languishing U.S. equities markets and a general consensus that the U.S. economy has slowed since
midsummer.
In fact, repeated instances all year long have shown that gold prices rally only when there are sufficiently
large short positions in the market. Traders put on short positions in anticipation of lower prices and are often
forced to cover them by buying them back when there are signs of prices rising instead.
In other commodity markets:
HOGS: Prices fell on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, reflecting weakness in the cash market. The
December contract fell 0.92 cent to 52.50 cents a pound. Analysts said packers are reducing their bids, while
farmers are less busy with their harvests and are moving more hogs to market. In addition, retail buyers didn't
embrace higher prices when packers tried to push the product market up. Ample beef and chicken availability
allowed retailers to choose other meats when they felt pork prices rose too much.
COCOA: Prices retreated as a result of selling by speculators and producers, traders said. The December
contract fell $5 to $830 a metric ton on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange. A metric ton is equal to
2,204.62 pounds. Speculators sold futures they had bought during the rally of the previous three sessions,

--- Page 237 ---
Page 237 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
when prices surged amid worries about escalating political tension in Ivory Coast, a big cocoa-producing
nation, ahead of its Oct. 22 presidential elections.

THE AMERICAS
Mexico Plans to Sell
Its Top Two Airlines
In Cintra Breakup
Mexico's deposit-insurance agency IPAB aims to sell the country's two largest airlines in next year's first half.
Gustavo Castillo, head of the agency's corporate-asset division, told Mexican radio that tender rules for the
sale of Aerovias de Mexico, or AeroMexico, and Mexicana de Aviacion could be published in early 2001. The
government controls more than 65% of voting rights in Cintra, the holding company of both airlines. The IPAB
owns about 51% of Cintra, which has a market value of about $550 million, Mr. Castillo said. Cintra had
revenue of about $3 billion in 1999. The IPAB assumed its Cintra stake through debt held by banks seized by
the government after the 1994 peso crisis. The IPAB was seeking to sell Cintra as a single company, but
Mexico's Competition Commission, or CFC, ruled Tuesday that the airlines must be separated before being
sold.
Cintra controls most of Mexico's main air routes. The sale of the two airlines could open the door to foreign
carriers seeking a foothold in Mexico, but Mexican regulations limit foreign ownership of domestic airlines to a
25% stake. Cintra said it was studying the CFC's decision. The CFC's chairman said the separate sale of the
carriers, which he said could raise $1 billion to $1.4 billion, would maximize returns and face no regulatory
constraints.
WTO Gives Canada Time on U.S. Auto Pact
The World Trade Organization gave Canada an additional four months to change a policy that provides
preferential treatment to vehicles imported from the U.S. The Canadian government claimed it needed until
May 1 to change the auto-trade pact, which has twice been ruled illegal by WTO trade experts. The European
Union and Japan, who brought the case, said Canada should have completed the changes last month. WTO
arbitrator Julio Lacarte-Muro, called in to make a binding ruling, said Ottawa should have until Feb. 19 to
make changes. Under its 1965 pact with the U.S., Canada allows vehicles from U.S. auto makers, including
Ford Motor, General Motors and DaimlerChrysler, to enter Canada free of tariffs. Japan and the EU argued
that a 6.1% tariff on imports from other countries violated fair-trade rules, a position upheld by a WTO panel.
It also found Canada's requirement that U.S. auto makers must build at least as many cars in Canada as they
sell amounted to an illegal export subsidy.
Murphy Agrees to Acquire Beau Canada
Murphy Oil agreed to acquire Beau Canada Exploration, a Calgary, Alberta, oil and natural-gas company, for
2.15 Canadian dollars a share, or a total of C$198 million (US$131.7 million), plus the assumption of C$183
million in debt. Murphy, El Dorado, Ark., said the acquisition would increase its western Canadian production
of natural gas by 80% and of natural-gas liquids by 40%, and would provide new exploration opportunities.
The offer, announced after the end of regular trading, represents a 27% premium over Beau Canada's
C$1.69 close in Toronto trading.
Barrick Gold Raises Tanzanian Reserves
Barrick Gold of Canada said gold reserves at its Bulyanhulu project in Tanzania have increased by 40%, and
it sees continued potential for expansion in reserves and production at the project. Barrick said successful
exploration on the Tanzanian property has increased reserves to 10.5 million ounces from 7.5 million ounces
at Dec. 31. Further increases in reserves are expected at the end of this year, it said. It said the project is on

--- Page 239 ---
Page 239 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
schedule and on budget to begin operation in the second quarter of 2001. The company said it is developing
the $280 million Bulyanhulu project with $200 million in financing, the largest bank financing ever granted to a
mining company in sub-Saharan Africa.
BRIEFLY:
-- Brazil's central bank said it is confident the sale of Banco do Estado de Sao Paulo, or Banespa, won't
suffer further legal obstacles to the new scheduled sale date of Nov. 20, announced late Tuesday. But Brazil's
Supreme Court must still rule on a Brazilian bankers union's appeal of the court's go-ahead for the Banespa
sale.
-- The Argentine National Transport Confederation, a truck-driver union, said it will end a four-day-old strike
after oil companies, at the Argentine government's prodding, agreed to cut diesel-fuel prices.

EUROPE
Data Indicate Slower Euro-Zone Growth
More soft economic data, led by surprisingly weak French consumer confidence, indicate euro-zone growth
likely peaked in the second quarter, economists said. The overall index of French consumer sentiment fell to
minus eight in September, from a reading of two in July, France's statistics agency said. The 10-point fall was
the largest one-month decline in five years. Meanwhile, the euro-zone purchasing managers' index for the
service sector continued to decline, as its business-expectations component fell to 67.3, from 72.1, the
European Union said. The recent softening in economic data is expected to ease pressure on the European
Central Bank, whose policy-making council meets today, to raise rates, though most economists expect a
quarter-point increase this quarter.
EU Extends Deutsche Post Investigation
The European Commission has widened the scope of its antitrust investigation of Deutsche Post, after finding
what it said is new evidence that the German postal service abused its dominant market position in Germany.
The commission said it extended the inquiry by its antitrust department after uncovering information indicating
that Deutsche Post offered incentives to customers who agreed not to do business with its competitors. The
developments come as Deutsche Post prepares to float 25% to 33% of its shares on the stock market next
month. A Deutsche Post spokesman said the case would have no effect on the government-owned
company's initial public offering. He also said Deutsche Post doesn't disclose the terms of its contracts with
large customers.
Woolwich Holders Approve Barclays Bid
Shareholders in British mortgage bank Woolwich PLC approved the company's proposed takeover by
Barclays PLC in a GBP 5.4 billion ($7.87 billion) deal that will more than double Barclays' share of the United
Kingdom's mortgage-lending market. Woolwich said the deal is expected to be completed around Oct. 24,
subject to final regulatory approval. Barclays is Britain's third-largest bank by assets, and the fourth-largest by
market capitalization.
BRIEFLY:
-- Cantrade Private Bank Switzerland, a unit of Swiss financial-services company UBS, and accounting firm
Touche Ross & Co., now Deloitte & Touche, agreed to pay $38.5 million to investors to settle a
seven-year-old fraud case involving a convicted independent currency trader and false audits of the trader's
performance.
-- German soccer club Borussia Dortmund has revived plans for a stock-market flotation that would give
Germany its first entry in a European group of listed clubs.
-- German investment firms Knorr Capital Partner and Value Management & Research will today announce
their intention to merge, creating an integrated high-technology venture capital and investment banking firm,
the two companies said.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Three Japan Banks to Create UFJ Group

--- Page 240 ---
Page 240 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Japan's Sanwa Bank, Tokai Bank and Toyo Trust & Banking unveiled details of their business integration
scheduled for April. The banks said they will create a new financial group, called UFJ Group, under the
umbrella of a holding company, to be called UFJ Holdings Inc. UFJ stands for United Financial of Japan. The
banks said 1,000 Sanwa Bank shares will be exchanged for one UFJ Holdings share; 1,000 Tokai Bank
shares will be exchanged for 0.62 UFJ share; and 1,000 Toyo Trust shares will be exchanged for 0.46 UFJ
share. UFJ shares will have a par value of 50,000 yen ($459). UFJ Group is one of four Japanese
megabanks that will be created by next spring. The other three are: Mizuho Financial Group, launched last
week; Sumitomo Mitsui Banking Corp.; and Mitsubishi Tokyo Financial Group.
Mitsubishi Motors Offers Free Service
Japan's Mitsubishi Motors said it will offer free maintenance services for all of its passenger cars in Japan.
The auto maker hopes the move will restore public trust and win back buyers in the wake of its recent
admission that it covered up automobile defects for two decades. The offer covers an estimated six million
vehicles and will run from November through June, but it excludes commercial trucks and buses. The work
will be performed at Mitsubishi Motors' service centers across Japan. A company spokeswoman said it
expects to spend 5,000 to 8,000 yen ($46 to $73) per car, putting the total cost of servicing at 30 billion to 48
billion yen ($275 million to $441 million).
Indosat to Seek Foreign Partner for Stake
Indonesian international long-distance operator Indosat said it will seek a foreign company as a strategic
partner to help the company raise its stake in mobile-phone operator Telkomsel to 51%, from 35% currently.
Indosat expects to pay as much as $400 million to gain control of Telkomsel. Domestic operator
Telekomunikasi Indonesia also aims to gain control of Telkomsel, in which it holds a 42.7% stake.

Fortunetelling: How to Read Unruly Markets' Tea Leaves
Fortunetelling: How to Read Unruly Markets' Tea Leaves
2,786 words
5 October 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Markets of some sort have been in existence for thousands of years. There have always been places where
buyers and sellers of goods and services have met, exchanged goods and traded information.
That's all a market has ever been, a place to exchange things and ideas. And that's what stock markets are
today.
As such, markets serve two distinct but interdependent functions. One, of course, is to facilitate commerce.
The other is to gossip about everything else. Often, the gossip is well informed and well reasoned. Investors,
traders and merchants frequently act on that gossip, which ultimately affects the prices of goods and services
sold -- often before the gossip, or news, is widely disseminated. In a market, information is the coin of the
realm, and so it has always been.
For the past 10 years, Americans have grown preoccupied with market activity, watching the daily ups and
downs much as they watch sporting events. The emergence of free, real-time market information has helped
democratize the playing field, giving individual investors information advantages once the sole purview of
professional investors.
By using the markets as a forecasting device -- that is, by listening to the message of the markets -- individual
investors can take the investment process to a new level of understanding. The deeper the knowledge of the
markets, the more aware investors are of the markets' forecasting ability, the less likely they will be blindsided
by unforeseen events.
To be sure, the message of the markets isn't always clear or easy to understand, even to the pros. At any
given time, the market is sending out lots of different messages. Deciphering messages can be challenging
even when investors are listening to the right ones.
Still, one can find innumerable examples in which market prices turned abruptly, well before the reasons for
the turn were clearly evident to many market participants. And those abrupt turns in the financial markets
often contain valuable information about a coming change in the surrounding economic or political
environment.
Only a handful of individuals recall how the stock market fell in the minutes after John F. Kennedy was shot,
but well before the news of the shooting ran on the news wires. How a decision by Federal Reserve
Chairman Paul Volcker in the early 1980s to slash interest rates helped end the stock market's long period
of malaise and set the stage for the bull market. How oil prices (a current concern as well) skyrocketed well
before Saddam Hussein invaded Kuwait in 1990. And how the troubles of the Thai currency, the baht, in
1997, were an early warning sign of problems in the world financial system that would pummel markets.
These examples illustrate how the markets communicate with the outside world, sending messages from
which an astute observer can profit. Since markets reflect the collective consciousness of investors, it is that
very moment when the collective consciousness starts to shift, just before perceptions change, sometimes
without obvious reason or warning, that investors who notice and act on the change stand to profit most
handsomely.
Indeed, professional investors are like market detectives; they follow clues wherever they lead. It is that
mind-set that individual investors need to adopt as well. Imagine, for example, if the markets could give you
advanced warning of troubled times. Would you spend less and save more if the markets were foretelling that
a recession would arrive in nine to 12 months? Would you refinance your mortgage if interest-sensitive stocks
told you that interest rates were about to go up?
Markets can send advance signals, messages if you will, about these and many other things. Sometimes the
messages are small, indicating that the fortunes of an individual company are about to turn. Sometimes the
messages are larger, suggesting that a shift in the economic environment is in the offing.

--- Page 242 ---
Page 242 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Either way, Wall Street professionals have known for years the value of information. As Gordon Gekko said in
Oliver Stone's movie "Wall Street," "If you're not inside, you're outside." Getting inside requires that investors
listen to the message of the markets. Here are some examples of how the markets have sent messages in
recent times:
---
After a series of punishing blows to the U.S. economy in the 1970s and early 1980s, no one on Wall Street or
on Main Street was ready to hear the siren song of the stock market in the summer of 1982. Economists
frequently predicted yet another dismal year for the economy as the nation suffered through a deep
recession.
We all remember its chief characteristics. Surging inflation, rising interest rates and high unemployment
had become the norm between 1980 and 1982. At the start of the decade, banks would raise the prime
lending rate to 20.5%, as inflation peaked around 13%. Few Americans could believe that a Renaissance
was about to arrive. But the markets would signal what was to come, even as the prophets of doom
proclaimed the dark days would last forever.
The stock market "broke out" in August 1982, staging impressive rallies in the late summer as volume at the
New York Stock Exchange exploded almost inexplicably.
For two years, the Dow Jones Industrial Average had been in a vicious bear market, torn about by the
economic conditions. Indeed, after crossing 1000 briefly in intraday trading for the first time in 1966 but not
ever closing above 1000 until 1972, the Dow industrials had fallen back and gone into a long slump. By
August 1982, the blue chip index would rest at 777, the lowest level it had seen in several years and nearly
25% below the 1000 mark first touched when Lyndon Johnson was president.
Even as stocks plunged back to their lows, something very important was taking place. Bond-market interest
rates began to decline, even as most investors were still betting that inflation and unemployment would
continue rising. The market was sending an important message that the economic outlook was improving.
Few people heard the call.
The bond market's shift had its roots in a disaster brewing in that hot and difficult summer that few saw
coming. But it was a disaster that would provide the impetus for an economic rebound in the U.S.
Latin American nations, to which many of the biggest U.S. banks had lent billions of dollars, were on the
ropes economically and threatened to default on their debt payments. There was a danger that the biggest
and once-proudest American financial institutions were about to crumble under the weight of Latin American
debt.
But Fed Chairman Paul Volcker rang the bell that signaled the start of a new bull market. To bolster big U.S.
banks in the event of a Mexican debt default, the Fed rapidly expanded the nation's money supply, while
slashing interest rates dramatically.
Most people had believed the debt crisis meant even more desperate times. But Wall Street veterans have an
expression that mirrors the notion that it's always darkest before the dawn. The veterans say, "Bailouts are
bullish."
Because of the Fed's move, bond-market interest rates began a decline that would take them from the high
levels of the early 1980s to a low of nearly 6% by 1986. That huge drop in interest rates and explosion in the
money supply would lead to an amazing economic recovery. When coupled with the Reagan-era tax cuts that
also came in that time frame, the markets rallied in anticipation of a new boom. Indeed, the Dow would vault
to 2722 in August 1987 from 777 in August 1982.
A bell often is metaphorically rung in the markets before major economic events occur. In 1982, the bell
sounded loud and clear.
---
Sometimes the messages are both politically and economically important, as was the case in the late spring
of 1990. On June 20, 1990, oil prices hit a low of $15.30 a barrel. In fact, for much of the year, oil prices had
been trending lower. But as summer began, prices began to rise . . . rather inexplicably.
Traders were on the lookout for the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, the big cartel, to rein in
production, and OPEC did manage a production cut that summer that helped to drive prices well off their
lows. However, oil prices just kept on rising, well beyond the level that would have been justified by OPEC's
move.

--- Page 243 ---
Page 243 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
So there had to be another reason that prices kept rising, a reason suggesting that the supply of crude oil
might soon be disrupted. In late July, oil markets suddenly were rife with rumors that Iraqi troops were
mobilizing somewhere near the Kuwaiti border. At the time, few observers of Middle East politics or oil-market
economics had publicly discussed even the remote possibility of another military excursion involving Iraq.
Saddam Hussein's country was still reeling from the recently ended eight-year war with Iran.
But war rumors lingered nonetheless. They became alarmingly specific by the end of July. On Aug. 2, Iraq
invaded Kuwait. Oil prices exploded. Saddam Hussein not only controlled Iraq's oil-producing capacity but
also Kuwait's. The prospects of reduced oil supplies from in and around the Persian Gulf sent oil markets into
a panic. By October, prices spiked to $40.40 a barrel, the highest level ever.
An analysis of oil-price trends leading up to the war show just how prescient the market really was. Oil prices
bottomed out and began rising six weeks before the start of hostilities -- even though few experts were
anticipating a war. Then oil prices peaked and began declining fully two months before the Gulf War began --
even though many political pundits and military analysts were predicting a prolonged war with a formidable
Iraqi foe, which likely would have resulted in skyrocketing oil prices, even higher interest rates and slumping
stocks.
Instead, when the actual fighting began on Jan. 17, 1991, a strange thing happened. Oil prices plunged. The
Dow Jones Industrial Average gained more than 100 points. The U.S. dollar jumped in value. A safe haven
investment, gold, also declined in a surprising manner.
The pundits were wrong. The markets were right. The smart money knew the war would be over in short
order and that oil supplies would not be permanently disrupted -- and that this would be good news for the
markets.
Many contemporaneous sources show that U.S. battle plans for a full-scale assault to liberate Kuwait were
finalized as early as October. Maybe not coincidentally, that's when oil prices peaked. In all of its twists and
turns, the oil markets accurately foretold the direction the war would take. If any market shows how markets
send messages, the oil market in the early 1990s clearly does.
---
The story of Asia in the 1980s and early 1990s was nothing short of remarkable. The newly industrialized
countries of the Pacific Rim were the pride of the region. So fast was the growth in Thailand, Malaysia,
Indonesia, Singapore and South Korea that they were nicknamed "Asian tigers." Because of this success, the
region attracted droves of Western investors, who poured $100 billion into non-Japan Asia in 1996.
But just a year later, in 1997, nearly all of Asia was in recession. By early 1998, some parts of Asia were in a
depression. The economic contraction came quickly and without warning. Or at least to the untrained eye.
Few Asians saw this catastrophe coming, and even fewer Americans did. Yet, it had a profound impact on
financial markets in the U.S. as well as Asia. While most Asians suffered greatly because of this calamity,
many Americans prospered economically because of it.
As always, there were warning signs. And, as always, very few people paid attention to the warning signs
flashing from Manhattan to Malaysia.
The signal of impending disaster came from a rather unlikely locale -- Thailand. On July 2, 1997, the Thai
government devalued its currency, the baht, in what was almost literally a non-event outside the currency
trading rooms of the world's financial centers. In the $1 trillion worth of currency transactions handled daily,
the baht is relatively insignificant.
Nonetheless, the devaluation would signal the onset of a two-year recession in Asia and would come to be
known as "the baht heard round the world."
The plunge in the value of the baht set off a chain reaction of currency devaluations throughout the region.
Much of the foreign investment money that had poured into Asia to finance its rapid growth was quickly pulled
out. The reasons for the run on Asian currencies are complicated and numerous, but the rapid erosion in
confidence exacerbated economic problems that were bubbling to the surface in the summer of 1997.
"Asian contagion," as it was called, wreaked havoc on markets that had no exposure to Asian economies.
The Dow industrials fell sharply as global investors fretted about a possible world-wide collapse. On Oct. 27,
1997, the Dow fell 554 points, at the time its biggest point decline ever.
In Asia, once-strong economies collapsed under a burden of plunging currencies and rising foreign debts.
Tens of billions of dollars were rushed to Thailand, Indonesia, Malaysia and Korea as the West bailed out the
newly troubled countries.

--- Page 244 ---
Page 244 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ironically, the U.S. benefited from the crisis. As economic activity fell sharply in the Far East, oil prices
collapsed and U.S. interest rates plunged, with the U.S. economy enjoying the equivalent of a
multibillion-dollar tax cut. The Asian crisis, oddly enough, extended the economic recovery here, just when
everyone worried it was about to run out of steam.
The fact that the message about a global economic event came from such an obscure corner proves, once
again, that markets do send messages. The real key is knowing where to look.
---
So how about today? What are the markets telling us now?
The frenetic action in the stock market has somewhat muddled the message of the markets. Volatility in
stocks and bonds reached historic proportions in the first few months of the year. The major averages moved
several hundred points in a single day on a routine basis. High-technology and biotechnology stocks had
bigger single-day moves than any market professional can recall witnessing.
But it has become obvious in recent months that the financial markets are getting a hint that not just the U.S.
economy but the global economy may be slowing noticeably.
Bond-market interest rates have fallen, almost inexplicably given the current apparent strength of the U.S.
economy, in particular. The Treasury market yield curve was inverted for months, meaning that short-term
interest rates were higher than long-term interest rates. (That's the opposite of what's normal, because
long-term debt generally carries higher yields to compensate investors for the higher risks of lending their
money for the long haul.) This inversion has been shown by the New York Federal Reserve to be the
single-most accurate predictor of a slowdown or recession of any available financial market indicator. As a
result, interest-sensitive stocks have rallied. Economically sensitive stocks and key commodity prices like
gold and industrial metals have declined, suggesting that demand in the global manufacturing arena may be
slowing.
The bottom line: The message of the market earlier this year is in stark contrast the euphoric moments at the
start of 2000, when the Internet boom and biotech mania lifted the Nasdaq Composite Index to new heights
almost daily. The message then was of an only-good-things-can-happen environment in which new
technology and great strides in genomics would change the way we work and live, and send stocks ever
higher.
But with the stock market performing the way it has lately -- the Dow industrials going up and the Nasdaq
dropping on one day, and then the reverse happening the next day -- the message has become particularly
hard to read, even for the most experienced investors.
Perhaps the best conclusion is that, while the technological changes transforming the economy and powering
stocks are likely still to come, a pause is likely before we can be euphoric again.
---
An excerpt from the book, "The Message of the Markets," by Ron Insana, anchor of CNBC-TV. Copyright
2000 by Ron Insana. Published by HarperBusiness, an imprint of HarperCollins Publishers Inc.

Unilever, Bestfoods
Will Shed Brands
Under EU Clearance
The European Commission gave conditional approval to the acquisition of Bestfoods of the U.S. by
Anglo-Dutch consumer-products company Unilever. The commission said the companies offered "a
significant package of divestments." Bestfoods accepted Unilever's bid in June of $73 a share, or a total of
$20.3 billion, plus the assumption of $4 billion in debt. The commission said the two companies'
"undertakings include the divestment of a significant number of brands," which have combined estimated
annual sales of 500 million euros ($442 million). These include Batchelors in Britain, McDonnells in Ireland,
Royco and Oxo in Europe, Lesieur in France and Bla Band in Scandinavia. Analysts said the required asset
sales were more or less in line with expectations.
A Unilever spokesman said the company was "happy" with the decision. "Now we can begin moving very
quickly to achieve synergies with some of Bestfoods' best brands, like Knorr," he said.
Anglo American to Review De Beers Stake
Cross-shareholdings between South African mining companies Anglo American and De Beers Consolidated
Mines "are no longer logical," Anglo American's chief executive, Tony Trahar, told reporters in Johannesburg.
He said the longstanding share-ownership situation "will be discussed and examined." Anglo American owns
32% of diamond company De Beers, while De Beers holds a 28.7% Anglo American stake. Mr. Trahar said
Anglo American, which is listed in London and Johannesburg and has a market capitalization of about $21.5
billion, should be viewed as a diversified natural-resources group. Its timber-products unit Mondi has proved
"a valuable offset" against gold's recent poor performance, he said. Separately, South Africa's National Union
of Mineworkers said 14,000 workers will begin an all-out strike at Anglo American Platinum, or Amplats,
beginning Monday, after wage talks broke down. But the Mouthpeace Workers Union's 8,700 members have
agreed to a two-year wage pact with Amplats, the world's largest platinum producer.
Finland's Outokumpu to Buy Avesta
Finnish metals and mining company Outokumpu said it agreed to acquire Sweden's Avesta Sheffield, in a
deal that will create the world's second-largest stainless steel producer after Germany's Krupp-Thyssen. The
partners will form a new company called AvestaPolarit, in which Outokumpu will hold 55% and Avesta
Sheffield shareholders 45%. Avesta's main owner, Dutch-British steelmaker Corus, will get 23% of the new
company. Both Corus and the Finnish government, which owns nearly 40% of Outokumpu, have approved
the deal. Outokumpu will put its steel unit into the new entity, and Avesta shareholders will get one share of
Outokumpu Steel for each Avesta share. The deal values the merged company at about 13 billion Swedish
kronor ($1.35 billion).

ASIA / PACIFIC
China Sets Up Fund
To Narrow Shortfall
In Social Security
China is setting up a social-security fund aimed at helping millions of workers laid off by state-owned
enterprises and reforming the country's underfunded pension system, state media reported. The National
Social Security Fund will be managed by a council headed by former Finance Minister Liu Zhongli and will be
under the direct control of the State Council, or cabinet. The government will allow the fund to purchase some
stocks, which would boost liquidity in China's stock market, brokers said. The fund would raise money for
pensions, medical care and unemployment insurance, which until now have been funded by state enterprises
and the government, the official Xinhua news agency reported. The fund would include money allocated by
the central government and from the sale of state assets and returns on the fund's investments.
China set up a new pension system in 1997, but it didn't cover millions of workers who were hired or retired
before then, leaving Beijing with billions of yuan in unfunded pension liabilities. China needs at least 1.8
trillion yuan ($217.4 billion), or 22% of gross domestic product last year, to cover unfunded pension liabilities
of its state employees, officials estimated.
Hong Kong Trade Gap Narrows Sharply
Hong Kong's exports and imports soared well beyond market expectations in August, as the city's economic
recovery steamed ahead. Exports jumped 18% from a year earlier by value, while imports surged 23%, the
government's statistics office said. The territory's trade deficit narrowed to 3.5 billion Hong Kong dollars
(US$448.7 million), from HK$8.12 billion in July. Economists had forecast exports would rise by a median 9%
in August, slowing from July's 9.6% growth, and that imports would increase 12%, compared with 11.6%
growth in July. The trade deficit had been expected to come in between HK$289 million and HK$6.8 billion.
The value of exports in August totaled HK$147 billion; of the total, re-exports accounted for HK$129.7 billion,
up 19.5% from a year before. Re-exports refer to goods originating in mainland China that are processed in
Hong Kong for export. The value of imports totaled HK$150.4 billion. A government spokesman said the
strong growth in exports was "underpinned by strong import absorption in all the major markets."
Chunghwa Telecom IPO Has New Setback
Taiwan's state-controlled Chunghwa Telecom was dealt a fresh blow, as 90% of subscribers for its initial
public offering failed to settle by the deadline Monday. The dismal response meant the island's dominant
telecommunications company has sold only 2.6% of its total shares, and has raised 27.5 billion New Taiwan
dollars (US$880.3 million) in the first tranche of its privatization program. But the IPO's lead underwriter,
Chinatrust Commercial Bank, said the low settlement rate wouldn't affect Chunghwa's plan to list on Taiwan's
stock market Oct. 27. The flop of Chunghwa's IPO, The government had offered to sell 16% of Chunghwa,
and had hoped to pocket NT$192 billion from the share sales this year. International fund managers have
said Chunghwa was too expensive at NT$104 a share.

EUROPE
Germany Plans Pension-System Reform
Germany's government unveiled a plan to reform the country's pension system and told the public to lower its
expectations of the state and rely more on private provisions. Under a plan presented by Labor Minister
Walter Riester, state pensions will fall over 30 years, contributions will rise, and cash grants or tax incentives
will be offered to those who join private pension schemes. The proposal, which must still be approved by
Chancellor Gerhard Schroeder's cabinet and the Parliament, marks a radical break with Germans' traditional
dependence on the state to secure their retirement. It also requires pension providers to guarantee payouts at
least equal to contributions.
U.K. Fines Nomura for Arbitrage Strategy
Britain's securities regulator reprimanded and fined Japanese investment bank Nomura International and two
employees in its equities unit for a strategy used to unwind the firm's Australian index arbitrage position in
1996. The Securities and Futures Authority fined the firm GBP 350,000 ($508,000), as well as costs. Robert
Mapstone, former co-head of Nomura's equity division, was fined GBP 60,000 and is to be expelled from the
register of directors and representatives. Gary Channon, former head of equity derivatives trading, was
severely reprimanded and also fined GBP 60,000.
Turkish Bank Privatization Is Delayed
Ahmet Necdet Sezer, Turkey's president, ordered the government to redraft a decree on privatizing state
banks, the latest delay to hit Turkey's International Monetary Fund-backed disinflation program. The order
may cause a two-month delay. Mr. Sezer sent the cabinet decree back because only a full parliamentary law
can offer the tax breaks envisaged to rehabilitate the state banks.
BRIEFLY:
-- British financial-software company CFS Group said it agreed to acquire U.S. Decision Systems, an
equipment-leasing software and services firm, for $128 million.
-- Scottish biotech upstart Scotia Holdings said the U.S. Food and Drug Administration rejected a regulatory
application for Foscan, its head-and-neck cancer treatment, sending Scotia shares down 63% in London
trading. Some analysts questioned Scotia's future as an independent concern.
-- Britain's Peninsular & Oriental Steam Navigation posted a slightly higher-than-expected 10% rise in
first-half pretax profit to GBP 192.1 million ($279 million), P&O's last results before the Oct. 23 demerger of its
Princess Cruises unit. Princess Cruises' operating profit rose 8% to $168.4 million.
-- Britain's Vodafone AirTouch will create a fund that will invest in Internet and wireless opportunities. The
initial fund will be capitalized at GBP 100 million ($145 million). Goldman Sachs Group will assist the new
Vodafone Ventures unit and will have the right to co-invest on a selective basis.

France Telecom Plans a Major Network
France Telecom said it plans to build a 15,000-mile communications network connecting 28 cities in North
America. The $200 million network will be linked with other networks in Europe, South America and Asia.
France Telecom said it chose France's Alcatel and Nortel Networks of Canada to provide equipment for the
