network. Level 3 Communications, Broomfield, Colo., will provide the dark fiber for the project, France
Telecom said.
Brazil to Auction Power Concessions
Brazil's National Electric Energy Agency said it will hold an auction Nov. 30 for concessions to build and
operate 11 new hydroelectric-power plants in four Brazilian states. The plants will generate a total of 1.4
megawatts, enough to supply 8.6 million people. The concessions will be for 35 years, with companies
expected to invest a total 2.4 billion reals ($1.3 billion).
Bank of Canada to Issue New Notes
The Bank of Canada said it would issue a new series of banknotes starting in January to fight counterfeiting.
The new series will include a 10 Canadian dollar (US$6.75) bill to be issued in January, and a C$5 note that
will be issued later next year. The C$20, C$50 and C$100 notes are expected to be introduced over two to
three years. The central bank in May began the withdrawal of the C$1,000 note as part of the government's
fight against money laundering.
BRIEFLY:
-- Peruvian mining companies Volcan Cia. Minera and Cia. de Minas Buenaventura plan to list on Madrid's
Latibex market for Latin American stocks. Volcan is Peru's largest zinc producer. Buenaventura is part owner
of South America's largest gold mine, Yanacocha. A Volcan executive said the company is also considering
listing on the Toronto Stock Exchange.
-- Canada's Alcan Aluminium said shareholders of Alusuisse Group tendered 80.7% of the Swiss aluminum
maker's shares to Alcan's stock-swap offer, clearing the way for the two companies to merge. The $4.7 billion
deal was subject to at least 67% of Algroup's shares being tendered. The transaction is expected to close
Oct. 17.
-- Latin American portal StarMedia Network and the Brazilian unit of PepsiCo said they are launching an
online service aimed at Brazil's teenagers. The firms have similar sites in Puerto Rico and Venezuela;
another is planned for Argentina.

Beyond Mere Intervention
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Beyond Mere Intervention
942 words
26 September 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Yesterday the euro slipped to an intra-day low of 87.1 cents, below the 87.3 cents at which the world's central
banks intervened Friday, suggesting again that the latest intervention is likely to be as feckless as most. Still,
a symbolic watershed has been crossed; the coordinated intervention means that one and all recognize that
exchange rates matter after all.
That's not to say that many signs of seriousness are emanating from Prague, where central bankers and
finance ministers have gathered for the World Bank-IMF annual meeting. Let alone from the chanceries of
Europe's capitals, which control the fiscal side of the policy mix, the ultimate source of the euro's problems.
For some weeks now, European Central Bank officials have been saying that the value of the euro is out of
line with fundamentals -- central-banker-speak for saying markets have it wrong. The ECB seems to hope
that a whiff of intervention will bring markets to their senses. U.S. authorities have been willing to
accommodate so long, they seem to be saying, as no one thinks they really mean it.
Still, we have just witnessed a little lesson in global interdependence. The euro's weakness popped out in
Silicon Valley; dollar prices for Intel chips meant higher euro prices for computers and slower European sales.
So Intel warned of disappointing earnings, and this rebounded to Wall Street. Markets not only wrote down
Intel's share price, but took a closer look at the high-flying multiples of other technology stocks. There are
even whispers about a potential slowdown in the surging U.S. economy. The weak euro, that is, is a cloud on
the recent miracle economy, even laying aside oil, not a big component of computer chips.
Despite its weak exchange rate, for that matter, the euro itself offers a similar lesson. Internally it is a
considerable success, a key factor in overcoming eurosclerosis and boosting real growth into an acceptable
3% range. Financial developments, particularly an impressive expansion of bond markets, bode well for the
future. In short, it was a very healthy thing to curb exchange-rate perturbations among the European
economies. If only we could do the same for the euro, the dollar and the yen.
In Prague, of course, this will be regarded as the biggest heresy since Jan Hus was burned at the stake in
1415. After buying $3 billion to $5 billion in euros on Friday, the central banks will turn around and sell $3
billion to $5 billion in domestic bonds. This "sterilized" intervention leaves the respective money supplies
unchanged, meaning it will have only the most momentary effect on exchange rates or anything else. That is,
the central banks cling to the belief that they each can maintain an isolated domestic monetary policy in the
face of a tightly integrated world economy.
Last Friday at an IMF panel in Prague, the current Nobel Prize economist Robert Mundell outlined what a
serious intervention would look like. First, it would have a clearly stated objective; he suggested a floor of 85
cents and a ceiling of $1.15 for the euro-dollar rate. Second, it would take place in forward markets as well as
spot markets. Third, it would not be sterilized, but allowed to change money supplies. And fourth, it would be
concerted with partners, the only condition the current intervention met.
Mr. Mundell also suggested that the European central banks use their gold reserves to mint a new gold coin
-- a europa worth 100 euros as legal tender but overvalued at current gold prices. "It was a mistake to delay
for three years the introduction of the paper currency and coins, and the production of a gold currency would
heighten general interest in the euro and at the same time put the EU's excess gold reserves to good use."
Maintaining an 85-cent floor on the euro especially implies a tighter monetary policy by the ECB. It has been
gradually raising interest rates since November, but this has not been enough to turn the tide. Euroland
prices, as measured by the Harmonised Index of Consumer Prices, or HICP, rose at an annual rate of 2.3%
in August after 2.4% in July. The index is expected to accelerate, since producer prices have been rising
faster and monetary aggregates remain above target. And, of course, social unrest has been rising from
energy prices, boosted by a high oil price, huge taxes and depreciation of the euro.

--- Page 252 ---
Page 252 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The ECB has hesitated to tighten further because of a fear it might endanger recovery in the real sector. But
the principal task of monetary authorities is to deliver price stability. At some point, they have to say they've
done their job on prices, and that growth in the real sector is up to their political counterparts. This means tax
and spending cuts to improve incentives for work, savings and production. And a reduction in the regulatory
overload that has exacerbated current energy prices through restraints on exploration and refinery expansion.
One of our hopes for the euro has been that a unified currency, by taking monetary policy out of national
hands, would force politicians of various nations to face up to fiscal and regulatory reforms. This seems to
have started, though only that, with tax cut proposals in Germany and France. If we have reached the point
where further depreciation of the euro is not tolerable in Europe or around the world, both monetary and fiscal
authorities may be one painful step closer to learning the right lessons.

ChiropractorsFuel ClaimsBy Workers
TEXAS JOURNAL
Chiropractors Fuel Claims By Workers
By Russell Gold
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,008 words
20 September 2000
The Wall Street Journal
J
Texas Journal
T1
English
(Copyright (c) 2000, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
AUSTIN -- Texas has among the highest workers' compensation claims costs in the nation, and employers
say the rising use of chiropractors and physical therapists is a big reason why.
Consider Justin Industries Inc. of Fort Worth, which has construction-materials plants in Texas and nearby
states. "You have the same people doing the same work, the same machines and same processes, but the
cost of claims is higher in Texas," says Jim Green, the company's risk manager. About 25% to 30% higher in
cases involving chiropractors or physical therapists, he says.
Several recent studies concur, finding that Texas chiropractors -- and to a lesser extent physical therapists --
are generating higher medical bills and seeing each patient more times than their colleagues in other states.
Factors such as medical inflation add to costs, says William Nemeth, medical adviser to the Texas Workers'
Compensation Commission, but the chiropractors "stick out like a sore thumb."
The cost per claim involving chiropractic services is $3,494 in Texas, nearly twice the big-state average of
$1,817, according to a comparison of the workers' compensation systems in eight states by the Workers
Compensation Research Institute of Cambridge, Mass. And chiropractors in Texas are pulling in a growing
portion of workers' comp medical payments -- 12.4% in 1998 compared with 9% two years before --
according to another report by the Research and Oversight Council on Workers' Compensation in Austin.
Physical therapists are also taking a larger share -- 12% vs. 9.8% two years earlier.
Terry Frakes, senior vice president of the Texas Workers' Compensation Insurance Fund, a state-chartered
carrier in Austin, says there is growing concern that chiropractic costs appear "to be out of line with the rest of
the medical" costs.
Chiropractors defend their services as cost-effective. "Are we able to avoid surgeries?" says Kevin Kanz, an
Austin chiropractor who serves as government affairs director for the Austin-based Texas Chiropractic
Association. "Are we able to get these people back to work faster? Our internal data indicates that is true."
The Research and Oversight Council plans to further analyze treatment results.
As payments to chiropractors have risen, so have their numbers in the state -- up to 2.1 chiropractors per
10,000 in 1999 from 1.9 in 1995, according to the Texas Board of Chiropractic Examiners. Gary Cain, the
board's executive director, calls Texas a "desirable" place to be a chiropractor, citing laws that grant injured
workers direct access to chiropractors, rather than requiring a referral. A growing number of out-of-state
chiropractors, he says, are transferring their licenses here.
So far, the increase in chiropractic and physical therapy costs hasn't produced a big run-up in workers'
compensation premiums for employers. But payouts in the state are rising. Carriers reported $1.22 billion in
payments for policy year 1999, up 11.1% from the two years before. And most carriers are losing money on
such coverage now, says Nancy Moore, a Texas Department of Insurance deputy commissioner. For now,
she says, carriers have been subsidizing their underwriting with income from their investments. But she
expects the competition for policies among carriers to subside and for premiums to start rising. "Things are
going to tighten up in Texas," Ms. Moore says.
No one wants to cut chiropractors out of the system, just place checks on their participation. Dr. Nemeth, for
instance, says chiropractors are still best equipped to handle soft-tissue injuries common to the workplace.
The question is how many visits per injured worker are too many.

--- Page 254 ---
Page 254 of 254 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In April, Texas Workers' Compensation Commission staff proposed requiring preauthorizations for
manipulations, a common chiropractic technique, after 18 visits or six weeks after the injury, whichever
comes first. The commission is expected to vote on the rule in October.
Three insurance carriers jumped the gun, according to Tom Hardy, the Texas Workers' Compensation
Commission director of medical review. Soon after the rules were proposed, the companies -- whose names
the commission said it couldn't provide -- began denying medical bills for lack of pre-approval. Commission
Executive Director Leonard Riley sent out a sternly worded reminder in July advising carriers that he deemed
the practice to "be unreasonably denying medical benefits."
Some doctors are worried too. At the Texas Medical Association's summit last weekend, the
Webb-Zapata-Jim Hogg County Medical Society offered a resolution to lobby the Legislature to allow only
medical and osteopathic doctors to be designated as primary treatment doctors under the worker's
compensation act. The resolution -- which, if enacted, could severely reduce chiropractors' access to injured
workers -- was referred to the Texas Medical Association's legislative council.
Meanwhile, Ted Wilson can give you an idea of how much can be spent on a chiropractor. In 1989, the
former maintenance man at a gasoline-pump manufacturer in Fannin County, suffered a serious spinal injury
while moving office furniture down a flight of stairs. By 1999, his chiropractic bill was $120,621, including
interest.
Travelers Insurance Co., Hartford, Conn., sued to deny payment, arguing in part the treatment was excessive
and never intended to cure Mr. Wilson. Last month, a Texarkana appeals court ordered Travelers to pay the
bill, as the services were "reasonable and necessary." Since the injury occurred the year before the State
Legislature overhauled the workers' compensation system in 1991, it is a so-called old law case. But the new
law allows even greater access to chiropractors and other medical professionals who provide pain
management. "We wouldn't have taken it up under the new law," says Ronald Wren, Travelers' attorney on
the case. Travelers would have simply paid the bills, he says.
Joe Smith, Mr. Wilson's attorney in Dallas, says while the chiropractor's bill was extreme, his client found the
kind of relief that eluded him on the surgeon's table and through pain medication. "You have to let the injured
guy be in control of his life," says Mr. Smith.
